PUB-DATE: February 26, 2000

SECTION: Section A; Page 6; Column 1; Foreign Desk

LENGTH: 952 words

HEADLINE: World Briefing

BYLINE: Compiled By Joseph R. Gregory

 BODY:

ASIA
 
AFGHANISTAN: IMPROVING U.N. RELATIONS -- The top United Nations official in Afghanistan, Francesc Vendrell, said the organization now had offices aiding local government and health services and monitoring human rights in four major cities -- Kabul, Kandahar, Herat and Mazaar-I-Sharif. He also said women were again working with relief agencies, after being barred from jobs outside their homes. Barbara Crossette (NYT) 

PHILIPPINES: BOMB ATTACK ON A BUS -- More than two dozen people were killed and many more were wounded when a bomb exploded in a bus aboard a ferry heading across Panguil Bay to Ozamis on the island of Mindanao, the police said. No group claimed responsibility, but similar attacks have in the past been attributed to Muslim separatists. Seth Mydans (NYT)
 
INDONESIA: CONCILIATORY WORDS FROM MILITARY -- The chief of the armed forces, Adm. Widodo Adisutjipto, said the military was prepared to give up all 38 of its seats in Parliament within the next four years. The move follows widespread demands for the armed forces to get out of politics after decades of authoritarian rule. Seth Mydans (NYT)
 
CHINA: TALKS WITH U.S. AS TAIWAN TENSIONS RISE -- With China raising new threats against Taiwan, the commander of the Pacific Fleet, Adm. Dennis Blair, arrives on Sunday to meet with Chinese military leaders. American diplomats said the four-day visit had been "in the works for quite a while," and was not specifically related to Taiwan. Elisabeth Rosenthal (NYT)
 
INDIA: COALITION TESTED -- In its first test since assuming power four months ago, the ruling Hindu nationalist-led coalition appeared headed for decisive election victories in state elections in Haryana and Orissa. But it appeared to be falling behind in Bihar, India's second-most-populous state. Celia W. Dugger (NYT)
 
EUROPE
 
NORTHERN IRELAND: ARMY BASE BOMBING -- A small bomb exploded early yesterday at an army base, the authorities said. No one was injured in the blast, the second in the past three weeks. The Continuity I.R.A., a splinter group that has refused to abide by the cease-fire reached by the province's main nationalist and unionist groups, claimed responsibility. Sarah Lyall (NYT)
 
GERMANY: DRUG CENTERS -- The government adopted a law allowing drug addicts to use special injection centers that provide clean needles and health counseling. Germany, Switzerland and Spain have been experimenting with such programs, but the United Nations said earlier this week that injection centers could be a violation of international law. Victor Homola (NYT)
 
AFRICA
 
SENEGAL: ELECTION ON SUNDAY -- The eight candidates in Sunday's presidential election wound up their campaigns, as the main opposition candidate, Abdoulaye Wade, warned of violence if the voting was not fair. President Abdou Diouf is running for a fourth consecutive term, but he faces three credible opponents and is not assured of victory. Norimitsu Onishi (NYT)
 
SIERRA LEONE: PEACEKEEPERS EXCHANGE FIRE -- United Nations peacekeepers exchanged fire for the first time this week with Revolutionary United Front rebels, who have repeatedly flouted a shaky peace accord. A United Nations statement said the peacekeepers fired on rebels trying to loot a village on Pepel Island, 70 miles north of the capital, Freetown, on Wednesday. The troops then freed prisoners held by the rebels. The rebels also blocked Indian and Ghanaian United Nations troops trying to enter the diamond mining heartland. (AP)
 
THE AMERICAS
 
CUBA: DISSIDENT CONVICTED -- The first major trial of a Cuban dissident in over a year ended after four hours, with the authorities sentencing Dr. Oscar Elias Biscet, 38, to three years in prison -- less than half of what state prosecutors requested -- for protests like hanging the nation's flag upside-down. Despite the international attention focused on the case, the Cuban police detained several dissidents and discouraged others from attending the trial. David Gonzalez (NYT)
 
ARGENTINA: SILENCE ON KIDNAPPINGS -- Jorge Rafael Videla, the dictator who ruled Argentina in the 1970's, refused to testify before a federal judge investigating his role in the kidnappings of babies born to political prisoners. Mr. Videla, who has remained under house arrest for more than a year, invoked his constitutional right to remain silent. Clifford Krauss (NYT)
 
ARGENTINA: NEW LABOR CODE -- As a threatened national strike fizzled, the lower house of the Congress passed a labor code designed to lengthen probation periods for newly hired workers, weaken the bargaining power of national unions and otherwise cut business costs. The bill now goes to the Senate, where President Fernando de la Rua may have to compromise with the opposition to enact his most important economic initiative. Clifford Krauss (NYT)
 
MEXICO: ANGER AT U.S. OVER DRUG COMMENTS -- After Ambassador Jeffrey Davidow said Mexico had assumed a leading role in international drug trafficking and compared it to Sicily's position as a world headquarters for the Mafia, the Foreign Ministry called in the American charge d'affaires, James Derham, to express "the surprise of the Mexican government" and to insist that Mexico is cooperating vigorously in the drug war. Sam Dillon (NYT)
 
MIDDLE EAST
 
ISRAEL, LEBANON: FIGHTING FLARES AGAIN -- After a lull of close to a week, fighting resumed in south Lebanon. Israeli warplanes fired six rockets, there was an exchange of ground fire near the Israeli border and a mortar shell landed in a field in northern Israel. No one was injured. Deborah Sontag (NYT)

TI GROUP, London, a specialized engineering and manufacturing company, said its vehicle-parts manufacturing unit, TI Group Automotive Systems, would cut 105 jobs and close a factory in Valdosta, Ga., in July.

HEADLINE: On Health Care, Gore or Bradley?

 BODY:

To the Editor:
Re "The Bradley Trip" (column, Feb. 24), by Bob Herbert: The implicit message in Al Gore's "step-by-step" approach to health care reform is that the steps will be in the right direction: the Clinton administration's approach since the failure of its comprehensive health plan. 
 Yet since 1993 the number of uninsured in the United States has increased by more than four million people. The number covered by Medicaid has declined by nearly as many. The paradox of a booming economy and dramatic increases in people without health insurance is hardly an argument for political caution. It's a sign of a failing health care system and failed leadership.
Mr. Herbert may be correct that our leaders will be afraid to take up this issue again. But only dramatic steps will lead to improvement.
 
JOHN W. RODAT
Delmar, N.Y., Feb. 24, 2000

HEADLINE: THE DIALLO VERDICT: THE REACTION;
Verdict Bares Sharp Feelings On Both Sides

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. McFADDEN 

 BODY:
Strong and deeply polarized emotions -- anger, bitter indignation and stunned disbelief on one side, and expressions of relief and vindication on the other -- rippled across the metropolitan area last night as politicians, community leaders and ordinary New Yorkers reacted swiftly to the acquittal of four white police officers in the killing of an unarmed black immigrant, Amadou Diallo.
In a case that had painfully divided the city along racial and political lines for more than a year, the verdict -- televised and watched in offices, in homes, in stores and shopping centers -- came in the late afternoon as an enormous emotional release for people whose feelings had been building toward a climax during the four-week trial. 
 There were no serious or widespread disturbances, but in Brooklyn and the Bronx, in Queens, Harlem and Midtown Manhattan, at City Hall and in the suburbs, the reaction was spontaneous, vehement and, judging by rough numbers in quick interviews and by the heat of the comments, largely negative.
Crowds chanting "Murderers!" gathered in a cold rain outside the courthouse in Albany, and outside the Bronx building where Mr. Diallo was slain in a hail of 41 bullets. As police officers watched silently, many in the crowds shouted angrily of injustice upon injustice.
Two men held up black wallets outside the vestibule on Wheeler Avenue where the victim died. "It looks like a gun," someone jeered. Mr. Diallo had been pulling out his wallet when the officers, who said they thought it was a gun, shot him.
"I cried when those cops went free," said Cecile E. Bailey, 57, who has lived on Story Avenue, near the scene of the shooting, for 28 years. "You don't shoot a human being 41 times. You don't shoot a dog 41 times."
Much of the reaction was predictable: the black and Hispanic communities in Harlem denouncing the acquittals as a travesty, while a mixed response was more apparent in Bensonhurst, Brooklyn, and the Riverdale section of the Bronx. "Justice was done, the system worked," said Steve Shek, 32, of Sheepshead Bay, Brooklyn. "It was a horrible accident, and the cops deserve to go free. They should never have been charged in the first place."
Commuters in Midtown and people on the streets of Harlem, Brooklyn and other sections of the city paused for interviews or to talk among themselves about the outcome. Most said they thought that the verdict was unfair, or that the prosecution had performed inadequately. Many expressed the hope that federal civil rights charges might be brought against the officers.
Some expressed no surprise, saying they had known when the venue was switched to Albany that the officers would be acquitted. Others said they were flabbergasted that the officers had not been found guilty of something, anything.
Jonathan Johnson, a black unemployed Harlem resident, was astonished. "Acquitted on all charges? Not even reckless endangerment?" he said. "I bet if a stray bullet had hit a white person they would have been convicted of something. It goes back to slavery. They think that we are property and they can kill us."
Many said that the issue was not that the officers fired at Mr. Diallo, but that they fired so many times.
Many immigrants, who had watched the trial closely, said the officers should have taken into account that there may have been a language barrier. "At least they should have understood that some people don't speak English well," David Cruz, 22, of the Dominican Republic, said in Spanish.
There were pleas for calm by City Hall, by Mr. Diallo's parents and by the Rev. Al Sharpton, who said: "We do not want to tarnish his name with any violence. Let not one brick be thrown. Let not one bottle be thrown. Do not confuse us with the violent ones, the reckless ones."
And despite the intensity of feelings over the verdict, there were no reports of serious or widespread violence, although a few scuffles broke out, people banged on cars outside the 43rd Precinct station and marchers tried to disrupt traffic on the Bronx River Parkway. At least 15 people were arrested.
Reaction to the verdict was hardly one-sided, and it often crossed racial lines. Some whites said they thought the officers should have been found guilty of something, while there were blacks who said they thought that the officers had received a fair trial and that justice had been done.
"I'm pleased with the outcome," said Mayimuna Pettiford, a black neighbor of one of the acquitted officers, Edward McMellon, in Park Slope, Brooklyn. "I've known him and his family for 10 years. He's not a racist. He's an empathetic soul."
At City Hall, Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani expressed sympathy for the Diallo family and called the shooting a tragedy, but he also voiced support for the acquitted police officers and, paraphrasing the judge, said that "the book is closed on this case."
He said the trial, which had been moved to Albany from the Bronx because of pretrial publicity and what he called a "carnival atmosphere," had been fair, and he expressed doubt that a federal review would find any basis for a new trial of the officers on civil rights charges.
The verdict even attracted attention in the presidential campaign. Former Senator Bill Bradley, speaking to reporters in Everett, Wash., said he was stunned by the acquittals. "I think that it shows that when racial profiling seeps so deeply into somebody's mind, a wallet in the hand of a white man looks like a wallet, but a wallet in the hand of a black man looks like a gun."
Vice President Al Gore, flying from Ohio to California, issued a more neutral statement, urging calm and noting that the case would now be reviewed by the Justice Department. "That review should be thorough and fair," Mr. Gore said.
In Albany, the acquitted officers and their families were hustled from the courthouse to waiting vans, but the crowd of protesters saw them and shouted "Guilty!" and "Murderers!" Later, Mr. Diallo's parents spoke briefly. Saikou Diallo said moving the trial to Albany was "the second murder" of his son, while his former wife, Kadiatou Diallo, thanked the family's supporters and asked for "your calm and prayers."
In New York, the reaction of political leaders was mixed.
State Senator David A. Paterson, a Harlem Democrat, said he was disappointed but not surprised by the verdict, calling it part of a pattern of acquittals of officers who had killed black people, including Michael Stewart and Eleanor Bumpurs. "Juries acquit because of the general premise that police officers are always acting in the right," he said.
Former Mayor David N. Dinkins said he was "outraged and very disappointed" by the acquittals, and said the verdict suggested that the Street Crime Unit (whose slogan is "We own the night") "can do pretty much as they wish."
The City Council speaker, Peter F. Vallone, did not criticize the verdict, but used the occasion to repeat his call for police reforms including residency requirements, and improved training and community relations. City Comptroller Alan G. Hevesi and the public advocate, Mark Green, offered similar statements.
But the real passion over the verdict was to be found on the streets of New York and Albany. A scuffle briefly broke out among factions of Diallo supporters outside the courthouse in Albany after the verdict. One faction was shouting in protest, while the other wanted demonstrators to remain quiet. The police quickly moved in.
In the Bronx, two people were arrested on charges of disorderly conduct and resisting arrest at 7:45 p.m. as they marched on the Bronx River Parkway toward the 43rd Precinct station on Fteley Avenue. Barricades were set up as a large crowd of protesters gathered, but the demonstration had grown quiet by 8:30 p.m. Later, 13 people were arrested in bottle-throwing and other protests.

HEADLINE: THINK TANK;
Rhode Island and Slavery? How Much to Read Into an Old Name

BYLINE: By Laurence Zuckerman

 BODY:
If the presidential candidates thought they were finished with bitter and parochial historical debates like whether the Confederate flag should fly over South Carolina's state capitol, they were mistaken. Tiny Rhode Island, which will hold its primary on March 7, could offer another uncomfortable controversy.
At a breakfast last month in honor of the Rev. Dr. Martin Luther King Jr., the Rev. Virgil Wood, a pastor in Providence and president of the Ministers' Alliance, a group of African-American clergy in the state, announced a campaign to remove the word "plantations" from Rhode Island's official name. Most people probably don't realize that its full name is State of Rhode Island and Providence Plantations. 
 Resentment over the name has simmered in the state's small black population for years to little effect. But the conflict in South Carolina revived the issue, igniting a new debate over the state's slave-era history.
Within a week of Mr. Wood's remarks, an editorial in the Providence Journal opposed changing the state's name, arguing that the word plantations had little to do with slavery at the time it was attached to the state name. "The slave trade was important to the later Colonial economy of Rhode Island, but slavery itself never was essential to farming here or comparable to its institutionalization in the South," the paper wrote. "To eradicate the word because of later associations in another part of the country makes no sense. It panders to outright ignorance of history."
Critics immediately responded that it was the Journal that was ignorant of history. On the Web site of The American Prospect magazine, Jeremy Derfner wrote that the Journal's reaction was consistent with a long record of denial of the prominence of slavery in New England as described by the historian Joanne Pope Melish in her 1998 book, "Disowning Slavery: Gradual Emancipation and 'Race' in New England, 1780-1860" (Cornell University Press).
Ms. Melish, an associate professor of history at the University of Kentucky, writes that on the eve of the Revolution one of every 14 people in Rhode Island was a slave, the largest proportion in all the New England colonies. She argues that the need to portray a virtuous North battling the slave-holding South during the Civil War resulted in the creation of a "mythology of a free New England" in the antebellum period and that the notion persists to this day.
Apart from the central role that the ports of Bristol, Providence and Newport played in the slave trade, Professor Melish makes the case that slavery was far more important to New England's economy than is commonly recognized by historians.
"What New Englanders like to do is admit the trade and deny the domestic institution of slavery," she said in an interview.
Professor Melish and with other historians acknowledge that plantation is a medieval English word describing farmlands that had no direct connection to slavery when Rhode Island received its royal charter in the 17th century. The state's name refers to settlements on the island where Newport is, which was then known as Rhode Island, and to those on the mainland, which were collectively called Providence Plantations.
That makes little difference to Mr. Wood, who argues that African-Americans are still pained by the word. "It is a requirement of a civil society that it does not insult some of its citizens knowingly," he said. "And there is no longer any room for not knowing."
Leading politicians in the state have not rallied to his call. Lisa Pelosi, spokeswoman for Gov. Lincoln C. Almond, a Republican, said that he "is inclined not to be in favor of changing the name of the state" but added that he could change his mind if the idea became more popular.
The state's full name appears only on official proclamations and some official stationery. "Most people don't refer to the state as Rhode Island and Providence Plantations, but some people have become very vested in the name all of a sudden," said David N. Cicilline, a Democratic state representative. "I'm not exactly sure why."
Mr. Cicilline said the name should be changed because "it carries the vestiges of a hurtful time in American history, and we don't need it." He recently introduced a bill that would convene a constitutional convention, which is required to revise the state's charter.
But Keith Stokes, the executive director of the Newport Chamber of Commerce, a local historian who is an expert on early African-American and Jewish settlement in the area and an African-American, said that changing the name would be "at best historical revisionism and and at worst downright censorship."
Though he is sympathetic to Mr. Wood's cause, he said the point should be to help people understand the complexity of the past, not to erase it. "History is history," he explained. "We can't revise it for the people of today."
Professor Melish, who grew up in Connecticut and lived for many years in Rhode Island, supports changing the name because "its continued use gives the subordination of African-Americans a sort of currency." The name is also objectionable, she said, because it appears to endorse the domination of the rest of the state by the city of Providence.

HEADLINE: Democrats Look to Montana For Break in Republican Sea

BYLINE: By MICHAEL JANOFSKY

DATELINE: HELENA, Mont., Feb. 22

 BODY:
The Rocky Mountain West is about as Republican as any region of the country gets these days. All five states -- Colorado, Montana, New Mexico, Utah and Wyoming -- are led by Republican governors, and each state has a Congressional delegation that is overwhelmingly Republican.
Elections this year are not likely to change the lineup in four of the states. But here in Montana, where Republican dominance is relatively new, Democrats believe they have their best chance for a major breakthrough. 
 Gov. Marc Racicot is barred by law from seeking a third term. Representative Rick Hill, a two-term Republican who holds the state's only House seat, is retiring for medical reasons. And Senator Conrad Burns, a Republican who once promised to serve only two terms, has given Democrats an issue by seeking a third.
"The pendulum is swinging back," said Bob Ream, chairman of the Montana Democratic Party, offering a view Republicans ardently dispute. "We think we have strong possibilities in all three races."
Unlike some of its neighbors, Montana has a rich Democratic history. Mike Mansfield served five terms in the House and four in the Senate through 1977. In 1988, Mr. Burns became just the second Montana Republican ever elected to the Senate, winning 36 years after the first, Zales Ecton, left it. Mr. Hill's predecessor was Pat Williams, a Democrat who retired after 18 years.
And three Democrats served as governor over 20 years before Stan Stephens won for the Republicans in 1988 and was followed four years later by Mr. Racicot.
Montanans have shown a streak of independence, as well. While Mr. Racicot just squeaked by in 1992 with 51 percent of the vote, Montanans favored Bill Clinton over President George Bush and gave Ross Perot one of his best showings anywhere with 26 percent of the vote.
Four years later, Mr. Racicot won with a resounding 79 percent of the vote -- his initial Democratic challenger, Chet Blaylock, died three weeks before the election -- but Bob Dole defeated President Clinton by only three percentage points.
"Generally, people in Montana are always open to new possibilities," Mr. Racicot said, reflecting the unpredictable nature of the state electorate. "We are ruggedly individualistic. Particularly in political activities, we have a very personal and high level of involvement."
At the top of the Democrats' wish list this year is Nancy Keenan, their House candidate, who has proved to be one of the most successful vote-getters in state history. A former state lawmaker who has served the last 12 years as superintendent of Montana's Office of Public Instruction, Ms. Keenan won more votes in her last election, 1996, than the state's Democratic senator, Max Baucus, did in winning a fourth term the same year.
Representative Patrick J. Kennedy of Rhode Island, chairman of the Democratic Congressional Campaign Committee, said the party was counting Mr. Hill's seat, one of 22 that Republicans are vacating for retirement or pursuit of higher office, as one of the six seats Democrats need to regain control of the House.
Republicans hold 24 of the 29 House seats from the Rocky Mountain States. Few, if any, incumbents are expected to lose, and the only open seat in the region is Montana's. But among Democratic challengers in the five states, no one has a better chance than Ms. Keenan, said Charles E. Cook Jr., a nonpartisan analyst in Washington who follows federal elections. He rates the race a tossup.
Ms. Keenan's chief advantage derives from her popularity and high name recognition. But she is facing another strong and well-known opponent in Dennis Rehberg, who stepped down as lieutenant governor to run against Mr. Baucus in 1996 and gave him his toughest race in four elections, losing by just five percentage points. Mr. Rehberg also won more of Montana's 56 counties in his race than Ms. Keenan did in hers.
New statewide laws on term limits have led to a traffic jam in the Democratic primary race for governor among three men who are friends and colleagues. Unable to run again, the men, Attorney General Joseph P. Mazurek, State Auditor Mark O'Keefe and Secretary of State Mike Cooney are all campaigning on the same themes -- raising incomes that are among the lowest in the country, expanding the economy and improving education -- but offering slightly different solutions.
The survivor is likely to face Judy Martz, the current lieutenant governor and the first woman to hold that position, who is facing a primary challenge from the right in Rob Natelson, a law professor at the University of Montana.
Mr. O'Keefe is regarded as the favorite among the Democrats although recent polls show that he would lose a close race to Ms. Martz.
The Senate race is probably the most difficult for the Democrats. After a narrow victory in 1988 to upset Senator John Melcher, a three-term Democrat, Mr. Burns had an easier time six years later, brushing aside Jack Mudd, a law professor, by nearly 2 to 1.
This time, Mr. Burns is facing Brian Schweitzer, a farmer who last ran for office in college, winning the presidency of the American Society of Agronomy Students.
Mr. Schweitzer is already in full attack mode, criticizing Mr. Burns as a pawn of special interests who went back on his word to serve only two terms. Matt Raymond, a spokesman for Mr. Burns, said that the senator would have been satisfied to quit after two terms if the Supreme Court had not ruled term limits for federal office unconstitutional and that Mr. Burns would respond to criticism after the primary.
That does not satisfy Mr. Schweitzer, who is substantially behind Mr. Burns in fund-raising. "Our economy is imploding," he said. "We need new leadership. People all over Montana are ready for a change."
As usual.

HEADLINE: THE 2000 CAMPAIGN: THE PROVING GROUND;
Ohio Like Michigan, With Higher Hurdles

BYLINE: By DAVID E. ROSENBAUM

DATELINE: COLUMBUS, Ohio, Feb. 25

 BODY:
This state resembles Michigan in its demographics and politics, and on March 7, Senator John McCain hopes to replicate here his stunning victory in the Michigan Republican primary on Tuesday.
To do that, he will have to overcome, as he did in Michigan, the support Gov. George W. Bush is getting from the popular Republican governor and most of the rest of the party's officials in Ohio. 
 He will also have to win, as he did in Michigan, the support of thousands of unionized factory workers who split from the Democratic Party in the 1980's to vote for Ronald Reagan and then returned to the fold to vote for Bill Clinton in the 1990's.
But he will have the advantage of favorable voting rules. As was the case in Michigan, independents and Democrats are free to vote and choose delegates in the Republican primary here. But unlike the situation in Michigan, there will be a Democratic primary that could siphon votes from Mr. McCain.
Today, Mr. McCain began running television commercials statewide, making him the first presidential candidate in either party this year to advertise extensively here. On Saturday, he will campaign in Cleveland, Columbus and Cincinnati, the first time he has set foot in Ohio in the race except for two quick fund-raisers last year. No other candidate this year has spent a full day campaigning across Ohio.
"We're starting off behind, but I really think he has a chance to win," said Senator Mike DeWine, the only politician of any note in Ohio who is supporting Mr. McCain.
Remarking on Mr. McCain's television advertisements and his ambitious schedule on Saturday, Curt Steiner, an experienced Republican organizer here who is working as a volunteer for the Arizona senator, said, "We now have clear evidence that John McCain is going to work to win Ohio."
So far, New York and California, where 263 delegates to the Republican National Convention are at stake in March 7 primaries open only to Republicans, have overshadowed Ohio, which has 69 delegates.
Even the newspapers have offered little coverage.
At a mall in Dublin, on the outskirts of Columbus, it was clear that none of the dozen people interviewed on Thursday night had begun to focus on the campaign.
A telephone company technician said he always voted for the candidate his union backed, but he did not yet know who that was this year. Mary Massey, a stay-at-home mother who hopes to get a real estate license, said she would certainly vote for a Republican but needed to know more about the candidates' views on abortion and education before she decided which one.
But now it looks as if things are about to pop. "The Michigan results were like a mug of hot coffee in terms of waking people up," Mr. Steiner said.
On the Democratic side, Vice President Al Gore was in Cleveland today speaking at a retirement home about long-term care. Former Senator Bill Bradley's campaign opened storefront offices in seven locations around the state this week and began hiring people to operate telephone banks, the campaign's spokesman, Alan Melamed, said.
The consensus in Democratic circles is that Mr. Gore should win handily. Union members and African-Americans are disproportionately important in Democratic primaries in Ohio, and Mr. Gore seems to have solid support from those groups.
Mr. Bush plans to be in the state early next week, his campaign said. And Gov. Bob Taft, the chairman of the Bush campaign in Ohio, said in an interview in his office on Thursday that he planned to spend an increasing amount of his own time and energy on the primary.
"Bush has got to win in Ohio to become the nominee," Governor Taft said. That may well be the case for Mr. McCain too, and he faces substantial obstacles.
The most serious one is that some of the kinds of Democrats and independents who provided the McCain margin of victory in Michigan, where only the Republicans were holding a primary, will doubtless vote in the Democratic primary in Ohio.
"I fully expect him to win the same proportion of the Republican vote he won in Michigan," Governor Taft said, "and I don't think there will be enough independents and Democrats to make up the difference."
In Michigan, Mr. Bush won two-thirds of the votes from Republicans. But more than half of the voters in the primary were independents or Democrats, and they went heavily for Mr. McCain.
Like Michigan, Ohio is a state with decaying cities and prosperous, sprawling suburbs; with old factories, heavily unionized, and sparkling new high-tech companies; with fertile farmlands and scores of booming small towns. In recent years, Republicans have dominated the governments in both states, but Mr. Clinton carried both states in the last two presidential elections.
Governor Taft said he did not expect to be embarrassed the way Gov. John Engler was in Michigan. Mr. Engler threw all his political weight behind Governor Bush only to see him lose, partly because some Democrats who did not like Mr. Engler voted for Mr. McCain.
Mr. Engler has been governor for nearly a decade. Mr. Taft has been in office for only a year. "I haven't had the time to accumulate as many enemies," Mr. Taft said.
In addition, Ohio does not have a tradition of crossover voting like New Hampshire and Michigan, said John Green, director of the Ray C. Bliss Institute of Applied Politics at the University of Akron.
The last public polls in Ohio showed Mr. Bush with a comfortable lead, but they were in early February, before the primaries in South Carolina and Michigan. That makes them irrelevant to the situation today, said Eric Rademacher, a pollster at the University of Cincinnati.
Mr. Bush has at least some organization in each of Ohio's 88 counties. Mr. McCain will have to rely on his commercials and the attention he draws from campaigning personally in the state. He is getting no help from party leaders, who last week abruptly canceled a Republican dinner in Cleveland where the senator was to be the featured speaker.
The two commercials Mr. McCain's campaign began broadcasting today emphasize his determination to take on what the advertisements call "special interests."
The senator has no organization in the state whatsoever and not a single paid staff member.
"To call us loose knit," said Mr. Steiner, "would be an exaggeration."

CORRECTION-DATE: March 1, 2000, Wednesday

 CORRECTION:
Because of an editing error, an article and a chart on Saturday surveying voter turnout this year referred incorrectly to a record for a Michigan's Republican primary. While more people voted this time than in the 1976 primary, this year's turnout was not a record. Because the voting-age population has grown, the turnout was 17 percent of those eligible, compared with the record of 17.4 in 1976.

HEADLINE: ECONOMIC VIEW;
Subjecting Greenspan's Theories to Peer Review

BYLINE: By LOUIS UCHITELLE 

 BODY:
ECONOMIC theory is wonderfully pliable. Just when the Federal Reserve seemed to be running out of reasons to slow the economy in anticipation of a rising inflation rate that is taking forever to rise, Alan Greenspan has in the last 10 days come up with a new theory that requires the Fed to act, and quickly.
Mr. Greenspan's thesis makes productivity both a blessing and a curse: a blessing, because it raises the supply of goods and services, and a curse, because -- working through the stock market -- it increases the demand. The demand, in fact, outstrips the newly augmented supply. Shortages then raise prices and the inflation rate. 
 That is quite a mouthful of a theory, but one hard to ignore when coming from the Fed's august chairman. The same proposition, offered by an ordinary economist, would end up buried in an economics journal, peppered with challenges from peers.
Listen to peer review from Robert M. Solow, a Nobel laureate at M.I.T.: "You cannot say that the Greenspan thesis is nonsense or cannot happen. But it is not enough to say that it is happening. You need a lot of numbers work to convince a person that what he says represents a clear and present danger."
Alan S. Blinder of Princeton University, a former Fed vice chairman, makes a similar point. "This is sound economic theorizing," he said, " but does it fit the facts?"
Fitting theory -- and policy -- to the facts has been a great source of angst lately at the Fed. To the Fed, as well as to many mainstream economists, the booming economy must result in too much demand and inflationary shortages. The problem is that shortages and inflation are not in sight. Still, the Fed has started down the path of higher rates to slow the economy and dilute demand.
That puts a lot of pressure on theory to justify a slowdown that will inevitably hurt a lot of Americans. Perhaps that is why Mr. Greenspan felt compelled to put a new layer of explanation on top of the two theories he has long proffered to explain how the nation has sidestepped rising prices this long but cannot do so any longer.
One holds that we have avoided shortages and inflation because the Asian crisis and a weak Europe diverted a lot of goods to America that could not be sold elsewhere. A strong dollar helped to keep the prices of these imports down, and oil was also abundant and cheap. Now, however, these "favorable supply shocks" are disappearing. The second theory says that the pool of available workers was deeper than anticipated, thanks in part to so many immigrants. But the pool is finally running dry and soon wages will rise as employers bid for workers. Companies will then raise prices to cover the higher wage costs.
These are old-line theories shared by many economists and forecasters. The new one puts Mr. Greenspan out alone on a limb. "Like any human being, he has his hunches and his sense of what is going on, and he is looking around for rationalizations," Mr. Solow said.
Mr. Greenspan's new rationale marries two observations. On the one hand, the economy is booming without inflation partly because productivity has risen; workers produce more goods and services per hour, and the revenue from the added output fattens profits and pays for raises, without higher prices. On the other hand, the voracious consumption that makes the economy so robust comes largely from rising stock prices; people feel wealthier, and this wealth effect prompts them to spend more than their incomes, borrowing to do so.
The new Greenspan link is this: The pickup in productivity has raised expectations that productivity will continue to rise, or at least level off at a higher rate than in the recent past. The prospect of better productivity ahead has raised expectations of higher corporate profits, and share prices have risen in anticipation, increasing the wealth effect and the spending. The rub, as Mr. Greenspan put it last week, is that the stock market is "creating additional purchasing power for which no additional goods or services have yet been produced." So excess demand will create inflationary shortages, unless the Fed's higher rates reduce the steam.
That is a neat theory. The stock market may have gone way up for quite rational reasons, not irrational speculation. But does this theory square with the facts?
Mr. Solow, for his part, is not convinced that the wealth effect is really the source of so much spending and investment. And Mr. Blinder wonders whether most of the improvement in productivity is behind us, in which case Mr. Greenspan's "thesis might be a better explanation of the last several years than the present."
Mr. Greenspan's theory, in fact, may be one more explanation that fails to justify the Fed's decision to raise interest rates. For now, anyway.

HEADLINE: INVESTING;
How to Beat Mr. Greenspan's Stock Market

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. HERSHEY Jr. 

 BODY:
WHEN Alan Greenspan frets openly that stock prices have been rising faster than household incomes, it's a good idea to assume that this is highly practical information, something other than his frequently opaque comments.
With wealth from stock gains an increasingly large factor in propelling the economy, Mr. Greenspan, the Federal Reserve chairman, fears that the additional spending this generates will revive inflation. 
 Taking him at his word implies that he would like for stocks to rise a little faster than the roughly 5 percent that incomes grew last year. Such an exercise is more art than science, of course, and Mr. Greenspan last week called this relationship a "diagnostic tool," not a target of policy.
Of course, not many people are likely to be satisfied with returns of 5 percent. But if that is what the overall market is returning, it is fair to assume that investors should be content to receive, say, double that amount.
And given last week's 357-point drop in the Dow Jones industrials, maybe even 10 percent is optimistic.
But how to hit that bogey? There are a number of strategies that are likely to provide a 10 percent return over the next year or so, and to do so without a sleep-depriving level of risk.
(One caveat: Trying to lock in a 10 percent return can mean relying on high dividends, which are taxed as ordinary income. So it may make sense for investors to use these strategies in an Individual Retirement Account or a 401(k) plan.)
A UTILITY STOCK The Dow Jones utilities index is yielding well over 4 percent, which gets an investor nearly halfway to that 10 percent goal right off the bat. But picking the right stock can mean a higher yield and better-than-average growth prospects. Consolidated Edison, which provides New York City and Westchester County with electricity and natural gas, is selling for little more than half its May 1999 high and now offers investors an 8.3 percent yield. The company is also buying back shares.
With Energy Nuclear of Jackson, Miss., reaffiriming interest in the Con Edison-owned Unit 2 of the Indian Point nuclear plant, despite a recent leak of radioactive steam, Con Ed stock is likely to rebound, said Barry Abramson, a PaineWebber analyst. Con Ed now trades at $26.1875.
"The stock is very cheap," he said, noting that Con Ed is acquiring Northeast Utilities, the biggest utility in New England. "The upside could be better than 10 percent, back into the 30's" by year-end. And Jeanne Mockard, manager of the Putnam Utilities Growth and Income fund, called it "one of the plainest vanilla" utilities, adding that it had very solid management.
A REAL ESTATE PLAY Real estate investment trusts, or REIT's, have lost about a fifth of their value over the last three years, so many investors presume that a lot of the risk has been rung out of the industry. But the highest-yielding REIT's may have trouble making their payouts; they have the poorest prospects for capital appreciation. So stick with quality.
One conservative candidate is AvalonBay Communities, which owns and runs apartment-house communities and yields 6.12 percent -- considerably less than many other REIT's. The company, based in Alexandria, Va., operates in expensive markets where space is tight and barriers to entry are high, mainly on the West Coast and in the Northeast.
That prompts Martin Cohen of Cohen & Steers Capital Management to suggest that AvalonBay, selling at less than 10 times projected funds from operations for 2000, could post a double-digit 12-month return from market appreciation alone. "Landlords have pricing power," he said.
A MUTUAL FUND OPTION Almost 71 percent of all diversified stock funds returned more than 10 percent in 1999. But the vast majority held big and risky technology stakes. That is one reason that Russel Kinnel, director of fund analysis at Morningstar Inc., suggested Lexington Corporate Leaders, an unusual $385 million no-load fund dating from 1935. It had just 4.3 percent of its assets in technology stocks last year. With a portfolio of 25 large-capitalization stocks that almost never changes, except for occasional spinoffs like Lucent Technologies from AT & T, the fund operates at very low cost and minimizes tax liability for shareholders. Such costs matter far more to investors when returns are relatively low.
The fund holds roughly the same number of shares in each of its companies and is always fully invested. It has lost money in only one calendar year since 1990, and yielded 3.36 percent as of Jan. 31.
"We have a lot of investors who see it as a core holding for domestic equities," said Richard Lavery, senior vice president of Lexington Management.
A BOND THAT CAN SIZZLE Convertible bonds -- and there are $400 billion of them worldwide -- can offer some of a stock's upside, but with limited risk. Clear Channel Communications, a San Antonio broadcasting and outdoor advertising company, has issued several of these hybrid securities, one of which John P. Calamos of Calamos Asset Management called an ideal candidate for producing a comfortable 10 percent total return.
While the company's 1 1/2 percent convertible debentures of 2002 pay very modest interest, he figures they could appreciate as much as 22 percent if the volatile common stock for which they can be exchanged goes up, adding that the company is a market leader in a consolidating industry. If the stock tumbles, the risk is at most a 7 percent loss, Mr. Calamos said.
"We have a nice risk-reward," he said. And barring insolvency, any loss can be avoided by holding these investment-grade securities until maturity in December 2002, less than three years away.
This Clear Channel issue closed at $97.23 on Friday, yields 2.55 percent and is convertible into 9.45 shares of common stock.
A BET ON EUROPE With the euro down 16.4 percent since its introduction at the beginning of last year, many strategists think a rebound is due. That would make a 10 percent return that much easier for Americans.
Although its dividend is less than 1 percent and the stock has more than doubled since last March, Royal Philips Electronics is as good a candidate as any in Europe to return 10 percent at minimal risk this year, said Thomas Murtha, a portfolio manager for Rowe Price-Fleming International.
Philips, the Dutch giant that suffered some setbacks in the 1990's, "is a great restructuring story -- starting to hit on all cylinders," said Mr. Murtha, who expects earnings to climb to 8 euros this year, from 5.22 euros. That should help drive the stock as high as 250 euros within 12 months, he said; it now trades at 198.80 euros.
Philips is gaining market share in consumer and other businesses, including semiconductors, Mr. Murtha said, but economic and market prospects in Europe, where spending on information technology has much room for growth, seem brighter than in the United States. "Their order book looks very, very good," Mr. Murtha said.

SECTION: Section 3; Page 1; Column 5; Money and Business/Financial Desk 

LENGTH: 1631 words

HEADLINE: PORTFOLIOS, ETC.;
A BondMaster Reflects On Flirting With Disaster

BYLINE: By JONATHAN FUERBRINGER 

DATELINE: NEWPORT BEACH, Calif.

 BODY:
IN the $14.7 trillion bond market, Bill Gross, who manages $90 billion directly for the Pacific Investment Management Company, is the big name -- and he has a track record for being right.
So it is not surprising that he is getting credit for realizing the impact that the Treasury's plan to buy back long-term government debt would have on the bond market. He seized the opportunity in late January and early February, and his voracious buying of long-term Treasuries for 21 trading days sent tremors through the bond market and earned Pimco's clients money. 
 But a closer look shows -- and Mr. Gross acknowledges -- that he did not do nearly as well as he should have.
He did not begin to buy his $8 billion of long-term Treasury bonds, bond futures and zero coupon bonds until Jan. 20. That was a full week after the Treasury said it would buy back up to $30 billion in long-term debt this year. And it was six months after it became clear the government would have to make such purchases, as the federal budget surplus allowed it to pay off billions of dollars of the publicly held government debt.
"I should have been shifting to that strategy long before January," Mr. Gross said last week in an interview in the partners' retreat at Pimco's headquarters here. He acknowledged that he had even recommended buying Treasuries in a newspaper article back in August, but failed to take his own advice. "Why it took so long for me, or us, I have no idea," he said.
To make matters worse, Mr. Gross passed up adding to his Treasury bond cache at the Treasury auction on Feb. 10, because he thought the bonds were too expensive, compared with the 10-year note at the time. "We should not have passed," he says now.
And in early January, Mr. Gross adjusted a derivative-based options bet on long-term interest rates, expecting them to remain over 6.30 percent. But the sudden rally in 30-year bonds turned that into a mistake. "I would love to tell you that Pimco made a coup, that we had made millions of bucks and put that in a headline," he said. But that is not the case. Instead, he said: "We haven't had a great offensive year but we played a pretty good defense. It could have been a Pimco disaster, but it wasn't."
Even after all this, his flagship mutual fund, the $30 billion Total Return fund, is not positioned the way he wants, because he still has too few long-term Treasury securities -- the current winners -- and too many mortgage-backed securities and corporate bonds -- the current losers. This just shows how hard it is to change a strategy quickly when a fund is so big. And it underscores the premium on acting as early as possible.
"The changes seem minuscule, and they are," he said, speaking in the context of managing a fund that has grown more than thirtyfold in the last decade. "We wish we were able to do more."
But he added that it was especially hard to figure what to do right now. "At this point," he said, "we are one with the rest of the bond market, wondering what is going to happen from here."
This is not an easy time for a man of Mr. Gross's reputation, a man who craves the Wall Street limelight from 2,780 miles away. "I'm not famous in the Hollywood sense, but I have had my minutes, and I must confess that fame is something I've always wanted, more than money or power," he wrote in his book, "Bill Gross on Investing."
Mr. Gross, 54, is far more driven then his mild manner implies. And while he appeared weary -- his mother, Shirley, had just died -- he was pleased by new strategies he had recently put in effect. But his most recent brush with fame is worrying him. The pending purchase of Pimco's parent, Pimco Advisors, by Allianz, the German insurance giant, will make him the best-paid executive on Wall Street, with a $40 million guarantee for each of the next five years. And he gets a salary to boot.
"The only real feeling is apprehension, is living up to the reputation," he said.
Still, Mr. Gross is not one to whine. He finished the last five miles of a five-day, 125-mile run -- on a challenge from the woman he loved at the time -- with a ruptured kidney. The breath in which he acknowledges an investment mistake is followed in the next with a new market strategy. And even if late and too little, his new stash of long-term Treasuries has earned $40 million for the Total Return fund and $300 million for the entire Pimco fixed-income complex.
It is this combination of endurance, fresh thinking and quick recoveries that has made him the most successful fixed-income fund manager over the last decade. While he has not been No. 1 in performance in any quarter, he is the No. 1 performer for the last 10 years among mutual funds followed by Lipper Inc. So far this year, the Total Return fund is 89th among 288 funds followed by Lipper, with a return of 0.95 percent.
It is the needs of his investors that drive him to to strive for consistency, rather than, as he puts it, "shooting the lights out" from quarter to quarter. "To be a survivor and a dominant force in the market, you have to be consistent," he said. "And that's the way you have to be with bond investors. They are conservative. You don't want to disappoint a bond investor for long."
That is just what he would have done if he kept to what was the consensus strategy for the fixed-income market as this year began. He expected Treasury yields to remain in a range of 6 percent to 6.5 percent. Because there would be little to be gained in such a range-bound Treasury market, his Total Return fund was 50 percent mortgage-backed securities, 20 percent investment-grade corporate bonds, 6 percent emerging-markets bonds and 3 percent high-yield, or junk, bonds, all of which were offering higher yields than Treasuries. The rest was in Treasuries. The maturities in the fund were clustered in the intermediate range, making their prices less sensitive to the small rise in interest rates he expected.
He hoped to enhance this return by using a derivative-based strategy known as a strangle. Mr. Gross sold options giving the buyer the right to sell bond futures to Pimco at a fixed price if interest rates rose beyond a set point. A second set of options required Pimco to sell bond futures to the buyers if interest rates fell below a set level. Together, the positions amounted to a bet that 30-year Treasury bond yields would stay between 6 percent and 6.70 percent. If they did, the options would not be exercised and his Total Return fund could pocket the $70 million premium for selling the options. If interest rates were above or below the range on the day the options expired, Feb. 18, he could lose the premium -- or more.
In early January, when the yield on the 30-year bond briefly hit 6.75 percent, Mr. Gross adjusted the option position to raise the yield at the bottom of the range to 6.30 percent. With yields so high, he did not think there was much chance they could drop that far by Feb. 18. "I figured it was easy money," Mr. Gross said.
He did not realize how wrong-footed his portfolio was until after the Treasury announced its $30 billion buyback of long-term debt on Jan. 13, setting off a rally that has sent the yield on the 30-year Treasury bond down almost two-thirds of a percentage point, to 6.14 percent on Friday.
While he was doing his aerobic yoga at the gym near his office on Jan. 15, Mr. Gross said, "the light bulb went on." The rush to buy 30-year bonds, he thought, would help bring long-term interest rates sharply below shorter-term rates, inverting the yield curve. This meant that long-term bonds were the place to be, because the price would rise as the yields, which move in the opposite direction, fell. And sticking with mortgages and corporates would be a loser. His option bet could also be in trouble.
So Mr. Gross and the seven others on Pimco's investment committee moved quickly. Pimco sold intermediate mortgages and intermediate Treasury securities and rushed to buy bonds, jolting bond traders, who sometimes refer to Pimco as the Beach, and helping set off panic buying and selling on Feb. 3 and Feb. 4, when the price on the long bond soared, then plunged.
All of the new long-term Treasuries, however, were not for the new strategy. About half, $3.7 billion, were needed to limit losses on the options bet if it turned sour.
And it did -- on Friday, Feb. 18, when the options were exercised as the yield on the 30-year bond futures dropped to 6.30 percent while the price rose to 94. Pimco had to give up $3.7 billion in 30-year bond futures at a price resulting in a loss, Mr. Gross said, of $50 million. It was the first loss he had ever had on a strangle, a strategy that had made about $200 million for the Total Return fund in 1999 alone.
While he moved late, Mr. Gross said figuring out what had to be done before many others "deserves a pat on the back."
"It felt like we were earning our paychecks," he said.
And he is working on new strategies. He is now betting that the growing federal budget surplus will lead to the shrinkage of supply of all maturities of Treasury securities. So he is buying them across the board. He said this strategy had made $100 million so far. "Nobody was focusing on the disappearance of Treasuries as an entire sector of the bond market," he said.
And he is not worried at all about the possible disappearance of the 30-year Treasury bond, the source of much of his grief this year. Mr. Gross has been in the fixed-income business since 1971, so he was around before the government began regularly issuing 30-year bonds in 1977. "The bond market will go on because it will find other benchmarks," he said. "I won't mourn its passing."
At least not as much as he regrets not buying 30-year bonds long before January.

HEADLINE: THE WORLD;
China's Own Witch Hunt

BYLINE: By ERIK ECKHOLM 

DATELINE: BEIJING

 BODY:
"WHO LOST TAIWAN?"
Imagine that the year is 2010 and that this headline is screeching from the People's Daily. China's Communist Party newspaper is pillorying the gang of traitors who allowed the nation's hallowed patrimony to be severed.
The scenario could go something like this: Hu Jintao, now President, Communist Party chief and chairman of the Central Military Commission, is the main scapegoat. He is in secret detention and the newspaper is calling for him to be tried for treason, along with several top generals and diplomats. 
 By 2010, the People's Liberation Army was supposed to have been able to take Taiwan by force, if it had come to that. But then the Taiwanese, fed up with years of threats from Beijing and encouraged by the ever deeper military ties mandated by the American Congress, finally voted to become independent.
And when the moment of truth came, after losing an initial battle in offshore islands to dazzling American technology, ashen-faced Chinese generals concluded that the opposing forces were simply too overwhelming to continue.
So: Who Lost Taiwan?
The question isn't being asked yet, of course, but imagining a day when it might be must be horrifying for China's leaders. For in China's history-laden politics, almost nothing matters more than the idea that Taiwan will eventually return to the motherland, especially now that Hong Kong and Macau are back in its bosom. And this emotional truth may help outsiders interpret the White Paper the Chinese leaders issued last week, warning more explicitly than before that Taiwan's foot-dragging in negotiations, let alone any declaration of independence, could force China to take "drastic measures."
"There is a growing sense of urgency in Beijing that Taiwan independence will not be the result of a dramatic declaration, but of a gradual drift towards that goal as the island plays for time," said Steven M. Goldstein, a political scientist at Smith College.
"Thus, in a collective leadership it has become imperative not only not to be the leader who lost Taiwan, but not to be the leader who allows this slow drift to continue," Mr. Goldstein said.
The White Paper was obviously intended to influence Taiwan's March 18 presidential elections. Americans have noted how ill-timed it was in other ways -- appearing just as Congress is taking up China's trade status, and a bill to strengthen military ties with Taiwan -- but it responded to some perfectly rational political imperatives in Beijing. And despite its belligerence, the report also contains terms for talks that by China's lights must seem so generous that they could be rejected only by an evil schemer -- like the current Taiwanese President, Lee Teng-hui, who has outraged the Chinese with his brinkmanship and his assertion of Taiwan's right to deal with China in a "state to state relationship," implying equal status.
Who knows whether President Jiang Zemin and other leaders really believe it when they say that most "compatriots" in Taiwan yearn for reunification, or when they say they are willing to risk a dreadful war? But they certainly don't want to be forced into a conflict that could destroy all they have built on the mainland.
At the same time, with hard-line military officers hovering and their own rhetoric pumping up the people, they cannot watch idly as Taiwan escapes. So the most important thing is to keep the dream alive. As long as it survives, so can their careers.
Mao once said that China would wait 100 years to recover Taiwan if it had to, Mr. Whiting noted. President Jiang has never set a deadline, in public at least. Nor does the report, with its warning against "indefinite" delays in negotiations. The day that China feels compelled to box itself in with a concrete deadline may be the day for really serious worry.
Notably, the new report does not even mention Taiwan's elections or the prospective new presidents. Rather, it demonizes the outgoing Mr. Lee. By presenting him as the chief culprit, the Chinese are making room for a fresh start -- but also putting the would-be successors on notice that they would repeat Mr. Lee's performance at Taiwan's peril.
Taiwan's own domestic politics may not allow much slippage from the brinkman's position staked out by Mr. Lee. But it is already clear, in the election campaign now under way, that all three candidates are searching for modest areas of common interest. FINESSING language about "one China" and "sovereignty" will be trickier, especially for the opposition candidate Chen Shui-bian, whose supporters lean more to independence.
daAny question can be discussed under the "one China" principle," the new report says, repeating the offer of better terms than Hong Kong got. Not only could Taiwan keep its capitalist economy, but "the central government will not send troops or administrative personnel to be stationed in Taiwan."
To Chinese who take reunification for granted, this seems like an incredibly good offer. But the Taiwanese, reveling in democracy and a prosperous economy, are acutely aware of the implicit assumptions. "We are electing a president here, not a governor," one official said.
Any negotiations will be plagued by agonizing wordplay and status issues, but Taiwan needs to find creative ways to keep the Chinese talking, it seems clear from last week's report. So long as they are talking, they will not be shooting. And nobody in Beijing will be asking who lost Taiwan.

HEADLINE: EDITORIAL OBSERVER;
The Precarious Nature of Latin Democracies

BYLINE: By TINA ROSENBERG 

 BODY:
In August 1962, the National Observer newspaper carried a report from South America with the headline "Democracy Dies in Peru, but Few Seem to Mourn Its Passing." The story described the indifference of Peruvians to a military coup that had just installed a junta in power. The young reporter was Hunter Thompson, who later became famous for a different style of journalism. As for Peru, much the same article could be published today.
Democracy swells and recedes in Latin America. In recent decades, the trend was favorable -- military rulers fell, leaving Cuba as the only outright dictatorship. But as in the 1960's and early 1970's, democratic rule is now being nibbled away in several countries, among them Peru. 
 The threat today is not from classic military dictatorship, but more subtle forms of autocracy -- often endorsed by a public angry at the corruption and ineptitude of its democratic political system. In Peru, President Alberto Fujimori will stand for a third term in April. He could do so only after sacking judges who ruled that a third term was unconstitutional, and he has rigged the campaign in his favor. In Ecuador last month, soldiers claiming to represent ordinary citizens against the political elite toppled the president and installed the vice president. In Venezuela, voters have granted sweeping powers to President Hugo Chavez, an army colonel who in 1992 attempted a military coup.
The history of democracy in Latin America is one of advances and setbacks. In 1960, for example, virtually every nation in South America had an elected civilian leader. By 1976, all but a few countries in Central and South America were dictatorships.
The climate for democracy in Latin America today is in many ways the best it has ever been, largely due to the end of the cold war. Washington is no longer helping to overthrow elected left-wing governments. The end of the cold war has also led to the abandonment of armed struggle by Marxist guerrillas in El Salvador, Guatemala and other nations. Peace is good for democracy. In those two countries, it has allowed civilians to assert greater control over the military, and peace agreements have helped make shallow democracies deeper and more inclusive.
Latin American nations are under political and economic pressure -- including from one another -- to keep the dictators out. In Chile, for example, when the nation's civilian leaders have attempted to prosecute military officers for Pinochet-era offenses, soldiers have responded with shows of insubordination. But this has not led Chile's upper class, the military's traditional backer, to call for a coup, in part because Chile's wealthy fear a reaction from the country's trading partners.
But the world is not as willing to focus critical attention on political systems that fall short of outright dictatorship. More important, voters will grant their leaders autocratic powers when they do not believe democracy has helped them. Democratic governments are currently unable to contain a crime wave in several nations that is persuading some Latin Americans to support undemocratic measures or strongman rule. As has often been the case, people in Venezuela, Ecuador and Peru have turned against the traditional party system because they see it as protecting the privileged. Globalization has increased inequality in much of Latin America and made the poor feel more neglected.
Dictatorship is no solution. Alongside their brutality, the continent's strongmen have brought even more theft and mismanagement -- which is usually why they have fallen. Although Gen. Augusto Pinochet gained worldwide praise for his economic reforms in Chile, he is an exception among Latin dictators.
Once in office, autocratic presidents can use their powers to grab far more than the usual advantages of incumbency. Peru used to have relatively clean elections, but few believe that Mr. Fujimori's electoral test in April will be a fair one. He has shut down virtually all critical television news and effectively controls the agencies that will oversee the election. Opposition politicians have been harassed, their campaign events are often sabotaged, and their paid commercials have been rejected by Lima's television networks.
Elected autocrats like Mr. Fujimori deserve scrutiny, just as outright dictators do. While the Clinton administration has been a consistent critic of rights violations in Peru, it must speak out more forcefully on flaws in the upcoming election. The most important lesson from the past, however, is for Latin America's democrats. If they cannot control corruption and make government work for the poor, they risk not only their hold on power but democracy itself.

HEADLINE: Indispensable Oil

 BODY:

To the Editor:
You report that the conventional wisdom has been that oil no longer matters in the age of the Internet (front page, Feb. 21). I guess that means that the Internet has freed us from the need to grow food, heat our homes, drive to work, fight wars, or manufacture and transport anything, since all of these activities depend heavily on oil. 
 And what about the soaring energy costs of producing and running our computers and the Internet, which consume more than 13 percent of the country's electricity? The brunt of this electric energy demand is borne by coal, but the mining and shipping of coal requires a lot of oil. When it comes to oil, the Internet's free lunch isn't very nourishing.
 
DAVID EHRENFELD
New Brunswick, N.J., Feb. 21, 2000

HEADLINE: RECKONINGS;
Dow Wow, Dow Ow

BYLINE: By PAUL KRUGMAN 

 BODY:
Early in his forthcoming book, "Irrational Exuberance," Robert Shiller -- who has done more than any other economist of his generation to document the less rational aspects of financial markets -- describes the full-page advertisement that Merrill Lynch ran a year ago, when the Dow Jones industrial average passed 10,000. "Even those with a disciplined long-term approach like ours have to sit back and say 'wow,' " the ad declared. And the accompanying stock market chart bore the caption "HUMAN ACHIEVEMENT."
In the year to date, however, blue-chip investors, battered by interest rate fears, have experienced more ow than wow. On Friday the Dow zipped through 10,000 again, this time heading the other way. Will we soon see another chart, with the caption "HUMAN FAILURE"? 
 O.K., O.K., the Dow is still more than 50 percent higher than it was in December 1996, when Alan Greenspan tried unsuccessfully to dampen our exuberance. And other stock indices have not felt the Dow's pain: the broader S.&P. 500 index is still well above its level of a year ago, while the Nasdaq -- whose heavy weighting of tech stocks has made it the unofficial bellwether of the "new economy" -- keeps setting new records.
But that divergence of fortunes is very much to the point: although the chatter is all about Mr. Greenspan's next move, the question this market really poses is, Who will own the future?
The very fame of the Dow tends to make us forget that this is even a question. In Steve Bodow's hilarious 1997 Slate article "Dow te ching," one of the koans reads: "The Dow does not exist, yet all things exist within it / O.K., only 30 stocks exist within it / But it seems like a much bigger deal." It is common practice to speak loosely of "the Dow" when we really mean "stock prices in general" -- I've done it myself -- and many people compound that looseness by talking as if the Dow stocks represented a claim on the future earnings of corporate America. But they don't: if you buy the Dow, you only buy a claim on the future earnings of the companies currently in the index.
That's not a trivial distinction: to be bullish on the Dow you must believe not just that American capitalism will prosper, but that today's corporate giants will share proportionately in that prosperity -- not just for the next couple of years, but far into the future. And I mean far. To take an extreme example, the calculation that underlies last year's best seller "Dow 36,000" assumes that the earnings of today's Dow companies will grow as fast as overall corporate profits forever; and half of what the authors believe to be fair value comes from projected earnings after the year 2070. (Actually, even then you don't get 36K -- more like 20 -- but never mind.)
So do you believe that 70 years from now the Dow companies -- not the companies that will be in the Dow then, but the companies that are in it right now -- will capture the same share of corporate profits that they do today? The investors bidding up the Nasdaq, not to mention those buying into I.P.O.'s, clearly don't. They believe that whatsit.com has a good chance of being tomorrow's Microsoft, and correspondingly that today's General Electric -- or even today's Microsoft -- may be tomorrow's Sears, Roebuck. To be a serious Dow bull, you must believe that the new economy will belong to old companies; to justify the price of tech stocks, you must believe that the future belongs to the upstarts. Both beliefs can't be right.
Both could, on the other hand, be wrong. Mr. Shiller believes that the whole stock market, not just the Dow, is inflated by a speculative bubble. I'm sympathetic but not entirely convinced. The social and psychological hallmarks of a bubble -- like the fact that the TV in my local greasy pizza place is now tuned to CNBC, not ESPN -- are plain to see, but so is the spectacular pace of technological progress. I'm not sure that the current value of the Nasdaq is justified, but I'm not sure that it isn't.
In any case, the fall in the Dow is not a verdict on the economy as a whole. As long as we have full employment and low inflation, I say let the blue chips fall where they may. 
http://www.nytimes.com

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: PRIVATE SECTOR;
If Only Stocks Were Votes . . .

BYLINE: By JULIE DUNN

 BODY:
Gary Bauer may be out of the race for the White House, but victory is still apparently his. Last year, according to his disclosure forms, he held some of the market's best performers, including Qualcomm (a $15,000-to-$50,000 stake as the year began, according to the forms) and i2 Technologies ($50,000 to $100,000). For the year, Qualcomm gained 2,683 percent, and i2, 542 percent (with a Friday sell-off still leaving it above last year's close). The value of his entire portfolio gained 183 percent, he says.
How could he campaign for president and still triumph in the market? "Maybe that was the secret of my success: I've been so busy I didn't have any time to sell last year," Mr. Bauer said in a telephone interview from Washington. There, he is chairman of the Campaign for Working Families, a political action group he started four years ago that supports conservative candidates. 
"I had a pretty good year," he added. "Obviously, I'm strongly in favor of abolishing the capital gains tax."
But Mr. Bauer does not favor just technology stocks. He mentioned Fannie Mae and cyclicals like General Motors as among those he feels are undervalued. "There are many buys in the Old Economy stocks being ignored right now that are at bargain prices," he said. "A year down the road a lot of people might be kicking themselves." JULIE DUNN
