HEADLINE: With Africa Watching, Senegal Casts Votes That Count

BYLINE: By NORIMITSU ONISHI 

DATELINE: DAKAR, Senegal, Feb. 26

 BODY:
The presidential election on Sunday in Senegal has one critical difference that sets it apart from past votes and that offers a snapshot of the evolution of democracy in sub-Saharan Africa: no one knows who will win.
The race offers Senegalese the choice among four credible candidates. President Abdou Diouf, who is seeking a fourth consecutive term, could become what was once the rarity of rarities in Africa: a sitting leader who loses power, not in a coup, but through the ballot box. 
 He would not be the first. But because he is the head of the party that has governed Senegal since independence from France in 1960, his loss would be significant on a continent that has become increasingly impatient with democracies that offer no real choices.
"The outcome of the election in Senegal is extremely important for all of Africa," said Chris Fomunyoh, who follows Africa for the National Democratic Institute, an American organization that promotes democracy and monitors elections. "Given the political history of Africa, events in one country have had contagious effects elsewhere. After Ghana gained independence, everyone else wanted it. Then military coups came into fashion in the 1970's, the single party state in the 1980's, and the beginning of democratization in the 1990's."
"So a lot of people are watching the elections in Senegal, which are the first competitive elections in Africa in the year 2000, to see what trends might emerge," Mr. Fomunyoh added.
If the election runs relatively smoothly, it would also lengthen the list of African countries that have recently undergone change, more or less peacefully and fairly, through elections -- positive developments in Africa that have been overshadowed by the chaos swirling around the war in Congo and the December coup in the Ivory Coast.
In the last year, sub-Saharan Africa's two giants, Nigeria and South Africa, held elections, and their new presidents have been pressing for more democracy in their regions.
In recent months, Namibia and Mozambique in southern Africa held elections that international observers judged free and fair. In West Africa, Niger and Guinea Bissau -- two countries whose former leaders had been removed by force -- successfully chose new presidents in elections that included runoffs.
And two weeks ago, the power of the ballot box asserted itself unexpectedly in Zimbabwe. Voters there rejected a proposal by President Robert Mugabe, whose government has been ruining the economy while plundering the Congo, to seize lands without compensation and place still more power in his hands.
A decade after the end of the cold war brought multiparty systems across Africa, 32 out of its current 54 heads of states have been chosen in elections against rivals. A quarter of a century ago, the figure was only 3. What is more, the pressure toward democratization has grown inside and outside the continent.
What once left people with mouths agape -- a leader voluntarily giving up power -- is no longer so surprising. Mali's president, Alpha Oumar Konare, has said he would not follow the urging of some of his backers to change the Constitution to allow for a third term, instead pledging to retire in two years.
This December, Ghana is scheduled to have a presidential election without President Jerry J. Rawlings. A former flight lieutenant who seized power in 1981, Mr. Rawlings said he would also respect the Constitution, which limits presidents to two terms, and step down.
In French-speaking Africa, recent events are being interpreted to mean that France will no longer unconditionally support loyal but corrupt leaders in its former colonies and that it may be shifting toward demanding more accountability.
Foremost, France did not intervene to save Ivory Coast's president, Henri Konan Bedie, whose party cheated in the 1995 presidential elections and who in recent years arbitrarily changed the Constitution to thwart his main rival.
After mutinous soldiers cast Mr. Bedie out of power in December, the only thing the French government offered him was exile in Paris. The new ruler, Gen. Robert Guei, has pledged to hold elections, though he has yet to announce a date.
If the French did not intervene in the Ivory Coast, their most important economic partner in Africa, Africa experts say it is unlikely that they would do so to support President Omar Bongo of Gabon or President Gnassingbe Eyadema of Togo.
Both men have succeeded in remaining in power for three decades with French support but are increasingly regarded as embarrassments. Under pressure, Mr. Eyadema, who has led the tiny country of Togo for 33 years and is Africa's longest-ruling leader, has agreed to hold legislative elections this year and has announced that he will not run for re-election in 2003.
These events have intensified the campaign here in Senegal. This country of nine million, which has never experienced military rule, has long been considered one of the more politically open in Africa, especially since its first president, the politician and poet Leopold Senghor, introduced multiparty politics in 1976 and resigned voluntarily in 1980.
Elections here, which international observers say have become fairer over the years, have often been followed by violence. The risks that that would happen again have grown as the opposition warned that it would automatically consider a first-round victory by Mr. Diouf as proof that the voting was rigged. In recent days, violence has increased at campaign stops, as well as in Casamance, the southern region that harbors a separatist movement.
In an interview, Mr. Diouf, 65, who had served for 11 years as Mr. Senghor's prime minister, said that he wanted to continue democratizing Senegal by decentralizing power. Should he be re-elected, he said he would create a constitutional commission to re-examine the country's balance of powers.
"I believe that democracy is adequately rooted in this country," Mr. Diouf said.
But Mr. Diouf's critics say that his Socialist Party has cynically curtailed freedoms inside the party and the country. Like Mr. Bedie in the Ivory Coast, the Socialist Party has amended the Constitution to suit its needs, the critics say.
They point out that in the last year, the National Assembly, which is controlled by the Socialist Party, changed the Constitution to eliminate term limits for the presidency. Mr. Diouf has defended the change and pledged not to run again in 2007 if he wins this time.
"What is democracy?" Mr. Diouf said. "It's the choice of the people.
"The National Assembly in a majority judged that it was not natural, if the country has a good president and wants to keep him, that it should be prevented from keeping him. It is democracy if the people have the possibility to express an opinion."
One of the president's main rivals in this election, Djibo Ka, disagreed. He said he favored a limit of two five-year terms for the presidency.
"In a country with a fully functioning democracy, where there is a culture of democracy and credible institutions, there is no problem with having no term limits," Mr. Ka said. "But in a poor country like this one, where the same party has been in power for 40 years and where power has corrupted its members, it's not a bad idea to have term limits."
Mr. Ka, 52, a former foreign minister, left the Socialist Party two years ago arguing for greater democracy. Another candidate, Moustapha Niasse, 61, also a former heavyweight in the Socialist Party, defected for similar reasons. The third, and perhaps strongest rival to Mr. Diouf, is the longtime opposition figure, Abdoulaye Wade, 74.
It is the first time that Mr. Diouf is facing such formidable competition. Because of Mr. Niasse and Mr. Ka, political analysts here say that it is unlikely that Mr. Diouf can garner the 50 percent of the votes necessary to win in the first round. His party has been losing support with each election, most recently in the 1998 legislative election in which Mr. Diou's party won just over 50 percent of the seats. If the opposition can unite after the first round, Mr. Diouf might lose in the runoff.
Although the choice of four credible candidates represents progress, political analysts here say the candidates are simply interested in acquiring power. None, they say, have focused on issues during the campaign or presented a particular vision for Senegal's future.
"The opposition talks of the need to alternate power," said Abdourahmane Camara, director of publication of Walfadjri, an independent newspaper that is often critical of President Diouf. "But the opposition has not presented a political alternative in terms of ideas or ideology. We're hoping that as our democracy deepens, we will have real political alternatives in the next election."

HEADLINE: More Rainfall Adds to Woes In Wide Swath Of Africa

BYLINE: By RACHEL L. SWARNS 

DATELINE: JOHANNESBURG, Feb. 26

 BODY:
Several weeks of torrential rains and floods, made worse when a deadly storm lashed the heel of Africa this week, have killed more than 150 people and swept away the homes and livelihoods of more than one million in four countries, relief agencies and government officials say.
The latest storm, which hit the region beginning Tuesday, has so far killed more than 60 people and left thousands homeless as fierce winds downed power lines and swollen rivers swallowed roads, houses and bridges in South Africa, Mozambique and Zimbabwe. 
 In many places, the rain was still falling today as rescue helicopters tried to deliver food, tents and blankets and pluck stranded people from rooftops, trees and farms that had become tiny snake-infested islands.
This week's storm was particularly destructive because it followed several weeks of rains that had had already battered the three countries and Botswana. Doctors and government officials fear the situation will only further deteriorate in coming days with increasing outbreaks of cholera, malaria and typhoid.
"The situation is going from bad to worse," said Kenny Mathiva, a spokesman for the local government in the Northern Province, which borders Mozambique, Botswana and Zimbabwe, and is the South African province most severely affected by the storm.
"Thirty-three people were dead by midday yesterday, and it's still raining," said Mr. Mathiva, who flew in a military helicopter today to assess the damage. "The housing is collapsing. The main routes are flooded.
"The people affected are some of the poorest of the poor," he added.
On Friday, the United States Defense Department announced that it would send emergency supplies to South Africa and Mozambique. The supplies, which will begin arriving on Monday, will include 140 tents, 6,900 blankets, 6,000 water cans and 160 rolls of plastic sheeting for emergency shelter.
The United Nations World Food Program is delivering food to more than 100,000 people in Mozambique, which has borne the brunt of this month's flooding. An estimated 300,000 Mozambicans have lost their homes, farms and cattle to the floods. And earlier this week, the United Nations and the Mozambican government issued an urgent appeal for $65 million for relief work in the storm-ravaged country.
"There are immediate needs for food, shelter and medicine," Ibrahim Gambari, the United Nations' special adviser for Southern Africa, said of Mozambique in a radio interview that was broadcast today. "But at the same time, the international community has to pay attention to the tremendous damage to infrastructure."
Mozambique, which turned its back on decades of war to build one of the world's fastest-growing economies, has nearly 200,000 acres of farmland flooded, 141 schools destroyed and already short-staffed hospitals and clinics struggling to cope with demand.
"Health services in the affected areas are overwhelmed by the number of patients and are working beyond capacity," the United Nations said in its appeal for emergency assistance. "Drug stocks are running out, and there is a serious shortage of health workers." Rescue efforts in the region have been hampered by power outages and flooded roads, which have made it difficult for government officials and relief workers to reach stricken areas.
In South Africa, Mr. Mathiva said the towns of Messina, Louis Trichardt and Thohoyandou were accessible only by helicopter. And the bad weather forced two copters, one carrying corn meal, blankets and tents, to turn back without delivering the desperately needed supplies.
In Zimbabwe, government officials told the United Nations that many people had been marooned by flood waters. Earlier this week, a bus was swept off a bridge into the Mudzi River, killing all its passengers. The storm, has killed at least 29 people in Zimbabwe and exacerbated its fuel shortage as rough seas have kept an oil tanker from docking in Mozambique's port city, Beira.

HEADLINE: BACKTALK;
A Voiceless Fan Longs for the Return of Messier

BYLINE: By ROBERT LIPSYTE 

 BODY:
I want Mark Messier and I want him now. Don't tell me about salary caps, dunce caps, capped teeth. Just go buy Mark Messier and stuff him back in a Ranger uniform and push him out on the prime line with Adam Graves. Do it before baseball melts the ice.
Who listens to a fan? We scamper along the sides of the eternal triangle -- athlete, agent, owner -- and sometimes get hung on the points. Who cares about us? The week began with news that Mariano Rivera had lost his arbitration case and would get only $7.25 million to pitch this season instead of the $9.25 million he asked for. Mariano kept smiling. A good sport. Maybe the Yankees could use the spared $2 million to hire a team of psychiatrists to work with their minor leaguers; too late for poor Darryl Strawberry, but then it was the Mets who brought him up too soon and with too little counseling. 
 Meanwhile, Marvin Miller's biographer is beating a drum to have rules so interpreted that the retired labor leader can be inducted into baseball's Hall of Fame. C'mon. A co-op in Cooperstown for one of the guys who made sure you can't afford to go to games? And they are keeping Pete Rose out? At least Pete gave me pleasure. I say, Marvin and Pete go in together or leave 'em both on the curb.
Who listens to a fan? Am I scandalized that Erick Barkley's life was improved by switching cars? St. John's gave him a platinum athletic scholarship so he could get them television gigs. I don't watch the Red Storm to see Erick read or drive. People have been giving Erick stuff ever since he picked up a basketball. It looks as though Riverside Church, where he once played, helped him with his prep school tuition. God helps those who have game.
So why is the National Collegiate Athletic Association so picky? Does it feel it's losing ground? The Amateur Athletic Union, once the power in American sports, but moribund the past few decades, is making a comeback with sneaker company money, running teams often coached by street agents, who, truth be told, probably do more for more young athletes than college coaches do.
Should I, Fan, care?
Ask Dennis Perrin, who is my new role model as a funny, smart and mean-spirited fan. His new book, "American Fan: Sports Mania and the Culture That Feeds It" (Spike/Avon), is hilariously vicious at the expense of Spike Lee, George Will, sportswriters, Michael Jordan, Nike and Jack Tatum. ("Were Tatum really great he would have crippled some fans as well.") Every fan should read it for moral support, then send it to Erick. If he can pass a test on it, he should get a brand new car.
Ask Jay Weiner, who is my new role model as a thoughtful, precise and idealistic fan. (I inadvertently maligned Weiner, a Minneapolis journalist, here several weeks ago when I had him jailed in the Patty Hearst case for harboring fugitives; he was jailed for refusing to testify against his friends.) Weiner's new book, "Stadium Games: Fifty Years of Big League Greed and Bush League Boondoggle" (University of Minnesota), is a lesson in politics and economics by a writer who believes that a city may well need a major league team to be major league. But Weiner goes on with pragmatic suggestions on how fans can make this all work in their own best interests.
Ask me.
If I were writing a book for fans, I'd call it "SkyBoxer: In the Arena but Above the Fray." That should be our goal, to watch all games over television, while eating and drinking in bed, with company, in a luxury box. This is the future of fandom. Who wants to get close to those brutes? If Reggie Miller isn't talking dirty, you might get hurt the next time a Marty McSorley forgets that the violence he gets paid for has certain boundaries.
But to be suspended for the rest of the season just for concussing a guy he has been fighting for years? I think McSorley should be replaced by Tonya Harding, the prototype skating goon, arrested last week for whacking her boyfriend with a hubcap.
What would bring me downstairs?
Mark Messier. I want Captain Dad back in town. I want to reclaim my inner puck, to see again that scream of a face floating over the Garden ice. Wiser heads say I'm wrong, that Messier is 39 and his $6 million contract (not even as much as the marked down Rivera!) and his powerful personality will upset the Rangers. I want them upset! I am a fan! The Rangers stink!
And Cablevision can afford Messier; it was ready to buy the Jets. Actually, if Marvin Miller wants my vote for the Hall of Fame, he should explain how Cablevision can be allowed to own the venue (Madison Square Garden), the teams (Knicks, Rangers) and the television channels, and still not satisfy my needs.
And while we're at it, I want Coach Mike Keenan and I want him now.
 
http://www.nytimes.com

HEADLINE: Albert Cleage Is Dead at 88; Led Black Nationalist Church

 BODY:
Bishop Albert B. Cleage, who after the urban riots of the 1960's built his Detroit church, the Shrine of the Black Madonna, into an important center for black theology and political power, died on Feb. 20 in Calhoun Falls, S.C. He was 88.
Bishop Cleage died while visiting Beulah Land, his church's new farm, where he planned to raise food for the needy and give city youths peaceful summer vacations. 
 The bishop, who changed his name in the early 1970's to Jaramogi Abebe Agyeman, presided for more than three decades at the Shrine of the Black Madonna, originally a Congregational church. The change came in the late 1960's.
There were other black secessionist churches, but Bishop Cleage's became one of the biggest and most influential, emphasizing black interpretations but retaining some traditional Protestant teachings.
Bishop Cleage was also drawn to politics. In 1973, Black Slate Inc., his political organization, helped Coleman A. Young become the first black mayor of Detroit, and the bishop was important in the careers of United States Representative Carolyn Cheeks Kilpatrick, Democrat of Michigan; former United States Representative Barbara-Rose Collins; and blacks who ran for smaller offices.
"He taught me the importance of organizing," Ms. Kilpatrick said, "and he created networks of political activists that continue to have a profound impact on Detroit's political landscape."
Bishop Cleage became a focus of national attention during the 1967 Detroit riot, when 43 people were killed, and in the turbulent years afterward. Splitting with both the white power structure and more moderate black leaders, he emphasized black separatism in economics, politics and religion.
"The basic problem facing black people is their powerlessness," he said. "You can't integrate power and powerlessness."
Trying to counter what he saw as white domination of religion, Mr. Cleage espoused a gospel of black nationalism. He installed a larger-than-life painting of a black Madonna holding a black baby Jesus, radical for its time, and preached that Jesus was a black revolutionary whose identity as such had been obscured by whites.
"That was the beginning of a whole new religious iconography," said Menelik Kimathi, chief executive officer of the Shrine of the Black Madonna. Mr. Kimathi, who joined a shrine youth group 30 years ago, added, "I think his legacy will be that he founded black liberation theology, the idea that the church could be more relevant to the day-to-day concerns of the community."
The Rev. Jim Holley, pastor of Little Rock Baptist Church in Detroit, said: "He brought a black consciousness to the church community like it never had been brought before. And it translated into politics."
In 1968, Life magazine called Bishop Cleage one of "the men who are speaking to black America," along with Jesse Jackson, Julian Bond, Eldridge Cleaver and Dick Gregory.
The bishop was national chairman of the Black Christian Nationalist Church, an umbrella organization for similar churches, and he wrote "The Black Messiah" (1969) and "Black Christian Nationalism" (1972).
Albert Cleage was born in Indianapolis on June 13, 1911. His physician father moved the family to Detroit, and in 1937 the son received a sociology degree from Wayne State University. He received a divinity degree from Oberlin College, then served churches in Lexington, Ky.; San Francisco; and Springfield, Mass. In 1951, he became pastor of St. Mark's Community Church in Detroit, and two years later he formed the Central United Church of Christ, which later became the shrine.
Though he had believed in integration -- some of the churches he served were racially mixed -- Bishop Cleage came to despair of the hope that whites would ever willingly help blacks advance. He also befriended Malcolm X, the Muslim leader, who had Michigan roots.
Bishop Cleage's vision of a church emphasized social service programs, reached out to young people and marched for civil rights. He organized black-owned businesses like grocery stores and bookstores, and built hundreds of units of housing.
All this activity made him frightening to many whites.
"When we take over, don't worry," he once responded. "We'll treat you like you treated us."
He later founded other shrines, including major ones in Atlanta and Houston. Today, Mr. Kimathi said, the church has about 50,000 members nationally.
In the 1970's, Bishop Cleage backed out of the national spotlight to concentrate more on church programs and working with young people. In the late 80's and much of the 90's, he lived in Houston, then returned to Detroit.
Bishop Cleage married Doris Graham in 1943; they divorced in 1955. His survivors include their two daughters, Pearl Cleage of Atlanta and Kristin Williams of Idlewild, Mich.; three sisters; a brother; six grandchildren, and one great-grandchild.

HEADLINE: Rising Tax Bills Fuel Anger in Canada

BYLINE: By JAMES BROOKE 

DATELINE: MONTREAL

 BODY:
In a hotel restaurant here, Jordan switched her cell phone to voice mail, nibbled on a slice of cold salmon, and then spun a libertarian view of taxes.
"I work strictly on a cash basis, so I don't get bit on the tax," said the 28-year-old college graduate, who prefers to go by her first name when talking about her "escort" work, a business that is growing rapidly in Canadian cities. 
 On the Pacific Coast, marijuana growers have propelled their illicit exports to the United States into a $1 billion-a-year business, making it one of British Columbia's top exports. And for Toronto's button-down professionals, Caribbean tax havens have become such popular winter vacation spots that Revenue Canada has increased its international auditors by sixfold, reaching 300 last year.
A tax bite is transforming the Great White North. Long seen by Americans as law-abiding Dudley Do-Rights, Canadians today increasingly dodge taxes in ways more commonly associated with Brazilians, Italians or even Russians.
"Canadians are not in the same league as the Italians or Brazilians," said David Giles, a University of Victoria economist, who estimates that Canada's under-the-table economy now approaches 20 percent of gross domestic product. "Canadians are clumsy. But they are blushing less and less. In fact, they are becoming brazen about it."
At the same time, more cautious Canadians, who once sniffed that their unruly neighbor to the south was born of a tax revolt, are now undergoing a very un-Canadian experience: a tax revolt.
Over the last five years, payroll taxes increased by an average of 26 percent and the government's total take of personal and corporate income jumped by almost 40 percent. Here in Quebec, home to North America's highest income tax rates, 51.7 percent, the tax bite means that the average resident works for the government until July 6. Shoppers in Canada also pay a sales tax that averages about 15 percent.
On Feb. 28, when the federal government's new budget is introduced, the governing Liberal Party promises to offer a modest tax cut, probably around $32 billion spread over five years. Much of Canada's tax load, government officials say, comes from paying interest on debts inherited from past Conservative and Liberal governments. The Liberals add that taxes pay for benefits -- Canada's health care and inexpensive college education. Finally, they argue, Canada's tax load is about average for the 29 countries of the Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development.
"If you look at only one aspect of life, maybe you would prefer living elsewhere, there is nothing forcing you to stay here," Prime Minister Jean Chretien told a reporter last year, drawing political sustenance from public opinion polls that indicated Canadians cared more about improving health care than about cutting taxes.
But public sentiment appears to be changing. A new national antitax coalition is gaining ground, and leaders of the tax revolt contend that Canadians do not compare themselves with Swedes and Finns.
"People look across the border at New Hampshire, where there is no sales tax, no state income tax and a low federal rate -- and people become pretty envious," said Martin Solberg, tax critic for the Reform Party, the largest opposition party and a leader of the new tax-cutting movement. Speaking from his home in Alberta, in the west, he said: "When we do 85 percent of your trade with the U.S., it is really important that we are competitive with the U.S."
The Canadian Tax Foundation, a Toronto-based research group, has calculated that during the 1990's, partly because of the tax load, average disposable income for Canadians declined 12 percent, while average American disposable income expanded about 12 percent.
"The 90's will go down in history as the worst decade since the Great Depression in terms of G.D.P. per capita," Joe Martin, chairman of the National History Society, wrote recently in Canada's leading history magazine, The Beaver.
Smelling blood, the Reform Party moved out of its western stronghold in February and started forming a nationwide conservative political coalition. First, it adopted a radical central plank: the reduction of top federal income tax rates to 17 percent from 29 percent.
This new coalition, the Canadian Alliance, is growing on bases in Ontario and Alberta, two voter-rich provinces already ruled by tax-cutting conservatives. In Ontario, Mike Harris easily won re-election last May, after he cut provincial taxes 30 percent during his first term. In Alberta, Stockwell Day, the provincial treasurer, plans to carry out by next January Canada's first provincial flat tax, a top rate of 11 percent.
With the emergence of this coalition, Canada's finance minister, Paul Martin, ended a self-imposed silence during the pre-budget season to criticize the opposition's flat tax idea.
"That 17 percent proposal would clearly either put our health care system or our education system into jeopardy, or it would put us back into deficit," Mr. Martin told reporters. "We're simply not going to do that."
Mr. Martin said: "We are going to reduce taxes, and we believe that we must reduce taxes. However, we believe that it must be done in a fair and balanced way."
Indeed, news leaks about impending tax cuts reflect a political recognition that many Canadians want tax cuts. In mid-January, the Liberal government proposed a government subsidy to keep National Hockey League teams in Canada. Public anger was so intense that the proposal was dropped in three days. No sooner had that uproar died away than the government became tangled in one of the most embarrassing political crises of its seven-year rule. An audit that showed sloppy or nonexistent accounting for $2.2 billion in "job creation" grants doled out by the government's biggest department, Human Resources.
In moving public opinion, tax cutters have found a powerful new ally in The National Post, a 16-month-old newspaper edited by imported British journalists who seem intent on conquering Canada with Margaret Thatcher's ideology.
In a typically scathing essay, Conrad Black, the British-Canadian owner of The Post, recently belittled Ottawa's success in erasing an inherited deficit of $29 billion, writing that "70 percent of the progress has just been piggybacking on American prosperity." Mr. Black and other critics say that much of Ottawa's budget balancing has come by cutting health and education transfer payments to provinces.
Instead of urging Canadians to compare themselves with Scandinavia, The Post suggests Ireland. Twenty years ago, a Post columnist noted recently, Ireland had half of Canada's per-capita income. After embarking on radical tax cuts, Irish per capita income is now 15 percent higher than Canada's.
Tax resistance seems to cross the economic spectrum here.
Business leaders stress that among the top industrialized countries, Canada's average corporate income tax, 43 percent, is only topped by Japan's rate of 48 percent. Canada's maximum capital gains rate of 37.7 percent is almost double the top American rate of 20 percent.
Canadian Internet entrepreneurs bridle under this country's system of taxing stock options when they are granted, as against the American approach of taxing them when they are exercised.
"You trigger a tax without having the money to pay for it," said David B. Perry, a senior research associate at the Canadian Tax Foundation.
For personal income taxes, Canada's top federal rate, 29 percent, affects income over $40,834. By comparison, the 28 percent tax bracket in the United States affects income over $43,850 for married couples and the maximum rate of 39.6 percent kicks in at $288,350. Largely because Canada uncoupled tax rates from inflation a decade ago, more taxpayers have shifted into higher brackets. And in contrast to the United States, there is no deduction for interest paid on home mortgages.
High taxes also contribute to Canada's century-old problem of a brain drain to the United States. With higher salaries and wider opportunities contributing to the American lure, a poll taken in December found that about a third of Canadian Internet users surveyed said they expected a family member to move to the United States in the foreseeable future.
A separate survey of Canadian M.B.A. graduates of the Harvard Business School found that the portion returning to Canada had plummeted to 25 percent of the class of 1998 from 58 percent in the late 80's.
"Canadians earn less and pay higher taxes for generally inferior public services than Americans," The Globe and Mail, a newspaper that generally backs the Liberal government, said in a recent editorial. Indeed, with tax evasion increasingly mainstream here, few eyebrows were raised when Statistics Canada reported in January that spending on house renovation had dropped by almost 40 percent during the 1990's. Canadians are not letting their houses fall down. They are cutting cash deals with contractors.

HEADLINE: Effort for Lowering Oil Prices Gains at U.S.-Saudi Meeting

BYLINE: By JUDITH MILLER 

DATELINE: RIYADH, Saudi Arabia, Feb. 26

 BODY:
The American effort to secure lower oil prices took an important step forward today when senior Saudi and American oil ministers agreed that there could be a better balance between oil supplies and demand.
The carefully worded statement issued here tonight by the Saudi Oil Minister, Ali al-Naimi, and Energy Secretary Bill Richardson did not state that boosting production would help end that imbalance or stabilize oil prices, which at more than $30 a barrel have hit their highest level in nine years. Nor did it commit Saudi Arabia to producing more oil. 
 But it did show, according to oil experts and diplomats here, that both Saudi and American officials are concerned that the soaring oil prices caused by production quotas imposed last year are creating speculation and shortages that jeopardize world economic growth.
In the statement, Saudi Arabia vowed that it would continue to "review the oil supply and demand levels to ensure market stability, prevent oil price volatility and avoid harming the world economy."
Officials said Mr. Naimi had stressed in his talks with Mr. Richardson that the decision to pump more oil could be made only by the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries, which is scheduled to meet in Vienna at the end of March to review production cuts it adopted a year ago after the price of oil fell below $10 a barrel.
But several oil experts and diplomats interpreted this statement to mean that Saudi and American oil officials had similar interpretations of oil market pressures and that the Saudis would try to persuade other OPEC members that some production increases were warranted.
Mr. Richardson's trip also suggests that a consensus is emerging among some OPEC states that the production cuts, which Saudi Arabia urged other members to adopt last March, may have outlived their usefulness. Oil prices have almost doubled since OPEC decided last year to reduce daily production by 4.3 million barrels.
Whether OPEC should pump more oil and at what pace will also be discussed in London on Thursday at a meeting of oil ministers from Saudi Arabia, Venezuela and Mexico. Before that, officials said, Mr. Richardson will meet with Venezuela's oil minister. OPEC members, together with nonmembers Mexico and Norway, produce about a third of the world's supply of oil.
Saudi officials said no announcement would be made before the OPEC meeting. But privately, several Saudi officials said they believed that an increase of more than a million barrels a day could be justified.
Yet differences persist over tactics and pricing strategy, not only among OPEC members but within Saudi Arabia's ruling family. While Saudi oil officials seemed impressed by American data showing that oil inventories in America have fallen to the lowest point in 20 years and that the world is consuming 75 million barrels of oil a day and producing only 73 million, a senior Saudi official wondered whether there was what he called a "false sense of crisis" spurred on by a media frenzy over rising prices and speculation.
"Reserves are meant to go down, especially during a harsh winter," Prince Saud al-Faysal bin Abd al-Aziz, the foreign minister, said in an interview. "That's what reserves are for, after all."
Saudi oil officials are concerned that rising prices for major consumers may jeopardize the economic growth upon which future oil sales depend. At the same time, they emphasize the need to build consensus in OPEC. Any suggestion that the kingdom is responding to American pressure would complicate that task, one diplomat said.
While the administration is said to be preparing measures to address shortages, Mr. Richardson reiterated today that oil from the strategic reserve should be used only to respond to a national emergency, not to affect price levels.
Asked about the discussions here, Mr. Richardson said, "This has been a very successful trip."

HEADLINE: Talk of a Merger Raises New Concern for Hotel-Hungry Atlantic City

BYLINE: By TERRY PRISTIN

 BODY:
It has been more than four years since the Las Vegas casino magnate Stephen A. Wynn announced plans to build a $2 billion hotel and gambling complex on a landfill in Atlantic City's marina district, and almost as long since MGM Grand said it would build a $700 million resort at the northern end of the Boardwalk.
New Jersey officials were so eager to get Mr. Wynn to bring badly needed hotel rooms and Las Vegas-style glamour to Atlantic City that they agreed to his demand for a new $330 million roadway and tunnel to whisk visitors from the Atlantic City Expressway to the western side of the city. That tunnel, known as the Brigantine Connector, is on schedule and expected to open in May 2001. 
 But neither company has yet broken ground. Both Mr. Wynn's Mirage Resorts and MGM Grand have been stymied by legal and political complications. And a bid a few days ago by MGM Grand for Mirage has suddenly left Atlantic City officials fearing further delays and the potential for serious business stagnation: with hotel occupancy near capacity, Atlantic City needs to add rooms fast to stop losing business.
On Wednesday, MGM Grand made an unsolicited $3.3 billion offer to buy Mirage. Two other major casino companies, Park Place Entertainment Company and Harrah's Entertainment Company, then said they were considering making their own offers for Mr. Wynn's empire, which is perceived as vulnerable because its lavish new resorts in Las Vegas and Biloxi, Miss., have failed to meet financial expectations.
Lou Toscano, the senior policy adviser to Mayor James Whelan, said Atlantic City feared that a battle between Mirage and other casino operators would lead to further delays in the Atlantic City projects, just at the moment when the impasse appeared to be ending.
"Takeover struggles have a tendency to freeze both the companies involved," Mr. Toscano said, "and this is not a good time for that to happen."
Mirage officials would not talk about what impact the proposed merger might have on its Atlantic City project. "All of the activity in Atlantic City is moving forward as planned," Alan M. Feldman, a Mirage spokesman, said Friday.
James J. Murren, the president and chief financial officer of MGM Grand, said it was "extraordinarily premature" to speculate about what would happen in Atlantic City.
If MGM Grand does acquire Mirage -- and no one can say whether it will -- the Atlantic City plans are likely to merge as well, resulting in one new resort instead of two, according to several analysts. The new hotel would most certainly likely be located on the larger 170-acre marina site rather than on the 30-acre Boardwalk parcel. "It is desirable because it's such wide open land," said David W. Anders, an analyst with Credit Suisse First Boston. "You could develop the property exactly as you want to."
The loss -- or postponement -- of one of the resorts would be a disappointment for Atlantic City, where hotel occupancy rates exceed 90 percent. Thanks to the expansions of several existing hotels, the city has nearly 12,000 hotel rooms. But Atlantic City could fill twice that many, despite increasing competition from casinos in Connecticut, said Jason Ader, an analyst with Bear Stearns & Company. The room shortage makes it difficult to attract package tours or scheduled flights by major airlines.
The construction delays have also placed a financial burden on the city, which is heavily dependent on the casinos for tax revenues. The city's tax base took a hit recently when the aging Trump World's Fair Casino closed. Last month, Mayor Whelan said he was "out of smoke and mirrors" and proposed a 3.7-cent increase in the property tax rate, the first since 1996.
Although about 48,000 people are employed in Atlantic City's casino industry, the city needs the jobs that the new resorts would add. The unemployment rate in 1998, the last year for which figures are available, was 13.4 percent, more than twice the rate for the state as a whole.
"The longer this drags out, the more frustrated people become," Mr. Toscano said.
Mirage is also a partner in The Borgata, a $750 million casino resort planned at the marina by the Boyd Gaming Corporation, which operates the Stardust Resort Hotel and Casino in Las Vegas. Boyd still intends to break ground this summer, said Rob Stillwell, a company spokesman. Designed to resemble an Italian village, the resort would be next to the new Mirage complex.
Long before MGM Grand made its takeover move, the Mirage project had encountered a series of hurdles. Donald J. Trump, a longtime rival, tried to block it through litigation, saying Mr. Wynn had been given preferential treatment because he did not have to pay for the land. (Mirage did have to pay to clean the former landfill, however, and is contributing one-third of the cost of the tunnel, with the state subsidizing the rest.) A protracted legal fight was also waged by residents who said the tunnel would destroy their middle-class neighborhood.
Mr. Toscano said Mirage also contributed to the delays by revising its design plans twice. On Wednesday, however, in what Mr. Toscano described as "the first major step," the company is scheduled to appear before the city planning board to seek approval for roads proposed at the marina.
MGM Grand also faced obstacles in assembling its Boardwalk parcel, but Mr. Toscano and Mr. Murren said that the last holdout among the property owners was on the verge of making a deal.

HEADLINE: Miami Beach Clubgoers Creating New, Unwanted Image

BYLINE: By PETER T. KILBORN 

DATELINE: MIAMI BEACH, Feb. 26

 BODY:
It is just past 9 o'clock on a Sunday morning and the Kit Kat lounge in South Beach has closed. A boy and a girl stand motionless on the sidewalk, clinging like mortared bricks. Three shirtless boys in black pants mill around them, and a girl in a white T-shirt gyrates and claps, lost to a beat that still hammers her head. She is a "roller," high on the drug Ecstasy.
Mari, in a red tube top and a clinging black miniskirt, is 20 but has a fake driver's license that gets her into the clubs here every weekend night and has for years, she said. "When I leave South Beach and see the sun coming out," she said, "I know it's been a good night." She does not use drugs, she said, but she says many kids she knows do. 
 Vaulting into celebrity as the backdrop of "Miami Vice," the hit television series in the 1980's, this oceanside gem of Art Deco whimsy and former resort of retirees has joined the company of the nation's most impudent and indulgent trend-setting societies, like Greenwich Village or Berkeley or Venice, Calif.
But South Beach has also inspired precarious excesses, especially among the young who frequent night clubs and the after-hours clubs, which first appeared three or four years ago and open at 4 a.m. "Eighty percent of the people going to the after-hours clubs use drugs," said Chief Richard Barreto of the Miami Beach Police.
So cautiously, the authorities have begun to crack down. So far this year, the police have corralled nearly 400 teenagers for curfew violations in the proximity of the clubs, compared with about 30 a month in the last half of last year.
In December, Mayor Neisen O. Kasdin of Miami Beach and the city commission moved the after-hours clubs' closing hour to 9 a.m. from as late as noon, and prohibited admission to people under 21. Last month, the commissioners voted to ask the state legislature to raise the admission age to 21 for all night clubs. Now they can admit 18-year-olds but not serve them liquor.
But the city has rebuffed Chief Barreto's appeals to shut down the after-hours clubs. The commissioners reached a "balanced decision," he said. They took account of the views that the clubs buttress the economy and burnish the community's image as a cauldron of creative vigor and its adult culture, as Mr. Kasdin put it, "of beautiful people."
South Beach stretches along the 17 southernmost blocks of Miami Beach, and the beach itself faces the Atlantic. With the nation's prosperity helping to fuel the nonstop creation of new businesses in modeling, new media, broadcasting and electronic commerce, Mr. Kasdin said, "South Beach is roaring."
The momentum is such, said Kevin Crowder, the city's development specialist, that business will need 700,000 square feet of new office space within three years (about several city blocks) -- 110,000 more than it already occupies.
The new business and the relentless growth of tourism have set off something close to anarchy on weekends along Collins Avenue and Ocean Drive -- a "controlled reverie," as City Commissioner Luis R. Garcia Jr. put it. "The chaos you see," Mr. Garcia said, "is because of different perceived values."
The cultural mix brings together races, diverse sexual orientations, languages and incomes. The average age of all Miami Beach plunged to 43 from 65 in the 1980's, with little change in the total number of people, just under 100,000. Mr. Crowder said the average age probably dropped another three or four years in the 1990's.
Fashions whip through South Beach. Against backdrops of ear-splitting music, shops display clothes on the mannequins that ignore any practical use. But people do wear them, in the clubs.
A trend among young men is a close-cropped, yellow-bronze mat of hair that glows in the strobe lights of the clubs. This look is hardest for men with black hair to achieve.
"Mine used to be green," said Michael Escamilla, 16, newly blond and with black fingernails, who was lingering with friends on South Beach's open Lincoln Road Mall. He tackled his dark hair first with a bleach. Then he applied yellow Manic Panic, a brand of hair dye. "The trouble is," he said, "it washes away after about 20 washes."
Tattooing is flourishing, with a parlor on nearly every block, and has evolved far beyond the World War II sailor's rite of passage. Brazilian-born Luiz Segatto said the popular variations are geometric or Chinese-character bands around the upper arm, all in black. He charges from $60 for a little tattoo, up to $10,000 for a "full body suit."
Carlos Ruiz, 20, a Cuban-American, stops in. A large red and yellow sunbust on his upper left arm is inscribed "Sunshine of my life. Betty." Betty is his mother. Tattooing hurts, he said. "It hurts enough so you say you won't get another," he said. "But I'm here to get another."
In the pyrotechnic scene inside the clubs, fashion turns lunatic. At the Bar Room and the Living Room, two prominent night clubs, and Kit Kat, one of the after-hours clubs, young women arrive in feather boas, stretch black miniskirts and bulky black platform shoes.
At Level, one of the newest clubs, with 45,000-square-feet of laser-illuminated dance floors, Fridays is gay men's night. On a recent Friday, most men had doffed their shirts, and some, in boxer shorts and boxing gloves, pumped their fists to hip-hop music. Waiters circulated selling bubble-gum filled lollipops. Dancing patrons waved neon-green glow sticks.
The glow sticks and lollipops, said Police Chief Barreto, suggest that the patrons are buying Ketamine, an animal tranquilizer also known as Special K, gamma hydroxybutyrate, or G.H.B., a muscle relaxer, and mesmerizing Ecstasy.
Ecstasy is dehydrating and causes users to grind their teeth, so lollipops and pacifiers offset the effects, Chief Barreto said, and glow sticks stimulate the senses. Another device, menthol inhalers, enhance the effect of some drugs.
"It is not unusual," Chief Barreto said, "to walk into a club and see some individual zonked out in a trancelike state with one or two inhalers hanging out of their nose." The fire department, he said, has been rescuing overdosed patrons at a rate of about three a month.
Such scenes have been jarring for both South Beach's image and its economy. In the early 1990's, Mr. Kasdin said, "beautiful people came to town, and people wanted to be around them."
"A whole night life grew up around this culture of beautiful people," he said. They were affluent people who stoked the economy with their spending in the shops, hotels and restaurants.
"But then the night life began to evolve," Mr. Kasdin said, "with more and more low-rent clubs catering to people who came from across the bay. A handful of clubs would let them in even if they were under age."
Merchants complain that the young club patrons simply come for the clubs, especially the after-hours clubs, ignore the stores and are tarnishing South Beach's glamorous image.
So the mayor and the commission have begun to crack down on the clubs. In addition to restricting their hours, officials have limited their operation to weekend and holiday nights and have barred any new after-hour clubs from opening.
That is an unfair assault on the clubgoers, said Leo Mena, the owner of Kit Kat. "There has not been one overdose or death in any of the after-hours clubs," he said. "It's not entirely true," he said, that some patrons use drugs. He said he throws out any one with drug paraphernalia. His patrons, he said, "just want to sweat and dance."

HEADLINE: Downloads From Uptown: A Web Portal in Harlem

BYLINE: By TERRY PRISTIN

 BODY:
As the co-producer of "New Jack City," a 1991 film starring Wesley Snipes as a Harlem drug lord, George Jackson introduced a mass audience to the sounds and sensibilities of inner-city streets.
More recently, after a string of other movies and a brief stint as president of Motown Records, Mr. Jackson embarked on a third career. He developed ambitious plans for reaching the expanding market for hip-hop and Latino culture through a network of World Wide Web sites. 
 With two close friends, he founded Urban Box Office Network, a year-old company that already employs 250 people at ramshackle offices in Chelsea. Later this year, it will become the first Silicon Alley company to set up headquarters in Harlem, bringing badly needed high-technology jobs to the neighborhood where Mr. Jackson grew up.
But there will be no homecoming. On Feb. 10, less than three weeks before the scheduled start of the company's first major Web site, Mr. Jackson died of a stroke. He was 42.
The death of Mr. Jackson, a 300-pound Harvard-educated bear of a man who was affectionately known as Papa, has devastated his young staff and left a void that his partners, Adam Kidron, a longtime British music industry producer, and Frank Cooper, the former vice president for business affairs at Def Jam Recordings, are struggling to fill. But they say they are not deferring any of their plans. And their backers maintain that Mr. Jackson left behind a company in good enough shape to withstand his loss.
"All the investors feel confident that the leadership and the depth is there to take it forward and make it a successful media company," said Kathryn Wylde, the president of the New York City Investment Fund, a private company that tries to stimulate job growth. The fund has given Urban Box Office $2 million.
The company also expects to receive from the Upper Manhattan Empowerment Zone, a federal, state and city program designed to foster economic development in Harlem, a $4 million loan to be used to renovate an industrial building at 126th Street and Amsterdam Avenue. And the company will receive tax breaks for hiring neighborhood residents, who make up one-fifth of its current work force, said Terry C. Lane, the president of the Empowerment Zone.
Mr. Lane said he hoped that Urban Box Office would inspire other Internet companies to move to Harlem. He said he had been talking to two dozen other companies.
Despite its location, the new venture will not be aimed exclusively at African-Americans and other minority groups, Mr. Kidron said. He defined the target audience as young people with an "urban mind-set."
Ken Smikle, the president of Target Market News, a Chicago-based company that monitors African-American media and marketing, says that although African-Americans have been slower than other groups to embrace the Internet, they are catching up fast.
"There are a lot of sites trying to go specifically after the younger portion of this audience," Mr. Smikle said. But he said no one else had matched Urban Box Office in scale.
The idea of creating a Web portal for urban music, entertainment and information did not originate with Mr. Jackson. But his friends and business associates say that his ebullient and forceful personality got the project off the ground. "He was the type of guy who would take iconoclastic people and create a family," said Doug McHenry, his film-producing partner. "He would be the glue and help them formulate the vision and make it practical."
Mr. Jackson was the drawing card for Flatiron Partners, a venture capital company specializing in the Internet that has invested $16 million in the online network. Jerry Colonna, the managing partner, said, "I'd be lying if I didn't say that within 10 minutes of meeting George, I knew I wanted to fund this project."
Mr. Colonna said he was impressed by the breadth of the company's goals. In addition to serving as a distribution system, Urban Box Office will offer original content, including situation comedies, dramatic series, talk shows and animation. Other sites will offer hip-hop blended with Latin rhythms and what Mr. Kidron called "the full range of e-commerce."
As Mr. Colonna said, "As a venture capital company, you want to back entrepreneurs who think big, not people who say, 'I want to get bought out by Yahoo.' "
The Web sites are being rolled out one by one, and company officials say the network itself will not go online until a critical mass has been reached. The most elaborate one so far -- www.indieplanet.com, which is intended to showcase independent musicians, artists and writers -- will make its debut on Tuesday.
The online network was actually the brainchild of Mr. Kidron, who produced albums for the Sex Pistols in the 1970's. Mr. Kidron, 40, said he initially hoped to create a cable channel but was unable to secure an outlet in New York.
During a visit to a public library in Yonkers, Mr. Kidron said, he began to understand the changes the Internet had wrought. As a group of teenagers logged onto the Internet to listen to music, he noticed that they relished being able to flit from site to site. "What was more important than the quality," Mr. Kidron said, "was that the kids could go on the Web and they could choose what they were going to program. That was the defining moment in building Urban Box Office and why it is structured the way it is."
As Mr. Kidron was developing his idea, Mr. Jackson was coming off a frustrating year. He had moved to New York in 1997 to try to rescue Motown, a legendary record label founded in Detroit that had been run into the ground. Since he was a neophyte in the music business, his arrival was greeted with skepticism. But Danny Goldberg, the former chairman of Polygram's Mercury Group, which then owned Motown, said he had hired Mr. Jackson to run the label for other reasons. "He had tremendous executive and leadership qualities that are extremely hard to find," Mr. Goldberg said. "The things he didn't know he could learn."
As Mr. Jackson acknowledged, the Motown job was a struggle. "There's tremendous pressure on me, nothing but pressure," Mr. Jackson told The Boston Herald a few months after he took the job. Before he had a chance to prove himself, however, Polygram was sold to the Seagram Company and he was dismissed.
When he teamed with Mr. Kidron, Mr. Jackson was initially reluctant to base the new company in Harlem. "He didn't want to go there and have us be this isolated thing of privilege in this place of enormous destitution and lack of opportunity," Mr. Kidron said. But eventually, Mr. Jackson came to see the advantages of locating in a world-famous neighborhood with deep cultural roots, his friend said.
Mr. Kidron does, too. "Harlem," he said, "is this amazing urban brand that has fallen into decay."

HEADLINE: THE 2000 CAMPAIGN: WASHINGTON;
A Mini-Tuesday, With Its Quirky Prize, Looms Larger

BYLINE: By B. DRUMMOND AYRES Jr. 

DATELINE: SEATTLE, Feb. 26

 BODY:
When the 2000 presidential campaign began, the Republican and Democratic primaries that will be held in Washington State on Tuesday were hardly more than blips on the political horizon, arcane little contests lying somewhere out on the trail between the first-in-the nation Iowa caucuses and the could-end-it-all mass of 13 primaries scheduled for Titanic Tuesday, March 7.
But because the early months of the campaign have been so unexpectedly topsy-turvy, the Washington primaries, with their quirky rules and oft-quirky voters, suddenly loom large, and candidates have been forced to change plans and divert scarce resources and time to compete. The Democrats were in the state today and they will be joined by Gov. George W. Bush of Texas on Sunday. Senator John McCain of Arizona plans to arrive on Monday. 
 There is opportunity, and danger, for all candidates in these contests, but especially for Mr. McCain, still trying to forge ahead of Mr. Bush on the Republican side, and for former Senator Bill Bradley of New Jersey, still badly trailing Vice President Al Gore on the Democratic side.
Washington has no party registration and voters may choose on primary day to vote in either contest. Mr. McCain and Mr. Bradley have been going after party switchers and independent voters, Mr. McCain particularly, while their opponents have been concentrating mainly on the hard-core base in their parties.
Given that at least half of Washington voters claim to be independents (many maintain that ideology is a private matter), there has often been a lot of voting flux in primaries.
"This can be a very tricky state when it comes to politics and voting," said David Brine, the communications director for the office of the secretary of state, which regulates state elections.
"Nothing is fixed in stone here," Mr. Brine said. "There's a long tradition of switching around in parties and on issues."
Mr. McCain and Mr. Bradley find themselves in competition for some of the same votes, particularly when they are talking up one of their favorite issues, government reform. In fact, Mr. Bradley has concluded that Washington not only offers him a big chance to go after independent voters but also offers what may be his last chance, with the help of independent votes, to post a victory over Mr. Gore.
Such a victory would come in a race that under party rules is essentially what is known as a beauty contest, with no convention delegations to be won. But Mr. Bradley nevertheless is committing huge amounts of time and money to what amounts to a bet-it-all gamble -- win a contest, any contest, anywhere.
The actual choosing of the state's allotment of 75 delegates to the Democratic National Convention will take place in a March 7 round of caucuses.
Mr. Gore, for his part, comfortably ahead in most recent Washington polls, has been forced to match the Bradley effort to protect his flank, something he had not anticipated.
Mr. Gore and Mr. Bradley were both in Seattle today to address a meeting of the state N.A.A.C.P.
While Mr. Gore attended a state Democratic Party chili dinner last night while Mr. Bradley held a chili dinner with supporters.
Mr. McCain is also committing significant resources to the contest here and, much to Mr. Bradley's frustration, seems to be dominating the chase after independents.
Mr. Bush, like Mr. Gore, is responding in kind with money and manpower.
The Republican contest is part beauty contest, part real primary and, like the Democratic contest, will be followed by a round of caucuses on March 7. Twelve of the state's allotment of 37 delegates to the Republican National Convention will be chosen on Tuesday, the rest on March 7.
Recent polls show the Bush-McCain competition too close to call, just as it was too close to call on the eve of voting in several earlier contests.
For Mr. McCain, coming off his surprise victory on Tuesday in Michigan, a Washington victory would add to the momentum he has been building as he seeks to overcome Mr. Bush's huge earlier lead over most of the country. This would be especially true if he were to win both the beauty contest and the 12-delegates contest, though he and Mr. Bush very well might end up splitting those contests because of the complex rules that govern them.
But if a McCain victory here is to have truly significant meaning, the senator must not only beat Mr. Bush but demonstrate in beating him that he can pull large numbers of conservative Republican votes -- the ultimate key to the party's nomination -- not just the votes of Republican moderates, dissident Democrats and independents, his victory mix so far.
He has not been very successful in doing that in other states, no matter how hard he has tried. But now that he has retooled his pitch -- "I am a Reagan Republican" is his mantra -- he may have a better shot at it, particularly here in Washington.
Washington Republicans tend to be more moderate than Republicans in many other states, though there are plenty of conservatives here. Certainly Washington Republicans are likely to be more receptive to the new McCain pitch than Republicans in Virginia, a conservative bastion and Bush stronghold that also will hold a primary on Tuesday.
"Washington is a McCain kind of state," said Kris Wilder, the director of the McCain effort here. "More than in most states, the Washington State voter has a mind of his own, of her own, just like John McCain."
Mr. Bush, for his part, needs to regain momentum in Tuesday's voting. He badly needs a success here to twin with a presumed victory in Virginia.
"Washington is a tough one but we feel good about the outcome," said Mike McKay, a chairman of the Bush effort here. "We feel we have the support and we're confident we have the organization."
Both campaigns are filling the airwaves with a round of last-minute television and radio advertisements, mailing out thousands of campaign fliers and inundating voters with telephone calls. The senator flew here for a day of campaigning just hours after his Michigan triumph and will return for another visit just a few hours before the polls open.
For Mr. Bradley, the Democratic contest here could be his last hurrah, because a loss in Washington would lengthen Mr. Gore's string of victories over him to four in a row. So unlike the other candidates, who are dipping in and out of the state so that they can also work the other states in the March 7 primaries, Mr. Bradley is remaining here almost the entire week leading up to Tuesday's vote. His main pitch: Mr. Gore is a conservative whereas he is the true liberal and Democrat.
"If we win this primary or have a very strong showing," Mo Elleithee, Mr. Bradley's spokesman, said the other day, "it will be a signal to the rest of the country that Bill Bradley is viable and this thing is not wrapped up."
Mr. Gore, confident momentum on his side, plans to visit the state briefly over the weekend but otherwise is leaving his campaign effort here up to surrogates, many of them top labor leaders and members of the state's Democratic establishment, like Paul Berendt, the Democratic state chairman. They have kept up a drumbeat of charges that Mr. Bradley is engaging in negative campaigning.
"He's taking the low road," Mr. Berendt said, "and what he's going to find is that Washington voters are going to punish him for it."

HEADLINE: IN BRIEF;
Zoning Incentives

BYLINE: By ELSA BRENNER 

 BODY:
Hoping to attract developers of high-rise apartments to downtown White Plains, Mayor Joseph M. Delfino is backing more relaxed zoning laws.
The proposals will be part of the second phase of the mayor's economic development plans to be submitted next month to the Common Council. 
 In many places downtown, developers cannot build apartment complexes higher than 13 stories. Mr. Delfino's plan would allow taller buildings with more units, making it more profitable for builders in the downtown area, George D. Gretsas, the mayor's executive officer, said.
Mr. Delfino said he felt the city could profit from the countywide dearth of rental space and, by increasing pedestrian activity in the downtown area, also help merchants.
The city had hoped that TishmanSpeyer Properties, which plans a shopping, movie and entertainment complex on the former Macy's department store site, would also build a 450-unit apartment building downtown. But the company recently dropped its plans.
Meanwhile, Mr. Gretsas said that other plans for a $125 million apartment and hotel complex near the Metro-North station and a supermarket on South Kensico Avenue are progressing.
A proposed development on 121 acres that are owned by New York Presbyterian Hospital would include a 60-acre public park, but that project has not been formally submitted. ELSA BRENNER
