HEADLINE: Deep in the Heart of Houston

BYLINE: By Phillip Lopate; Phillip Lopate is the author of "Totally, Tenderly, Tragically: Essays and Criticism from a Lifelong Love Affair With the Movies" (Anchor Books).

 BODY:
There is big houston and little Houston. It is possible to edit the city into an extraordinarily pleasant, civil, amenities-rich archipelago of urban spaces -- always aware, however, in the back of one's mind, of that infinitely nondescript, ever-expanding strip-mall, industrial edge of town, the receding periphery that is larger Houston. But then, this is what the intrepid traveler always does: edits a personal version of Florence, say, into a set of reliable gelaterias and inspiring Botticellis or restful Boboli Gardens benches.
I lived and worked in Houston from 1980-88, basked in its uniquely kind, welcoming spirit, saw it go through boom years, when it seemed almost pathetically anxious to become a "world-class city," witnessed the recession of the mid-80's, which saved much from the wrecking ball and gave everyone a breathing spell in which to contemplate what kind of city they really wanted. The paradox is that, now that it no longer cares about being accepted as a world-class city, Houston has a much better chance of being perceived as one. 
 I went back recently to see what differences had been wrought: the new buildings and museums, the revitalized downtown and changing neighborhoods. Yet I was chary, too, about revisiting a place that had so nurtured me.
I could tell I was back when I hit the corner of Bissonnet and Main. If I had to pick a single spot to which I return in reveries of Houston, it would be that corner, where Mies van der Rohe's Museum of Fine Arts faces the Isamu Noguchi-designed Sculpture Garden, with the tin-shedded Contemporary Arts Museum off to the side. The whole ensemble has that strange combination of reassurance and disquieting emptiness that gives parts of Houston the dreamlike character of a de Chirico. My friend Stephen Fox, an architectural historian and a walking encyclopedia of the city, calls the Mies "the finest modern building in Houston." Its daunting purity is, however, a challenge to curatorial ingenuity, since temporary walls chop up Mies's noble, sweeping spaces. The Sculpture Garden, which, when it opened, received criticism for its exposed starkness, has filled out nicely with tree growth, becoming a pleasurable oasis for contemplation and a bag lunch.
I made it a point to stay at the venerable Warwick Hotel, right in the middle of the museum district. The Warwick is where generations of Houstonians prommed and wedded; it feels comfortably stodgy, a dowager of a hotel, no room faxes or minibars (for those extras you might try a Doubletree or the St. Regis), but wonderful views of downtown Houston to the north, and Hermann Park (Houston's Olmstedian public space), Rice University and the humongous Texas Medical Center to the south. It is also next door to the Museum of Fine Arts, whose new addition by the distinguished Madrid architect Rafael Moneo, the Audrey Jones Beck Building, I was eager to see.
Maybe because much of the place got built before the current generation's eyes -- going from a sleepy town of 500,000 in 1950 to the country's fourth-largest city -- architecture is the premier art in Houston; it attracts the most kibitzers.
Houstonians had watched the Moneo building's construction with apprehension: it seemed large, and the stone facade, tasteful and urbane, was not exactly iconic, like Gehry's Bilbao Museum (the new standard). But the interior is quietly spectacular: it keeps opening up in surprising vistas, with shifting ceiling heights and atriums. Expensive to build ($83 million), the new wing has gorgeous detailing, a rich blend of materials: limestone, granite and marble all warmly interacting, with touches of bronze for color.
One of the problems any Houston museum must deal with is how to accommodate the big Texas sky: too much natural lighting can harm artworks, while nothing but artificial light makes one feel like a mole. Across the way, Mies perversely designed a facade of magnificent, curved glass windows, which then had to be reined in by curtains and blinds. Moneo, who likes to use natural lighting, came up with another solution: roof lanterns, which would draw in sunlight, tamed and evenly dispersed throughout the building. Enclosed gallery rooms speak in the vocabulary of a classical European museum, and will be perfect for housing the permanent collection. The elegant Moneo wing operates as a good neighbor, freeing the Mies pavilion for large, dramatic modern paintings and sculpture. Together, they catapult the museum's exhibition space to sixth-largest in the nation.
Not far away -- within walking distance, in fact -- is the other art "campus," the Menil Collection. If the Museum of Fine Arts has a corporate, invincible, National Gallery air, the Menil enclave is more idiosyncratic, personal, familial. In truth, there is nothing else like it in America. First, Dominique and John de Menil bought up all the wooden cottages on two parallel blocks (Branard and Sul Ross), painted them the same shade of gray and rented them to creative folk and arts organizations. Then they built the Rothko Chapel on the property, implanting those dark masterpieces of Mark Rothko's in a meditative space that would, Menil-fashion, combine spirituality and art. They sprinkled various sculptures, by Tony Smith, Barnett Newman and Mark di Suvero, on the lawn. Then Mrs. de Menil, after her husband's death, commissioned Renzo Piano to build a museum to house the family collections, which were especially strong in surrealism, tribal cultures, Byzantine and contemporary art. Piano, co-architect of the Beaubourg in Paris, came up with the brilliant solution of designing what looked like an oversize wooden bungalow, with gray cypress clapboards, so that it fitted perfectly into the neighborhood. Its intricate system of skylighting through baffles directs natural light to sweep dramatically through the galleries. The stained plank floors of the spacious museum are also distinctive, like those of a Japanese temple. An amazing piece of architecture.
Not content to stop there, Mrs. de Menil had Piano design the smaller, cubelike Cy Twombly Gallery across the street: this homage to a single living painter is the sort of treatment every artist dreams of.
Twombly's paintings from the 1950's to the present, most notably his "blackboard" series, are exhibited in nine connected rooms, which have stretched cotton fabric ceilings to diffuse the natural light. The effect is of looking up at a sail in becalmed weather. To complete the ensemble, Mrs. de Menil had her architect son Francois design a Byzantine Fresco Chapel Museum, and saw to it that a Dan Flavin sculpture of fluorescent tubes was permanently installed in nearby Richmond Hall. Then she died, leaving the museum directors and trustees (including her heirs) to quarrel among themselves.
If this serene, utopian art village is to survive more or less intact, it will require a new breed of Houstonians to shoulder the responsibility initiated by this extraordinary patron, and to commit themselves to her exigent standards.
Houston's flatness and unboundedness -- it sprawls over a larger area than Los Angeles -- give it a theoretical quality, inviting developers and dreamers to imagine imposing schemes both greedy and utopian.
One of the most impressively visionary ventures to have arisen in recent years is Project Row Houses, in the Third Ward in east-central Houston. The city's black communities have historically settled in the Third, Fourth and Fifth Wards, in shotgun houses and paint-peeling shacks, in ghettos routinely starved for infrastructure and redevelopment funds by the city fathers. Since 1992, the artist Rick Lowe and his co-workers have taken a rundown set of one-story wooden houses on Holman Street, originally built for cheap tenant housing, and renovated them, assigning half to artists for work space and installation projects. The artists, both local and international, are encouraged to interact with the surrounding community while developing their spaces.
The other half of the houses are set aside as residences for single mothers, who are encouraged to turn their lives around with the aid of educational classrooms and child care. The personable Rick Lowe raised the money for purchasing and rehabbing the property largely from local philanthropic foundations and wealthy families. He also got a group of Rice University architecture students to build a new two-story house for single mothers, and recently acquired the El Dorado Ballroom, formerly a mecca for black musicians, a block away.
The idea of a grass-roots arts project that also provides affordable housing and social services is particularly noteworthy in a city that has turned its back on public housing, allowing most of USHA-financed Allen Parkway Village, in Houston's oldest black community, the Fourth Ward, to be torn down. The Fourth Ward had the ill luck to be in the shadow of downtown, and therefore ripe for the kind of "urban renewal" that displaces poor families and replaces them with speculative gentrification. The Third Ward, where the row house project is located, has the cushion of a little more distance, and developer indifference, to allow its plans to mature.
Another grass-roots effort that has helped to transform Houston is the rehabilitation of old loft buildings in downtown's Market Square area. I had heard that downtown was becoming more of a magnet after work hours; but it was hard to picture any life taking hold in that steel-and-glass zone of jumbo corporate headquarters, with formidable street-level lobbies. As it turns out, all the vaunted downtown vitality -- the new restaurants, tapas bars, movie theater, art galleries -- is on the fringes, in the remnants of an older downtown that had miraculously been spared development. As in other American cities, like Minneapolis and Los Angeles, this somewhat seedy area of warehouses and bail bondsmen suddenly looked good to artists and historically minded entrepreneurs. Floor by floor, storefront by storefront, they began bringing the area back to life in places like the adventurous new Lawing Gallery, the Notsuoh Cafe ("Houston" spelled backward), a funky coffeehouse where the prosperous and the poor commingle, and Tasca, an elegant new restaurant.
Already in place, downtown, were the major performance institutions -- the opera, the symphony, the ballet, the Alley Theater -- that constituted together a Lincoln Center-like phalanx, top-heavy with blue-chip culture. Now, Houstonians have somewhere to get a bite or a drink afterward. Or they can stroll along the Buffalo Bayou, one of the many streams that run through the city. The park still has that look of an architectural sketch, scrawnily detailed, isolated, more potential than reality, but in time the local people may take to it, the way San Antonians do their River Walk. For now, it is graced by an impressive public artwork, Mel Chin's "Seven Screens," a set of stainless steel towers with panels derived from drawings by Houston's children.
And rising a few blocks away from downtown's heart is the giant new Astros baseball stadium with a retractable roof. No great shakes architecturally -- in the de rigueur, turn-of-the-19th-century style of Baltimore's Camden Yards or Cleveland's Jacobs Field -- it's still a winning proposition to local sports fans who'll be able to walk to a game after work.
How Houston is changing may be symbolized by the fact that, less than 40 years ago, the city built the Astros' first home, the Astrodome, in the middle of an empty field on the edge of town and proclaimed it "the eighth wonder of the world." Now it's passe, turned over to cattle shows and rock concerts, while downtown beckons the fans.
Even with all this concentration of resources, some local observers, like Stephen Fox, are worried that downtown won't be able to continue drawing enough customers. He remembers how similar efforts in the past ran aground.
A persistent problem is downtown's lack of retail: all the big department stores except one (Foley's) relocated to the Galleria-Post Oak area, a paradise for mall shoppers and a nightmare for urbanists, just beyond the loop, the ring highway 610 that encircles little Houston. What speaks in favor of downtown's continuing revival is the fact that people have started to live there. A turning point was the decision in 1996 to convert the historic Rice Hotel to an apartment building, instead of tearing it down in proper Houston fashion.
That, as well as the loft dwellers around Market Square, ensures downtown at least some 24-hour presence.
The visitor new to Houston tends to bounce between downtown and the museum district, with shopping detours to the Galleria. One misses a lot of the charm and the soul of Houston that way. I recommend getting in a car and driving around the residential neighborhoods, such as Montrose, the West End, North and South Boulevards, West University Village, the Heights and River Oaks.
While too much attention has gone to Houston's so-called Emerald City image, the commercial towers that photograph well from 65 miles an hour even as they disappoint up close, the unreported story is its domestic architecture. Building on the past achievements of earlier Houston architects like John Staub, Karl Kamrath and Howard Barnstone, who set the regional standard of refined modernism with their ranch houses, mansions and apartment courts, a new set of local architects has sprung up to do brilliant work on small, relatively inexpensive houses. William F. Stern, for instance, designed a beauty of a wood-frame residence at 1202 Milford, that has the cozy feeling of a tree house; Carlos Jimenez has perfected the austere, glass-brick look with a dash of tropical elan, and Cameron Armstrong has worked wonders with his tin houses in the West End.
Alas, for every one of these architectural gems over the past decade, there have been 10 hideously clunky, oversize neo-Victorian town houses, wedged tightly together for maximum economic profit.
Houston was said to be a new kind of metropolis, a car city that subverted the concentrated, street-wall verities of European urbanism. The truth is somewhat more complex: having spread its borders wide and thin, it continues to fill in the empty pieces, like Los Angeles. More pedestrian-friendly zones proliferate, even if one has to drive to the edge of them and park the car.
For the truly intrepid traveler, there are the vast suburban tracts of Houston's "outer Siberia." The majority of Houstonians live in these areas, and I'm sure a lot of peculiar, interesting stuff must be going on in them, even if it's not yet clear to me how to decode it from the front seat of my car. One afternoon I headed out in the direction of southwest Houston, through Bellaire and Sharpstown, past South Gessner toward Alief (these names conjure up to me the place where ancient mariners expected a steep falling-off of the earth's surface). I found miles of street signs in Chinese and English, acres of Vietnamese and Chinese strip malls, the Jade Buddha temple and a Confucian temple. This was no mere Chinatown but a whole world of Asian Houston -- perhaps the future.
I got back in my car and returned to the museum district, where I checked out two new entries: the Children's Museum of Houston, designed by Robert Venturi and Denise Scott-Brown, a cheerful, playful structure, and the Holocaust Museum Houston, by Ralph Appelbaum, which is sober, moving and anything but playful.
Then I went home. I had seen enough good architecture for a while. The pleasurable sense that Houston keeps getting better was counterbalanced by the fact that no one in the outside world seems to know or care. It remains the most underrated of American cities.
 
Hanging Out in Houston
Warwick Hotel, 5701 Main Street (telephone: 713-526-1991), has double rooms starting at about $169, with weekend rates starting at about $119. The Hunt Room restaurant is open for breakfast, lunch and dinner. Dinner entrees include red snapper, Chilean sea bass, medallions of beef and veal chops. Dinner for two, with wine, is about $100. The Terrace on Main, open for lunch and dinner, is more casual, with tables indoors and outdoors. Specialties include a spinach, raisin and roasted garlic tart and cumin-grilled scallops with tabouli. Dinner for two, with wine, is about $75.
Doubletree Hotel, 400 Dallas Street (713-759-0202), has double rooms for $195, $119 on weekends. There are two restaurants. Dover's, open daily for dinner, offers salmon steak and filet mignon. Dinner for two, with wine, is about $60. The Brasserie is open for breakfast, lunch and dinner.
St. Regis Hotel, 1919 Briar Oaks Lane (713-840-7600), offers double rooms for about $295, weekend rate, $135. The Remington Grill, with Art Deco decor, is open for breakfast, lunch and dinner. Beef, chops and seafood are on the menu, and dinner for two, with wine, costs about $130.
Tasca Kitchen and Wine Bar, 908 Congress Street (713-225-9100), features American cuisine, with European, particularly Spanish, overtones. Among the tapas served are fried squid and snail ravioli with anchovy butter. Main dishes include fried cornmeal-crusted catfish with crayfish mashed potatoes and grilled beef tenderloin with blue-cheese ravioli; for dessert there is bourbon-praline cheesecake. Dinner for two, with wine, is about $100. Open for lunch and dinner, Monday through Friday, dinner only on Saturday.
Mark's, 1658 Westheimer Street (713-523-3800), serves such specialties as cumin-dusted snapper in a lemon-herb sauce, bourbon-roasted pork tenderloin with yams and gingered apples and, for dessert, warm chocolate cake. Dinner for two, with wine, is about $100. Open for lunch and dinner Monday through Friday, for dinner only on weekends.
Fox Diner, 905 Taft Street (713-523-5369), serves burgers, sandwiches and more ambitious dishes like roasted salmon, fried chicken with gravy and mashed potatoes and, for dessert, pecan pie. Alcoholic beverages are available with a $3 membership. Dinner for two, with wine, is about $50. Open Monday through Friday for lunch and dinner, Saturday for dinner and Sunday for brunch.
Butera's Fine Foods, 4621 Montrose Boulevard (713-523-0722), is an Italian delicatessen and cafeteria with table service. Specialties include oversize sandwiches and 20 different salads. Wine and beer are served, and dinner for two with wine is about $25. Open daily for lunch and dinner.
Diedrich Coffee, 4005 Montrose Boulevard (713-526-1319), serves coffee, tea, pastries and bagels. Open daily, 6 a.m. to 11 p.m., Friday and Saturday until midnight.
Notsuoh Cafe, 314 Main Street (713-222-0443), is coffee bar that specializes in sandwiches: a particular favorite is the vegetable hummus sandwich. All of its cakes are home baked. Open daily, 6 a.m. to 6 p.m.

SECTION: Section 6; Part 2; Page 63; Column 2; Sophisticated Traveler Magazine 

LENGTH: 3126 words

HEADLINE: Entering New Castle, Del.

BYLINE: By Francine Prose; Francine Prose's novel "Blue Angel" is forthcoming from HarperCollins.

 BODY:
For years, i confess, i'd come to think of Delaware as that long, rather terrifying bridge between Maryland and New Jersey -- specifically, the Delaware Memorial Bridge that you cross (and cross and cross) on the way to Washington from New York. So it was a revelation and a pleasure actually to stop in Delaware, and to discover that what lives more or less underneath the bridge is not the troll one might sensibly expect, but rather the attractive town of New Castle. Old and lovely without being
cutesy or pretentious, relaxed but high functioning, cared for but not precious, fossilized or overrestored, New Castle is a rarity: a model of historical preservation and small-town civility in which the owner of that adorable brick Federal or imposing ivy-covered Victorian is more likely to drive a pickup truck than a BMW. 
 New Castle, with a population of just less than 5,000, is the sort of village you'd expect to find in Europe, a place that has noticed and prized its own good looks, and halted construction and demolition at a high point in its architectural history. Throughout the 20 or so square blocks that make up the town's historical section, an 18th-century Dutch cottage is around the corner from Federal mansions with soaring ceilings, which in turn neighbor Colonial houses painted in airy pastel colors and gabled Victorian dwellings in the more somber palette of the late 19th century.
It's also one of those towns (like Charlottesville, Va.) that demonstrate the great aesthetic appeal of old brick. New Castle's sidewalks are brick, as are many of its buildings, the walls that enclose the gardens and the paths that run among the spires of iris and lupine, the bushes of peonies, rhododendrons and roses. Everyone here seems to garden -- some in borders on green lawns sloping down to the Delaware River, some in tidy plots no larger than big-city yards. Time and sunlight have nurtured the perennials, and faded the brick into shades of sienna and salmon that, one imagines, Martha Stewart would offer a fortune to replicate, and to market.
From the first moment you register some marvelous architectural detail -- the pigtail curlicues of a Carpenter Gothic porch, the Palladian grace of a fan window, an elaborate chimney above a stepped roof straight out of a Bruegel painting -- you're obliged to slow down and pay attention to what each building has to offer. This is but one of many reasons why a visit to New Castle can function as a rest cure. I've never been more reluctant to phone into my answering machine at home than I was during the two days, this past spring, that my husband, Howie, and I spent there.
New Castle just barely provides that surge of tourist adrenaline inspired by acquisitiveness and new venues for shopping. The old part of town has a pleasant general store but no real grocery, a few interesting antiques shops, most of which stay open only from 10 to 4 and a rare-book dealer whose business operates "Saturdays, by appointment, and by chance." There are no luxury hotels, or, for that matter, hotels, just a few small bed-and-breakfasts, including the charming and well-equipped (private baths, phones and TV sets) Armitage Inn, where the tradition of hospitality goes back to the 18th century, when the house functioned as a stopping place for postriders.
Every year, on the third Saturday in May, New Castle goes all out and puts on "A Day in Old New Castle," a house-tour extravaganza during which 50 or so of the village's most gorgeous houses fling open their doors and show visitors that they are as beautiful inside as out. The people who live here seem to love it; everyone mentions it, sooner or later. And though I'll admit to a mild phobia of private-house tours, I can see the point of this one, which would have spared me the trouble of rudely trying to peek into people's living rooms and gardens. On the other hand, I don't know if I would have enjoyed New Castle quite so much if I'd had to troop through its narrow lanes in a large crowd convened to admire someone's silver tea service and someone else's snapdragons.
Even during tourist season, weekdays in New Castle are quiet. By late afternoon, the streets empty out, and the action, such as it is, moves to the Battery -- a lush park, shaded by fragrant flowering trees, that borders the Delaware River. It's immensely relaxing to spend the early evening by the shore, sitting on a bench, watching tugboats and thrillingly huge tankers sail slowly past, letting a horse-chestnut tree drop pale flowers on your head and idly wondering why the park isn't crowded with urban planners observing how variously and harmoniously this community makes use of its waterfront.
People come to the Battery to fish, jog, stroll, read and just hang out. In fact, on the afternoon we spent there, the passers-by were so lovely, the little vignettes they enacted so endearing -- the elderly couple roller-skating hand in hand, the un-self-consciously multicultural group of bright-eyed teenagers playing catch, the two women in their 60's doing their best to power walk but slowing down to gossip, the handsome young parents with babies in strollers -- that I couldn't help wondering if we had somehow stumbled into "The Truman Show." Because there is that about New Castle, too: After two days in town, you start recognizing faces in the street, and familiar names in the graveyards.
For lovers of historical cemeteries, old New Castle is paradise. Surrounding the Immanuel Episcopal Church -- a stately, austere structure that, from some angles, resembles van Gogh's painting of the "Church at Auvers" -- are tombstones dating from the early 1700's. Many have inscriptions that are not only touching but also, by modern standards, verbose. So we learn of Mary McWilliam, who died in 1749: "In her did conspicuously shine all the Devine & Amiable Virtues of the Human soul Piety without Ostentation Humility without Affectation unbounded Charity filial Duty Conjugal Love & Affection Maternal Tenderness and Indulgence but with Oeconomy beloved in living and in death lamented." In one especially lovely corner are the graves of the Van Dyke family, whose house on Delaware Street was the site of a wedding at which the Marquis de Lafayette gave away the bride. A nearby headstone is inscribed with a weeping beech, its shape familiar from Colonial funerary paintings and samplers, and also, as it happens, from the gardens of New Castle. Seeing these gracefully drooping trees -- in person, as it were -- makes it clear why they are such potent symbols of bereavement. And the unusually handsome calico cat that makes its home in the Immanuel Church cemetery recalls those sleek, well-fed, slightly spooked felines that appear, often in the clutches of their child-owners, in American primitive portraits.
One way to get a quick sense of -- and a lightning tour through -- New Castle's lively history is to tag along behind the squads of schoolchildren who regularly spill from yellow buses, equipped with pencils and assignment sheets covered with questions they're required to answer. Predictably, the kids are less susceptible than adults to the town's quaint charm. I watched a boy in skater shorts and a bowl haircut consider the goose-egg-size cobblestones on Market Street and tell his friends, "Man, I'm not walking on that, no way." But there's plenty to keep them busy as they race from one historical marker to another.
Their work begins at the handsome town green, allegedly paced-off by Peter Stuyvesant, who established Fort Casimir here in 1651, as a means of monitoring and intimidating the Swedes, who had settled the area in the 1630's. Victorious in a 1655 battle with their Swedish neighbors, the Dutch rechristened Fort Casimir "New Amstel" only to have their claim challenged by the British, who renamed the town New Castle. (An interesting history could be written of site names in the area, perhaps beginning with the nearby town of Odessa, where there are still other magnificent old houses that visitors can tour. Its name was changed from Cantwell's Bridge, in 1855, by town fathers who -- apparently practicing magical thinking -- believed that their flagging economy could somehow be revived if they adopted the name of the thriving Russian port.)
On New Castle's Market Street, a statue of William Penn commemorates the Quaker settler's receipt of what is now Delaware in a land grant from the Duke of York. In 1704, Delaware broke away from Pennsylvania, and New Castle was designated the colonial capital. The town retained its political importance, fleetingly, when Delaware became a state. In 1777, the capital was moved to Dover, the first in a series of setbacks (the Delaware Canal and the long-distance railroad were located some distance from New Castle, and in 1881 the county seat was changed to Wilmington) that stunted the town's economic growth while, in the process, blessedly safeguarding it from some of the more damaging side effects of rapid modernization, prosperity and growth.
A less frantic method of tracking the town's history is to take the brief, undemanding tours of several of its exemplary dwellings and public buildings. The Dutch House Museum dates from around 1700 and contains household objects -- ice skates, wooden clogs, a Bible in Dutch and a practice sampler -- from this early period. Far grander is the 1738 Amstel House; its artifacts include what must be the world's most ingenious oven timer: a candle burning down the length of a small notched post. Taking us through the Court House, our amiable guide showed us the room in which the State Assembly met until 1777 and where the Declaration of Independence was read; an instructive exhibit on the town's role in the Underground Railroad; and, best of all, the interior of the old courtroom, where a window in the floor offers a view of the foundations of the original 1689 courthouse, burned down by a prisoner condemned to hang for breaking and entering.
The most ambitious of the museums is the Read House, built in 1801. Like the stately homes in Odessa, this one has its Balzacian history of fortunes made by successful, self-made men and, in some cases, squandered by hapless older sons. The Read House also shares with Odessa's Corbit-Sharp and Wilson-Warner houses the concept of a museum as a sort of doll house, subject to periodic refurnishings by its curators. The rooms are regularly redecorated for Christmas, "summer dress" and so forth. When we were there, the theme was the War of 1812, during which the citizens of New Castle assumed that they were about to be attacked by the British, and got ready to flee en masse. The objects in the museum were packed and prepared for flight, stacked in the hall, the sitting rooms and bedchambers; it was sort of fun and sort of confusing to see an authentic, period version of the mess I'd left at home.
The Read House pays tribute to Philip and Lydia Laird, a stylish couple who sound a bit like Gerald and Sara Murphy, and who played an important role in New Castle's restoration and preservation. In 1920, they bought the Read House even though it was on the seamy side of town, a fact that, one supposes, would hardly have daunted a pair who built a speakeasy in their basement, on the model of a German rathskeller, and held lavish parties on their roof overlooking the river.
At 4 on a thursday afternoon, the kitchen at Jessop's Tavern was closed, but we were told we could sit at the bar and have a bowl of soup -- a corn chowder that was quite simply the most wonderful corn chowder I'd ever had, and probably ever will have; you'd hesitate a million times before you made a soup like that at home. For if most of historic New Castle's major construction ended early in this century, its cuisine (despite an occasional sun-dried tomato) ended at the midpoint.
Dining in New Castle, you understand why people liked the food of the 1950's, that innocent era before anyone knew that there was a problem with cholesterol, with heavy cream, butter and salt. The town's restaurants, you suspect, might not speak the same language as the health-and-weight-conscious, dressing-on-the-side tourists interrogating the waiter about a dish's every ingredient.
Considering the generous quantities of cream, crab meat and tomato used in its seafood soup, the David Finney Inn could hardly fail to produce a small masterpiece of old-fashioned American cooking, and the Arsenal on the Green Restaurant, housed in a structure built in 1809 to store ammunition, produced a model spinach and bacon salad.
Returning for dinner at Jessop's Tavern, we ordered a bottle of inexpensive wine (New Castle is not a place for the extensive, thesis-quality wine list) that turned out to be surprisingly good. We joked about not being able to find the label and vintage again, out in the real world, after we left New Castle. Indeed, the town does give one that feeling of having vanished off the edge of the known world, fallen down the rabbit hole, slipped into another time zone. Every time we left the historic center, we got lost, and got lost again returning, perhaps because the landscape changes so drastically the moment you leave.
Just as New Castle was saved from disfiguring change by apparent economic and political misfortune, so now, again, it seems paradoxically protected by its threatening surroundings. The high-speed rush of Interstate 95 is only a few miles away. From one end of town, you can see the Delaware Memorial Bridge. Every night, the prodigious refineries to the south produce a festival of lights, really rather pretty when reflected in the river. And the distant but unmistakable view of the Salem Nuclear Power Plant, visible from the Battery, makes it unlikely that the rich will be outbidding each other for prime waterfront property.
But perhaps that's what it takes for a town to be left alone and survive, undisturbed and vital, with a population on whom history, architectural beauty and a well-tended and utilized riverfront has so clearly had a salutary effect.
On the first afternoon we spent in New Castle, a vintage three-masted frigate with its sails lowered was floating in the river just offshore, bobbing gently in the wake of giant freighters transporting truck bodies and cement pipes. By the next morning, the old ship was gone. It was explained to us, logically, that over the Memorial Day weekend there was going to be a gathering of tall ships in Wilmington harbor, and that the ship was part of the flotilla. Still, it was hard to keep from imagining that the frigate's appearance and disappearance was a fortuitous, ghostly visitation, staged for our entertainment by the town to underscore its rich and colorful past.
 
A Taste of New Castle
Armitage Inn, 2 The Strand (telephone: 302-328-6618; fax: 302-324-1163), was built in 1732, and was for many years known as the Van Leuvenigh House after an early owner, Zachariah Van Leuvenigh, a patriot during the American Revolution. There are five double rooms, with bath, and rates range from $110 to $155, including breakfast. No smoking, no pets, no children under the age of 10. Open year round. Credit cards accepted.
Jessop's Tavern and Colonial Restaurant, 114 Delaware Street (302-322-6111), is named for Abraham Jessop, a barrelmaker, who built a structure that is now part of the tavern in 1724. Specialties include a variety of chowders, turkey pot pie and porterhouse steak on a bed of wild mushrooms; for dessert there is bread pudding topped with butterscotch brandy sauce and cinnamon whipped cream. Dinner for two, with wine, is about $65. Open Monday through Saturday for lunch and dinner.
David Finney Inn, 216 Delaware Street (302-322-6367), built in 1685, is named for an 18th-century owner. Among the restaurant's specialties are seafood soup, baked Chilean sea bass with sauteed greens, black bean cake and lobster potato puree and chateaubriand for two with bearnaise and bordelaise sauces; desserts include macadamia meringue with whipped cream. Dinner for two, with wine, costs from $75 to $100. Open daily for lunch and dinner.
Arsenal on the Green Restaurant, Market Street (302-328-1290), housed in a structure built in 1809 to store ammunition, has undergone renovations and is scheduled to reopen around March 1 with a new name, The Arsenal at Old New Castle. Specialties will include shrimp, scallops and lump crab meat in puff pastry, pork tenderloin medallions with apple stuffing and applejack sauce, and, for dessert, deep-fried tortillas with berries and cream. Dinner for two, with wine, will cost about $75. Open Tuesday through Saturday for lunch and dinner, Sunday, 12:30 to 6:30 p.m.
Cellar Gourmet, 208 Delaware Street (302-323-0999), offers vegetarian selections, including homemade soups, salads, broccoli or spinach quiche and soy burgers; muffins, scones and pies are also served. A meal for two is under $20. Open daily 9 a.m. to 4 p.m.
 
Delaware Delights
In New Castle, Del., the Dutch House Museum, 32 East Third Street (302-322-9168), and Amstel House, 2 East Fourth Street (302-322-2794), are run by the New Castle Historical Society. From March 1 to Dec. 31, they are open 11 a.m. to 4 p.m., Tuesday through Saturday, 1 to 4 Sunday; closed Monday and holidays; open on weekends in January and February. Admission: $2.25 for one museum, $4 for both.
Court House, 211 Delaware Street (302-323-4453), is open year round, Tuesday through Saturday, 10 a.m. to 3:30 p.m., Sunday, 1:30 to 4:40; closed Monday and holidays. Free guided tours are available. Admission: free.
Read House, 42 The Strand (302-322-8411), is run by the Historical Society of Delaware. From March 1 to Dec. 31, it is open 10 a.m. to 4 p.m., Tuesday through Saturday, noon to 4 Sunday; closed Monday and holidays; open on weekends in January and February. Admission: $4.
In Odessa, Del., some 15 miles south of New Castle, there are several historic buildings open to the public that are operated by the Wintherthur Museum. Known collectively as the Historic Houses of Odessa (302-378-4069, 4050), they include: the Collins-Sharp House, Second and High Streets, a log-and-frame building dating from 1700 where cooking and gardening demonstrations are presented; the Wilson-Warner House, Main Street, a fine example of Delaware Georgian architecture, built by David Wilson, a merchant, in 1769; and the Corbit-Sharp House, Main Street, also Georgian, completed in 1774 by William Corbit, a tannery owner, and sold in 1928 to H. Rodney Sharp, who restored it. The houses are open on varying days and at varying hours from March through December. Admission: $5 for one house, $7 for two, $8 for three.

SECTION: Section 7; Page 14; Column 1; Book Review Desk 

LENGTH: 1409 words

HEADLINE: Waiting for Lefty, Then Righty

BYLINE: By David Glenn; David Glenn, who contributes to Dissent and Lingua Franca, is writing a book about the city of Milwaukee and the American social contract.

 BODY:

THE PARADOX OF AMERICAN DEMOCRACY
 Elites, Special Interests, and
 the Betrayal of Public Trust.
 By John B. Judis.
 305 pp. New York:
 Pantheon Books. $26. 
 John B. Judis began his last book, "Grand Illusion: Critics and Champions of the American Century," with a smart essay about the agonies of Herbert Croly. The author of "The Promise of American Life" (1909) and a founder of The New Republic (where Judis himself now serves as a senior editor), Croly is remembered for his insistence that national regulation was required to protect American democracy from the effects of the post-Civil War corporate oligarchies. Less well known today are the knots in which Croly tied himself as he pursued this argument. He distrusted labor and other populist social movements; he hoped instead that reform would come at the hands of benign elites wielding the tools of what he considered "disinterested" social science.
In the early years of Woodrow Wilson's administration, something like this actually seemed to be happening. But after World War I, Croly was bitterly disappointed by the follies of Wilson's wise men at Versailles and by the crass self-interest of the business lobbies that dominated 1920's Washington. Croly withdrew from politics and spent his last decade bitterly wondering, in Judis's words, "how the disinterested progressive intellectual . . . could influence a public and a corporate elite committed to the quest for individual gain."
Judis's new book, "The Paradox of American Democracy," plunges into the same questions that brought Croly to such frustration. Judis hopes to rebut, or at least to complicate, a common school of sociology, associated with C. Wright Mills and some of his disciples, that holds that a small "power elite" acts to stymie fundamental reform in the United States. On the contrary, Judis argues, reform is most successful precisely during periods when a certain type of elite policy analyst emerges. He has in mind the members of organizations like the National Civic Federation, which brought together business and labor leaders at the beginning of the century; people like the Ivy League-educated architects of the New Deal, who were branded class traitors in their day; and intellectuals like those at the Brookings Institution and the Ford Foundation, who supplied social science and policy advice to the ambitious liberals of the Kennedy and Johnson administrations. These elites occupied what Louis Brandeis, referring to lawyers, called "a position of independence, between the wealthy and the people, prepared to curb the excesses of either." It is only when such high-minded elites are weak or absent, Judis suggests, that crude economic interests rule and petrochemical lobbyists are invited to rewrite the nation's regulatory laws, as happened in 1995. This is the paradox of American democracy.
One important virtue of this book is that Judis offers his paradox as an observation, not as a Grand Unified Theory of progressive politics. He clearly values the ideal of disinterested policy analysis, but understands (much better than the young Croly did) that democracy cannot be reduced to an antiseptic contest among "scientific" policy prescriptions. Judis never denies that mobilized social movements are fundamental, and he acknowledges that the high-minded elites he describes may have been as much symptoms as causes of the reform periods in which they appeared. The National Civic Federation was founded in 1900, after a decade of bloody labor conflict; the New Deal came amid a huge surge of left-wing militance and in the shadow of foreign tyrannies; and the Ford Foundation's 1960's antipoverty research coincided with summers of urban rioting. A historian of the C. Wright Mills school might look at this picture and say, with some good reason: Of course American society is going to cough up reformist elites during times when its fundamental stability is threatened.
But Judis understands that history can't be bottled in such deterministic maxims. He wants to bring our attention to the details and the contingencies of these elites' histories -- to an appreciation that the American establishment has "its own ethos and worldview," which can't "be fitted into conventional Marxist or sociological categories." In this respect, however, the book never quite goes for the kill. Judis provides quick, judicious sketches of his high-minded elites; but his argument might have been richer had he written a full-immersion portrait of at least one of the organizations: its fundamental beliefs, day-to-day operations and relationships with other political actors.
Judis also acknowledges, but never really tries to dissect, the dark side of center-left policy elites: their tendency to slide into technocratic hubris. In many ways the Kennedy years marked the apotheosis of the disinterested policy maker. The White House kept business, labor and civil rights leaders at arm's length, but opened its doors wide to the Council on Foreign Relations, the Brookings Institution and the RAND Corporation. But while we look back in admiration at the social movements that emerged in the early 1960's, the Camelot brain trust itself is remembered for . . . what? For Vietnam, and for its willful blindness to the simmering angers within American society. Kennedy himself told the Brookings Institution in May 1963 that "most of the problems . . . that we now face are technical problems, are administrative problems." Somewhere offstage, Betty Friedan, Malcolm X and George Wallace shook their heads and smiled.
This pattern of arrogance, of course, does not by itself refute Judis's central argument -- that the ideal of disinterested policy making is crucial to democracy, and that this ideal has been seriously corroded during the past quarter century. In the book's strongest chapters, Judis describes the business counteroffensive of the mid-1970's, the period when modern conservative think tanks and corporate lobbying took shape. Judis presents this material with far more detail and subtlety than most other liberal commentators. Among other things, he is attentive to the irony that progressives themselves helped set the stage for the rise of moneyed interests by enacting reforms that weakened the traditional powers of political parties. And he has a fitting rage at the parade of Carter, Reagan and Bush administration veterans who have moved straight into million-dollar K Street berths: "By becoming business lobbyists, the former officials deprived the country of responsible mediating voices. By making it appear that even the most distinguished Americans could be bought, they contributed to public cynicism about all political opinion, including that of elites."
This is a valuable and consistently provocative book. But I wonder, finally, if Judis's well-earned anger at Washington corruption hasn't clouded his judgment about very recent events. His account of the 1994 health care train wreck, for example, rightly deplores the power and demagogy of the insurance lobbies, but underplays the failures of Ira Magaziner's arrogant, closed-door White House task force. Here is where a deeper understanding of Kennedy-era hubris might have been helpful. Just as Kennedy's team consistently underestimated and misunderstood the desires of the civil rights movement, the Clinton White House was extraordinarily clumsy in its relations with the social movements (especially labor) that might have been expected to fight for its health care plan.
Finally, what about the possibility of conservative reform campaigns and disinterested elites? The strange-bedfellow coalitions -- churches, business, neighborhood organizations -- that have fought for school vouchers in Cleveland and Milwaukee can't be dismissed as the mere products of narrow corporate interests or free-market dogma. Indeed, in these coalitions street-level social movements and think-tank elites seem to have listened to and learned from each other far more harmoniously than the left's components managed to do during the health care fight.
These doubts and questions are intended as a tribute to, not a critique of, the arguments Judis raises in "The Paradox of American Democracy." This book will provoke long and diverse reflections on the state of our politics, and on why, if he's still watching from beyond the grave, Herbert Croly is probably in as much anguish as ever.

HEADLINE: Building a Better Pigeonhole

BYLINE: By Bruce Weber; Bruce Weber is an arts reporter for The Times.

 BODY:

THE CLUSTERED WORLD
 How We Live, What We Buy, and What It All Means About Who We Are.
 By Michael J. Weiss.
 Illustrated. 323 pp. Boston:
 Little, Brown & Company. $29.95. 
 There are two kinds of people in this country, one of which believes there are 62 kinds of people in this country. Michael J. Weiss is among these, and 12 years ago his book "The Clustering of America" described the work of a man named Jonathan Robbin, who had divided the nation into ZIP codes and, using census information, public opinion polls and other data, lumped together different codes with similar statistical profiles.
These groupings -- population segments within which people buy the same things, get their information and entertainment from the same sources, vote the same way and generally behave as consumer clones of one another -- were called clusters, describing what Weiss calls "distinctive lifestyle types." At that time, there were 40 of them -- Robbin gave them distinguishing and vaguely offensive names, like "Blue Blood Estates" and "Shotguns & Pickups" -- and the cluster became a fundamental term in business thinking, the niche in niche market.
Now, in "The Clustered World," Weiss has enlarged his scope. In it he tells us that Americans now constitute 62 clusters -- as defined by Claritas Inc., a marketing information company in Arlington, Va., that has broadened Robbin's work -- and that the rest of the world has become clustered too, as marketing companies abroad have found it appropriate to divvy up those populations as well. Not only that, Weiss tells us, Americans may well have more in common with their similarly clustered brethren in another country than with their fellow citizens in another cluster.
This is all mildly interesting, mainly because it provides a framework for Weiss to present a wealth of amusing arcana about the habits of people within vaguely defined boundaries. In American college towns (the "Towns & Gowns" cluster), for instance, tequila and Dove Bars are popular, and twice as many people watch the soap opera "Another World" as do people in the rest of the country. In the "Military Quarters" cluster, that is, people living on or near military bases, people are four times as likely to watch "Hard Copy" as the average American, and more than twice as likely to watch "Ricki Lake"; they also eat more than their share of canned chili. Among young middle-class exurban families (the "New Homesteaders" cluster), furniture refinishing, downhill skiing and cats are in; chess, HBO and tractor pulls are out. Call waiting, it turns out, is heavily used in two kinds of clusters: those with affluent suburban families (because of their teenagers) and those with working-class minorities (black Americans spend more than an hour and a half a day on the phone, three times the norm).
Unfortunately, Weiss is no mere presenter of data. Rather, he seems to think of himself as a kind of Margaret Mead of marketing, an interpreter of the customs of the inhabitants of these strange, newly discovered lands. Indeed, he says that in 15 years of studying geodemographics, as the statistical portraiture of circumscribed populations is known, he has become a true believer. He actually describes his experience as "my long love affair with clusters," and it has convinced him, he says, of "their power to divine the mysteries of how we live."
This is a rather monumental claim for the clever and effective marketing tool that clusters represent, and Weiss never approaches substantiating it. It's difficult to tell whether this is merely an example (one of many in this book) of cliche-ridden writing and imprecise thinking or whether Weiss is rather wackily out of sync with his material. That clusters are useful -- for businesses seeking customers, for candidates seeking votes, for nonprofit organizations seeking new contributors, for health organizations tracking the incidence rates of disease -- is amply demonstrated here. That they are sociologically important is not, and it is unnerving -- weird, actually -- that Weiss repeatedly asserts it anyway.
On the opening page, for example, Weiss says that clusters reveal both "profound and trivial" things about American lifestyles: "What prompts some of us to eat Brie and others to devour Velveeta cheese?" he writes, as a sample of, presumably, one of the trivial things clusters can tell us. His answer is presented a couple of pages later in a chart comparing Velveeta buyers and Brie buyers in Washington, where, Weiss writes, "a fault line exists between the fans of Brie cheese, who tend to hold down executive jobs and write the laws of government, and those of Kraft Velveeta, who maintain the service economy." So affluent, influential people eat more Brie; blue-collar folks eat more Velveeta. That's not exactly evidence that being elected to Congress "prompts" a taste for French cheese, is it? (And a fault line between cheese eaters? An overripe metaphor, to say the least.)
This willful inflation of logic pervades the book to such a degree that the whole isn't logical. In addition, the author's truly awkward way with words -- Granted, not all diseases are cluster sensitive," he writes at one point, meaning (I think) that some infectious germs are not influenced by the socioeconomic status of their victims -- often clouds his intentions further. Weiss writes about the "popularity of clustering." What does that mean? Who likes to cluster?
Most telling, even the central concept of the book -- the cluster -- is ill explained. What is a cluster, anyway? Weiss never acknowledges the cluster as a concept, an artifice; rather, he seems to think that such a thing exists in nature. But he never defines it absolutely. At various times it is equated with a neighborhood type, a population segment, a lifestyle type. He says clusters are used to "demarcate a variety of small geographic areas," and that the "core truth" of what we learn from them "can be simply expressed: You are like your neighbors." But he also declares that "thanks to technological advancements, no physical contact is required for cluster membership." And if he sometimes speaks of a cluster this way, as something one belongs to, like a fraternity, at other times he apparently views a cluster as a palpable place.
"In clusters like Hispanic Mix," he writes, "home to downscale, predominantly Hispanic families, you can live most of your life without needing to speak or read English. "
There is something intellectually alluring about the idea of redrawing the boundaries among the world's communities to create a new paradigm for observing civilization. That's what Weiss seems to think he's done here, and maybe there are some clusters whose residents will agree with him. In the Skeptical Readers cluster, however, which includes my neighborhood, we find books that analyze humanity as a collection of target markets a little depressing.

HEADLINE: Fort Bragg's Changing Tide

BYLINE: By SUSAN ALLEN TOTH; SUSAN ALLEN TOTH's most recent book is "England for All Seasons" (Ballantine).

 BODY:
WHEN I'm walking on Fort Bragg's Glass Beach, I'm easily distracted. If I look out to sea, restless swells break over seaweed-drenched boulders. At my feet, the sand glitters with pebbles of green, red and yellow. This rough beach was once the town dump -- hunks of rusted metal blend into the dark rocks -- and now, after decades of sea-polishing, trash has turned into jewels. On the headland shore above, huge smokestacks at Georgia-Pacific, the forest products company, belch white and gray dragon-puffs into the sky.
Glass Beach hints at some of the hidden pleasures -- and contradictions -- of Fort Bragg, a logging, fishing and commercial center with a population of about 6,000 on the midnorthern coast of California. Just eight miles north of artsy Mendocino, a Victorian picture-postcard village full of galleries and trendy restaurants, Fort Bragg, which shares Mendocino's startlingly beautiful coastline and back country, has remained largely working-class. 
 But after many quiet years, change is arriving in Fort Bragg with almost alarming speed. California's Highway 1, which runs through the town, is lined with establishments like Baxman's Gravel andWoody's Weld-All. But on our most recent visit, in July, my husband, James, and I were surprised to find that the town's business district had taken on a cosmopolitan spirit.
Art galleries and bookstores are popping up near the Sugar 'n' Spice Discount Store and the Tip Top Lounge. Antiques and collectibles fill several stores; the 300 block of North Franklin is now known as "Antique Row." Merchants and the local Chamber of Commerce, realizing the attractions of Fort Bragg's early-1900's painted-wood streetfronts, have begun to publicize their Historic Downtown Business District.
A few doors down from the Headlands Coffeehouse, a down-to-earth place filled with local residents, the Tattoo Tavern's picture windows display portfolios of tattoo art (as well as an unadvertised flesh-baring show, since patrons lie back in a dental chair and get their elaborate tattoos in full sight of the street). Around the corner, but a world away, the gift-filled Hot Pepper Jelly Company sells its own fancy jams, relishes, chutneys and vinegars in flavors like lavender-and-blueberry or apricot-and-rosemary. The Company Store, which sold hardware and groceries to generations of mill workers and their families, is now a flashily restored mini-mall recalling the building's history in vintage redwood but selling contemporary art, crafts and boutique clothing.
Despite its deceptively old-fashioned name, the North Coast Brewing Company, opened in 1988, was named by the Beverage Testing Institute as "one of the 10 best breweries in the world." Its eclectic menu offers not only lumberjack-heavy food like "killer nachos," but also fresh Pacific red snapper crisped in Scrimshaw beer batter and French fries.
But Fort Bragg is not merely retooling itself as a retro town. For more exotic tastes, the new Cafe Prima offers such traditional dishes from Kenya as kimanga cha mboga -- cassava root and kabocha squash, boiled, mashed and mixed with red beans. At the Mendo Bistro, upstairs at the Company Store, pastas include sweet-potato gnocchi with gorgonzola sauce and spiced pecans. The Old Coast Hotel, built in 1892 and recently refurbished, will serve up a bowl of its specialty, shellfish cioppino with crab, prawns, mussels and clams.
Fort Bragg's lumberyards are visibly languishing, and asking about this decline in the industry is almost guaranteed to spark a heated discussion about its probable causes, from overlogging to cheap imports. But several of Fort Bragg's attractions recall a more prosperous past. An imposing Victorian mansion, built by the timber magnate C. R. Johnson, is now the Guest House Museum, restored by Georgia-Pacific and given to the city. Operated by the Fort Bragg-Mendocino Coast Historical Society, it focuses on the logging industry.
At 700 North Main, now the gingerbread-Victorian Pudding Creek Inn, a Russian count nicknamed "Bottle Brown" once lived. Somewhere on the property he is supposed to have hidden a trove of jewels, which, like most such treasures, remain unfound.
From the old railroad depot, built in 1924, the California Western Railroad runs daily excursions on the Skunk Train, a leisurely half or full-day trip into what remains of the redwood forest. (The vintage trains, with their gas engines, were named long ago by loggers who claimed, "You can smell 'em before you can see 'em.")
But what brings James and me back to Fort Bragg, again and again, is not really its history or its changing fortunes but its setting. The Mendocino coast -- the plunging, rocky, surf-tossed stretch from Gualala to Westport -- is still a gloriously unspoiled part of California. It is far enough from San Francisco to deter hordes of day-trippers: a three-and-a-half-hour inland drive on Highways 101 and 128, far longer on the scenic but tortuous coastal Highway 1. In the middle of this coast, where redwoods still sometimes crowd close to the road, Fort Bragg offers a perfect base for exploration.
Within a 20-minute drive of the town are state parks and reserves with challenging or restful walks for an hour's or a day's hike. Between Mendocino and Fort Bragg, Highway 1 gives direct access to Russian Gulch State Park, whose short oceanfront trail circles past a blowhole (an ocean-cut tunnel collapsed at its inland end) called the Devil's Punch Bowl. On the other side of the highway, linking trails covering more than nine miles climb through redwoods, fir, laurel and other trees that cover the canyon of Russian Gulch.
Less than two miles north, another road angles off the highway to Point Cabrillo Preserve, on a windswept peninsula crowned by a lighthouse. A short drive away, a roadside parking lot at Jughandle State Reserve provides a starting point for a two-and-a-half mile inland trail, called an "ecological staircase," at Jughandle State Reserve. This trail winds past creekside bogs, then through meadows and scrub forest, and finally up to a "pygmy forest," full of stunted trees and shrubs. Even in mid-August, at the height of tourist season, most of these trails are remarkably peaceful.
One of our favorite walks does not appear in tourist brochures. Driving north from Fort Bragg, 11.5 miles from the Pudding Creek railroad trestle that is one of the town's landmarks, James and and I watch for a familiar sign: "Vista Point." Many Vista Points, similarly posted, abound on the Mendocino coast, but this one is special. Parking in a small pulloff, we follow an unmarked trail that drops steeply, past a sheltering band of pines, to a meadow of waving tall grasses that flow straight to the edge of the sea-cliffs.
From there we can look back at the high treeless hills that rise behind the road, as well as many miles up and down an almost empty coast. To the north, the road curves and disappears into a faint blue mist. As we thread our way along a narrow path along the edge of the cliffs, we can always catch glimpses of the seals that sun themselves on the low rocks offshore. We too bask in the vast space and silence, broken only by the rumble and roar of the surf below.
SEALS are also on display, more publicly, at MacKerricher State Park, about two miles north of Fort Bragg. Here a wheelchair-accessible boardwalk loops over spongy terrain past an overlook with viewing benches, where protected and much-admired seals gather on a chain of half-submerged rocks below. Walking from MacKerricher's parking lot along an abandoned seafront road, we can arrive in about half an hour at Ten Mile Beach. The piecemeal road, shattered and washed away in many places, finally disappears, testimonial to the violent winter storms that have battered this section of coast.
Although Ten Mile Beach has a pedestrian name, it unrolls into miles of wide firm sand washed by constant surf. On the shore side, a long line of high dunes hides Ten Mile from the distant highway. Since it is far too dangerous (and too cold) for swimmers or surfers, the beach is virtually deserted.
We are not always alone on Ten Mile Beach. Seals often swim by, poking their heads above the low riffs of crumpling surf. Once, during a March visit, when the spouts and flukes of migrating whales punctuate Fort Bragg's offshore horizon, I took a solitary walk along a far stretch of Ten Mile. Suddenly, almost in front of me, a whale rose up vertically in the low foaming surf -- I could never have imagined that water deep enough to cover such a huge animal -- so that, for an instant, I could see its sidewise eye. Then, almost in the same instant, it disappeared. It was as though I had seen a vision; and indeed I had.
From late December to mid-April, gray whales migrate back and forth along the Mendocino coast. The whales have become much-advertised tourist attractions. In March, Fort Bragg, as well as Mendocino, celebrates a Whale Festival, when restaurants compete in seafood chowders, local specialty brewers host a beerfest, and musical events might include the Spyhopping Harmonicas or a barbershop quartet.
During the whale season, several boats leave from Noyo Harbor for whale-watching excursions. Fairly close sightings are almost guaranteed, and a captain will sometimes cut his motor and drift among the whales. When we tried one of these expeditions, I found it exciting to hover close to a lolling pod of partly submerged whales -- but I still remember much more hauntingly my brief lone encounter on Ten Mile Beach.
Almost every walk in and around Fort Bragg leads to the ocean. At the Mendocino Coast Botanical Gardens, whose entrance lies just off Highway 1, the front section displays grouped special collections, including dwarf conifers, heathers, succulents, rhododendrons, and more than 165 varieties of fuchsias. (Fuchsias, which thrive on this coast, reach their apotheosis at Fuchsia rama, a large retail nursery on the north side of Fort Bragg.)
From the more formal beds, woodsy trails then veer past a small bog and rise through a narrow gorge, darkened by tall Monterey cypress and lined with luxuriant ferns. Following a creek that tumbles with golden brown water in early spring, the trails snake up and down. Then they join and suddenly emerge from a tunnel of cypresses onto an open headland, dashed with surf from the rocks below.
Even when we stay indoors, we remain on the sea. Although its Main Street is lined with motels and inns, Fort Bragg also abounds in "vacation rentals," private homes rented by absent owners through commercial agencies. On a recent visit, we splurged on California-style luxury at "Seashine," a contemporary three-bedroom house on its own peninsular point.
Seashine was a stunning place to call home. Its floor-to-ceiling windows brought unimpeded views of sky and sea into every room. On the sheltered deck we could watch an ocean sunset, or even enjoy the rain, from the depths of a hot tub. Then, sitting in front of the fireplace, we could catch the first glint of moonlight on the water -- or, early in the morning, the first faraway white jet of a passing whale.
At the end of our week, as we left Fort Bragg, I wondered what I would remember most: the polished-trash pebbles at Glass Beach, the many colors of spiky heather at the Botanical Gardens, the tall-grass meadow at Vista Point, or the barely muffled surf outside our bedroom window. Unlike many vacation towns, Fort Bragg resists a single defining image. Perhaps that is a sign that the town is still alive and changing, ready to surprise me upon our return.
 
Whale-whatching and the Skunk Train
 
Things to Do
The Fort Bragg-Mendocino Coast Whale Festival will take place on March 18 and 19 in Fort Bragg, featuring a classic car show, seafood chowder tasting, a Microbrewers' Beerfest, an arts and crafts fair, a footrace on the headlands and other events. Whale watching on boats out of Noyo Harbor will be offered. Admission varies for individual events. On March 4 and 5, a similar festival will bring chowder tasting, wine tasting and strolling musicians to Mendocino. Information: (800) 726-2780.
The Mendocino Coast Botanical Gardens, at 18220 North Highway 1, Fort Bragg, (707) 964-4352, are open daily 9 a.m. to 5 p.m. from March to October, 9 a.m. to 4 p.m. from November to February. Admission $6.
The Skunk Train, Highway 1 and Laurel Street, Fort Bragg, (707) 964-6371, offers a half-day trip from Fort Bragg to Northspur water station; $27 for adults, $14 for children. A full-day outing, with an hour's lunch stop in Willits, costs $35 and $18 respectively. The half-day trip runs daily March 1 through Nov. 30, plus the last two weeks in December. The full-day trip runs April 15 through 30 and May 27 through Oct. 28.
 
Places to Stay
Vacation rentals on the Mendocino Coast are available for as little as one night in some cases, though high cleaning fees (added, with taxes, to the basic rate) make a longer stay more economical. Rates vary with the location, size and luxuriousness of house; the number of occupants, and the season.
We rented Seashine from Shoreline Vacation Rentals, (888) 942-8284 or (707) 964-1444; www.shorelinevacations.com. The rate for two people in summer for seven nights is $2,041, including tax and cleaning fee; discounts for off-season and longer stays in season are possible. An in-town cottage rents for $837 a week.
Other agencies, with different properties but similar rates, are Mendocino Coast Reservations, (800) 262-7801 or (707) 937-5033, www.mendocinovacations.com; Coast Getaways, (800) 525-0049 or (707) 937-9200, www.coastgetaways.com and Robison Properties, (800) 359-4649, www.pacific
.net/robison.
Fort Bragg has many motels, some with ocean views. Most are on Main Street, which doubles as busy Highway 1. The three-year-old Surf and Sand Lodge, at 1131 North Main Street, (800) 964-0184 or (707) 961-0130, fax (707) 964-0314, has 30 rooms, most with ocean views, and doubles ranging from $59 to $175.
Bed-and-breakfast inns include, among others, the 14-room Grey Whale Inn, 615 North Main Street, from $100 to $180, (800) 382-7244 or (707) 964-0640, fax (707) 964-4408, with doubles from $100 to $180, and the eight-room Weller House, 524 Stewart Street, (707) 964-4415, (877) 893-5537, fax (707) 964-4198, a handsome Victorian built in 1886, with a 900-square-foot ballroom where breakfast is served and doubles range from $95 to $165.
Camping is available at many of the nearby state parks, including Russian Gulch and MacKerricher, as well as the private campground at Caspar Beach.
 
Places to Eat
Main Street Diner and Get It to Go, at 322 North Main Street, (707) 964-7910, is open for lunch and early dinner, closing at 7 p.m., and is closed Sunday. The diner serves beef stew, meat loaf and specials such as chicken-fried steak. Entrees are under $8. No liquor is served, but customers can bring beer and wine.
The North Coast Brewing Company is at 444 North Main Street, (707) 964-3400. Open daily noon to 11 p.m. At dinner, burgers are $6.95; fish and chips, $9.95; cioppino, $16.95. A pint of ale is $2.75.
The Mendo Bistro is upstairs at 301 North Main Street, (707) 964-4974. Northern Mediterranean food emphasizes pasta and seafood. Lunch Monday through Friday, dinner nightly. Three-course dinner for two, around $60 with a bottle of wine.
Cafe Prima is at 124 East Laurel Street, (707) 962-0753. Closed until March 10; call for hours. Dinner for two with wine or beer, $30 to $40.
The Old Coast Hotel, at 101 North Franklin Street, is a bar and restaurant as well as a 13-room hotel. Dinner is served Thursday through Monday, lunch Friday through Sunday. Its specialty, shellfish cioppino, is $11.95. Dinner for two with wine, around $40.

HEADLINE: ART/ARCHITECTURE;
On Staten Island, the New Media Are the Message

BYLINE: By HERBERT MUSCHAMP 

 BODY:
PETER EISENMAN'S design for the Staten Island Institute of Arts and Sciences brings into focus a theme that has preoccupied him in recent years: the relationship between the history of places and the history of ideas. The $65 million project, which partly straddles the St. George terminal of the Staten Island ferry, is a favorite of the borough president, Guy V. Molinari. The building will house a new branch of the Staten Island Institute, called the Center for Electronic Culture, and will feature displays on the new media that are helping to bring a global culture into being.
Situated near the mouth of New York Harbor, the center will honor a city that began to expand the circulation of Western ideas more than three centuries ago. It offers a microcosmic version of the global forces that are reshaping the contemporary urban condition. Many museums today use advanced media technology in the presentation of shows, the creation of Web sites, the expansion of programs outside the museum's stone and mortar walls. At the Center for Electronic Culture, these new media become the message. 
 Plans for the Center include these exciting upgrades: an Infohall, described as an electronic town square, with games, projections and other media attractions; a museum, featuring five long, serpentine galleries with interpretative displays, along with a library, bookstore and cafeteria, and an Industrial Documentary Center, with research stations for new developments in high-tech communications.
Now, check out these deflating downgrades: our central cities and their architecture, an ancient communications medium that will remain with us so long as people don't tire of ruins. There's no longer any doubt that cities, buildings and interiors are being rapidly affected by new media. Manuel Castels, the urban theorist, has charted what he calls the space of flows, the global network through which information and money are transmitted in ever-shifting patterns without regard for traditional boundaries of city, state and nation. Tourism is the social equivalent of this electronic space. The tourist attraction is one of its architectural devices. The Center for Electronic Culture will be a new attraction for a city that increasingly relies on tourism to shore up its economic base. Eisenman's design gives the space of flows an architectural shape.
The center is integrated with the ferry terminal building and the transportation systems that converge there: boats, trains, buses, cars as well as the parking lots, roads, platforms and piers that serve them. The center's most conspicuous form is a large rotunda, made of intersecting, twisted, tapering oblongs, which rises 44 feet above ground level. The curving shapes recall the sculptures of Clement Meadmore, while the building skin, slit with openings for skylights, will remind some viewers of the steel and concrete roof designed by Eero Saarinen for his Trans World Airlines terminal at Kennedy Airport.
Unlike Saarinen or Meadmore, however, Eisenman asserts that he arrives at his forms by a systematic design process. The process starts by draping a two-dimensional grid over the site. The grid assumes the contour of the site's topography. It is then superimposed with shapes derived from the transportation systems: ferry lanes, train platforms, bus ramps and so on. These shapes are extended into three dimensions and then packed with the building's program: galleries, library, cafeteria, offices and anything else that fits.
No doubt there's a certain amount of gimmickry, not to say hocus-pocus, involved in this process. The point is that, once again, Eisenman has convinced himself of the logical basis for his forms. They are not to be seen as willful, Expressionist shapes but as emanations of a site, its natural topography and its patterns of social use, frozen in architectural form. Obviously, the design bears no resemblance to contextualism, in the customary sense of the term. Unlike the 1992 design by Venturi, Scott Brown for the Manhattan terminal of the Staten Island ferry, for example, Eisenman's plan does not mimic the surfaces of adjacent or prior structures. Rather, it links the specifics of its physical location to the larger grid of Western ideas.
Eisenman has been using this design method at least since 1982, when he designed the Wexner Center for Art in Columbus, Ohio. In that project, the particulars of place are represented on an organizing grid derived from the city's street system and the partial reconstruction of an old armory building that had once occupied the site. The history of ideas is represented by the expansion of the grid into an abstract, three-dimensional form of white painted steel. This form, which resembles a scaffold, contains the Wexner's gallery spaces. It signifies the classic framework of Cartesian thought.
The scaffold also brings to mind the roughly contemporaneous work by Sol Lewitt and is thus a clear indication of the source for Eisenman's topographical method of design. Like Lewitt, Eisenman is a Conceptual artist, but one who happens to work in the architectural field. Like Frank Gehry, who has derived inspiration from Robert Rauschenberg, Ellsworth Kelly and other artists, Eisenman has drawn architecture into a wider frame of cultural reference. This is a natural move for architects of cultural institutions, and one that should be welcomed by those who esteem Beaux Arts edifices for incorporating painting and sculpture into their designs. In this sense, Eisenman's Staten Island project must be seen as a legitimate heir to the Metropolitan Museum of Art, the New York Public Library and other landmarks.
Since Eisenman is our foremost anti-Classicist, this line of descent is not easily apparent. Nor is Eisenman's work routinely discussed in the context of Conceptual Art. This is partly a result of the gap that divides architecture from other art forms, and partly a result of the modern attachment to originality. Eisenman himself is hardly innocent of this attachment. He has always wanted it both ways. He likes to startle viewers with novel forms and with claims that his projects are helping to undermine the grand Western tradition. At the same time, he is possessed by a deeply conservative craving to belong to that tradition.
Perhaps he's entitled to have it both ways. Even so, the contradiction needs to be clarified. Eisenman is what used to be called a New York Intellectual. But he's working at a time when that species has become nearly extinct. There is, for now, no community of informed opinion to give his work either support or constructive resistance. If there were such a community, the Conceptual roots of Eisenman's architecture would be less obscure, and his forms would appear less startling. He would not have been branded a theorist nor would the audience for his work been largely confined to academia. In fact, the principal interest of importing Conceptualism into architecture lies with the potential to extend that movement's intellectual framework beyond the art world cloister into the larger public realm.
EISENMAN is not the only contemporary architect who draws on or parallels Conceptual themes. A significant strain of contemporary architecture comes out of what Harold Rosenberg called the dematerialization of the art object. John Hejduk, Diller and Scofidio, Bernard Tschumi, Lebbeus Woods, Neil Denari and others have created memorable projects relying on conventional building materials. The emerging generation of computer-based architects are Conceptualists, whether they're aware of it or not.
This work stands in a critical relationship to the conventions of mainstream practice, but it also reveals the extent to which even traditional architecture is conceptually based. Architects produce ideas, plans, blueprints -- concepts -- that, like Lewitt wall paintings, will be realized by others: executive architects, space planners, construction companies and such. The completed building, in turn, takes on an expanded life in the mediated form of published drawings, photographs and other images (like those published here). At both ends, that is to say, architecture is a medium of consciousness. It can transform our inner as well as our outer landscapes.
Conceptual art is thus an ideal form for reckoning with the pressures of globalization. The space of flows does not reside solely in software, streams of electrons, and pixels on a screen. It also exists in the realm of information and ideas. It lives, that is to say, in the head. Architecture like Eisenman's capitalizes on information as the international currency of the global marketplace.
The design vocabulary of the Staten Island project -- the grid, the diagrammatic abstraction -- points to another consequence of globalization: our increasing awareness of the distinctiveness of Western thought. In the emerging global culture, the West has begun to look at itself with an anthropologist's eye. We walk down streets full of buildings, advertisements, fashions and window displays designed to appeal to normal tastes, and say to ourselves: "How weird! Somebody thought of that." Seen in this way, Eisenman's Staten Island design does not seem weird at all. Mediating between places and ideas is a natural, even an essential function for architecture to take on in changing times.
New York intellectual life was created by immigrants, and the global network made possible by tourism and the media has gone far toward compensating for its erosion. As an enduring cultural center, New York deserves a place where this history can be told. Thanks to the Staten Island Institute, we will have one.

SECTION: Section 2; Page 53; Column 1; Arts and Leisure Desk
