HEADLINE: ART/ARCHITECTURE;
A Japanese Treasure Moves to the Midwest

BYLINE: By RITA REIF 

DATELINE: INDIANAPOLIS

 BODY:
OVER 20 years, Alan Strassman, a Boston investment manager, collected 95 Japanese paintings from the Edo period (1615-1848). Twenty were gloriously painted screens that included depictions of a field of tall grasses glowing gold in the moonlight, corpulent gods wrestling, evocative scenes from "The Tales of Genji," wild dancing in the streets near a tea house and a Noh theater performance in an aristocrat's courtyard. His collection became one of the largest and finest of its kind in private hands.
From the start, when he bought his first scroll painting -- which showed a tiger gazing at the moon (prompting him later to call his Edo works the Tiger Collection) -- Mr. Strassman was dazzled by, as he put it, the "unique flat perspective" in Japanese art. "It was striking and beautiful," he said. 
 He was also impressed by the history and stories depicted in these works on paper and silk, and soon assembled a library of 1,400 books on the subject. "Much of Edo painting has roots in mythology, roots in history, roots in some kind of narrative that is deep in the Japanese psyche," he said. "There is something literary that lies behind each image, making these paintings especially meaningful."
As the collection grew, he enjoyed planning exhibitions of the works in his home, frequently changing them to avoid overexposure to light and moisture. He even had a vault built there to store them. Their fragility and his space limitations made it impossible to view all of his collection at one time.
But Mr. Strassman will finally be able to see most of these Edo paintings when they go on view in "300 Years of Japanese Painting: New Treasures for Indianapolis," an exhibition that opens at the Indianapolis Museum of Art on May 20 and remains until Aug. 6. Last month, the museum acquired 70 works from the Tiger Collection, including the 20 screens. The museum paid more than $7 million, and Mr. Strassman donated five screens and his library.
The show will not include 25 scrolls that Mr. Strassman still owns; he plans to give them to the Museum of Fine Arts in Boston, where he is a trustee and was board president from 1993 to 1997. But the exhibition will also include five Edo scrolls that were bought by the Indianapolis museum from Leighton Longhi, the New York dealer from whom Mr. Strassman acquired most of his Edo art. "These works strengthen the collection at key points," said James Robinson, the museum's curator of Asian art.
The acquisitions, with a total value of $10 million, represent the largest purchase of Japanese art by an American museum, and are the Indianapolis Museum of Art's second-largest purchase, exceeded only by the $30 million it paid in 1998 for 101 paintings and prints by Paul Gauguin and his Pont-Aven followers.
The museum's Japanese additions complement these works as well as works by other early modernists of the late 19th century, most of whom were influenced by Edo paintings. The acquisition more than doubled the museum's Japanese art holdings; its Edo collection now ranks with those of the Metropolitan Museum of Art, the Museum of Fine Arts in Boston, the Smithsonian's Freer Gallery and the Los Angeles County Museum of Art.
"These works, which are in immaculate condition, are of a quality and rarity no longer available in the art market at any price," said Bret Waller, the Indianapolis museum's director. "The weakness of the economy in Asia in recent years became a major factor in making this purchase possible. And we were lucky: the prices for Japanese art had not escalated as swiftly as those paid for other artworks."
The acquisition is part of the museum's $160 million development plan, which will include an expansion of the Asian art galleries to accommodate the Japanese acquisitions. Until now, such holdings were overshadowed by the museum's substantial Chinese art collection, the core of which was donated by Eli Lilly, the pharmaceutical magnate, who was the museum's largest benefactor until his death in 1977.
The expansion also reflects the museum's awareness of the growing Japanese presence in Indiana. Today, there are some 200 Japanese companies, including Toyota, Subaru and Isuzu, in the state, employing more than 40,000 workers. Since 1993, Indiana has led the nation in attracting new Japanese investment and now ranks third, after California and Ohio, in the total amount invested: $8 billion plus.
The museum's search for Japanese art began in 1997, when Mr. Robinson alerted dealers in this country and abroad that the museum was interested in acquiring a major holding. But he never expected that he would luck into anything like the Tiger Collection, which includes all the major traditions of Edo painting. The screens are the stars of the exhibition, revealing the virtuosity that the Japanese achieved in this art form during the Edo period, a level of screenmaking unmatched anywhere else.
"When all the scrolls and screens are exhibited together, I hope that the extraordinary density will give people a sense of the opulence of this collection," Mr. Robinson said. "It cuts a broad swath through Edo painting, something that should knock your socks off."

HEADLINE: FILM;
Jarmusch Still Fills the Role of Favorite Outsider

BYLINE: By LAURA WINTERS; Laura Winters's most recent story for Arts & Leisure was about the French director Olivier Assayas.

 BODY:
ONE day a few weeks ago, the filmmaker Jim Jarmusch was hunkered down in a corner of the Pink Pony, a favorite hangout near his Lower East Side home, trying to have a quiet conversation. But a steady flow of colleagues and well-wishers kept wandering by -- drawn, it seemed, by his signature silver hair, as well as by his laid-back manner. After the umpteenth interruption, Mr. Jarmusch smiled wryly. "It's still odd to me if somebody on the street knows who I am," he said. "I'm not interested in the whole celebrity cult thing. Is it better to be semi-known or semi-unknown?"
The 47-year-old Mr. Jarmusch personifies this riddle better than almost any other American director today. An intensely private man, he became a downtown celebrity with his second movie, the art house hit "Stranger Than Paradise" (1984). With its throwaway but graceful style and no-budget economy, "Stranger" helped pioneer the American independent film movement. "When Jim hit with that film, he forged the way for all of us," says Spike Lee, who attended film school with him at New York University. 
 Though Mr. Jarmusch is still an emblem for many younger filmmakers, his path after "Stranger Than Paradise" has not always been easy. His last few films have drawn only modest audiences in the United States -- though abroad they draw crowds and his laconic humor is admired as deeply American. An industry outsider, he has become a world-cinema insider, with a loyal band of A-list collaborators -- "a strange, international biker club," says his friend, the musician Tom Waits. His reputation has drawn Hollywood stars like Winona Ryder, Johnny Depp and now Forest Whitaker, who stars in Mr. Jarmusch's new film, "Ghost Dog: The Way of the Samurai."
"Ghost Dog," which opens on Friday, is Mr. Jarmusch's first non-documentary in four years. Like much of his work, it weaves together strikingly disparate elements: he calls it a "gangster samurai hip-hop eastern western." And yet, despite its oddness, "Ghost Dog" has a wild-card commercial potential -- both because of its star, Mr. Whitaker, and because of the film's highly bankable soundtrack, created by the hip-hop master the RZA, of the Wu-Tang Clan.
The film follows an urban loner named Ghost Dog, played by Mr. Whitaker, who makes his living as a Mafia hitman. A modern-day samurai, he follows the precepts of the "Hagakure," the early-18th-century Japanese warrior codebook. Every samurai swears undying loyalty to a master, and Ghost Dog's master is Louie (John Tormey), a small-time mobster who once saved Ghost Dog's life.
When one of Ghost Dog's missions goes awry, Louie's own bosses, a comical bunch of aging mafiosi, decide that the lone gunman has to be eliminated.
Mr. Jarmusch began writing the film, he says, with the idea of "a character who was a likable killer." He tends to write with particular actors in mind, and in this case he was thinking about Mr. Whitaker: "Forest is very gentle and soulful," he says, "but there's a dark side to his presence which I haven't seen used very often. I wanted to make a character who had that contradiction."
When the filmmaker Sara Driver ("When Pigs Fly"), Mr. Jarmusch's longtime girlfriend, gave him the "Hagakure," the book became "Ghost Dog's" spiritual well. Mr. Jarmusch has aphorisms from the "Hagakure" written out onscreen -- solemn lines like, "The end is important in all things." But the film has his signature zany humor as well, in his portrait of America as a place of crossed cultural wires. The mafiosi can't afford to meet in an Italian restaurant, so they hold court in a fast-food Chinese joint. "Jim likes to put things together that might explode," says Mr. Waits. "He may be a white-haired beatnik from Ohio, but he knows how to rub two sticks together."
Mr. Whitaker, whose implacable presence anchors the film, has long been interested in Eastern philosophy and spent many hours discussing his character with Mr. Jarmusch. "It was one of the most collaborative experiences I've had with a filmmaker," Mr. Whitaker said. "Jim really pays respect to his inspirations."
In fact, Mr. Jarmusch decided to make "Ghost Dog" an outright homage to certain gangster classics. The film is punctuated with direct references to films like Jean-Pierre Melville's "Samourai" and Seijun Suzuki's "Branded to Kill." But Mr. Jarmusch pays homage as well to whole cultures that he admires. "I didn't cast Forest because he was black," Mr. Jarmusch said, "but having a black character created a lot of nuances that I wanted in the story. I wanted hip-hop culture to be in the film."
The director, who used to play in a downtown band called the Del-Byzanteens, was struck by the Wu-Tang Clan's synthesis of African-American and Asian motifs. So he tracked down the RZA, their elusive leader, to do "Ghost Dog's" soundtrack.
"Three weeks later I get a call," he recounts. "And RZA says, 'Meet me in a blacked-out van at 3 a.m. on 50th Street and Broadway.' I go there and he gives me a tape and says, 'Yo, check this stuff.' I ask if he has any directions for me and he says: 'Nah. This is hip-hop style! Try it in the film, juice pieces together.' "
Beneath the film's playfulness, however, lies Ghost Dog's pained awareness that his code of honor is vanishing in the modern world. This melancholy comes directly from Mr. Jarmusch. "I've tried from the beginning to have a code," he says, "but you learn real fast in the film business that that's a good way to get eaten alive."
He grew up far from the film business, in Akron, Ohio, where his father worked for B. F. Goodrich. After attending Northwestern University for one year, Mr. Jarmusch transferred to Columbia. When he spent a semester in Paris, he discovered the Cinematheque and the films of Yasujiro Ozu, Carl Dreyer and Robert Bresson.
Returning home, he entered film school at N.Y.U. and became the assistant to the filmmaker Nicholas Ray, who in turn introduced him to Wim Wenders.
When Mr. Jarmusch finished his student film, "Permanent Vacation," Mr. Wenders gave him some leftover film stock, which Mr. Jarmusch used for part of "Stranger Than Paradise."
With the help of two investors, Mr. Jarmusch completed the film, a deadpan, black and white feature about three drifters on an impromptu road trip. It went to the Cannes International Film Festival in 1984 and stunned Mr. Jarmusch by winning the Camera d'Or for best first feature. "We were at Cannes, sleeping on the floor, and then the water got cut off," he remembers. "When I had to do a television interview, I shaved using leftover tea water."
When "Stranger" was released later that year, Mr. Jarmusch saw himself hailed, at 31, as the creator of a new film style. The film's influence extends even to directors today like Kevin Smith, who directed "Clerks" and "Dogma.""Seeing 'Stranger Than Paradise' is an empowering experience," said Mr. Smith. "Here was a movie that they made for a hundred, two hundred grand, and suddenly the power was in the hands of the people."
Mr. Jarmusch found all the attention rather overwhelming. But at another film festival, he met a young Italian actor named Roberto Benigni. He asked Mr. Benigni to star with Mr. Waits and John Lurie in his next movie, in which they would play three innocents who escape from a Louisiana jail.
At the time, Mr. Benigni spoke no English. During the shooting of "Down by Law," Mr. Jarmusch and Mr. Lurie patiently taught him their own version of the language. "I learned that a cup was a 'clamp,' " Mr. Benigni said, laughing, on the telephone from Rome, "and that 'to urinate' was 'to flame.' The entire set used these words."
Mr. Benigni credits Mr. Jarmusch for having introduced him to America. "I love really Jim's way to live and to direct actors," he says. "He let me improvise during rehearsal time and then left me an open door." He adds, mysteriously, "He is a real poet, a cross between brains and beauty -- between Orson Welles and Brigitte Bardot."
Mr. Jarmusch's fascination with the coexistence of cultures permeated his two next films, "Mystery Train" (1989) and "Night on Earth" (1992). "Mystery Train" is set in a seedy but evocative Memphis, while "Night on Earth" was shot nocturnally in five cities around the world. Rather than accept Hollywood production money, Mr. Jarmusch began at this point to seek foreign financing. "I've been stubborn, but I've kept artistic control over my films," he said. "I see them through every step of the process, even owning the negatives."
This involvement explains the occasionally long stretches between films. With "Dead Man," his most ambitious project, he shot a hallucinatory black-and-white period Western with Johnny Depp as a fugitive befriended by a Native American.
The film was panned by critics when it was released in 1996, but it has recently undergone a startling renaissance, with many end-of-century wrap-ups (including a current series at the Film Society of Lincoln Center) naming it one of the best films of the 1990's. Mr. Jarmusch is typically modest about this. "If it goes through the culture in some minor way, I'd be glad," he said.
Before "Ghost Dog," he shot a concert film/documentary of his friend Neil Young and his band, Crazy Horse, called, simply, "Year of the Horse." And he has appeared in cameos in some of his friends' films, including Billy Bob Thornton's "Sling Blade" and Wayne Wang and Paul Auster's "Blue in the Face."
The acting is a lark for him, whereas his own films are deeply personal. The sympathy for the outsider which has inspired all of his work is especially clear in "Dead Man" and "Ghost Dog." "A bunch of old white men have run things so far," he said. "That's why I've always been interested in people who don't fit in. I have friends who are in prison, off the grid, living on reservations. I learn more from them, somehow, and I respect them."
NOT many people know that before making his name as a filmmaker, Mr. Jarmusch worked as a factory welder, a hydraulic drill-gun operator, a moving man and a process server for Legal Aid. He would be the first to admit that the people he encountered in these professions would not, probably, go to see his films.
But his far-flung experiences have shaped his concerns as a filmmaker, just as they have shaped his desire to reach an audience, whether it be big or small. "It's very important to me that the audience completes the electric current, because otherwise a film is just a dark lamp sitting in a corner," he said. "I'm not trying to teach a lesson to anyone.
"I'm just trying to make a world which they can enter and observe, bringing with them whatever has shaped their own lives."

HEADLINE: SPRING THEATER/VISIONS OF AMERICA;
What It Means to Be Both Cuban and American

 BODY:
A GENERATION separates the Cuban-American playwrights Maria Irene Fornes and Nilo Cruz. Ms. Fornes was 15 when she left Cuba in 1945 -- long before Fidel Castro seized power in the 1959 revolution. Mr. Cruz was 9 when he and his parents left in 1970.
Today, nearing 70 and epitomizing the outsider tradition of the Off Off Broadway theater movement, Ms. Fornes is being celebrated by the Signature Theater Company with a seasonlong festival of her work. The world premiere of her latest play, "Letters From Cuba," continues through March 12 at the Signature. Directed by the author, it is based on more than 200 letters she received over 30 years from an older brother who remained in Cuba. After writing some 30 plays, musicals and operas, "Letters From Cuba" is the first time that Ms. Fornes has dealt directly in her work with her Cuban heritage. 
 Mr. Cruz, who is almost 40, has explored his own sociopolitical roots in such plays as "Dancing on Her Knees" and "A Park in Our House." He discovered theater in 1981 as a member of a local troupe in Miami, and in the late 1980's directed Ms. Fornes's play "Mud" at the South End Alternative Theater there. Ms. Fornes saw it and invited him to become a member of her Intar Hispanic Playwrights Laboratory, now defunct, where he started to write seriously. His current play, "Two Sisters and a Piano," directed by his frequent collaborator, Loretta Greco, runs through March 12 at the Joseph Papp Public Theater. It is based in part on the lives of his sisters and a cousin in Miami and on the house arrest of the dissident Cuban poet Maria Elena Cruz Varela, who in 1991 was forced by Cuban security agents to eat some of her political writings and was sentenced to two years in prison.
Ms. Fornes and Mr. Cruz talked recently in Manhattan with Randy Gener, a reporter for Theatre.com and a freelance theater critic, about the influences of their homeland and adopted country on their playwriting. Here are edited excerpts from the tape-recorded conversation.
 
Q. As Cuban-Americans and as dramatists, how strongly have you taken on social and political matters in your two new plays?
MARIA IRENE FORNES. My play never mentions Castro.
NILO CRUZ. Neither does mine.
FORNES. Your play mentions a person in prison. So it is more directly about politics. My play deals with life in Cuba but it is set in two places -- Cuba and America. On the stage set, there is a regular apartment in New York but only a roof in Cuba. The Cuban world on the roof has stars above it all the time. There is a girl who receives letters in America written by my brother in Communist Cuba. But he could be living in another city where there aren't too many advantages. It could be a small Italian village. It's not that I was avoiding talking about politics. It just didn't seem to be the energy of the play. What interested me was that all of my family had left Cuba, and my brother was still there. Yet he was never a sympathizer with the Castro regime.
Q. Why did he choose to remain in Cuba?
FORNES. To me, it has to do with attachment. It's something that we usually don't think of: place as a love. It seems almost corny -- I don't know why I'm crying. But I'm so moved by the idea of loving a place. Usually you love a person and you cannot live without them. People have an attachment to their animals, their dogs, there's a love there. But the love for place is so hard and so beautiful.
The New York characters in my play are artists: there is a poet, a painter, and the girl who is a dancer -- my alter ego. In Cuba, there is my brother and his son. In the play, the character who is my brother usually has a letter in his hand, and as he speaks to the person in the New York apartment, he reads the actual text of my brother's letters. We have been corresponding for the last 30 years.
My brother is now 80. Rafael is the oldest and I am the youngest of six brothers and sisters. In the play, he is called Luis. I guess in some ways I have wanted to write about Cuba but I did not know exactly how.
Q. Nilo, your play, too, has your family stitched into it -- the two sisters are based on your own -- although the story also involves the experiences of a real-life dissident poet under house arrest who received letters from her husband in France.
CRUZ. Yes, the character Maria Celia, the older sister in the play, writes letters to her husband, and the substance of the play is the letters he writes to her from France. I come from the Irene Fornes school of playwriting. She was my professor and my mentor. I pretty much assemble my plays in the same way Irene does because that's what I learned. I think it's the only way to write.
As Irene was talking, I was realizing that there are so many parallels. Of course, her play is very different from mine, but my characters go up to the roof, even though they are under house arrest. It's a way to feel outside, in the world; it's almost like getting away with murder, to write while on the roof. In fact, the first line of the script is: "My dear husband, I am standing on top of this roof wanting to leap into the sky and send you this letter."
Although my sister Marilyn was not under house arrest, she was never able to leave the country. She always wanted to leave. She got married when she was 16. My older sister, Martha, who was 19, got to leave Cuba for the United States via Spain.
Q. How come Martha left earlier than Marilyn did?
CRUZ. She left in 1980 with her husband because she didn't have any children. My sister Marilyn didn't leave because her child was 8 years old. The government always kept people tied to Cuba. Marilyn came to visit us once with a special permit, but she could not come to this country with her husband and child. Since they were left behind, she had to go back. She's 46 now. She has two children and they have both been able to leave Cuba. It was all a scheme: she had to divorce her husband so he could leave Cuba first and she could come back here and remarry him.
In writing "Two Sisters and a Piano," which is set in 1991, I also drew on the life of a cousin who left a marriage behind in Cuba. I remember her in Miami, always anxious to get mail from her husband. She lived through his letters.
Q. How do generational differences affect your identity as Cuban-Americans?
FORNES. I came to the United States with my mother. My father had died. We had no means of support in Cuba. We came here for economic reasons. It might not be ideal, but you can work here and earn a living. In Cuba, it wasn't so. When we came here, there was no sadness whatsoever. My mother loved it. I thought I was in a Hollywood movie.
CRUZ. Irene is more of an immigrant. I'm more of an exile. I came to the United States with my mother and father. My father was in prison for two years because he was very outspoken about politics. I grew up with my mother, two sisters and my grandmother. My mother had to wash clothes to earn a living. We were not wealthy. I lived about 10 years under Castro, and that's all I knew. I would overhear my parents talking about getting their passports together, but I could not talk about it to friends. I remember having to lie in school.
FORNES. The difference between Cubans who leave because they had to, because Cuba under Castro is unbearable, it's such a different story from mine. It must be the most painful thing in the world to leave a place that you love.
CRUZ. People might think that my plays are about politics, but the politics are very simple. "Two Sisters and a Piano" is about two women who, even though they live in very harsh conditions, make the best of their lives. They create a little paradise in their house. Even though they are under house arrest, they bring out the beautiful china and use a tablecloth. It's the integrity of that, the dignity of it, that moves me.
Q. When you write, do you feel a greater responsibility to Cubans or to Cuban-Americans in the United States?
FORNES. I don't feel any responsibility at all. If I write something about Hispanic people, it's because I am attracted to writing about it. It may sound selfish, but in my workshops I teach people to write about whatever comes to their minds. You have to approach the state of dreaming, where you put yourself into contact with things that are saying something. The only way to do it is by allowing part of your mind to dream.
Many Cuban-Americans are too obsessed with Castro. I don't blame them -- they have lost what they knew, their home, their jobs, everything in their whole lives has been taken away. If I lost that, I don't know what I'd do. But sometimes I don't think they are interested in anything else. Their minds have been frozen, almost.
I remember when I went to Havana in 1962. It was the first celebration after Castro took over. There were all these slogans. People would say slogans to identify themselves, or identify something they liked.
New York has a number of Cuban-American playwrights who are emerging, but because of the revolution of 1959 I think in some ways the question of a writer's politics becomes an obstacle to developing a real Cuban-American style: either you are anti-Castro if you live here or pro-Castro if you are there.
In spite of this, some Cuban-American writers here have been able to develop their own personal voice, imagery and style. I would say that Cuban artists tend to display a light surrealism, as opposed to Mexican surrealism, which feels heavier -- in epic rituals, there are always images of skeletons and candles.
Cuban-Americans put on view an almost subconscious imagery that is a mixture of African culture and Catholic spirituality. In the home of any Cuban family, very middle-class and very white, you will see, for instance, on an altar, a picture of the Santa Barbara a Roman Catholic saint . But everybody knows that the picture does not represent an incarnation of the Catholic Virgin Mary. That picture is a disguise for a Yoruban deity, like a double exposure. They see one image, but they are really dancing to another. The two images have blended together. When you think of a Cuban-American style, it has to do with a different coloring, a different tempo.
CRUZ. My identity as an artist is what defines who I am more than anything else. I don't identify with a lot of Cuban-Americans in Miami, or with Cubans in Cuba. I find that Cubans in Miami tend to be too radical. I also feel that some people in Cuba are still very dogmatic. Lately, I've been writing a lot about Cuba. I feel that my own reality was so tinged by the political situation of my country that I have to reflect on it. I'm interested in documenting history, but ultimately my plays are about being an individual. Belonging to a particular group, left or right, entails a political loss. When you embrace your whole being and all that you can be in this world, that's the strongest position.

SECTION: Section 11; Page 9; Column 1; Real Estate Desk

HEADLINE: In the Region / New Jersey;
As Mortgage Rates Rise, Options Help Buyers Cope

BYLINE: By RACHELLE GARBARINE 

 BODY:
NO ONE will argue with the truism that with every upward tick in mortgage interest rates some people are pushed out of the housing market.
Nonetheless, as the Federal Reserve keeps raising the basic rate -- this month's .25 percent increase was the fourth since June -- and home prices continue to rise, the economic equivalent of a one-two punch has so far only "cooled, not killed off" New Jersey's overheated housing market, said Keith T. Gumbinger, vice president of HSH Associates, publishers of mortgage information based in Butler. 
 Over the last 12 months mortgage rates rose by 1.55 percentage points, putting the average rate for a 30-year-fixed mortgage at 8.51 percent last week , compared with 6.96 percent at the same time last year. Still, the New Jersey Association of Realtors in Edison reports that 141,900 existing homes were sold in the state last year, up from 129,900 the year before, and that the average sale price had increased to $205,000 from $197,300.
Timothy Richards, president-elect of the realtors association, said that while there has been a thinning out of buyers "the market is moving forward, just not as frantically as it has in the past year."
Industry professionals say there are several reasons why the upward movement on prices and mortgage rates has not turned the market downward. For one thing, the state's strong economy and job growth have kept consumer confidence high and the demand for homes continues to outstrip the supply. For another, banks are offering different loan products and enhancements that help neutralize the higher rates by providing buyers with monthly payments that are still within their grasp.
Though rates are a lot higher than they were a year ago they are still reasonable when compared with the rate of 16.1 percent reached in 1981, "and even then people were out buying houses," said Brian Carey, an economist with the Mortgage Bankers Association of America in Washington.
Adjustable rate mortgages are gaining popularity among buyers as a way of avoiding the higher rates, at least for awhile. According to the Mortgage Bankers Association, 28 percent of all loans originated in the country in the third quarter of last year were adjustable-rate mortgages, compared with 12 percent for the same time a year earlier.
Such loans come in a variety of forms. One gaining in acceptance is the hybrid adjustable, which carries a fixed rate for one to 10 years, after which the rate adjusts upward or downward up to 2 percentage points a year, based on a pre-established formula. The rate cannot exceed 6 additional percentage points over the life of the loan.
Banks have enhanced such loans with a "lock-in, float-down" option, which essentially allows borrowers to lock in their mortgage rate before it goes even higher and, if rates go down, reduce their rate when they close on their home. As rates drop, buyers are also able to switch to a fixed-rate mortgage prior to closing.
"People are more comfortable with risk and with refinancing, so their mindset is, 'I can get out of the mortgage if rates go down,' " Mr. Gumbinger said.
The lock-in, float-down adjustable-rate mortgage is the kind of loan William and Liza Wallach, of Manhattan, took out through a broker for the five-bedroom center-hall Dutch colonial in Montclair that they bought for over $500,000 earlier this month after a lengthy search on both sides of the Hudson River. The Wallachs, both lawyers in their 30's, are moving with their two young sons from the loft in Manhattan's West Village that they recently sold to gain more space and a front and back yard for their children. The house, for which the couple paid about $100,000 more than the asking price after winning a bidding war, is just a few blocks from where Mrs. Wallach grew up.
"With two children we had to move," said Mrs. Wallach, who is at present not practicing law. "And as obscene as prices seem in New Jersey they are reasonable compared with those in Manhattan."
BUT just as the Wallachs found the house they wanted, rates went up, said their mortgage broker, Melissa Cohn, president of the Manhattan Mortgage Company, which has offices in Hackensack. Though the couple initially was wedded to a 30-year fixed-rate loan with a 60-day lock-in period, Ms. Cohn said they switched to a seven-year adjustable loan with a 90-day lock-in, float-down option.
"This way they were able to lock in a 7.125 percent rate through the time of their closing and the difference in their monthly payments is great," she said. With a fixed-rate loan at 8.375 percent their monthly payment on a $500,000 loan would have been $3,800. With the adjustable it is $3,368, a savings of $432 a month, or $5,100 a year, Ms. Cohn said.
"Consumers are buying for today not tomorrow," she said, noting that the average time people stay in a home is five to seven years.
"Who knows how long we'll be in this house," said Mrs. Wallach. "It seems smaller now -- it's not the Versailles I originally thought it was."
Other mortgage options that help keep the lid on monthly payments, but are used less frequently, are so-called negative amortization loans and the 40-year fixed-rate loan.
Negative amortization offers the borrower an artificially low, or "teaser," rate for 60 days -- though some lenders will allow him or her to pay the low rate for as much as a year, said Ms. Cohn of Manhattan Mortgage. But the deferred payments on the higher interest rate must ultimately be made up, she added. The 40-year loans reduce the payments by stretching them over a longer period, but the total amount buyers ultimately pay is higher.
For instance, Mr. Gumbinger said, for a $100,000 loan the borrower with a rate of 8.51 percent would have interest charges of $252,250 on the 40-year loan, against $177,064 on a 30-year loan. And the difference in the monthly payments is scant, he said, noting that with a 40-year loan it would be $734 a month, while for a 30-year loan it would be $769.
Still, Chet DeStefano, senior vice president at First Union Mortgage Corporation, based in Morristown, a subsidiary of First Union Bank, said his company started offering 40-year mortgages in January in response to the diverse needs of borrowers and the rising rate environment. Such loans, he said, "are not for everyone." But, he added, they work for "first-time buyers, whose potential earnings are ahead of them, and want more purchasing power and a comfortable monthly cash flow and for high-net-worth individuals who want an increased cash flow and need a tax writeoff."
At the same time, banks are also willing to commit to the current rate for up to one year, but here, too, buyers usually will pay for that privilege. Mr. DeStefano said the longer lock-in rates are typically for people who are waiting for their homes to be built. He said for a nine-month lock-in, the maximum for First Union, his company charges 1.5 percent of the mortgage amount. On a $150,000 loan the charge would be $2,250; for six months it would be $1,500, or 1 percent.
And what if mortgage rates continue to go up, as they are expected to do? Mr. Carey of the Mortgage Bankers Association in Washington said his group was predicting that the Federal Reserve would raise rates again next month followed by at least one more increase by the first half of the year.
Mr. Gumbinger said mortgage rates would have to increase one-half to one full percentage point to have an impact on the market. Jacelyn Botti, regional vice president at Weichert Realtors in Morris Plains, one of the state's larger residential brokerages, said rates would need to go to the double digits for an impact to occur.
"The key," Mr. Carey said, "in determining what happens to the housing market going forward is the continued strength of the economy and the wealth creation of the stock market."
For now, Ms. Botti said, the single condition having the most impact on the marketplace is the lack of inventory of existing homes to sell, which is about 10 to 15 percent lower in most areas this year compared with the same time last year. That, in turn, pushed the volume of sales down and home prices up, she said, adding that the situation is compounded by the fact that new home production also is not keeping pace with demand.
For instance, she said, as of the fourth quarter of 1999 the median sales price of existing homes sold in Somerset, Middlesex and Hunterdon Counties was $221,200, up 23.3 percent from $179,400 the same time in 1998. "Home buyers," she said, "are pushing to buy before home prices and mortgage rates go up even higher and because they have waited so long to find a house."
As the spring buying season approaches, Mr. Richards of the realtors association said, "we'll see a resurgence in activity."

SECTION: Section 11; Page 9; Column 1; Real Estate Desk

HEADLINE: In the Region / Connecticut;
Rehabilitating Historically Significant Enclaves

BYLINE: By ELEANOR CHARLES 

 BODY:
WORK will begin next month on restoring 16 deteriorating houses in Frog Hollow, a devastated area of Hartford long plagued by drugs, crime, vandalism and large-scale abandonment. The project is the first to be implemented under a new state law, effective Wednesday, called the Connecticut Historic Homes Rehabilitation Tax Credit Program.
The purpose of the law is to rehabilitate modest but historically significant neighborhoods where single- and multifamily homes have fallen into such serious disrepair that the area cannot attract investment. Most of the houses have been bought at foreclosure. Announcements of the program will be distributed next month by the Connecticut Historical Commission to chief elected officials and preservation organizations in targeted towns. 
 "The law does not provide for an income test for ownership of the restored homes," said Linda Spencer, director of the commission and one of the legislation's crafters, "although the definition of a target area does say 'where residents have incomes below the median.' We expect to start getting requests for applications over the summer."
In its effort to increase the availability of affordable housing and at the same time preserve national- and state-landmarked structures, the legislature identified 29 municipalities where homeowners, nonprofit corporations and developers can apply for tax credits to help rehabilitate inner-city historic districts.
The designated communities are Ansonia, Bridgeport, Bristol, Danbury, Derby, East Hartford, East Haven, Enfield, Griswold, Groton, Hartford, Killingly, Manchester, Meriden, Middletown, New Britain, New Haven, New London, Norwalk, Norwich, Plainfield, Shelton, Stamford, Torrington, Vernon, Waterbury, West Hartford, West Haven and Windham.
Within two years a total of 50 houses on Mortson Street and Putnam Heights in Frog Hollow are expected to be restored as one- to four-family units. Completed single-family houses with about 2,000 square feet will be sold for around $80,000, far below the $120,000 cost of restoration. Two-family homes will cost around $95,000; three- and four-family homes will be priced higher based on restoration costs. Property taxes will remain at pre-rehab levels -- a few hundred dollars a year, for five years.
"Frog Hollow," said Kenneth D. Johnson, senior program officer of the Hartford office of the Local Initiatives Support Corporation, "is a 19th century Victorian neighborhood and the best example of Perfect Sixes" -- three-story, six-unit structures with bowed facades, a type of construction endemic to Hartford.
(The national nonprofit corporation is a financial intermediary that puts together public and private investments to rebuild neighborhoods.)
Special-permit zoning will allow the buildings and lots to be, in legal terms, split down the middle, producing two symmetrical, three-story units that comply with the new law's one- to four-unit rule.
The way the program works, a property owner may apply to the Connecticut Historical Commission for a tax credit equal to 30 percent of the rehabilitation cost. Renovation of each unit in a multifamily dwelling may not exceed $30,000, but total rehabilitation costs of any project must be higher than $25,000.
Cost estimates and plans must be approved by the commission, which then reserves the tax credit from a $3 million annual pool supplied by the state. The credits must be purchased by a corporate entity chosen by the property owner, and cannot be issued until the project is completed.
If individual homeowners, rather than owner-developers or nonprofits, apply for the program, they would designate a bank as the corporate purchaser of the tax credit and take a loan from that bank of, say, $30,000 for the work to be done. The bank would collect $9,000 in tax credits from the commission and reduce the loan by that amount -- to $21,000 at the end of construction.
In Stamford, the sale price of rehabilitated homes in the South End, where some 250 structures could be eligible for the program, would be higher, reflecting the city's vibrant economy. The South End peninsula, stretching from the Metro-North railroad south to Long Island Sound and from the East Branch of the harbor to the West Branch, is listed in its entirety on the National Register of Historic Places.
T HE South End has many commercial, industrial, and residential buildings, but less than 10 percent home ownership," said Timothy Beeble, Stamford's Community Development Director. "Many two- and three-family homes are owned by elderly people who could end up selling to an investor." He said he befelt the tax credit program could assure the city of an opportunity to rehabilitate those properties as affordable housing. Preliminary talks with Pitney-Bowes, which is situated in the South End, and Chase Bank as potential corporate purchasers of tax credits have not yet produced commitments.
Many towns, including Stamford, have housing initiatives of their own that can be combined with the tax credit program. "Under a federal Housing and Urban Development program," said Mr. Beeble, "we can lend $15,000 per unit for rehabilitation. If someone wanted to spend $45,000, they could borrow $15,000 from us on a zero-interest deferred loan to be repaid when the house is sold, get $13,500 as the 30 percent tax credit then borrow $16,500 from a bank."
A tax credit of $1.5 million for the first 16-house phase of construction in Frog Hollow has been assigned to the Travelers. The insurance company has committed a total of $7.4 million to the $10 million overall cost of the project, including 20 new single- and two-family houses to be built outside the tax credit program.
Citibank's Hartford office will provide a $1.5 million construction loan, to be paid off by the Travelers, which can then apply the tax credit to its state corporate tax. Tax credits cannot be applied to personal income taxes. The credits offer only a dollar-for-dollar reduction.
Once restored, multifamily buildings must have one unit occupied by the owner. Private or nonprofit developers must sell their restored buildings to such an owner-occupier. Investors in the program believe that the restored neighborhoods will rapidly appreciate in value.
Martin Legault, president and chief executive of the Corporation for Independent Living, said owners would have to sign a deed restriction. "If they sell the house in the first five years they must repay the subsidy in full -- possibly $20,000 to $40,000," he said. "Repayment in the sixth year would be 50 percent of the subsidy. Nothing would be due after that."
Because the market value of the completed buildings is always less than the cost of rehabilitation, other contributors are filling the financial gap with amounts still in negotiation. Among them are the nonprofit Connecticut Housing Investment Fund, the Connecticut Housing Finance Authority, Fleet Bank Community Preservation Loan Fund, and Hartford's Department of Economic and Community Development. Developers include the Corporation for Independent Living, Broad Park Development Corporation, Greater Hartford Realty Management Company, and the Milano Corporation.
W E don't make money on this," said Andrew Bassette, vice president of corporate real estate for the Travelers. "We are using our funds to make possible the buyout of the construction loan, but there is a six-month to a year window in which we lose the ability to use that money and we can't deduct the loss from taxes."
Citibank and the Travelers, members of Citigroup, the giant financial services group based in Manhattan, look for ways to improve the quality of life in cities where they are established.
"The quality of life in Hartford," said Mr. Bassette, "is a major impediment to attracting young people right out of college. They are more interested in Boston and New York. But once they get here they find the quality is pretty darned good -- with some of the richest cultural institutions in the country" -- including the Wadsworth Atheneum, America's oldest continuously operating art museum; the Hartford Stage Company, the Hartford Symphony Orchestra, the Hartford Ballet, the ornate State Capitol, Bushnell Park and Trinity College.
Edward Musante, director of Norwalk's Redevelopment Agency, said that his city would look into the program, possibly dovetailing it with an ongoing Housing Rehabilitation Low Interest Loan Program. A state and nationally designated Historic District in South Norwalk extends from Washington Street south to Hanford Place, and from Water Street west to South Main Street. "Some 50 to 75 houses could be eligible," he said.
Many houses along Danbury's historic district on Main Street date back to the 18th century.
"They are predominantly three- and four-family units," said Dennis Elpern, the city's planning director. "They are occupied and in need of major renovation. The problem has been that it's not financially rewarding to the owners to make those kinds of repairs. This sounds like a very welcome program."

SECTION: Section 11; Page 1; Column 2; Real Estate Desk

HEADLINE: Taking Sides in the 6 Percent Evolution

BYLINE: By DENNIS HEVESI 

 BODY:
THE deed, dated July 20, 1668, conveys a 15,000-acre swath of northern New Jersey, now including the Borough of Rutherford, from Tantaqua, a chief of the Lenni Lenape Indian Nation, to an Englishman, William Sandford, "for the following consideration:"
"One hundred and seventy fathoms of black wampum, 200 fathoms of white wampum, 19 watch coats, 17 guns, 60 double hands of powder, 10 pair of breeches, 60 knives, 67 bars of lead, 1 anker of brandy, 3 half vats of beer" and various other sundries. 
 The commission on the deal, said Peter Van Winkle, a Rutherford real estate broker -- tongue slightly inserted in cheek -- "was probably 6 percent."
"It may have been, because 6 percent has long been the customary commission," said Mr. Van Winkle, who offers farsighted historical perspective on such matters. It was in 1692 that his great-great-great-great-great-great-great-great-great grandfather, Waling Jacob Van Winkle, founded the Rutherford real estate firm that still bears the family name.
So just how far back does the traditional 6 percent brokers' commission on residential deals go? "Certainly as far as I know," said Mr. Van Winkle, "and as far as what my father told me, and his father before that."
So far back that the 6 percent figure seems etched in stone -- at least in the minds of many, and maybe most, real estate customers.
But it's not.
And in recent years, some brokers have been chipping away at that set-in-stone perception -- advertising their willingness to negotiate commissions or even to offer flat-rate fees -- despite the fierce and often carefully reasoned resistance of their peers. One broker in Queens said his firm takes a "Home Depot, Priceline approach, where you can name your own price," much to the chagrin of his competitors. It is a phenomenon impelled, particularly over the last five years, by a housing market in which listings are precious, prices are dear and homeowners can be demanding -- a classic seller's market.
"The most dreaded words in our industry are, 'Will you take less?' " said Barbara Corcoran, chairwoman of the Manhattan brokerage firm that bears her name. "The commission we charge as a firm is 6 percent, but we're constantly questioned." Ten days ago, the Corcoran Group held an in-house seminar for its sales agents to discuss strategies for "Holding On to That 6 Percent Commission."
Clark Halstead, founder of the Halstead Property Company, is adamant about holding the 6-percent line. "If you need brain surgery," he asked, "would you negotiate a lower price with your surgeon? You would probably decide you need the best expertise and be willing to pay." Lower commissions, he insisted, mean diminished interest by potential co-brokers from other firms, leading to decreased exposure to the market and, consequently, less than top dollar.
To be sure, brokers' commissions have been entirely negotiable since 1950 when, in a case called United States v. National Association of Real Estate Boards (predecessor to the current National Association of Realtors), the Supreme Court ruled that fixed commission schedules constituted restraint of trade under the Sherman Antitrust Act.
"Brokers can never discuss the commission among themselves," said Barbara Frechter, president of Glenjay Realty in Forest Hills and past president of the Long Island Board of Realtors (which includes Queens). "I can only discuss it with my sales agents, not with another brokerage" -- which might end up splitting the commission, assuming one firm takes the listing from the seller and the other finds the buyer.
"When people teach sales classes," said Ms. Frechter, "they say, 'X commission,' without the number, because it's such a stringent law. And in the 80's, when you joined the real estate board, they showed you a movie and the jail door slams shut to teach you that you are never to discuss the commission with another broker."
Since that 1950 Supreme Court ruling, and subsequent legal opinions that bolstered the high court's original finding, there have been varying attempts at discounting commissions -- both by brokers and sellers; most legal, some questionable; some profitable, others not.
"In the old days," Ms. Frechter said, "a seller might tell you: 'This is what I want for the house; anything you get above that is yours.' It's called a net commission. But, by law, you can't take a listing that way. You have to tell the person what the commission is up front."
These days, Eileen Scott is a sales agent for Mr. Van Winkle. But back in the late 80's, Ms. Scott said, "my husband and I opened a Help-U-Sell franchise."
"It was a fixed-rate commission: $3,450 for a house priced up to $150,000," she said. "After that it switched to $4,450. That was it."
For that set fee, Ms. Scott would help her clients sell their homes. "They would do their own advertising, and also the showings," she said. "We would put a sign up: 'For Sale By Owners, Assisted by Help-U-Sell.' "
It didn't work.
"We couldn't survive because of the expenses of running an office and charging such low commissions," Ms. Scott said. And the timing for the Help-U-Sell franchise company was unfortunate -- a depressed housing market. "The promises were that we would capture at least 10 percent of the market, whereas, after the first year, it was more like 4 percent," Ms. Scott said. "By the third year, after we lost all of our money, we closed the doors."
TWO years ago, Help-U-Sell, which had about 100 franchises, was purchased by the American Pacific Financial Corporation, and now has 158 franchises nationwide -- seven in the New York area. "It was ahead of its time in the 80's," said Rick O'Neil, Help-U-Sell's president.
These days, Ms. Scott and Mr. Van Winkle hold a decided preference for the standard fee. "Most of our residential commissions are 6 percent, but on occasion we do deviate," Mr. Van Winkle said.
"Peter and I agree that if you need to take 5 percent, you take 5 percent," Ms. Scott said. "But in the long run, when you consider the expenses, we'd rather get our 6 percent."
Since 1995, on the other hand, newspaper advertisements placed by the Fleet Street Properties Company in Rego Park, Queens, have asked, "Why Pay More Than 4?"
And the logic of the question seemed unassailable to Nicholas Cerrito Jr. Mr. Cerrito, 40, an air freight dispatcher, has lived with his wife, Marie, and two children in Babylon, on Suffolk County's South Shore, for 10 years.
"And I bought a rental property in North Babylon in 1992," Mr. Cerrito said. Last year, the Cerritos put that rental home on the market.
"A friend told me about Fleet Street, that they only charge 4 percent," Mr. Cerrito said. "I had it on the market for $137,000; 30 days later, I closed for $135,000." Mr. Cerrito was pleased with the 4 percent, rather than 6 percent, commission. The difference, in cash to him, was $2,700.
Thomas Henry is president of Fleet Street Properties. "We've been in business here for 54 years," Mr. Henry said. "For the past five years, we're doing this special deal: 4 percent. We put it on the multiple listing service under LIBOR" -- the Long Island Board of Realtors, which lists 1,550 brokerage firms -- "and we split 50-50 with the selling broker."
For sellers who would like to negotiate with buyers themselves, Mr. Henry said, "We have a program where we will list a property with the M.L.S." -- for a flat fee of $199.95 -- "and they can name the commission they wish to pay."
Mr. Henry said he recommends that the seller choose a 3 percent commission, "because this is what the selling broker would get on a 6 percent split, and we still have the right to get the 3 percent, if we sell it ourselves."
Of his company's "Home Depot, Priceline approach," he said, "We're doing a good business."
"At first, five years ago," he said, "they threatened us with lawsuits, the other brokers. We got calls saying, 'You're killing the business. We're not going to sell your listing.'
"We're not killing anything," Mr. Henry insisted. "Now they've capitulated and they can't get enough of our listings. They seem to accept that splitting 4 percent is better than splitting nothing."
Even splitting 3 percent -- on a property selling for $1 million or more -- can be profitable, said John P. Noonan, past president of the Brooklyn Board of Realtors and president of the Park Slope brokerage firm that bears his name.
"We've had this huge increase in real estate values and there's certainly a lot of room to negotiate and still get a handsome commission," Mr. Noonan said, citing several factors bearing on his decision to accept a commission lower than 6 percent. His primary consideration would be if the homeowner gave Mr. Noonan's brokerage firm the exclusive right to sell the home, without the participation of a broker representing the buyer.
"My personal feeling is that if a seller gives an exclusive to a broker, it's certainly worthy of a reduced commission payment," he said. "You're not competing with other brokers and it's a more comfortable way of working." The listing broker, in that instance, would keep the entire commission.
BESIDES being granted an exclusive, Mr. Noonan said he would consider a reduced commission if he had several "good prospects" lined up to buy the property promptly, and if the house was appropriately priced in the current market. "And, certainly, if I'm involved in a bidding war between clients from different brokers, I would cut a commission to make the deal work," he said.
Asked if he would negotiate commissions on a home priced for less than $1 million, Mr. Henry said: "We don't see $100,000 houses any more; if we see anything below $300,000, it's a rarity. So any of these could be negotiable, absolutely."
Numerous wrinkles exist in the seller-broker relationship.
Essentially, there are two opportunities during the marketing process for a seller to seek a reduced commission: at the time the listing agreement is being written and, again, when a deal with a buyer is about to be reached.
At listing time, the broker's resistance to a reduction may be stiffer, probably prompting the argument that a lower commission will dampen the interest of all the other brokers who belong to the multiple listing service.
If, however, at the time the deal is about to be struck, the broker says to the seller something like, "Your asking price was $250,000, we have an offer for $225,000," the seller could say, "Cut your commission a bit and we have a deal."
"It happens occasionally," said Michael Finder, a real estate lawyer in Manhattan. "A seller might try to persuade a broker to reduce the commission to conclude the deal."
A seller has no obligation to accept anything less than the listed price. Yet, as Mr. Finder pointed out, "once the broker does produce a purchaser willing and able to pay that asking price, the seller runs the risk of litigation with the broker if the seller refuses to proceed with the deal."
Some listing agreements tie the commission fee to a specific asking price, but most commissions are tied to a certain percentage of the final purchase price. "Almost always," said John Hall, former chairman of the real property section of the New York State Bar Association, "the commission is computed as a percentage of the selling price, rather than a percentage of the listing price." And in some cases, a flat commission amount is agreed upon, regardless of the selling price, Mr. Hall said.
"I have a theory on all this bargaining," said Ms. Corcoran of the Corcoran Group: "that sellers believe that apartments today sell themselves -- and they're right."
BUT in the current torrid market, Ms. Corcoran pointed out, "where brokers truly earn their money is in 10 times the number of showings and navigating the bidding wars through the feeding frenzy."
R. Kenyatta Punter, president of the Greater Harlem Real Estate Board, and of the brokerage firm that bears his name, said that commissions may also rise. "It can be indicative of a property that is unusually difficult to unload," he said.
"For example, rooming houses are difficult to sell because financing is not readily available for that type of building," Mr. Punter said, "although they are getting scooped up for family use in my community."
In a case like that, a broker might ask for 7 percent, Mr. Punter said, "versus a building on a very desirable block, say a brownstone on Striver's Row or Hamilton Terrace. A broker there may be willing to take 5 percent because it tends to move quickly, because of demand in that marquee location."
Mr. Halstead, however, believes that bargaining is bad business -- for both the broker and the seller.
"There are two tools a seller needs to be certain he's getting the best price," Mr. Halstead said. "One is a battle-hardened, energetic broker with a full quiver of marketing arrows. The other is a full competitive commission, because what the seller wants is all the brokers in town motivated to show his property."
His company, Mr. Halstead said, has 250 agents. Once the listing is shared with members of the Real Estate Board of New York, he said: "That brings several thousand agents and their customers in. And the point is that what the seller wants is as many interested parties as soon as possible in order to insure the best price."
Mr. Halstead did the math to illustrate how reduced commissions squeeze brokers.
If, for example, a Manhattan apartment sells for $1 million, the standard commission would be $60,000 with, most likely, a 50-50 split between the listing brokerage firm and the firm that brought in the buyer.
"Generally, for the listing firm," Mr. Halstead said, "of the $30,000 received by them, 50 percent goes to the sales agent, leaving $15,000 to address marketing expenses and overhead. For a $1 million property, marketing expenses could easily be, say, $5,000 to $7,000."
Those expenses would include, in Mr. Halstead's evaluation, "the most expensive agate advertising in the world -- that's classified advertising -- offered by The New York Times at $18 a line," before quantity discounts.
IN addition, he said, "We have to take photographs, put the property on our Web sites; do a brochure or a fancy oversized postcard. We have to hold open houses, if the board allows. And we do mailings to people in the building and in the neighborhood."
For a multimillion-dollar property, display advertising might be required in glossy publications like Quest or Avenue, or in The Times's Sunday magazine, he said, "and we're talking there, now, rates of $8,000 for a quarter page."
"These are the economic realities of operating a real estate brokerage," Mr. Halstead said.
Just such realities prompted an audacious experiment two years ago by Long & Foster, the largest brokerage firm in Washington -- a standardized commission of 7 percent.
"Let me give you the Washington plea," said Wes Foster Jr., the company's president. "In the last 15 years, the amount of money the company has left after we've paid our sales agent -- what we call the company dollar; it's what we use to pay our overhead and hopefully have something left over -- has dropped from around 45 cents of the whole dollar to 29 cents."
"This is a national problem," Mr. Foster said.
The experiment was short-lived.
"A total, ugly failure," Mr. Foster said. "We wish we'd never done it. It cost us market share, and we backed away quickly, in a couple or three months. But it stuck with some of the public and made them angry with us."
Long & Foster has since expanded into titles, mortgages and insurance, "so that we can give the customer a one-stop shop," Mr. Foster said, "and us a chance to make a little more profit."
These days, he said, the struggle is on to maintain the 6 percent commission, "which we really need to stay in business."
"Right now we're doing O.K. because it's a good market," Mr. Foster said. "But I can tell you this: when another slow market comes, you're going to see real estate companies going out of business left and right, because the margin is too slim. It's tough."

HEADLINE: 'Hidden' Workers Nurse Hope of Amnesty

BYLINE: By JOHN RATHER 

 BODY:
AS members of an underground in plain sight, Hispanic workers in the United States illegally lead furtive lives on Long Island, dodging authorities even as they are welcomed with open arms by some employers.
Fearing an official knock at the door of their often illegal and crowded rental houses or a raid by immigration officers at work, the laborers trade social invisibility and official nonexistence for jobs as landscapers, bus boys, field workers and maids. Their wages are low, but still far more than they could earn at home. 
 For the thousands of undocumented Latinos in Nassau and Suffolk caught between these economic imperatives and Americans' ambivalence, news that the United States labor movement now favors amnesty for illegal immigrants has raised hope for better days.
"They feel so happy," said Carlos Canales, an organizer at the Workplace Project in Hempstead, which aids Latinos. "They think that finally the sun is rising for them."
On Horse Block Road in Farmingville, where several hundred Latinos arrive with the rising sun to stand near two 7-Eleven stores and in sidewalk groups in hopes of job offers from passing tradesmen, word of "amnistia" spread rapidly. But to many it had a far-off ring, and will not put money in their empty pockets.
"I have nothing," said a man in his 30's in a knit cap and a U.C.L.A. sweatshirt, patting his pants pockets, as he waited for work on Tuesday. "I will do anything. I am here for the money."
From Mexico City -- a place, he said, with no jobs and too much crime -- he had come to Long Island because he heard there was money to be made, as much as $8 or $9 an hour for the lucky ones. Amnesty and a green card, he said in halting English, would make job-hunting much easier.
Backing for an amnesty, which reverses years of opposition to immigrant workers because it was felt that their presence depressed wages, emerged at a meeting of the A.F.L.-C.I.O. this month in New Orleans.
A resolution by the executive council said blanket amnesty would recognize a growing segment of the labor force vulnerable to exploitation. It also called for an end to most sanctions against employers who hire illegal immigrants.
With some business organizations backing similar action to help industries with labor shortages, officials and organizers at immigrant groups on Long Island said an amnesty that went beyond a limited decree in 1986 was now possible.
"What's interesting is that even before the official announcement from New Orleans, there had been a buzz in the immigrant community about this," said Patrick Young, the program director of the Central American Refugee Center in Hempstead, which is known as Carecen. "Most of the immigrants on Long Island are here legally, but they may have relatives and friends here who are undocumented. And they realize that the current system lowers the working conditions for both the documented and the undocumented."
Mr. Young estimated that there were 30,000 to 40,000 undocumented Latinos living on the Island, but he said the undocumented population did not appear to be growing.
A Census Bureau estimate on July 1, 1998, put the Hispanic population at 98,000 in Nassau and 116,000 in Suffolk. The figures are largely those for American citizens and legal immigrants, but may also include an unknown number of illegal aliens. There are also groups of illegal immigrants from Ireland and Poland, but their numbers are far smaller.
According to the Census Bureau, Puerto Ricans, who are American citizens, account for half the Latinos. The largest immigrant groups come from El Salvador, Colombia, the Dominican Republic and Mexico.
Cecilia Moran, the director of the Centro Salvadorno in Hempstead, said an amnesty would recognize the importance of the undocumented work force and allow immigrants who had been virtually in hiding for years to live openly.
"It is wonderful because they will pass from anonymous persons to people who can count in this society," she said. "These are people who are not only living here and getting salaries here, they are giving something."
Matthew T. Crosson, the president of the Long Island Association, the major business group, was more restrained in his reaction to the news from New Orleans.
"It is a proposal that should be studied with an open mind, but not necessarily one that should be adopted," he said. "The cost implications to the federal, state and local governments need to be assessed," including items like Medicaid.
Mr. Crosson said that while labor shortages were "a severe problem" on the Island, it was not clear "to what extent undocumented aliens help resolve that problem."
"They are clearly a part of the labor force," he said. "How significant is difficult to assess."
A major cconcern is that the prospect of amnesty will cause a surge in illegal immigrants eager to gain footholds in the United States before the amnesty takes effect.
But support groups said many of the beneficiaries would be undocumented workers who had lived here for a long time. "It's a fiction to say they are not here," Mr. Young said. "Why not recognize that they are here and create a way for people with clean records to be here legally?"
The immigrants' stories are often heart-rending. A woman who gave her name as Rosa said she had come from Ecuador in 1988 on a tourist visa, and remained when it expired. Divorced and now 47 years old, she earns $140 a week off the books as a housekeeper and lives with a friend in a Hempstead apartment.
A son, 20, also lives on the Island and works when he can find employment, but another son and the rest of her family remain in Ecuador. Her father and two brothers have died while she has been away, and this month her mother had a stroke. She paid for the funerals, and is paying now for medicine and treatment for her mother.
Going home is not an option.
"No, it is impossible," she said through an interpreter. "How could I? I am the only one who can make money, and I am sending money for medicine and treatment."
Amnesty, she said, would be "fabulous."
"It would give me an opportunity to get a better job and pay taxes," she said. "It gives me hope that one day it will be better and I will see my mother while she is still alive." She would also like to become a citizen.
Some employers facing labor shortages favor amnesty. A commercial landscaper who spoke on condition his name not be used said amnesty would end unfair competition from small businesses that hire undocumented workers for low pay.
"It's hard to tell who is legal and who isn't, and some of the people who are hiring don't ask any questions," he said. "That's why these small guys are squeezing us out of business." With amnesty, undocumented workers "would be getting benefits and they would also be paying taxes," he said.
Angelo Auricchio, the president of the 400-member Long Island chapter of the New York State Restaurant Association, said amnesty could be a boost for restaurant owners, who faced mounting difficulty finding workers.
"We have to rejuvenate, and since there has been such an influx" of undocumented workers, he said, "I would think the way to do that would be to legalize them."
Mr. Auricchio said he could not say whether many restaurants employed illegal aliens. But he said the 1986 amnesty brought greater numbers of legal immigrants into the business and reduced labor shortages then.
Without legal status, undocumented workers have little choice but to accept rock-bottom wages without complaint, said the Rev. William Brisotti, administrator of Our Lady of the Miraculous Medal Roman Catholic Church in Wyandanch.
"A lot of communities are very slow to admit they have this population in their area," he said. "But they are there, they are excellent workers, many businesses depend on them, and generally speaking they are being abused."
Father Brisotti said it was not unusual for laborers to be cheated. "They do the work and the guy only pays them half of what he said he would, and they are afraid to do anything about it because he might call immigration and report them," he said. "These are the kind of things that would end if there were amnesty."
Mr. Young said current rules hurt both legal and illegal immigrants.
"Some employers only want to hire the undocumented," he said. "That disadvantages workers who have documents. And the employer can hold over the heads of the undocumented workers the threat of being handed over" to the Immigration and Naturalization Service.
"With the documented workers," he added, "the threat is that if they don't want to work for low wages, the undocumented will."
Among the men outside the 7-Eleven on North Ocean Avenue and Horse Block Road on Tuesday morning, the quest for work of any kind seemed desperate. Dozens advanced rapidly on a person they took for a potential employer, quickly surrounding him. "You need someone? You have work?" they asked.
The store manager came out to usher the men out of the parking lot and behind a fence at the edge of his property. Speaking in English, he also warned them not to come off the curb and stand in the busy street.
"There are 50 of you now, and this summer there will be 200," he said. "Somebody will get hurt."
He was trying to be helpful, the store manager said (though he would not give his name), but if the men would not stay out of the way, he added, he would have to call the police.
The men stared at him blankly. One, an intense man in his 20's in an Army fatigue jacket, related to the others in Spanish the warning about "la policia."
The manager told the men, "I have to feed my family, just like you have to feed your family." A young man behind the fence stared down at the manager's new brown leather shoes.
An old pickup truck paused at the parking lot entrance.
"Don't stop there! Don't stop there!" the manager yelled. The driver pretended not to understand. Two men came from behind the fence and climbed in beside him. After brief discussion they drove off.
Over the next hour, no other trucks stopped.

SECTION: Section 14CN; Page 1; Column 1; Connecticut Weekly Desk 

LENGTH: 972 words

HEADLINE: Looking for 'Connecticut' in the Credits

BYLINE: By FRANCES CHAMBERLAIN 

 BODY:
HOW many times do you go to the movies and see places that look like the wood lot down the road or the center of a neighboring town? The location may not show up in the credits, but there's a good chance you were right, because Connecticut is still a popular spot to shoot movies.
Katherine Ray, who was director of the Connecticut Film Office until this month, had spent her days with location scouts, helping them find the perfect setting within the state for a film shoot. "A Little Bit of Lipstick" will be under way soon in New Milford and the Mystic area. A previous film, "Coventry," was shot in Coventry. A movie called "Snow Days" was shot in Westport and Greenwich; "The Thomas Crown Affair" featured water stunts in Norwalk Harbor; and a Hallmark Hall of Fame production, "Grace and Glory," was shot in Watertown and Waterbury. 
 The film office is being run by the Connecticut Economic Resource Center and is now headed by Guy Ortoleva, who said a 1995 study found that film is a $300 million industry in the state. Under the umbrella of the state organization, smaller film commissions have been formed in individual towns, all with the same goal -- bringing film production into the community to boost not only the arts but also the local economy.
The Southeastern Connecticut Film Office in New London, formed five years ago, is in an area with a rich history of film-making. After "Ah, Wilderness," done in 1935, there have been many others, including "The World According to Garp," "Mystic Pizza," "The Hunt for Red October, "Amistad," and "All Shook Up."
Connecticut is generally willing to cooperate with film makers, Ms. Ray said. "We recently staged a series of three bank robberies with chase scenes and got a road to do the chase scenes," she said.
The scenes were filmed in New Milford.
"It was a Japanese film crew, looking for vehicles," said Frank Weller, chairman of the New Milford Film Commission. "Someone told me they were going to film in New Jersey, and I told them I thought we had a better selection of locations. It was a weekly TV show about a female character who robbed 36 banks in Canada by dressing as a man and then stuffing her disguise in a shopping bag immediately afterwards. The segment filmed in New Milford involved a stunt driver, dressed in drag, crashing a car on Ridge Road after robbing three local banks."
The New Milford Film Commission was formed about 18 months ago, and its five members are appointed by the mayor. Saun Ellis, commissioner of the New Milford Film Commission, said its purpose is to encourage production of films and sponsor events. "People in the industry are anxious to locate outside of New York City and we'd love to see the industry move up here."
Other municipalities also have film offices. The Hartford Tourism Office has a person who works with the film industry. Danbury has a film commissioner, and the Stamford Economic Development Office is creating a film commission.
The Connecticut Film Office coordinates the efforts of local film commissions, including Hartford, the Southeastern Connecticut Film Office, Danbury and New Milford.
"If MGM calls and says they are looking for something, I can call each film commission and ask if they've got it," Ms. Ray said. "I keep a data base of everyone who works in the state."
For the New Milford Film Commission, the big coup was to lure Campbell Scott (grandson of George C. Scott) to shoot part of his new film "Final" in two locations in New Milford. The film stars Denis Leary, who lives inf Washington, Conn., and Hope Davis.
Other Connecticut projects in the works include one being produced by Killer Films that was shot at Fairfield Hills Hospital, Danbury and Waterbury. A film called "Happy Accidents," shot in Branford and Guilford, was shown at Sundance.
"Blue Eyes, Blue" with Eric Clapton was filmed in Hadlyme, and "Judging Amy," a CBS series, will be shot in Hartford.
Not all of the films are coming from big companies. Paul Lombardo, 36, runs Speed Twin Films in Glastonbury. He wrote, produced and directed "Love . . . At Your Disposal," a romantic comedy that Mr. Lombardo shot all around Connecticut, including in Rocky Hill, New Britain and Glastonbury. He also used local actors and crew members.
Mr. Lombardo, who also acts, said his full-time job is working at his family's waste disposal company, Trash-Away Inc., in New Britain.
"You have to do something to pay the bills. I'm an aspiring trash man forced to make films," he said, laughing.
Another important aspect of film production in the state is the work of people like Ron Howard of Glastonbury, who owns Lion Heart Films. As a free-lance director for programs like "60 Minutes" and "20/20," he became interested in producing documentaries. "I came here 10 years ago for "Mystic Pizza" he said. "I liked New England so much. It isn't a center for film production, but it's starting to pick up and there are a lot of good people here. The state doesn't understand the importance of the industry like some other states do."
Mr. Howard's work in Connecticut has focused on documentaries. A piece on the abolitionists of the state is scheduled to air on C.P.T.V. this spring.
Film production in Connecticut needs to be encouraged, Ms. Ray added, but the challenge is luring the industry away from Canada, where filmmaking is subsidized, the value of the dollar is greater, and the union structures are different.
"The only way to combat what's going on in Canada is to work on economic growth, get financial institutions to invest in films, get Connecticut writers to be paid for writing scripts," she said. "If we have a vision like that, we can turn things around. If we have a starry-eyed vision of Hollywood, it won't happen here. We all have to work together with the film offices."
