HEADLINE: POSTINGS: Art Commission Awards;
8 Are Honored For Excellence In Design

 BODY:
What is the architectural firm du jour -- lionized for the sphere-in-a-jewel-box Rose Center at the American Museum of Natural History -- doing at a sewage plant on the banks of Newtown Creek?
Being honored by the New York City Art Commission for excellence in civic design. 
 The Art Commission weighs the aesthetic appropriateness of architecture and works of art on city-owned property. Every year, it cites outstanding design in municipal projects, in the hope of encouraging city agencies to do even more. At City Hall tomorrow, the panel will give awards to eight projects, most of them not yet under construction.
 Among the winners are the disinfection and support buildings at the Newtown Creek Water Pollution Control Plant in the Greenpoint section of Brooklyn, designed by the Polshek Partnership for the Environmental Protection Department. The Polshek office also designed the Rose Center.
"Each building is an elegant composition that uses color and materials in a very bold way," said Jean Parker Phifer, the president of the 11-member Art Commission, who is an architect herself. "The level of effort is similar to an institutional building and that's so gratifying."
Joel A. Miele Sr., the environmental protection commissioner, said the focus on aesthetics and the choice of a renowned architect for a waste-water plant reflected the agency's goal of being a good neighbor. "The projects we build tend to clash with the community if care isn't taken," he said. The $237 million project is to be completed in 2004.
Other award recipients are:
* Harlem River Boathouse, Swindler Cove, Manhattan: Robert A. M. Stern Architects, for the New York Restoration Project.
* Hunts Point Teen Center, 765 Manida Street, the Bronx: Hanrahan & Meyers, in association with Castro-Blanco Piscioneri & Associates (recently acquired by Arquitectonica).
* Streetscape plan for lower Manhattan: Cooper, Robertson & Partners; Quennell Rothschild & Partners; Vollmer Associates and Pentagram Design, for the Alliance for Downtown New York.
* Improvements at the Alley Pond Park, Queens, interchange of the Long Island Expressway and Cross Island Parkway: Vollmer Associates.
* Restoration of murals, including the dome, at the New York County Courthouse, 60 Centre Street: EverGreene Painting Studios.
* Conservation of a Romare Bearden mural at the Bellevue Hospital Center: Luca Bonetti.
* New Chinese Scholar's Garden at the Staten Island Botanical Garden: Demetri Sarantitis and the Landscape Architecture Company of China.

HEADLINE: New Landlord, Old Tenants, Hard Questions

BYLINE: By ALAN S. OSER 

 BODY:
"IT was a beautiful offer," said Marie Johnson, a former homeowner from Queens and a tenant since 1991 at the Martha Washington Hotel. "But some of the women did not believe it was possible. They thought he was tempting us and would not live up to his promises."
The developer in question, Kevin P. Maloney, is now in the process of trying to live up to those promises, made to 83 longterm tenants at the Martha Washington. These tenants accepted an offer under which their rooms will be upgraded and re-equipped and they will be able to stay as long as they wish at no increase in rent. In return, they backed the developer's application for a certificate of no harassment to permit the conversion of the single-room-occupancy Martha Washington into a modern tourist hotel. 
 The building is near the middle of the block between Madison Avenue and Park Avenue South, with entrances on 29th and 30th Streets. Mr. Maloney and his partner, Ziel Feldman, principals in the Manhattan-based Property Markets Group, paid $18 million last year to buy it from Bernard Sillins, a longterm owner-operator of single-room-occupancy hotels. Three-quarters of its 423 rooms, most of which have no bathrooms, were vacant at the time. The conversion is creating the Hotel ThirtyThirty, with 370 modern rooms and suites. Its first three floors are scheduled to open in April.
While 83 of the tenants supported the conversion plan, 37 did not. The 29-member Martha Washington Tenants Association, represented by MFY Legal Services, which provides free legal services to tenants under a city contract, charged harassment by the owner. It demanded as a remedy that the owner agree to keep 150 rooms permanently at a rent level affordable to low- to moderate-income singles. But Property Markets Group received the certificate despite the charge, after which it declined to give the tenants that MFY Legal Services represented the same deal it gave the signing tenants, noting that they had rejected it over a period of eight months. There are 13 such tenants, according to Property Markets Group.
Now MFY Legal Services is in court, claiming that the certificate of no harassment is invalid. The certificate is a prerequisite to making significant room alterations in an S.R.O. hotel. Without it, the developer cannot get a permit from the Department of Buildings to add bathrooms to any rooms, whether vacant or occupied.
The owner's refusal to make anything other than a "punitive" offer once it had its certificate of no harassment left the tenants association no alternative to filing the lawsuit, said Janet Ray Kalson, the MFY Legal Services attorney. In the suit the tenants association maintains that the housing agency erred in failing to hold a public hearing on the harassment charges. The agency replies that it found no reasonable cause to believe that harassment occurred, and therefore no hearing was required. Justice Charles J. Tejada is hearing the case. An unfavorable decision could block the scheduled hotel opening in April.
One question that the Martha Washington situation raises is whether it is in the interest of single-room-occupancy tenants who think they have been harassed in a conversion to insist on a remedy that is unrelated to their own welfare as tenants. If the charge fails, the tenants may be left with nothing but the status quo. And it is unlikely that they care what happens to future rents in their rooms once they move out.
Another question is whether it is sound to attempt to "preserve" obsolete housing in buildings that have been given substantial economic value by the upsurge in Manhattan tourism.
At the Martha Washington, Property Markets Group estimates the total cost of its conversion effort at $49 million. Allocated across the 370 suites and rooms the company is creating, the acquisition alone amounted to an investment of $48,000 per unit, or "key," in the language of the hotel business. The total costs, including construction and the fees and services that constitute "soft" costs, come to $130,000 a unit.
For the public sector to match the acquisition price, it would have to have made or backed a competitive offer from a nonprofit group of at least $42,550 a room ($18 million for the 423 existing rooms). Nonprofits rarely pay more than $10,000 to $15,000 a room, and usually take much longer than a private buyer to close on a purchase.
It is not that the city has been inactive in helping to create or maintain single-room-occupancy housing units. Over the last 12 years a total of 5,548 S.R.O. units in 59 projects have been completed with city or state funding or both, figures from the city's Department of Housing Preservation and Development show. Twenty additional projects with 1,200 housing units are in construction or renovation, the agency says. Under the city loan program, 60 percent of new tenants must come from the city's shelters; the rest are neighborhood residents.
The S.R.O. housing unit is typically a tiny one-room apartment with no private bathroom or kitchen, although new publicly assisted units often have both bathrooms and kitchen equipment. Using the broadest definition of an S.R.O. unit, a study done for the city in 1993 found that there were 46,700 such units citywide. Seven percent of them were found to be in dilapidated buildings.
"When the real estate market is high it's difficult to preserve this resource," said James A. Krauskopf, president of the Corporation for Supportive Housing, a national organization that supports service-enriched housing for homeless and disabled people. In his view, consideration should be given to placing a moratorium on conversions of S.R.O. buildings to standard modern hotel rooms "unless the cap can be raised on the total development cost allowed per unit under the S.R.O. loan program."
THE city's cap currently stands at $95,000 per developable housing unit, a figure that includes both acquisition and rehabilitation costs. After acquisitions, the buildings generally require a substantial level of rehabilitation to rooms, building systems and common areas. For buildings that appeal to the private market, the nonprofits usually have no way to compete, mainly because of cost. But it also takes about nine months before city underwriting is complete, rent-assistance subsidies are committed, a loan is authorized and a sale can be consummated. Sellers prefer buyers who can make rapid commitments.
"I don't think we should be trying to compete in the private market for high-cost buildings," said Claire Haage, president of Housing & Services, which manages 700 units of supportive housing and acts as developer for nonprofit housing organizations. "But we should be trying to get hold of more buildings and vacant land that the city owns and no longer has use for. And we should also be building new housing."
While insisting that the law against harassment must be vigilantly enforced, Richard T. Roberts, the city's housing commissioner, said that it was not possible in a healthy real estate market to preserve all single rooms permanently for low-income occupancy. "We have a supportive-housing production program, not a single-room-occupancy preservation program," he said. This will increasingly include new construction of units for low-income singles, he said.
At the Martha Washington, a small group of pro-rehabilitation tenants, dissatisfied with MFY Legal Services' representation of the tenants association, organized on their own, and with financial help from the developer hired their own lawyer, Mitchell H. Kossoff, a partner in Kossoff Alper & Unger in Manhattan. From a one-page letter that covered the basic benefits, he negotiated the detailed contract that the 83 tenants signed over a period of six months. He also represented the pro-conversion tenants in public meetings, hearings and court proceedings.
"We needed to find a lawyer who had handled a case of this nature," said Brenda Waites Bolling, a hat designer who has lived at the Martha Washington for 10 years. Ms. Bolling took a leading role in finding a private lawyer. "I realized we were getting an offer that was just unbelievable, and I had faith in Mr. Maloney, although some people did not trust him," Ms. Bolling said.
For no increase in rent, the tenants who signed up are to get full bathrooms, color television with free cable service, free air-conditioning, and new or relocated closets. During construction the tenants are offered refinished rooms within the hotel. But they are not obliged to move out of the own rooms. If a tenant decides not to have a private bathroom, a public bathroom is to remain on that floor.
Tenants who move during construction may move back to their room when it is completed. During the reconstruction they pay no rent. Current rents range from $225 to $700 a month, and average $450 a month, the management says. When and if the room is vacated, it becomes part of the general inventory of hotel rooms, for which guest rates are expected to average $130 a night.
Property Markets Group, through a division called the Citylife Hotel Group, also owns and operates the 300-room OnTheAve Hotel, a reconstruction of the former Broadway American Hotel at Broadway and 77th Street, and Habitat Hotel at Lexington Avenue and 57th Street. The Habitat is the former Allerton Hotel, a 430-room S.R.O.
Property Markets Group was denied a certificate of no harassment at the Allerton last year after a finding by the housing agency that harassment had occurred under the former ownership.
After a three-year wait, an owner can reapply for a certificate of no harassment. "We're going to offer the tenants there a program similar to the one we offered at the Martha Washington," Mr. Maloney said.

HEADLINE: YOUR HOME;
Choosing Oil Heat or Natural Gas

BYLINE: By JAY ROMANO 

 BODY:
HOMEOWNERS who have been thinking about converting their oil heating systems to natural gas recently found a reason to stop thinking and start acting when heating oil prices rose earlier this month to more than $2.60 a gallon in some areas.
While prices have since fallen to under $1.50 a gallon, natural gas suppliers say that the demand for oil-to-gas conversions has significantly increased. One of the area's largest natural gas providers, Keyspan Energy -- the parent of Brooklyn Union, which supplies natural gas to homes in Brooklyn, Queens and Staten Island -- recently reported that it had signed up 1,492 new gas customers in January, up from 962 for the same month last year. 
 But as alarming as the recent increase in oil prices may seem, oil dealers say that homeowners should make sure to "crunch the numbers" before making the decision to abandon oil for natural gas.
"It's simple economics," said Robert Frezzo, a principal of Jules Frezzo Oil Service in Union City, N.J. "If you want to change from oil to gas to save money, the first thing you should do is figure out how much you're going to be saving on heating bills. Because if you aren't going to be saving money, you're never going to recover the money you spent on the conversion."
Even without considering the costs of conversion, oil prices have in recent years been less than those for gas, when computed on the basis of British Thermal Units, or B.T.U.'s, In fact, according to statistics compiled by the New York State Energy Research and Development Authority, a state agency that monitors utilities, the average price for home heating oil was lower than the average price for natural gas on a "B.T.U. for B.T.U." basis in each year from 1993 to 1998, the last year for which such figures are available -- and, of course, well before the recent runup in oil prices.
"And 1998 was a banner year for oil," said John Maniscalco, executive vice president of the New York Heating Oil Association, a trade organization based in Manhattan. Mr. Maniscalco said that the average price for oil in that year was 34 percent less than the average price for natural gas.
"People have to look at the big picture," he said. "Oil has a track record of being less expensive than gas for the last six or seven years. So do you really want to make a decision to spend thousands of dollars based upon one 30-day blip in prices?"
The most obvious consideration for those who are thinking about converting from oil to gas is whether gas is available for a particular property. Joseph Alba, manager of gas conversions for Public Service Electric & Gas, a utility based in Newark, said that for customers who have already installed gas appliances like hot water heaters and clothes dryers, the existing gas supply to the house should be adequate.
Those who do not now have a gas line running to their house, however, can have one installed -- usually free of charge -- if there is a gas line in the street outside the property in question. If there is no gas line running down a particular street, he said, it is unlikely that a utility would install one unless it could be reasonably assured that several homes would utilize the service.
For homeowners who have access to gas, the experts say, the decision to convert should be made only after figuring out the total cost of the conversion -- an amount that may include hidden costs that could add thousands to the final price tag. Mr. Frezzo, who is a licensed plumber in New Jersey, pointed out, for example, that while parts and labor for a new gas boiler would normally cost anywhere from $3,000 to $4,000, homeowners often fail to take into consideration the costs associated with abandoning the existing oil storage tank.
In many places, he said, local law requires underground tanks to be tested for leaks, emptied of oil and filled with sand. Some jurisdictions, however, require removal of the tank from the ground. Accordingly, he said, the cost of dealing with the old tank can range from hundreds to thousands of dollars. And just leaving an underground tank empty, he said, is asking for trouble since it will eventually corrode and could even collapse.
Mr. Frezzo added that homeowners should also resist the temptation to save money on an oil-to-gas conversion by replacing only the burner assembly on the existing furnace. "You can put a gas gun in an existing boiler for about $1,500," he said. "But unless you have a fairly new boiler, you're probably going to be wasting your money because the inefficiencies of older boilers are outrageous."
Another possible addition to the cost of converting to gas results from the fact that since natural gas produces cooler exhaust gases than oil, a chimney flue that was properly sized for an old oil furnace might be too big for a new gas furnace.
"It might appear that bigger is better," said Ashley Eldridge, technical director of the National Chimney Sweep Guild, a trade organization based in Indianapolis. "But with modern gas appliances, the flue gas temperature is lower. And if the flue is too big, the exhaust gases can condense to form hydrochloric acid that can eat away at the lining."
The remedy, Mr. Eldridge said, is generally to install a liner inside the existing flue -- a job that can cost several hundred dollars, depending on the size of the liner.
For homeowners who crunch the numbers but nevertheless remain undecided, some natural gas providers are now offering some additional incentives for converting switching from oil to gas. Keyspan Energy, for example, is now offering new customers their choice of $700, a free gas boiler or interest-free financing on the boiler and the additional installation costs.
"When you're asking people to choose between oil and gas, you're asking them to make a 25-year commitment," said Robert Mahony, president of the company. "So you better have an aggressive marketing campaign."

HEADLINE: Commercial Property /Telecommunications;
Wiring Buildings for High-Speed Internet Access

BYLINE: By JOHN HOLUSHA 

 BODY:
THERE is little doubt what business the Intellispace company is in. Right at the entrance to the company's offices at 1156 Avenue of the Americas, at 45th Street, there are racks of telecommunications equipment that stand between the reception area and the executive desks.
But there is nothing traditional about the equipment or the company's approach to the telecommunications business. It is all related to the development of the Internet. 
 Intellispace is one of dozens of companies to which building managers are turning to prepare their properties for the high-tech future, providing telecommunications services to their tenants. From Bell Atlantic to the newest startup, each seems to have a different technical approach and economic model, with most providing a mix of local and long distance voice service plus video and Internet connections.
The Internet connections have become increasingly important, particularly for the young companies, usually referred to collectively as dot-coms, that conduct virtually all their business on the Internet. According to figures compiled by Insignia/ESG, the real-estate services company, 75 percent of all the commercial leases executed in Midtown South in the month of January this year were by such technology-related companies.
While most personal-computer users are content to rely on dial-up modem connections with Internet service providers, despite the occasional difficulty in gaining access, for companies developing businesses on the Internet, continuous, reliable, high capacity connections are essential. Other concerns in the field include OnSite Access, Allied Riser and Eureka Broadband.
"We are just at the beginning of a technological revolution," said Marc Josephson, the founder and chairman of Intellispace. "It is very difficult to do both voice and the Internet, so we just do Internet." Not everyone might agree with this assessment, but Mr. Josephson has chosen the Internet-only approach to differentiate his company from competitors.
He said that designing a system for the Internet optimizes the connections for Internet use rather than trying to add Net services to a system designed to carry voices. And, he said, standard voice and data services may one day be available over the Internet in a manner less costly than the present system.
Mr. Josephson said he became convinced about the commercial future of the Internet when he connected the building at 55 Broad Street in the summer of 1995. The building, owned by the Rudin family, had been sitting empty. It has since filled with Internet-related tenants and become a leading example of how technology can help cure real estate ills.
SINCE then, Intellispace has wired Rockefeller Center, the Jacob Javits Convention Center and 190 other buildings containing over 55 million square feet of space. Recently it reached an agreement with Hartz Mountain Industries to wire 18 properties in New York and northern New Jersey.
Among the Manhattan buildings wired by Intellispace are about 40 managed by Williams Real Estate for third-party owners. But Williams executives said they are still trying to choose among the vendors for buildings owned by the principals of Williams. "We are still trying to choose the right carrier," said Robert Getreu, a senior managing director of the firm. "We want the most flexibility for the tenants and a full package of services."
As a property manager, he said, he tries to differentiate among the many companies offering communications services using three criteria: the financial capability to deliver promised services, the range of services offered and the technology being used.
Intellispace's approach is to equip a building for very high speed communications and then sell each tenant the capacity it needs. It installs a device called a router, which is capable of handling 100 million bits per second of information and runs individual optical fiber connections to each tenant.
By way of comparison, a simple voice telephone line can carry 56,000 bits per second.
Although all the traffic in a building goes through the same piece of equipment, Intellispace officials say internal controls prevent one client from seeing another's messages. Each customer has its own dedicated fiber connection to the router, they add.
The company makes the installation at no cost to the landlord, who nevertheless must provide space for the router and space in the vertical shafts in a building, called risers. Finding riser space can sometimes be an issue, particularly in older buildings, real estate executives say.
"As building managers we are concerned that we may fill the risers with so much telecom wiring that when we want to do something for a building there will be a problem," Mr. Getreu said.
Intellispace also makes deals on a nonexclusive basis, so other Internet communications companies can offer competing services to tenants, easing landlord fears that tenants will be reluctant to lease space in a building with only one Internet connection.
The landlords also share in the revenue produced by tenants' use of the system, typically receiving 10 percent of revenues once a certain threshold has been reached. After an initial charge of about $2,000 for wiring a tenant's space and establishing the connection, a typical monthly fee for 1 million bits of capacity -- which would be typical for a dot-com with about 15 employees -- is about $1,000, said Bob Froberg, Intellispace's senior vice president and chief operating officer.
He said the system is flexible and can add capacity quickly if a company grows or just has a short-term need. If a company using 5 megabits of capacity suddenly needs 50, he said, the change can be made in a few days.
Intellispace executives say they are targeting the dot-com companies in the buildings they have wired and selling them reliability.
The company runs a minimum of two fiber connections out of each building and links them to two high-capacity fiber "rings" around Manhattan that connect to long-distance carriers such as Level 3 Communications, Cable & Wireless and GTE Internetworking. A third ring is due to go in operation within 60 days, company officials said, adding to redundancy.
The system is designed so that if any circuit is interrupted, all the traffic is switched to one of the backups.
ALTHOUGH this system bypasses the traditional telephone company switching system, Mr. Froberg noted that Bell Atlantic built the company's fiber rings in Manhattan.
"The dot-coms live on the Internet so they need high-speed, dedicated service," Mr. Froberg said, adding that the ability to scale up the amount of service is important as well. "But other businesses are seeing the need for employees to have access to the Internet."
For a company that is selling the future, it is using well-established marketing techniques. It is mounting a print advertising campaign on the city's subways and commuter railways. And it is using a direct sales force -- live people talking to other live people -- to persuade landlords to allow their buildings to be wired and tenants to sign up for service.
"We'll do things like host a breakfast in the lobby of a building to explain what we are offering," Mr. Froberg said.
The company recently received a $35 million investment from Chase Capital Partners, an affiliate of Chase Manhattan Corporation, and Weston Presidio Capital, both venture capital operations.
Intellispace officials say they are using the funds to expand regionally and, they hope, nationally. The company has opened offices in Philadelphia and Princeton, N.J., and is preparing to open offices in Boston, Stamford, Conn., and Washington.
"We are using the investment to expand rapidly," Mr. Josephson said. "In 2000, we expect to be in Baltimore, Richmond, Atlanta, Chicago, Dallas and San Francisco."

HEADLINE: ENVIRONMENT;
Deer May Lose Sunday Day of Rest From Hunters

BYLINE: By RICHARD BRAND 

DATELINE: TRENTON

 BODY:
New Jersey legislators have proposed a number of laws, including one that would end a 97-year-old ban on Sunday hunting, in an effort to control the exploding deer population.
The proposals have been supported by the state Division of Fish and Wildlife and by state farmers, who complain that deer are eating crops and threatening their livelihood. But animal rights groups and outdoor recreationalists say that an extra day of hunting will scare hikers away from visiting the state's wooded areas. 
 The Sunday hunt bill is one of 11 being considered by state lawmakers to deal with deer. Two bills offer funds for deer fertility research, and two others would require the state to reimburse municipalities for cleaning up road kill. Two more bills call for the state to keep records of traffic accidents involving deer, and another would create a method for hunters to donate deer meat for the needy.
Another bill would give the Department of Agriculture the power to grant hunting licenses to farmers, and the last two require applicants for open space preservation funds to impose deer-management plans.
According to the state Division of Fish and Wildlife, New Jersey's deer population, which was almost wiped out at the turn of the century, has grown to 170,000 today, with a sharp rise from two mild winters.
A Rutgers University survey issued in 1998 attributed 68,000 acres of annual crop damage to deer, saying the damage cost farmers $6 million to $10 million each year.
Farmers say they will be unable to make ends meet if something is not done soon. "The situation is very acute here," said State Senator William Schluter of Hunterdon County. "We want green acres and we want to have farms preserved, but if we can't control the deer numbers then we can't preserve farms."
Mr. Schluter is sponsoring a bill that would end a "blue law" ban on Sunday hunting, letting municipalities decide whether to allow Sunday hunts.
Last week, the Senate Economic Growth and Tourism Committee approved the Sunday hunting bill, and it will go before the full Senate this spring with the support of the state Environmental Protection Agency's Division of Fish and Wildlife, which oversees hunting in the state.
"The most effective method to control deer populations is still sport hunting," said the director of the Division of Fish and Wildlife, Jim McDowell. "And too many deer are impacting on the farmer's ability to make a living."
The bill's opponents say that adding another hunting day will be dangerous for residents of the most densely populated state in the country.
"Most New Jersey citizens choose not to hunt for sport," Nina Austenberg, Director of the Humane Society's mid-Atlantic office, wrote in a letter to state senators. "With more than 100 days for hunters to kill deer, the overwhelming majority of citizens deserves access to public lands without fear or risk from hunter's guns and arrows for at least one day a week."
Mr. McDowell says hunters and hikers can safely share the woods.
"The hunters in this state have an extremely good safety record," he said. "Only one or two participants have been killed in the last few years. It's safer than swimming and golf. We invest a lot of money in the enforcement of safety regulations and its paid off in a safe recreational activity."
Mr. McDowell added: "The recreational use of deer pumps $106 million dollars a year into the state's small business economy. Deer are both an asset and a liability."
Today the deer are the bane of farmers, motorists, and home gardeners, and they are largely responsible for the prevalence of Lyme disease across the state.
Although the state does not keep data on deer-related accidents, some counties and towns keep count. Union County, for example, reported 670 deer-related accidents between 1992 and 1998, and Hunterdon County confirmed at least two fatalities in the last year.
Farmers remain the most broadly affected group.
"Farmers are willing to lose a percentage of their crop to wildlife damage. It's the nature of the beast," said Mary Mott-Kocsis, a member of the Hunterdon County Board of Agriculture who owns a 3,000 acre farm with her husband and his brother. 'But when you consistently lose 20 percent of your crop, it's too excessive.'
Hunters killed 75,283 deer during the season that ended last month, and slightly over 60,000 the year before.

HEADLINE: ON POLITICS;
For House Candidates, Inflation Is a Big Issue

BYLINE: By Laura Mansnerus; Laura Mansnerus reports from Trenton for The New York Times

DATELINE: TRENTON

 BODY:
The cost of living is high in this state, for politicians as for everybody else. And this year, while complaints focus on a Senate race that promises to devour millions, House seats are going to be expensive, too, and a few will be amazingly expensive.
The inflation shows up across the board. In the 1998 elections, primary and general, all the candidates for all 13 House seats raised a record $17.5 million. In the current cycle, House candidates raised nearly $8 million just by the end of 1999, five months before the primary and almost a year before the general election. 
 The giving and spending will probably accelerate as the national party organizations get to work on a few campaigns judged critical to control of the House, where Republicans now hold a six-seat edge.
One contest alone, the race to unseat Representative Rush Holt in the 12th District, is expected to cost the participants a total of $4 million, which Mr. Holt attributes not only to the perpetually high cost of media time in the state but also to "the fact that I probably have the biggest bull's-eye on my back of any member of Congress."
Mr. Holt wrested the central Jersey district from the Republicans in 1998 and has been fending them off from the day he did. He expects to raise and spend $2 million, more than twice his total for 1998, and expects his opposition to do the same.
In 1999 Mr. Holt raised $968,075, with $665,339 in the bank at the end of the year. The two Republicans fighting for the nomination to oppose him, Dick Zimmer and Michael Pappas, were trailing, Mr. Zimmer with $674,390 in receipts and Mr. Pappas with $123,476. And for now they have the expense of beating up each other.
Still, the Democratic Congressional Campaign Committee, while enjoying the messy split among Republicans, is not letting go of this one. "We stand ready to help him in any way," John Del Cecato, a committee spokesman, said of Mr. Holt. "Financial support, any counsel he needs, human resource help, anything he needs."
The Republican Congressional Campaign Committee feels the same way about whoever wins the June 6 primary. Representative Holt's seat is "a top priority," said Jill Schroeder, a committee spokeswoman. "It's an expensive year, but in particular this race is expensive because Rush Holt is one of the more vulnerable members."
Another race that is already engaging the national operatives is Susan Bass Levin's challenge to Representative Jim Saxton in the Third District, in South Jersey. While she has just a fraction of the $1 million-plus that Mr. Saxton has in the bank, Ms. Levin, the mayor of Cherry Hill, is working fast; she raised a total of $219,296 in 1999, before announcing her candidacy, half of it in the last 10 days of December.
Then there is the seriously crowded Seventh District, where Democrats are given decent odds of winning the seat that Bob Franks is vacating to run for the Republican Senate nomination. As of the end of December, four Republicans and three Democrats had raised a total of $1.4 million. Since then, two more Republicans and one more Democrat have joined the race.
Not all of the major money raisers are in close contests. Some incumbents raise large sums just to scare off opposition, and some of the richer incumbents, it is understood, will dispense money to allies in need.
Two of the richest, Representatives Robert Menendez (just over $1 million in receipts last year) and Frank Pallone (almost $600,000), both Democrats, raised cash furiously for possible Senate campaigns. Now, with virtually no opposition for their House seats, both could probably win re-election without leaving their offices.
For the participants not yet released from fund-raising, the experience is taxing. Ms. Levin said her days were getting longer, with at least two hours a day on the phone at campaign headquarters. "I'm also meeting people for breakfast and lunch," she said. "Sometimes two breakfasts."
For his part, Representative Saxton will have to spend much of his $1 million on himself, whereas in recent years, he said, he had been in the "enviable position of being able to help somebody else." (In 1998, Mr. Saxton won by 31 percentage points, against an opponent who raised a grand total of $2,740.) He is doubtful that the national party organization, which can make only small cash contributions anyway -- will be very helpful. "It's kind of hard," he explained, "to convince the powers that be that you need help above and beyond what you can do when you have a million dollars in the bank."
Ms. Levin's response: "He has a million and I'll have to have a million, and maybe more. Then, when I'm elected, I'll fight tooth and nail for campaign finance reform."
That is what Representative Holt said in his 1998 campaign, and upon taking office he signed on to a bill to restrict the ever-larger soft money contributions to party organizations.
Now that the stakes in his district have doubled in two years, Mr. Holt said, "all of this just highlights the need for reform."

SECTION: Section 3; Page 24; Column 1; Money and Business/Financial Desk 

LENGTH: 1234 words

HEADLINE: YOUR TAXES;
At Least for Now, Retirees Find That New Jobs Come at a Price

BYLINE: By DANIEL F. CUFF 

 BODY:
AMERICA needs more workers. Without them, the nation's economic boom may be threatened, if current workers can command big pay raises and thus bring inflation into the picture. Luckily, there is a solution readily at hand: a gray horde of retirees, many of whom want to work at something.
But there's also a catch: 
 If you want to hang on to all your Social Security benefits, you'd better not work too much. If you are under age 65 and collecting benefits, you can earn up to only $10,080 a year before giving up some Social Security money. And if you are 65 through 69 you can earn up to just $17,000 this year; in 2001, the cap will rise to $25,000.
Why punish the ambitious? That is a question being asked in Congress. House Republicans have introduced a bill to allow Social Security recipients from age 65 (but not recipients under 65) to earn as much as they want with no threat to their retirement benefits. And President Clinton has said he will sign such a bill if it comes to him with no links to other issues. So things could change fairly soon.
But for now, financial penalties are still in effect. Some 800,000 Social Security recipients aged 65 through 69 gave up part of their benefits last year because they earned too much.
John E. Abate found out about this recently while keeping busy consulting for Lucent Technologies; he had retired in 1998, at the age of 67. Something that his financial adviser had told him was nagging at the back of his brain. The adviser, Doug Stives, an accountant with Wiss & Company in Red Bank, N.J., who had helped him make the transition to retirement, explained to Dr. Abate that he should keep his earnings below $15,500 (the limit then in effect) if he went back to work in 1999.
With all the confusion attendant to starting a new way of life, Dr. Abate, who holds a Ph. D. in electrical engineering, put the information on the back burner and began his consulting work. But then he realized that he should get in touch with his adviser.
"John came in to see me for 15 minutes, and we went over his options," Mr. Stives said. "He decided to stop consulting for the rest of the year, and he said, 'If I hadn't come to see you, this could have been a pretty expensive situation.' "
"Yes," Dr. Abate said the other day, "there are unfortunate consequences for people who make some money."
Although workers who earn more than the cap will end up with a higher income than if they had not done so, they may well lose most of their gains.
Dr. Abate, who lives with his wife, Mary, in Holmdel, N.J., is typical of the new retirees who are in good health and in good financial standing with plenty of energy to pursue hobbies and work opportunities.
The Abates have four grown children and five grandchildren. In his spare time Dr. Abate, who had a distinguished career as a research scientist and a manager with AT&T Bell Laboratories, studies music at Monmouth University and plays the clarinet, saxophone, piano and organ. He bicycles and swims, monitors his investments on the Internet and is looking into volunteer work.
But he wants to keep his hand in his life's work, and that is why he was consulting for Lucent, which was spun out of AT&T. When he realized he could not continue without imposing a penalty on himself, he decided to turn over his project to a colleague. He decided against one option, which would have meant finding a way to push part of his consulting fee into the next year.
Under Social Security rules, benefits for people who are 65 through 69 are reduced by $1 for every $3 in earnings over $17,000. But that is not all. These recipients must pay Social Security tax on their earnings, even though doing so will not enhance their future benefits. And they must pay income tax as well.
"The end result is an effective tax on earned income of about 85 percent, which is ridiculous," Mr. Stives said.
There are limited ways around this. One route suggested by Mr. Stives was for Dr. Abate to incorporate and limit his salary to $17,000 a year. That way, he could profit from consulting and not hurt his Social Security benefits. Setting up what is known as a C corporation would allow him to limit his salary, but profit would be subject to corporate income tax. Or, he could set up an S corporation, which would pass through its profits to shareholders of the company.
Of course, there are limits to how creative you can get with these situations. Say you founded an instantly successful Internet company making millions of dollars but paid yourself only $17,000. You would probably hear from a tax agent. "You would need to prove $17,000 is reasonable compensation for what you are doing," Mr. Stives said.
Another option is retreat. Suppose that you are receiving Social Security benefits and decide that retiring was a mistake, that you want to work full time and make as much as you can. Not to worry, according to Avery E. Neumark, a principal in the accounting firm of Rosen Seymour Shapss Martin & Company: You can call the Social Security office and explain that you want to drop out of the program; later, when you decide to claim your benefits, your latest earnings will be included in the computation of benefits, so that you may be eligible for a larger benefit.
For people planning to work heavily during retirement, it would generally make sense to defer putting in for Social Security benefits. People who wait until age 70 get a much bigger benefit, and there is no limit on earned income as far as Social Security goes.
The Social Security Administration contends that you should not necessarily keep your earnings under the limits because you think you will lose too much money. On its Web site (www.ssa.gov), it provides examples showing that even if you give back some of your Social Security benefits, you can still come out ahead in overall income. That may be true, but if you worked since you were a teenager to earn those Social Security benefits, it hurts to give any back.
The agency also explains that if you work for someone else, only your wages count toward the earnings limit. If you are self-employed, net earnings, not gross earnings, are counted, allowing room for deductions -- a home office for those who qualify, for example, or costs for computers, cars used in work, or even employees. At any rate, nonwork income like investment earnings, interest, pensions, annuities and capital gains are not counted.
Still, the self-employed should look at the tax consequences of taking on new projects. A person with investment income, for example, or whose spouse was still working, could find that freelance work made his Social Security benefits subject to income tax. For married taxpayers filing jointly, 50 percent of Social Security benefits are taxable if income as calculated on a work sheet is $32,000 to $43,999, and 85 percent taxable if income is $44,000 or more.
People in the first year of retirement may disregard all of the above. There are a different set of rules for that period. Generally, people who have retired in midyear have earned more than the yearly earnings limit, so Social Security will give them a full Social Security check for any whole month they are retired, regardless of yearly earnings.
In any case, check for details with the agencies involved or advisers like Mr. Stives. There are all sorts of ins and outs.
 
http://www.nytimes.com

GRAPHIC: Photo: John E. Abate, with his daughter Holly, decided to limit his consulting work after retiring rather than lose some of his Social Security benefits. (Frank C. Dougherty for The New York Times)
 
Chart: "From Regular I.R.A. to Roth I.R.A. and Back Again"
In a regular I.R.A., pretax money is contributed, and taxes are due on distributions. The reverse is true with a Roth. After-tax money goes in, and distributions after age 59 1/2, including money earned in the account, may be tax-free.
 Taxpayers whose adjusted gross incomes are $100,000 or less may convert regular I.R.A.'s to Roth I.R.A.'s but will have to pay taxes based on the value of the taxable holdings at the time they do so.
 What are known as recharacterizations back to regular I.R.A.'s are also allowed. The intention was to provide a way to undo the conversion for taxpayers who turned out to be ineligible for Roths, but the law does not limit their use to that purpose.
 Here, a taxpayer whose account dropped in value was able to cut his tax liability by recharacterizing and then reconverting to avoid paying taxes on the original value of the I.R.A.
 
A taxpayer has $10,000 in a traditional I.R.A. invested in a mutual fund.
The taxpayer decides to convert the traditional I.R.A. to a Roth.
 
If no further action is taken
Balance: $10,000
Tax due in 28% bracket: $2,800
 
The fund drops in value to $7,000, so the taxpayer chooses to recharacterize the I.R.A.
The taxpayer turns the Roth I.R.A. back into a traditional I.R.A.
 
Balance: $7,000
Tax due: $0
 
The fund rises in value to $8,000, so the taxpayer decides turn the I.R.A. back into a Roth.
The taxpayer owes taxes on only $8,000, not the original $10,000.
 
Balance: $8,000
Tax due: $2,240
 
(Source: RIA)

SECTION: Section 3; Page 25; Column 1; Money and Business/Financial Desk 

LENGTH: 1698 words

HEADLINE: YOUR TAXES;
Beating the Tuition Blues and Earning Some Tax Breaks

BYLINE: By CHARLES DELAFUENTE 

 BODY:
THERE are many ways that Eleanor Terzian and Antonio Ciolino could save on income taxes while also saving for their son's college education. They could invest in a child's name, buy savings bonds, open an Education I.R.A., build up savings in a state-sponsored tuition-payment plan or even increase contributions to their own retirement plans.
The problem faced by Ms. Terzian and Mr. Ciolino -- and many couples like them -- is that they have to choose among these available strategies, because the tax code does not allow all the breaks to be used at the same time and sets income limits on others. Further, some of the choices are complicated by scholarship-eligibility rules that depend on future income. 
Predicting which strategies will work best years from now is tough, to say the least.
Ms. Terzian and Mr. Ciolino, who live in Mineola, N.Y., have so far chosen just one way to put away college money for their son, Giancarlo, 11. When he was about 5, they bought $5,000 of stock in three major companies for him. (They declined to specify the companies.)
"I'm not a fan of mutual funds," Ms. Terzian said, differing with most financial planners. "I'd rather pick a stock and take my chances."
The shares are owned by Giancarlo, with his mother as custodian, under the Uniform Gifts to Minors Act, a law adopted by most states that allows a parent or other custodian to set aside money in a minor's name. The arrangement gives the minor the tax responsibility while the custodian makes the investment decisions.
Monetary gifts to Giancarlo, including some from his grandfather, of about $200 a month, also have been invested in those same stocks. And Ms. Terzian participates in the companies' commission-free dividend-reinvestment plans.
From a tax standpoint, this has been "a reasonable first step" in a college-financing plan, said Sidney Kess, a New York tax lawyer and accountant.
Because Giancarlo's stock has yielded annual dividends of less than the amount of a standard exemption for a dependent, which was $700 for 1999, he has never had to pay any tax. If he had more than $700 in dividends, he would owe tax at the 15 percent rate on the next $700. After that, under the kiddie tax rules, his income would be taxed at his parents' rate of 28 percent.
With some planning, Giancarlo will not have to pay much tax on the increase in the stock's value. When he turns 14, he will no longer be subject to the kiddie tax and his income will be taxed at the lowest rate, currently 15 percent, or 10 percent for long-term capital gains.
The College Board, which tracks the cost of higher education, says the average annual cost of attending a private four-year college, including tuition and room and board, was $20,500 during the 1999-2000 school year. The average cost of a public school was $10,909. Multiply that by four, and it is clear that most people will need to make every smart financial move they can.
There are other steps that can be taken by Ms. Terzian, a teacher in New York City, and Mr. Ciolino, a chef who is both an owner of and a salaried employee of Spazzi, a restaurant in Fairfield, Conn.
They could buy United States Series EE Savings Bonds. The interest is tax free for joint filers with income below $79,650 (or $53,100 for individuals) if the proceeds are used for college. But the tax break is phased out after the parents' income reaches $109,650 for couples or $68,000 for individuals in the year the bonds are redeemed.
Giancarlo's parents could also open an Education I.R.A. for him, or he could open his own, but the maximum contribution to each child's account is only $500 a year. Because of that low ceiling and the various strings attached, such accounts are not popular. The amount is not deductible from income, but the growth of the investment is tax free if the money is withdrawn to pay tuition bills. Married taxpayers with adjusted gross income of more than $160,000 cannot contribute, nor can singles with adjusted gross income of more than $110,000. However, a child can open the account, even with gifts from ineligible parents.
Alternatively, the couple could invest in a state-sponsored prepaid tuition plan or a college savings plan. (The Internal Revenue Service, however, does not permit both that and an Education I.R.A.) According to the Ayco Company, a national financial planning firm based in Albany, the Montana Family Education Savings Program (800 888-2723), is the only prepaid state tuition plan available to residents of every state. The plan will calculate how much is needed to pay for the college education, including room and board, of a child of a given age. If that much is invested over a prescribed period, the plan guarantees to pay for the child's education. For Giancarlo, the Montana plan would require a lump-sum investment of $94,238 or six annual investments of $18,000 for a private college. The earnings would be tax free.
As Ayco pointed out in a recent report to its clients, the drawback of such a plan is that the account holder does not benefit if asset growth outruns the inflation rate for college tuition, now about 4.25 percent a year. What the plan does provide, the report notes, is a guarantee that the money will be there when it is needed.
Among state-sponsored college savings plans, Ayco says one that stands out is the New York College Savings Program (877 697-2837, www.nysaves.com), which Mr. Kess calls "a terrific new thing." That would be the next step in his suggested strategy for Giancarlo.
Unlike the Montana plan, college savings plans are not tax-exempt, according to Ayco, but no tax is due on the investment's growth until the principal is withdrawn for college. New York State residents can also deduct contributions of up to $5,000 a year each from their state income taxes, a break worth $686 a couple to most middle-income and upper-income taxpayers and less to low-income parents.
But the fund, not the investor, decides on the investment mix, and Ayco says the New York fund's investment allocations "are very conservative." Ayco suggests that a growth mutual fund might provide a higher return for younger children.
Several other states offer similar plans. Details are available on the Internet (www.savingforcollege.com).
A drawback of both the Education I.R.A. and the state-sponsored plans is that the money belongs to the child, not the parents. That can make a big difference to a child who is being considered for scholarship aid.
Most colleges determine a student's financial need using a formula that requires a student to contribute 82 percent of his or her assets over four years, but requires parents to contribute only 21 percent of their assets. One way to escape the kiddie tax and also avoid limitations on financial aid is to use a child's assets for purposes other than tuition once he or she turns 14 but before it is time to apply for aid.
The state-sponsored plans are so new that it is not yet clear whether they will be counted in the formula that reduces scholarships based on a student's assets, although many financial planners predict that they will. Michael H. Iacolucci, the chief technical, training and investment officer for Ayco's financial-related services division, said an informal survey showed that some colleges had not yet decided the issue.
Another strategy available to Mr. Ciolino is to contribute $2,000 to a traditional I.R.A. every year. He can deduct the contribution from current income because he is not covered by a pension plan and because the couple's adjusted income is less than $150,000. If Ms. Terzian were not covered by a plan, either, she could take the same step. (For people who are covered by a plan at work, I.R.A. contributions are deductible only if the gross income on a joint return is less than $61,000.)
Normally, I.R.A. withdrawals are subject to a 10 percent penalty if the account holder is less than 59 1/2 years old. But withdrawals to pay a dependent's tuition are free from penalties, although income tax is due on the amount withdrawn.
A similar strategy for parents is to increase contributions to voluntary retirement plans at work if they are not investing the maximum. The contributions reduce taxable income dollar for dollar, and most plans will allow participants to borrow against their holdings.
Ms. Terzian, for instance, contributes 14 percent of her salary to a tax-deferred 403(b) retirement plan. If she put in another $2,400, as she could, she would cut her New York State and federal tax bill by $791.
The 401(k) plans sponsored by corporations are similar, and many companies make them even more attractive by matching a portion of their employees' contributions. Employees can contribute up to 15 percent of their income, to a maximum of $10,500 a year in 2000.
David S. Rhine, a partner who is national director of family wealth planning at BDO Seidman, the accounting firm, said that "for the average middle-income taxpayer, retirement plans are the best way to save for college." That is because of employer matches and wider investment choices, he said. Also, retirement savings do not count against the financial aid formulas, he said.
Mr. Rhine recommends borrowing over withdrawing from retirement funds as part of a strategy of maximizing financial aid. A parent who withdrew $20,000 in retirement funds during a child's freshman year, he explained, would have $20,000 more in income that year, and that might disqualify the child from getting aid the next year.
Another borrowing strategy with favorable tax implications for parents who own homes is a home-equity loan. The interest on loans up to $100,000 is deductible.
One other strategy mentioned by Mr. Kess is for Giancarlo to earn some money each year when he is old enough. If he earned $2,000 as a kitchen helper in his father's restaurant each summer, he would owe virtually no income tax. If he put that money into a Roth I.R.A., the principal, which is not subject to tax, could be withdrawn to help pay tuition. Mr. Kess would leave the account's earnings alone, because they could become the foundation of Giancarlo's own retirement plan.

HEADLINE: YOUR TAXES;
How the 2000 Ballot Could Amend Form 1040

BYLINE: By RICHARD W. STEVENSON 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
RARELY have voters had such a clear opportunity to let Washington know what they want to do about taxes.
It is a presidential election year, and between the two major parties, the four leading candidates are offering a spectrum of proposals. At one end of the spectrum is Gov. George W. Bush of Texas, who generally wants to provide tax relief of Reaganesque proportions; at the other is former Senator Bill Bradley, who has called for only limited tax cuts to reduce poverty and improve health care. Mr. Bush's Republican rival, Senator John McCain of Arizona, champions a substantial tax-cutting package, although much smaller than Mr. Bush's, and Vice President Al Gore supports modest tax cuts. 
 Taxes always play some role in presidential politics, but this year they are an even bigger issue than usual, because there is a huge pot of money on the table, estimated at as much as $1.9 trillion over the next decade, depending on what assumptions are made about government spending. In addition, Social Security is projected to generate a surplus of more than $2 trillion over that period, but it is expected to face severe problems longer term, as the population ages.
The two parties more or less agree that the Social Security surplus should go toward shoring up the retirement system or reducing the national debt. But with the federal government shifting from an era of crippling budget deficits to one of large and growing surpluses, tax cuts are at the center of a fundamental ideological debate over how the government should apportion the torrent of tax revenue being generated by the strong economy.
Send it back to the taxpayers, say conservatives, who contend that the surplus will otherwise be squandered by the government. Invest it in education, health care and other social needs rather than handing out tax breaks to the rich, argue liberals, who see the flood of revenue into the Treasury as a chance for government to make a real difference.
Moderates in both parties say that there is enough money available to balance those two goals. They also assert that a substantial chunk of the surplus should go toward dealing with an aging society's long-range problems, most notably the financial strains facing Social Security and Medicare.
Voters seem to favor using the surplus first to bolster Social Security and Medicare, according to a New York Times/CBS News poll this month. The poll found that 56 percent of those surveyed made preserving the old-age programs their top priority when it came to allocating the surplus, followed by reducing the national debt, at 17 percent, and cutting taxes, at 13 percent. Some analysts think that Mr. Bush's defeats in the Republican primaries in New Hampshire and Michigan are evidence that the Republican tax-cutting orthodoxy lacks its former political punch, and that voters are, as Mr. McCain is betting, at least as intent on using the surplus to address the long-term needs of the nation.
"There's a clear set of options," said Clint Stretch, a tax policy analyst at Deloitte & Touche, the accounting firm. "You can get small, medium-sized or big. You can get tax cuts for low earners, for the middle class or for everybody. And you can get it simple or you can get it complicated."
To some extent, the partisan and philosophical differences on taxes are also playing out this year between Congress and President Clinton. Republican leaders plan to bring up a variety of tax-cutting proposals in a piecemeal fashion, and the House has already passed a Republican-sponsored cut in the "marriage penalty," which has left some two-income couples paying more in taxes than two single people would if they had the same incomes. There is some hope in both parties for compromise this year on a few tax issues, including this one.
But the basic direction of tax policy is unlikely to be settled until the voters have decided whom to install in the White House and whether to leave Congress under the Republicans' control.
"After the election, assuming the surplus numbers remain big, we will come to a consensus or a compromise," Mr. Stretch said. "But it has to be next year and not this year."
THE debate comes down to two basic questions: How much of the projected surplus should go toward tax cuts? And how should any tax cuts be spread among low- , middle- and high-income taxpayers?
Mr. Bush's proposal would cut taxes by $483 billion over five years and by about $1.3 trillion over 10 years. Its centerpiece is a gradual reduction in marginal income tax rates, meaning that everyone would get a tax cut. Under current law, there are five income tax rates: 15 percent, 28 percent, 31 percent, 36 percent and 39.6 percent. Mr. Bush would replace those five brackets with four: 10 percent, 15 percent, 25 percent and 33 percent.
Mr. Bush would also double the child tax credit to $1,000 a child and increase the income level at which the credit phases out. He would allow two-income couples to take a deduction of up to $3,000, to reduce the marriage penalty, and would phase out the tax on large estates.
Mr. Bush argues that by giving a tax cut to the working poor, his plan would help people struggling to climb into the middle class. He also contends that the plan would appropriately return excess revenue to the people who had earned it in the first place.
"We're dealing with the people's money, not the government's money, and I want to give people their money back," Mr. Bush said during a debate in South Carolina just before his victory in the primary there. "And if you're going to have a tax cut, everybody ought to have a tax cut."
But Mr. McCain and Democrats contend that Mr. Bush's plan is fiscally reckless, because it allocates all or most of the excess revenues from general taxation to tax cuts, leaving little or nothing to bolster Social Security and Medicare or to reduce the national debt further. And they argue that Mr. Bush's plan would give far too much of its benefits to the wealthiest taxpayers.
An analysis by Citizens for Tax Justice, a liberal-leaning research group, found that more than one-third of Mr. Bush's tax cut would go to the wealthiest 1 percent of taxpayers -- those with incomes of $319,000 or more -- while the bottom 60 percent of taxpayers, those with incomes of less than $39,300, would get 11 percent of the benefits. Mr. Bush says his plan fairly gives the biggest tax cuts to the people who pay the biggest tax bills.
MR. McCAIN'S tax-cut plan is valued at $238 billion over five years and $500 billion over 10 years. Its centerpiece is an expansion of the lowest income tax bracket, 15 percent, to cover higher incomes.
Under the plan, the ceiling for the 15 percent bracket would rise to $70,000 from $43,050 for married couples filing jointly, and to $35,000 from $25,750 for single taxpayers. The effect would be to give a $3,504 tax cut to a couple with taxable income of $70,000 or more.
Mr. McCain's plan would also double the child tax credit to $1,000 a year, expand tax incentives for savings and investment, reduce the tax on large estates and reduce the marriage penalty for some people by increasing the standard deduction for couples. Mr. McCain would offset a portion of the tax cuts by closing corporate tax loopholes.
His pitch is that the plan is modest enough in size that it leaves plenty of money from the surplus tax revenues to deal with other needs. By expanding the 15 percent bracket to cover millions of additional taxpayers, he says, his plan amounts to a start on creating a system of flatter tax rates. And he argues that his plan gives much less of its benefits to the wealthy than Mr. Bush's plan does.
"I want a balanced approach," Mr. McCain said in the South Carolina debate. "The difference between Governor Bush's proposal and mine is that I put a whole lot of money into Social Security, Medicare and paying down the debt. He puts a whole lot of money into tax cuts."
An analysis by Citizens for Tax Justice found that only 1.8 percent of the benefits of the McCain plan would go to the wealthiest 1 percent of taxpayers. Most of his proposed tax cuts would go to a broadly defined middle class: more than 52 percent to taxpayers making $65,000 to $130,000, and 21 percent to taxpayers making $39,000 to $65,000.
Mr. McCain's plan, however, would do almost nothing for taxpayers with incomes below $39,000. Mr. Bush asserts that Mr. McCain's plan would leave too much of the surplus in Washington, where, Mr. Bush says, it would inevitably be spent not on Social Security but instead on bigger government and wasteful programs. And Mr. Bush regularly criticizes Mr. McCain's plan, contending that it would not help the working poor.
NEITHER of the Democratic candidates is making tax cuts a big part of his campaign. Mr. Gore has proposed allocating $250 billion to $300 billion to tax cuts over the next decade for specific goals. In particular, he supports expanded tax incentives for education and retirement savings, a reduction in the marriage penalty for low- and middle-income couples and a permanent extension of the tax credit for corporate research and development.
As a former member of the Senate Finance Committee and one of the fathers of the 1986 overhaul of the tax code, Mr. Bradley arguably has more experience with tax policy than any of the other candidates. But he has offered no detailed tax proposals.
He has suggested using most of the surplus to deal with two big issues, reducing child poverty and finding ways to make health care more accessible and affordable. The only specific tax cuts he backs are tax breaks on health insurance payments, an expansion of the earned-income tax credit for the working poor and an expansion of the child care credit in a way that would help low-income people.
Mr. Bradley has also proposed clamping down on corporate tax loopholes, a step also favored by Mr. Gore.
Mr. Bush and Mr. McCain agree on one thing: that the Democratic candidates' lack of enthusiasm for big tax cuts reflects the Democrats' desire to expand the size and influence of the federal government. The Democrats, their Republican opponents say, believe that Washington is better qualified to use taxpayer money than the taxpayers themselves.

LENGTH: 496 words

HEADLINE: NEIGHBORHOOD REPORT: FORT GREENE;
Long-Absent Greyhounds May Return To Brooklyn

BYLINE: By TARA BAHRAMPOUR 

 BODY:
A decade after Greyhound stopped direct long-distance service to and from Brooklyn, two local businessmen are trying to return the bus company to the borough. But some community board members have raised concerns about noise and pollution from buses.
Darnell Canada, president of the Fort Greene Empowerment Organization, a community group, and Wallace Scott, its financial secretary, say they hope to open a Greyhound office on Myrtle Avenue near Prince Street by May. Greyhound tickets can be bought in Brooklyn, but passengers must go to the Port Authority in Manhattan to board buses. 
 "An incredible amount of Brooklyn people trek over to the Port Authority," said Ike Davis, Greyhound's manager for New York City and Long Island.
He said the company would lease the space and pay for phone lines, computers and personnel training, with Mr. Canada and Mr. Scott responsible for ticket sales, hiring and supervision. Buses would stop at the station two or three times a day.
The chairman of Community Board 2, Robert Evans, says members have had mixed reaction to the plan. "We were getting people who were concerned," he said. "Some were overjoyed, some were not."
Roy Vanasco, head of Community Board 2's traffic and transportation committee, said: "As far as I'm concerned, there's no need for any more buses coming through here. You just can't park in front of people's stores and houses, with the smoke going in their doors and windows."
The plan would would have to go before the borough president's office and the community board, and would require the approval of the City Planning Commission. Greg Brooks, the chief of staff for Borough President Howard Golden, said, "Nobody has contacted the president's office regarding a Greyhound station."
Mr. Davis said Greyhound had already been considering a Brooklyn station before the two businessmen approached him.
Victor A. Vora, who sells Greyhound tickets out of his three card shops, in downtown Brooklyn and the Flatlands section, said that in recent years he had explored the idea of returning Greyhound buses to Brooklyn. He said he would like to build a bus terminal, and said that the area known as Dumbo (Down Under the Manhattan Bridge Overpass) would be a good location. But Mr. Davis said that while Mr. Vora had discussed the idea with him, he had not presented a formal plan.
Mr. Scott, who has worked as a driver and a supervisor of drivers for Greyhound, said he and Mr. Canada were negotiating with Red Apple Realty, developer of the site, for a three- to five-year lease. He said the Greyhound name could attract other businesses to the area. "We can revitalize Myrtle Avenue and at the same time bring in more employment," Mr. Scott said, saying that the bus station could create about 30 jobs.
A consultant to Mr. Scott and Mr. Canada, S. Eric Blackwell, said he would consider other locations, like East New York or the Brooklyn Navy Yard, if necessary. TARA BAHRAMPOUR

SECTION: Section 12; Page 1; Column 1; Automobiles 

LENGTH: 1050 words

HEADLINE: BEHIND THE WHEEL/Chevrolet Suburban;
Still XXXL, but Softer

BYLINE: By LEONARD M. APCAR 

 BODY:
BY the early 90's, as Chevrolet Suburbans multiplied on American roads, they came to be viewed as symbols of automotive excess. The Suburban had big V-8 power, 18 feet of pressed metal, seats for up to nine and enough room to move a lot of Hillary Clinton's White House mementos up to Chappaqua.
Then excess became fashionable and the Suburban, once a favorite of ranchers, road crews and Texans -- who have their own views about personal space -- became something of an extra-large family wagon. There it was on city streets, in cramped parking lots, waiting outside grade schools. 
 But women, who now account for 60 percent of Suburban drivers during the week, were not crazy about making U-turns in these land barges, or the glares they got from Subaru drivers down below.
So when General Motors contemplated a redesign of its big banana, it didn't forget that the Suburban was finally living up to its name. Families liked it for its anything-but-a-minivan appeal and its interior acreage.
G.M. sketched carefully. Its all-new design gives families what they want -- more usable space inside -- without abandoning the Suburban's lineage as a can-do truck.
G.M. is also making more of the distinctions between the two types of Suburbans, which went on sale late last year: the 1500 model that does family duty -- 96 percent of Suburbans are bought by married adults with children -- and the beefier 2500 favored by rescue squads and road crews. The two are identical in size inside and out, but the 2500, which is based on the frame of the big three-quarter-ton pickup, offers more towing capacity. My test truck was the 1500.
While the exterior has been redesigned, it does not look a lot different. At a glance, you hardly notice the new curve here, the sculptured panel there, the extra bulk on the bones. But the inside grew a bit and got a few minivan touches like optional captain's chairs, replacing a bench, in the second row of seats. The third seat got wheels, making it a little easier to remove, and access to that seat was slightly improved. The drop-down tailgate was eliminated in favor of a one-piece hatchback door. (Old-fashioned, side-hinged doors, akin to those on commercial vans, are still available, too.)
And because they are used more as cars than as trucks, just about everything about the ride and handling became softer. It is now slightly easier to turn on a curve or maneuver in a garage. But parallel-parking is still a high art, never mind navigating fast-food drive-throughs.
The new Suburban holds the road better on twists and turns, but, again, this is still a truck to its very core. Nothing conjured up in Detroit's dream works is going to make a Suburban into a jaunty roller skate.
The foundation comes from the redesigned Chevrolet Silverado pickup; the frame, which is crucial to ride, handling and vibration, is no small matter in a truck whose body and frame are still bolted together the old-fashioned way. This frame, shared by the Silverado and GMC Sierra and the Suburban's GMC counterpart -- now called the Yukon XL -- is a three-part affair that was re-engineered to reduce welds and weight and to increase strength and durability. The truck's foundation is indisputably stiffer and quieter.
In the rear, coil springs were added to most Suburbans to level out the ride, which could be bouncy on the previous model, with its leaf springs.
The new body makes the 2000 Suburban roomier than its predecessor in the ways that count: room for heads, hips and feet. On the outside it's a tad shorter, but not enough to matter. It is wider and taller, but more important, you can sense the tighter turning circle when you loop around to catch the turn that you missed.
All this adds up to a better truck. It is definitely easier to maneuver the big guy, and it seems much quieter and better put-together than the creaky, lumbering living rooms I remember from the 80's.
It is also easier to stop, thanks to a serious brake redesign. G.M. finally acknowledged that the old brakes never inspired much confidence. The 2000 models come with decisive four-wheel disc brakes, instead of a mushy front-disc, rear-drum combination.
In the pricing department, the most luxurious versions have been marked up the most, more than 10 percent for the fancy four-wheel-drive LT. That model, with front and rear air-conditioning and automatic climate control, exceeds $42,000. The plainest 1500 starts at $26,400 with rear drive and $29,337 with four-wheel drive, but these are for fire chiefs and deer hunters.
In most respects, the redesign is cautious. Seating is plush, of course, and the storage bins and cup holders are sufficient, if not as abundant as in today's minivans. Power seat controls are still on the side of the seat, an awkward reach for big hands that have to be wedged in next to the door. The glove box is fairly sized, given the air bag stuffed in nearby; the latch has shifted to the left so the driver can reach it.
The front bench has been smartly split in a 40-20-40 proportion; the middle backrest folds to provide an armrest and storage.
In safety tests, the Suburban excels if for no other reason than its size. The frame is designed to absorb more energy from a head-on collision. There are four air bags, including two in the sides of the front seats.
The standard engine is a 5.3-liter, 285-horsepower V-8, compared with the 5.7-liter, 255-horse V-8 of the previous model. By refining an engine that dates to the celebrated small-block V-8's of the 50's, G.M. has managed to improve power, emissions and fuel economy slightly. The mileage rating is 14 in town, 16 on the highway. But in these days of $1.50-a-gallon gas, filling the standard-sized tank costs about $50 and gets you 450 miles down the road.
The Suburban is no longer the nation's No. 1 road hog, a title that now belongs to the Ford Excursion, which is more than 7 inches longer. Toyota is coming to market this fall with the Sequoia, which will be only a bit smaller, and DaimlerChrysler is said to have a similarly large S.U.V. in the works. But the Suburban will not be easily knocked off its pedestal; half of all sales go to presumably satisfied previous owners.
 
INSIDE TRACK: Sorry, cowboy, this makeover was for mom and the kids.

HEADLINE: WEDDINGS;
Nicole Shidler, Vincent Sampson

 BODY:
Nicole Robin Shidler and Vincent Thomas Sampson were married last evening by Rabbi Gershon Fisher at the Riviera Country Club in Pacific Palisades, Calif.
Mrs. Sampson, 28, is a project director for Moving Beyond Cancer, a study that focuses on the long-term care of breast cancer patients. It is conducted at the Georgetown University Medical Center in Washington. She graduated magna cum laude from the University of California at San Diego and received a Ph.D. in clinical psychology from George Washington University. 
 Mr. Sampson, 32, is legislative counsel at UWC-Strategic Services on Unemployment and Workers' Compensation, a trade organization in Washington that lobbies on behalf of businesses. He graduated from the University of California at Los Angeles and received a J.D. degree from Santa Clara University, as well as a master's degree in labor and employment law from Georgetown. He is the son of Irene Sampson and the late William James Sampson.
The couple met at a November 1995 mixer in Washington for University of California alumni. "Nicole had incredible presence," Mr. Sampson recalled. Not wanting to seem too forward, he invited her to a party he was organizing. "It was just a ruse to get her phone number," he said. "I don't really organize happy hours."
As Ms. Shidler and Mr. Sampson began to date, they found they shared a bond as Californians in Washington. "But there's really no career common ground, and we like it that way," he said. Ms. Shidler said, "I teach him some medicine, he teaches me some politics."
When Ms. Shidler, a daughter of Linda and Joseph Shidler of Agoura Hills, Calif., moved away in 1998 to spend an internship year in Philadelphia, Mr. Sampson proposed on the steps of the Capitol. It was an unseasonably mild November night.
"He told me to turn around, close my eyes and turn around again," Ms. Shidler recalled. "I opened my eyes. I saw stars. I saw the Washington monument. I saw Vince on one knee." She understood, or heard, little else.
She said: "For the longest time all I could remember was, 'Blah blah Nicole blah blah Nicole blah blah. Will you marry me?' The only sad thing about it was I had to go back to Philadelphia to finish my internship."
