HEADLINE: BUSINESS;
10 Employees. No Headquarters. $45 Million Payoff.

BYLINE: By JULIE FLAHERTY 

DATELINE: BOSTON

 BODY:
FOR its first two and a half years, the Valent Software Corporation was a most virtual of companies. The chief executive lived in Massachusetts, the president was parked in Utah, the engineering team was based in Ohio and a handful of other employees worked out of their dining rooms and basements. The "workplace" consisted largely of cell-phone calls, e-mail correspondence and meetings in hotel lobbies.
Except for a small office in Woburn, Mass., that it rented primarily as a place to receive mail, Valent did not have a brick of property to its name. 
 But earlier this month, this 10-employee company found itself worth $45 million in stock when it was bought by Lycos Inc., a leading Internet portal with some 900 employees. And Lycos, which will use Valent's technology to expand its network of online clubs, did not seem to care one iota about Valent's incorporeal existence.
"We value the product, the number of users and the revenue stream; that's what matters to us," said Ron Sege, executive vice president of Lycos, which is based in Waltham, Mass., and has acquired nine businesses since 1998.
By limiting staff size and keeping down overhead costs, more and more start-ups are following the same no-frills recipe for being bought out, said Charlene Li, a media analyst at Forrester Research in Cambridge, Mass.
"When I talk to these small companies, quite a few of them in the back pocket say, 'No, we don't want to go I.P.O., we just want to be acquired by an America Online, a Yahoo,' " she said. "They are trying to be low cost. Until they really need the space, they just work out of the basement."
Almost from Day 1, the goal was to "get acquired," acknowledged J. Scott Benson, the former chief of Valent, who was instrumental in brokering the Lycos deal. Early on, he had Lycos in his sights.
"Lycos is an acquisition engine," he said.
And Lycos was happy to oblige. Mr. Sege said it was interested in Valent's product as a tool for its own growth.
Despite their makeshift offices, Valent's founders were not wet-behind-the-ears entrepreneurs. The idea for Valent came from Leigh Turner, former director of Internet engineering at CompuServe, who in the fall of 1997 told Mr. Benson, a business acquaintance, about his plan for a service that would help companies communicate internally. Mr. Benson had already found considerable success as a founder of Software.com, an e-mail services and software provider. To direct operations, they called in Anthony Antonuccio, a veteran of computer start-ups including Vivo Software Inc., a videoconferencing business, who had made his first million by the time he was 30, a decade ago.
They decided that the Valent intranet idea was more marketable as an Internet services platform for creating virtual communities, or "clubs." The technology would allow Web users with common interests like sports or gardening to communicate through instant messages, online chats and photo albums. Valent's founders said they used $500,000 to $600,000 in start-up money, some from their own savings and some from individual investors.
The company sold the service to several clients, including the portal NeoPlanet.com, but Lycos quickly became Valent's largest partner.
Mr. Turner lived in Columbus, Ohio; Mr. Antonuccio in Park City, Utah; and Mr. Benson in Hopkinton, Mass. With all of them in their 30's and 40's and settled in their communities, none was eager to move, so the company stayed decentralized. The three men worked out of their homes, and so did the seven people they brought on board. "We had meetings in my dining room," said Ann-Marie Sweeney, a senior engineer who worked out of her house in Acton, Mass. "There were big sheets of white paper taped all over my porch one day, the white board set up in my den."
She knew some colleagues only as voices on the phone. "There are still guys out in Ohio that I haven't met, that I've been working with for two years," she said. "But it always felt like a real company, because everyone did have the expertise. They knew how to communicate."
To keep in touch, they used their own product, creating a Valent "club" online where they would hold meetings, chat or share files.
"Since our product was community communication tools, a lot of times we wound up using the tools to replicate what it would be like in an office," said Mark G. Staton, Valent's marketing analyst, who worked out of his apartment in Cambridge. "Suddenly Anthony would instant-message me -- 'Hey, what's going on with this?' -- like he had knocked on the door of my office."
Mr. Staton, who at 27 is the youngest employee, said his bosses and co-workers were disciplined enough to make the unstructured situation work. And he was impressed that "even though everyone worked really hard, everyone kept the priorities of their lives in order."
For most, those priorities included finger painting and nap time. Ms. Sweeney would often write code after putting her 5-month-old son and 3-year-old daughter to bed. Ted Weiss, who handled distribution and partnership, convinced his wife to convert her wardrobe room into office space for him. That way, he was just a few minutes from the day care center their son and daughter attend. All but three employees have young children, so flexible schedules were a given.
"All we were after were results," Mr. Weiss said. "We didn't care if someone was coding at 2 in the morning."
That did bring the desired results, and, ultimately, the deal with Lycos.
Spending $45 million in stock for a software service is not surprising, said Ms. Li, the Forrester analyst. She noted that Excite@Home, a Lycos competitor, recently paid $1 billion to acquire Bluemountain.com, the electronic greeting card company. Just as Excite bought that free online service to increase traffic to its site, she said, Lycos expects that expanding its clubs will get users to stick to its network.
"The problem is that it is very difficult to monetize stickiness," Ms. Li said. "The real money these days is to get people to shop. The connection between clubs and shopping isn't as strong as it potentially could be."
R. BENSON would not say exactly what each Valent employee gained in the $45 million stock deal, but he did say that all had equity in the company, and that most are now at least a million dollars richer.
Though Mr. Benson has again been bitten by the entrepreneurial bug, this time to run his own record label, eight of his nine Valent colleagues have signed on with Lycos. They will continue to run the club service, this time all together, from an earthbound workplace: Lycos's headquarters in the Boston suburb of Waltham.
While the workers in Ohio are still scouting for Boston-area homes, five from the Valent team are already commuting to the Lycos office -- including Mr. Antonuccio, who pulled up his Utah stakes.
As empowering as working independently was, Mr. Antonuccio doesn't see himself jetting off to start another company anytime soon. "If you asked my wife that, she'd hang right up on you," he said. "This was a planned objective, to add a little more normalcy to our lives."
For Mr. Staton, who worked for a large advertising firm before heading to Valent, going back to "Cubeville" has brought "culture shock." Ms. Sweeney still works at home on Fridays but says she misses not being able to work in her pajamas all the time.
It may take a while for their families to get used to the new schedule, said Mr. Antonuccio. "This is the first time I've had an office since 1997," he said. "My kids are really wondering where Daddy goes now every day."

HEADLINE: BUSINESS;
In Atlanta, Both a Feast and a Famine of Jobs

BYLINE: By ANNE BERRYMAN 

DATELINE: ATLANTA

 BODY:
THE layoff announcements have seemed to come in waves in the last month.
The Lockheed Martin Corporation said it would cut 800 workers at its local plant beyond the 2,000 who lost their jobs last year. BellSouth announced that it was eliminating 1,300 local jobs. Worst of all, Coca-Cola, long a bulwark of the economic and social scene here, said that it was laying off 2,500 workers -- 40 percent of its Atlanta work force. 
 In a front-page article, The Atlanta Journal-Constitution asked, "Is something going wrong?" The question echoed worries that Atlanta's economy was in deep trouble.
A wide consensus of economists, government officials and business leaders here contend, however, that if Atlanta's economy has run into a rough patch, it is not because there is a shortage of jobs. On the contrary, they say, there is a lack of people to fill them, particularly in technology.
The job cuts have received wide publicity. But in reality they are not having a big impact on the job market, with unemployment hovering just above 2.5 percent. The job losses make a splash, said David Echols, a statistician at the Georgia Department of Labor, "but the ripple is never going to make it to the shoreline."
Donald Ratajczak, director of the Georgia Economic Forecasting Center at Georgia State University in Atlanta, said: "It's going to hurt. But we think that Atlanta will still be a significant job generator for 2000."
Last year, Atlanta created 113,600 jobs, more than any other major metropolitan area in the nation. By comparison, metropolitan New York, defined as New York City plus Putnam, Rockland and Westchester Counties, created 105,600 jobs in 1999, said Michael L. Wald, regional economist for the United States Department of Labor in the Southeast. New York City alone created 92,100 jobs.
In Atlanta, 15,000 technology workers are needed, said Sam A. Williams, president of the Metro Atlanta Chamber of Commerce, even though the "dot-coms and telecommunications companies in Atlanta added more than 30,000 workers" last year.
"We're talent starved," he added.
So even with the layoffs, the chamber is running advertisements in college newspapers at Rutgers, Harvard and New York Universities, as well as others around the nation. It is also reaching out to students with its own Web site. The ads beckon the young to fast-paced fun and work in Atlanta, where they can "live in the current."
The corporate layoffs, however, have not been the only recent negative publicity for the city. Atlanta's battle with urban sprawl has also been the subject of news reports. So has the seamier side of local night life, after two stabbing deaths here the morning after last month's Super Bowl resulted in murder charges against three men, including Ray Lewis, the professional football star.
State and local officials are keeping a distance from the employment issues. Gov. Roy E. Barnes is concerned about the hardship for workers making transitions to other positions, said Henry Thomassen, the governor's economic adviser. "There is, of course, nothing we can do about it," Mr. Thomassen said.
Mayor Bill Campbell has issued no public statements concerning the job cuts, his spokesman confirmed.
Randy Cardoza, commissioner for the Georgia Department of Industry, Trade and Tourism, said each company had "good solid reasons for doing what they did."
"It's a little hard to interfere with the decisions that corporate America has any right to make," Mr. Cardoza said. "Everybody from a personal standpoint is very sorry for the people who are losing their jobs."
Not all of the workers will have skills to match those needed for technology positions, he said.
FOR many laid-off employees at Coca-Cola, this is an extremely painful time. Few have been willing to talk publicly about it.
"All my friends are being let go," said one employee who has worked for the company for 20 years and who spoke on the condition of anonymity.
She worries about a laid-off worker in his late 40's. "Who's going to hire him?" she asked. She also worries about women who have become their family's primary breadwinners. "They have $200,000 to $300,000 homes and they don't have jobs," she said.
Tim Jacobi, 32, laid off on Tuesday after two years as a Coca-Cola marketing manager, was more sanguine. "I want to stay in Atlanta because of my wife," he said. "I'm hoping to find a decent job here. So, we'll see. It's a strong economy. If ever there were a time to be laid off, it's now."

HEADLINE: BUSINESS: GRASS-ROOTS BUSINESS;
A Revival of Older Suburbs As Ethnic Businesses Take Hold

BYLINE: By JOEL KOTKIN; Joel Kotkin is a senior fellow at the Pepperdine Institute for Public Policy. His column on the Main Street economy appears the fourth Sunday of each month. E-mail: grassrts@nytimes.com

DATELINE: HEMPSTEAD, N.Y.

 BODY:
FOR years, the old central core of this aging Long Island suburb seemed in permanent decline. Yet today, the once nearly deserted shopping areas have become a beehive of activity, much of it fueled by newcomers from Central America.
"When we opened 10 years ago, we were practically the only one," recalled Roberto Rivas, at his crowded Main Street restaurant, Antojitos Salvadorenos. "There wasn't much going on. Now people are opening stores around here." 
 Such activity is a boon for Hempstead. And it is representative of a revival taking place in some old shopping districts in the wave of suburbs that trace their lineage to the building boom after World War II. Once prosperous commercial hubs, some of these areas suffered major declines in the 1970's and 80's, victims of competition from new shopping centers, an aging population and the continued push of affluent whites further to the periphery.
Of course, the current commerce prevalent in downtown Hempstead -- Latin record stores, a dance club, travel agencies, ethnic eateries -- will not remind old-timers of the glory days of the 1950's, when the area was the upscale retail jewel for the burgeoning, and virtually all white, suburbs of central Nassau County. Yet the Hispanic-led surge does represent a marked improvement over more recent times.
The Central Americans "are very entrepreneurial and opening lots of small businesses," said Glen Spiritus, commissioner for community development for the Village of Hempstead.
"The village is changing," he added. "We will never be what we were -- the shopping center for all Long Island -- but we're better."
A wave of immigrants from Central America began arriving in Hempstead in the late 1970's and the 1980's -- some, like the Salvadorans, to escape civil war and others to escape poverty. Like immigrants from elsewhere a generation earlier, they found an economic niche: working in restaurants and taking care of the lawns and the children of people in more affluent neighborhoods nearby. With their earnings, some managed to open businesses; some became customers.
The Hispanics, Mr. Spiritus said, helped reduce the retail vacancy rate in downtown Hempstead to roughly 10 percent, from nearly 70 percent in the early 1990's. In fact, said Emilio Ruiz, publisher of La Tribuna Hispana, a local weekly, the village now serves as a kind of commercial and cultural focal point for other, smaller communities on Long Island, whose Hispanic population is estimated at 200,000 to 250,000.
Similar revivals have taken place in other older suburbs, including the heavily Asian and Latino communities around sprawling Sun Belt cities like Houston, Dallas, Atlanta and Los Angeles.
The new ethnic diversity marks a sharp contrast to the immediate postwar era, when these suburbs were bastions of homogeneity. From 1950 to 1970, a period of intense suburban development, 95 percent of suburbanites were white. "The people you want for neighbors are here," noted an advertisement for one Long Island development in the 1960's.
The demographic shift started in the 1970's, when African-Americans began moving in large numbers to the suburbs. In the next two decades, middle-class American-born Hispanics and Asians as well as upwardly mobile recent immigrants surged in, particularly into the older, closer-in suburbs. Today, nearly 51 percent of Asians, 43 percent of all Hispanics and 32 percent of African-Americans live in suburbs.
The immigrants have moved to suburbia, rather than to the cities, for many of the same reasons that others before them did: cheaper land, more open space, better schools and business opportunities, said Robert Bruegmann, a professor at the University of Illinois at Chicago who has studied suburban development. "It's the classic American pattern."
This development is particularly notable in regions surrounding cites like Los Angeles, New York, San Francisco, Chicago, Washington, Houston and Miami, where immigration has been heaviest.
Perhaps no place has changed more rapidly than the San Fernando Valley, which mushroomed into a major bedroom community in Los Angeles during the postwar boom years. In the 1960's, the valley was roughly 90 percent white. By 1997, according to county estimates, Latinos accounted for roughly 41 percent of the valley population, and Asians 9 percent.
As it has in Hempstead, the movement of immigrants, particularly Latinos, has transformed several shopping areas in the San Fernando Valley into districts that cater to the new population. In many cases, this has meant large new investments like the Gigante supermarket, a 55,000-square-foot, ultra-modern facility in the working-class Arleta neighborhood.
Jose de Jesus Legaspi, a developer who works with Gigante, one of Mexico's largest grocery chains, said the company was increasingly placing its stores not in traditional inner-city barrios like East Los Angeles but in suburbs like Arleta, Buena Park and Walnut.
"People have to realize that the Asian and Latino markets now are primarily suburban," Mr. Legaspi said. "When you have 3.2 million Latinos in Los Angeles County, you can't expect them all to live in the inner city."
THE rush of new businesses has generally been good for these communities, though Mr. Legaspi and other marketing experts are experiencing more competition for the new suburbanite dollar.
In Hempstead, for example, where membership in the Salvadoran Chamber of Commerce has grown from 10 a decade ago to more than 200, Mr. Rivas's restaurant must compete for diners with scores of newcomers.
"It used to be we were the only ones in the market," he said. "Now there's a lot more competition from other Salvadoran people. It makes you have to work harder."

HEADLINE: The Two Faces of Bushwick;
A Troubled Brooklyn Neighborhood Is Mending. But Its Leaders Are Feuding Over the Size of the Gains and What to Do Next.

BYLINE: By JULIAN E. BARNES 

 BODY:
THREE paces down the front walkway, Assemblyman Vito J. Lopez stopped to admire the two-story brick house before him. The home was one of the first his social service organization helped build in Bushwick a decade ago. Since then, he has spurred the construction of hundreds more new houses.
"To me it's beautiful," said Mr. Lopez, a towering man with the sound of Brooklyn in his voice. "You might say it's not Manhattan, but you should have seen what was here before." 
 Another day, inside a church school less than a mile away, Msgr. John J. Powis opened a classroom door and saw a different Bushwick. Five preschoolers, each with a different learning disability, were playing with blocks and crayons. Father Powis boasted of the teachers' ability to ready the children for kindergarten, but he despaired of their future.
"They get all prepared," said Father Powis, who speaks in a soft voice and often wears a sad smile. "And then they get into the public schools and they lose it all."
A decade-long infusion of new housing and a significant drop in crime has formed the foundation of what many neighborhood political leaders call the Bushwick renaissance. But amid the construction projects, an intense discussion has begun over how much Bushwick has really improved and how it should move forward. In many ways, Mr. Lopez and Father Powis represent opposite poles of the debate.
To supporters, Mr. Lopez, 59, a longtime civic and political organizer, is the chief architect of the renaissance. Through his chairmanship of the Assembly's housing committee, his strategic alliances with the Giuliani administration and the sprawling social service organization he founded in 1973, the Ridgewood Bushwick Senior Citizens Council, he has built, renovated or sponsored 1,200 homes and apartments in the last decade.
If Mr. Lopez is the architect of the renaissance, Father Powis, 66, has shouldered the role of chief critic. For the last decade he has served as pastor of the century-old St. Barbara's Roman Catholic Church, one of the most influential institutions in the neighborhood. Father Powis, who speaks fluent Spanish, has rebuilt the congregation as Mr. Lopez rebuilt housing, raising its weekend attendance from 800 to more than 1,400. He has also brought several nonprofit groups to the neighborhood. These groups provide a range of social services and organize residents on issues like school reform.
On one point virtually everyone agrees: life has improved in Bushwick, a poor, largely black and Hispanic neighborhood nestled between Williamsburg and Bedford-Stuyvesant just across the Queens border from Ridgewood. No other neighborhood suffered more when a citywide blackout in July 1977 ignited an outburst of arson and looting. When the embers cooled three years later, Bushwick had lost 20 percent of its housing, a third of its population and nearly half its businesses.
Today, most of the tracts of charred, vacant land have given way to new homes. Between 1990 and 1997, the neighborhood had a net gain of 738 new housing units, a number likely to increase in this year's census count. As in the rest of the city, crime has dropped sharply, as a result of a strong national economy, less crack cocaine use and more aggressive policing. In the last six years, murders are down 72 percent and robberies 58 percent.
But for every gain, a problem lingers. Bushwick has the borough's highest number of children on public assistance and its second highest rate of hospitalization for asthma, and the city's highest rate of childhood lead poisoning. The graduation rate at Bushwick High School is 35 percent, compared with 61 percent for the borough.
With these competing visions as a backdrop, Father Powis and Mr. Lopez have been at odds for more than a decade.
"They don't see eye to eye," said Milagros Negron, a Bushwick resident who has worked for two community organizations. "Vito sees it like he's done all this wonderful work, and Father Powis lives here and doesn't see a change. Wherever you go, you see the friction between them."
The dispute between the men, which at its core is a philosophical difference over how best to help people in need, began over housing and now extends to a long list of issues. Mr. Lopez believes that two-family homes are best for Bushwick. Father Powis supports one-family homes. Mr. Lopez says the police have removed many drug dealers from the streets. Father Powis says the nature of the drugs may have changed, but they still destroy families.
The rivalry is so intense that there are even two local newspapers espousing the two men's view. The People's Voice, founded last September by Father Powis and a community group he brought to the neighborhood, Make the Road by Walking, has praised reform efforts by groups allied with St. Barbara's and criticized the local school superintendent, the police and Mr. Lopez.
Ridgewood Bushwick publishes its own newspaper, The Bushwick Observer, which focuses on the improvements in the community, often crediting them to Mr. Lopez and his allies.
Mr. Lopez believes that Father Powis's critical stance threatens those gains by undermining local morale.
"I believe the neighborhood is starting to turn around," he said. "Does it need improvement? Yes. But the way to do that is not to say everything is no good."
Father Powis takes issue with the idea that examining problems will hurt the neighborhood.
"We are not putting down Bushwick," he said. "We are trying to pull it up."
 
An Old-Fashioned Boss With Big Plans and High Aims
 Mr. Lopez's 6-foot-4-inch frame appears to fill any room he enters. Where his arms do not reach, his booming voice does. His tone and message often shift abruptly. One moment he is the former social worker, eager to discuss how to help the poor. But with little provocation he is the demanding political boss, condemning his detractors or soliciting agreement from supporters.
Mr. Lopez was born in Bensonhurst, took a graduate degree in social work from Yeshiva University, and opened a city-run center for the elderly in Bushwick in 1971. Two years later, he founded Ridgewood Bushwick to provide services the center for the elderly could not offer.
By the time Mr. Lopez and his wife, Joan, from whom he is now divorced, arrived in Bushwick, the neighborhood had begun its transformation from the city's largest Italian neighborhood to a predominantly black and Hispanic one. Mr. Lopez, who has two adult children, is primarily of Italian descent; his surname comes from a Spanish grandfather. When he moved to Bushwick, Mr. Lopez spoke no Spanish, and even today his fluency in the language is limited.
Mr. Lopez was a long shot when he entered the 1984 Democratic primary in pursuit of the Assembly seat being vacated by Victor Robles. His surname helped, but more valuable were the two organizations he controlled, Ridgewood Bushwick and Brooklyn Unidos, his political club.
Because of redistricting, Mr. Lopez now represents only half of Bushwick. The rest of his district is in Williamsburg, where he now lives. Nonetheless, he is the neighborhood's most influential politician, and by virtue of the growth of Bushwick Unidos and Ridgewood Bushwick, one of the most powerful men in Brooklyn. Mr. Lopez has also built power through an eclectic mix of strategic alliances with politicians like Mayor Giuliani, Gov. George E. Pataki and Assemblyman Clarence Norman, the Brooklyn Democratic leader.
Later this year, Mr. Lopez will decide whether to run for public advocate in 2001. Although the office has little power, Mr. Lopez said was is drawn to it because he felt he had accomplished many of his goals in Bushwick, and that the advocate's office had a broader constituency.
His goals have also been influenced by his health. In 1993, two forms of leukemia were diagnosed, and his doctors gave him three years to live. The cancer is in remission, Mr. Lopez says, but chemotherapy has weakened his immune system, leaving him susceptible to other illnesses.
"I have a certain amount of time given to me," Mr. Lopez said. "I'd like to get as much done as I can in the next few years."
If he runs for citywide office, Bushwick's renaissance will be the foundation of his candidacy. On a recent day, he gave a guided before-and-after tour of the neighborhood to make his case for its rejuvenation.
As his van approached a vacant lot, newly fenced but already filling with rubbish, Mr. Lopez ordered the driver to slow down. "That's what Bushwick was, a thousand of these lots that you see here," he said.
Two blocks away on George Street is the home of Lena Rivera, a school bus escort who is a leader of the local block association. The homes on her block were built in 1989, the first erected by the New York City Housing Partnership in conjunction with Ridgewood Bushwick. When Ms. Rivera first moved in, she said, the neighborhood was overwhelmed by crime. Now she is no longer afraid to go out at night, and friends who had left are returning.
"We need a lot more housing just like this," Ms. Rivera said. "This is the best thing that happened to Bushwick."
From there, Mr. Lopez's van drove past condominiums renovated by Ridgewood Bushwick, past two of four federally subsidized apartment buildings for the elderly, past the site of a planned $6 million youth center and a $40 million nursing home under construction, soon to be completed. He concluded his tour at the headquarters of Ridgewood Bushwick at 217 Wyckoff Avenue. According to the most recent available public records, the organization has an annual budget of $6 million, employs 241 people and serves 6,000 individuals each year.
Although home building is the heart of the group's effort, the organization runs several other programs, including legal aid for tenants, apartment winterization, adult education, after-school tutoring and many services for the elderly, including the Christmas Day delivery of 2,700 meals.
"The greatest accomplishment, besides the new housing, is the heightened sense of morale," said Angela M. Battaglia, who heads the group's home-building program. "The people feel united. It's the feeling of, 'I saw the neighborhood burn and I saw it come back.' "
 
A Priest Finds His Ministry On Hard-Scrabble Streets
When Father Powis was ordained in 1959, he said, the Catholic Church did little to prepare priests to serve poor urban parishes, the places in which he has ministered for nearly his entire career. He spent 5 years in Fort Greene and 25 years in Brownsville. In 1989 he was assigned to a parish in the more affluent City Line neighborhood of Brooklyn, where he was born and raised, but he believed his talents could be put to better use in a needier community. So he went to St. Barbara's, where he has been ever since.
Over his black shirt and clerical collar he wears a flannel shirt. It keeps out the cold and makes his parishioners feel at ease. When he speaks, his voice drops almost to a whisper as he comes to an important point, forcing his listeners to pay attention.
But Father Powis's demeanor disguises the role he carved for himself in Bushwick. In the mid-90's he filled the balcony of City Hall with his neighbors in a successful protest against a city plan to eliminate street fire boxes. In 1998, he marched his parishioners to Central Avenue and Menahan Street every Sunday for three months to demand a traffic light, which the city eventually installed. During Easter week, his parishioners parade through the streets acting out the Stations of the Cross; when the man in the role of Jesus stumbles, it is in front of a house where drugs are sold.
"We try to connect religion with real life," Father Powis said. "Religion is not an opiate. You have to be involved and make a change in the community."
He said he admires much of what Ridgewood Bushwick has done, but he sees Mr. Lopez as a man who has used his organization to build up a Tammany-style political machine. Those who receive Ridgewood Bushwick's help, Father Powis says, are expected to support Mr. Lopez, his allies and their civic endeavors.
"Why does he feel he has to control everything?" Father Powis asked.
Mr. Lopez denied that implication, although he acknowledged that the organization encouraged its clients to participate in the community. "If those people at Ridgewood Bushwick wanted to, they could bring 2,500 people together," he said. "They could generate three or four thousand votes. And they have done that repeatedly."
Father Powis also insisted that the neighborhood needs institutions and leaders independent of Ridgewood Bushwick and Mr. Lopez. And Father Powis has inspired an emerging cadre of other dedicated leaders to get involved with neighborhood issues.
"Father Powis was the first activist I ever knew, although I didn't know it was activism then," said Julissa Gonzalez, 17, one of the first female altar servers in St. Barbara's and now a youth organizer for Make the Road by Walking. "He just wants what is right for the community."
Make the Road was founded in 1997 by two New York University Law School graduates, Andrew Friedman and Oona Chatterjee. Father Powis helped bring the pair to Bushwick and gave them temporary space in St. Barbara's until they moved to a Grove Street storefront the following year.
Make the Road has 11 employees and a projected budget of $500,000, almost double last year's because of increased grants from private foundations, Mr. Friedman said. Last year the group offered 2,000 people legal counseling and served 800 at its food pantry.
In addition to those services, Make the Road spends much of its time organizing residents to help themselves. It formed a tenants' group that has sponsored cleanups of vacant lots and protests for more parks. A committee of Spanish-speaking women organized by Make the Road has lobbied federal officials for better translation services at welfare offices and hospitals.
"If you love somewhere, what you need to do is what people do with their children," Mr. Friedman said. "You work to make them stronger, better and more vital."
 
A Long-Standing Rivalry Intensified by Press Volleys
 Although the ill feeling between Mr. Lopez and Father Powis is of long standing, the newspaper published by Make the Road has intensified the rivalry. The second issue of The People's Voice in December contained a long editorial that attacked Mr. Lopez and his allies for "the stranglehold that they have in this community."
The Bushwick Observer, published by Ridgewood Bushwick, touts neighborhood improvements like the construction of a new park and other achievements of Mr. Lopez. By contrast, The People's Voice has kind words for reform efforts led by Father Powis's allies and criticizes institutions the other publication routinely praises. The founders of The People's Voice assert that Mr. Lopez has focused too narrowly on housing and the elderly.
"There are new buildings," Mr. Friedman said. "That's a terrific accomplishment, maybe. But that that's not what it is about. That's not enough. It's sickening how far from enough that is."
That assessment has clearly annoyed Mr. Lopez, who in conversation mangles Make the Road by Walking's name, perhaps intentionally, calling it Walk Don't Run.
"They should roll up their sleeves and build some housing," he said.
It was over the issue of housing that the rivalry between the two men began a decade ago. Father Powis wanted to bring to Bushwick the Nehemiah housing program -- a program to build affordable single-family houses in poor neighborhoods -- that he had helped start in Brownsville.
Mr. Lopez said the Nehemiah program required large tracks of vacant land, which were unavailable in Bushwick. Instead, he supported the New York City Partnership for Housing's program. Partnership homes can be built on any vacant city-owned lot.
Father Powis believes that the Partnership houses require too many subsidies, cost too much for many working people, and drive up rents on unimproved buildings nearby. The houses currently being sponsored by Ridgewood Bushwick cost $235,000 to build and are sold for $185,000.
The two men also differ on crime and drugs. In recent years the drug trade in Bushwick has shifted from crack to marijuana, and as a result violent crime has declined. But illegal drug use continues.
Mr. Lopez acknowledges that. But he ridicules Father Powis's portrayal of Bushwick as a place where drug dealers' bullets are heard through the night.
"Whatever you want to think of the neighborhood, crime is now down and people want to stay in the community," Mr. Lopez said. "No one lies with those statistics."
Father Powis, by contrast, contends that the drug trade remains strong and continues to devastate the community, encouraging students to drop out of school and become petty dealers. A study by John Jay College of Criminal Justice shows that among Bushwick residents between 18 and 24, 45 percent of blacks and 35 percent of Latinos use marijuana regularly. Despite the thousands of drug arrests each year, Father Powis said the police need to do far more to control the problem.
"So crack is down, but drugs in general are not," he said. "Kids still depend on drugs for their livelihood."
If an area of partial agreement exists between Father Powis and Mr. Lopez, it is over the state of the schools. Both men agree they desperately need improvement, and both have helped create after-school and summer programs for students. But they disagree over who bears responsibility for the generally dismal achievement scores for children in the district. Father Powis says Mr. Lopez should use his political influence to fight to remove the current district superintendent, Felix Vasquez. Mr. Lopez says the issue is not the superintendent, but the neighborhood's poverty.
Some Bushwick residents say that certain issues get lost in the argument between the two men.
"I think they are both about building their own empires," said Rick Curtis, an associate professor at John Jay College who has studied drug use in Bushwick for a decade. "I don't know if they are concerned about the neighborhood as a whole, or just their constituency or their parishioners."
But Dr. Curtis may hold a minority view. Most Bushwick residents seem to think that either Father Powis or Mr. Lopez offers the best strategy for helping the neighborhood.
Jose Olmo, 41, a behavior specialist at a school near Tampa, Fla., left Bushwick last year after 10 years of leading several nonprofit groups in the neighborhood and watching the two spar. Mr. Olmo is an ally of Father Powis who believes that both men's resolves remain firm.
"I really see these guys as two heavyweight champions who are boxing it out for 15 rounds," Mr. Olmo said. "Now it's the 14th round. We will have to see who has the strength to last."

HEADLINE: YOUR TAXES;
Making the Most of That Stock-Option Gold

BYLINE: By ABBY ELLIN 

 BODY:
THOSE poor dot-commies. Not only do they work themselves to the bone, but they also face the ominous task of paying taxes on all their hot stock options.
Of course, not every option holder is a millionaire. After all, it isn't easy to pick a start-up whose stock will take off when it goes public, enabling insiders to exercise options for pennies that will be immediately worth megadollars. 
 And for those who have chosen their companies well, it can still be a daunting task to figure out what their holdings are worth in a very volatile market, when to exercise their options and how to keep taxes from gobbling up their gains.
Consider the experience of Abhi Shelat, who at 24 has exercised two kinds of options that have become widespread in the New Economy: incentive stock options, intended to motivate executives to make sure the company does well, and nonstatutory stock options, which may be awarded by new companies in lieu of cash compensation.
Directly out of Harvard, where he earned a degree in computer science in 1997, Mr. Shelat was the first person to be hired by E.piphany Inc. (then called Epiphany Marketing Software), which develops software for managing customer relationships. Two and a half years later, he is worth several million dollars -- "I don't want to give a fixed value," he said -- and is a graduate student at M.I.T.
E.piphany, based in San Mateo, Calif., went public last September at $16 a share, and its stock soared to $275 in just three months. It is now trading at $187.
Mr. Shelat was hired at $72,000 a year and was granted incentive stock options, or I.S.O.'s, that would have allowed him to buy stock in the company at 6 cents a share. Originally, that would have given him 0.45 percent of the company, but that stake was diluted when the company issued additional stock.
During his tenure -- he quit in February 1999 -- he received two additional stock option grants allowing him to buy shares at 15 and 25 cents apiece. These strike prices, as they are known, differed from those in his first options because, in order to receive the best tax treatment when an I.S.O. is exercised, the strike price cannot be less than the fair market value at the time the options are granted.
"With an I.S.O., there are no tax ramifications on the granting of the options or when you exercise the option and buy the stock," said Prof. Patrick Colabella, a certified public accountant and chairman of the Global Institute for Taxation at St. John's University in Jamaica, Queens, which researches new taxation methods nationwide.
Rather, taxes will be due after the stock is sold. If the stock is not sold until two years from the date the option was granted or one year from the date it was exercised, whichever is later, any capital gain will be treated as long term, subject to a maximum tax of 20 percent at the federal level. "Should you sell the stock before that time, you'll have to treat the gain as compensation, or ordinary income," Professor Colabella said.
So anyone who believes that his stock will keep climbing has a strong tax incentive to delay selling. But those who fear that their highfliers may be coming down to earth may want to sell quickly -- even though that means paying taxes as high as 39.6 percent at the federal level.
The tough choices do not end there, Professor Colabella said. Once options are exercised, the difference between the strike price and the fair market value of the stock is considered income for purposes of the alternative minimum tax, which is intended to make sure that affluent taxpayers do not take advantage of too many tax breaks. Under the A.M.T., the definition of income is more inclusive than it is for the regular tax system, and certain deductions are denied. The A.M.T. rate for upper-income taxpayers is 28 percent.
"At this point you own the shares but have not realized any cash gains," Professor Colabella said. "So although you don't think you owe any tax, you very well might. And if you're in a borderline situation, you'll unexpectedly pay an A.M.T. on all or part of the unrealized gain."
With that potential pitfall in mind, Mr. Shelat took steps to minimize his tax liability.
In his original agreement with the company, it would take four years for all his options to "vest," meaning that he would retain ownership even if he left the company; one-eighth would vest every six months. But E.piphany allowed him to exercise 75 percent of his options after the company went public. Because that exceeded the vested percentage, Mr. Shelat signed a repurchase agreement that allowed the company to buy back any stock he purchased with the unvested portion.
Professor Colabella said the new agreement "spreads out the A.M.T. impact" for Mr. Shelat.
Finally, Mr. Shelat submitted what is known as an 83b election form (the name referring to a section of tax law) to the Internal Revenue Service, informing it that he would rather be taxed on the difference between the strike price and the fair market value in the current tax year than in the year in which he decided to sell the shares. That meant that he would be subject to a higher tax rate, but he hopes that it will be on a lower overall gain than if he held on for a long time and the shares soared.
AFTER leaving E.piphany last year, Mr. Shelat worked as a consultant for a friend's company and was given the second type of option -- nonstatutory options, known as N.S.O.'s, which he exercised soon afterward. Those options were taxable as compensation when they were exercised.
"The whole tax thing is very confusing, but it's possible to figure out," Mr. Shelat said. He prepared his return last year using Turbotax, the Intuit software program, and he intends to do so again this year. And he said he had learned the tax consequences of exercising his options and selling or holding the stock "from older people who have gone through the same kinds of things and the I.R.S. Web site."
In total, Mr. Shelat said, "I think I paid around $18,000" in taxes in 1998. "This year, I expect to pay a lot more from capital gains and from the N.S.O. options," he added.
Referring to a Hindu goddess of prosperity, Mr. Shelat said: "Laksmi smiled on me during my brief foray in the Valley rat race. If I owned 0.45 percent" of E.piphany today, "it would be worth nearly $24 million." Now, he said, his holdings are "greater than several million dollars, and less than $24 million."
Mr. Shelat, who grew up in Austin, Tex., is happy to be back in school and not have to worry about finances for a while. He won a National Science Foundation fellowship that paid his tuition and a stipend at M.I.T., and he bought a house in the Beacon Hill section of Boston. He lives frugally, although he plans to buy his mother a new car; he is quick to point out that he has not yet treated himself to a television set nor indulged his penchant for Zegna suits.
"The best outcome from my E.piphany detour is not the money, but rather that I figured out how to be fulfilled in my life," he said. In addition to teaching and researching in computer science, he plans to make a documentary film about diplomats, run a marathon in less than seven-minute miles, work on a Spike Lee film and learn how to build Shaker-style furniture. "The secret," he said, "is to find something that you really just love to do and pursue it with utopian exuberance and reckless abandon."

SECTION: Section 3; Page 1; Column 1; Money and Business/Financial Desk 

LENGTH: 2652 words

HEADLINE: The Tax Maze Begins Here. No,Here. No . . .

BYLINE: By DAVID CAY JOHNSTON 

 BODY:
MUCH of the tax agenda that Republicans promised in the "Contract With America" has been enacted into law in the last five years. But as Newt Gingrich, the contract's author, is fond of saying: There is no free lunch.
The contract's new tax breaks for middle-class parents, investors, the self-employed and others have come at a price that millions of Americans are discovering as they engage in the annual ritual of filing their income tax returns: a complexity that might make even Rube Goldberg indignant. 
 Federal tax laws and regulations filled 40,500 pages when the Republicans took charge of Capitol Hill in 1995, according to CCH Inc., a publisher of tax information. In the last five years, 6,400 pages have been added, CCH said, making the tax laws 16 percent longer. That pace of growth, almost 10 percent faster than in the previous five years, required major revisions of tax forms for individuals.
And the time it takes to fill out a tax return is growing, too. The Internal Revenue Service estimates that it will take a taxpayer 35.8 hours to fill out a Form 1040 and Schedules A through D, up from 30.6 hours five years ago and 28.6 hours in 1989.
People with doctoral degrees and even some tax lawyers and accountants say they find themselves stumped by the requirements to qualify for some of the new tax breaks and complete the necessary forms. Indeed, last week, the American Bar Association's tax section, the American Institute of Certified Public Accountants and the Tax Executives Institute jointly urged Congress to make tax simplification a priority.
Joseph Harris, a retired professor of neurochemistry at Arizona State University in Tempe, said that for years he and his wife, Irene Dorothy, did their own taxes.
But after Mrs. Harris died in 1998, the professor decided that the tax system's rules on capital gains had become too complex even for his numerically inclined brain.
The I.R.S. instruction booklet, combined with documents sent to him by investment houses, indicated that he had a huge capital gains tax bill on stocks he sold after his wife's death. In fact, no taxes were due, because Arizona is one of nine community property states where, when one spouse dies, the other avoids capital gains on investment gains recorded until the day of the death. Professor Harris's brokerage firms had overlooked that provision in reports to him, and the I.R.S. instructions were unclear.
It took a visit to LaVon Martin, a Phoenix accountant, to straighten out Mr. Harris's Schedule D, the form for reporting capital gains and losses on investments.
"At one time, I thought about becoming a lawyer, so I am not averse to legal thinking," Professor Harris said. "But by the standards of what I was used to trying to understand, the tax law had suddenly become much much more complicated. English is a beautiful language, because you can describe things so elegantly and with such precise shades of meaning that you can make everything fine and sharp, but what I found was muddled."
Much of the complexity is testimony to the political horse-trading by which tax laws get written. After 1997, Schedule D grew from a simple 23-line form into a mind-boggler with 36 lines devoted just to calculating if capital gains must be paid at the higher rates for earned income.
Representative Bill Archer, the Texas Republican who is chairman of the House Ways and Means Committee, said that "much of the complexity was due to compromises Republicans had to make to get President Clinton's signature on their bills."
"President Clinton wanted 'targeted tax relief,' which meant that the tax code became more complicated," he added. "For instance, the Schedule D capital gains form is a direct result of the Clinton White House rejecting our approach that would have made capital gains taxes very simple," by taxing half the gains.
What Mr. Archer calls simplification the White House sees as favors for the rich, resulting in its insistence on rules to phase out many of the tax breaks as incomes rise.
Elliott Manning, a law professor at the University of Miami in Florida, and Lawrence M. Andress, a tax lawyer in Coral Gables, Fla., made an exhaustive study of how these phase-out rules, which critics call "clawbacks," increase the real tax rates that people pay.
The highest statutory rate, 39.6 percent, applies to incomes of more than $288,350. But the highest real rate in 1996, the study found, was 93 percent -- paid not by the rich, but by an individual with income of $27,327 to $32,768 who was 65 to 69 years old, collecting Social Security and working at a low-paying job. Congress is considering repealing reductions in Social Security benefits for retirees who return to work.
"The phase-outs hide the real rates people pay," Professor Manning said.
Taxpayers are not the only ones drowning in complexity. One 1998 law made more than 1,200 changes in the code that governs I.R.S. operations, the agency estimated. Here are some ways that the tax law has grown muddier over the last few years -- and accounts of taxpayers' efforts to cope.
 
Problems With Phase-Outs
A tax credit of $500 per child was the centerpiece of the Taxpayer Relief Act of 1997, the most comprehensive of recent tax laws. But instead of a simple tax credit for all parents, something much more complex resulted from negotiations between Congressional Republicans and the Clinton administration.
Families with three or more children cannot fully benefit, because the maximum credit is $1,000. And because the credit can be used only to write income taxes down to zero, a family with two or more children and an income tax bill of less than $1,000 gets only part of the credit.
Many other parents lose, too: the credit begins to phase out for single parents with $75,000 of income and for married couples with $110,000 -- squarely middle-class households in high-cost areas like the urban centers on either coast.
David and Barbara Gordon of Liverpool, N.Y., a suburb of Syracuse, were frustrated to learn from their accountant, Marc Albaum of New York, that the credit wasn't meant for them. The phase-out rules will reduce their credit this year from $1,000 to just $100 -- not much help with the costs of raising David, 11, and his sister Shaina, 9.
"I think it is unfair," said Mrs. Gordon, who is studying for a doctorate in geology at Syracuse University and working as a freelance consultant; her husband is an engineer at Lockheed Martin. "I work as hard for my money as anybody else," she said. "My children are just as important as anyone else's children are."
There are at least 11 education tax breaks, according to H & R Block, including tax-free interest on some United States Savings Bonds and the new Hope and lifetime learning credits, each with its own rules and phase-outs.
In customer surveys, Block found that many people were overwhelmed by the number of options, said Kathy Burlison, Block's manager of tax operations training.
 
I.R.A. Intricacies
Most of Arthur A. Spetter's peers have retired. But at 67, Mr. Spetter still turns up every morning to teach social studies at Adrian Block School in Flushing, Queens -- "even though economically it is costing me money, because I like teaching," he explained.
By collecting a pension and Social Security, Mr. Spetter could have more money in his pocket than he earns after taxes and expenses.
A few years back, he did take advantage of an opportunity to pull his retirement savings out of a state-run plan and roll them into Individual Retirement Accounts.
"Good thing I did that," Mr. Spetter said, "because when Congress said you could covert to a Roth I.R.A. and your earnings would never be taxed again -- and they said you could spread the taxes on the I.R.A. you were converting over four years -- I did it."
A rule in effect only in 1998 allowed people to undo their Roth conversions and then reconvert, as often as they wished. With stocks rising to new heights and then falling as economic turmoil in Asia spread to Russia, Brazil and the Long Term Capital hedge fund, many people switched in and out of Roths -- including Mr. Spetter, who did so a total of five times over the range of his investments.
Why all the switching? Because with a Roth, you pay taxes on your contributions, and then make withdrawals tax free; with a traditional I.R.A., you invest pretax dollars and pay taxes on withdrawals. So as stocks fell, it made sense to withdraw shrunken assets from a Roth, convert them to a traditional I.R.A. and then move back into a Roth.
This was a short-lived instance of complexity in the tax code offering real benefits for taxpayers who could understand and surmount it. But Mr. Spetter's mutual fund company, like many others, was not fully prepared to negotiate the twists and turns of I.R.A. conversions and reconversions, and it sent him and the I.R.S. inaccurate reports that would have cost Mr. Spetter a painful sum, had he not caught the errors.
Mr. Spetter, who studied for an M.B.A. and is certified to teach accounting to high school students, said he complained, but the fund company "never got it straight."
"Finally," he said, "they sent me a check for $500 and told me to go hire a professional to straighten it out."
He could figure what he owed the I.R.S., Mr. Spetter said. But putting it down correctly on his tax return was another matter. So he went to Ed Slott, an accountant in Rockville Centre, N.Y., who publishes a newsletter on retirement accounts.
"Art understood what he owed, but he tried and tried and could not figure out how to make it flow through the tax forms to produce the right number," Mr. Slott said. "When I finally got through this, his number was right, but the process is so mind-boggling that it is sending people like Art with a very good education in accounting to specialists to deal with retirement account problems."
Another of his clients, Joe Ciaccio, a retired teacher in Levittown, N.Y., said he was told by his mutual fund company that he and his wife, Eileen, could each contribute $2,000 annually to a Roth I.R.A., because their income was far below the $150,000 at which married couples begin losing eligibility.
Such advice is wrong, however: A person must have earned income to contribute to a Roth I.R.A., and the couple's income was almost entirely from pensions and Social Security.
"I put $12,000 in over three years, and then Slott tells me you can't do that," said Mr. Ciaccio, who had to withdraw all but $1,800 from their Roths. "When the mutual funds can't keep track of all the rules, there are just too many of them."
 
Health Care Concerns
As an architect, mall owner and now an urban planning consultant, Angelo J. Chiarella is used to working on intricately detailed projects. But he said that even his experience as chairman of Via Health, a nonprofit health care provider that owns four hospitals in Rochester, did not prepare him to understand Medical Savings Accounts.
The accounts became available in 1996. They allow individuals to invest pretax dollars in accounts where the money can grow tax free until it is needed to cover medical expenses.
The Golden Rule Insurance Company of Indianapolis, which sought the legislation, wanted the accounts to cover anyone and with rules that it said would benefit people who were already ill as well as those who were saving to cover future medical bills.
At first blush, Mr. Chiarella thought he might be an ideal candidate for a Medical Savings Account, based on articles he had read that emphasized their value to the self-employed. But the compromises in the law enacted by Congress and signed by President Clinton made his decision very complicated.
"I'm over 65 now and on Medicare," he said, "but Medicare doesn't cover prescription drugs." He figured that he could use one of the new accounts to pay his prescription bills with tax-advantaged dollars.
But the more he learned about the accounts, the more befuddled he became. "I found that the formula for what's deductible, and how much the deductions are worth, is beyond complex," Mr. Chiarella said.
He turned to his accountant, Steven G. Schwartz of Rotenberg & Company in Rochester, for advice.
"Valuing this benefit requires a lot of tax gymnastics," Mr. Schwartz said. "Three different deduction limits apply. Out-of-pocket expenses -- which under the Medical Savings Account are capped at $5,500 -- are deductible, but only after they exceed 7.5 percent of your income. Then there is the insurance premium, which for my client's family would be $3,151, which is 60 percent deductible. And there is the Medical Savings Account contribution, which is 65 percent deductible for my client, because he is self-employed."
Mr. Chiarella had one question: How much would he save if he opened a Medical Savings Account, rather than paying for a policy to fill in gaps in Medicare coverage?
Mr. Schwartz could not answer that question. "From my point of view, the issue became impossible," he said. "There are so many uncertain factors that I have no idea how to make an informed opinion for my client about whether this is a benefit he should take or not."
Mr. Chiarella said he decided to go with his gut and not open an account. 'I don't think it is worth it," he said. "But what I don't understand is why we get a tax law with a series of convoluted rationalizations instead of straightforward policy."
 
I.R.S. Keeps Eye on Earned Income Credit
THE last thing that many middle-class taxpayers may care about when it comes to taxes is the Earned Income Tax Credit, a negative income tax intended to encourage people to work instead of going on welfare.
They might think again: divorce, disability or just a spell of joblessness could put them in line for the credit. But instead of having their tax obligations wiped out or collecting up to $3,888, they might face a protracted struggle with the Internal Revenue Service.
"Many people think that tax complexity applies only to high-income people, but it also affects the poor, especially when family and tax matters come together," said Nina E. Olson, executive director of the Community Tax Law Project in Richmond.
The I.R.S. is under orders from Congress to crack down on abuse of the credit. And the agency is getting so picky that even minor errors on a return, like mixing up the names and Social Security numbers of children, can cause a claim to be rejected.
Just applying for the credit adds complexity for low-income taxpayers. People without children who make less than $50,000 can use Form 1040EZ. But those with children must file the more complicated Form 1040A; additional forms are needed to deduct child care expenses and get the Earned Income Tax Credit.
Louisa L. Ramirez, 25, of Jersey City said she had never filed a tax return before this year, when she picked Marc Albaum, a Manhattan tax accountant, out of the telephone book and hired him to bring her tax situation up to date. She acted, she said, because she had heard she "could get some percentage off my taxes," because she has a 2-year-old daughter, Anya.
Miss Ramirez, who made $15,000 last year, qualified for a tax credit of $1,882.
One further complication to be aware of: The first person to use a Social Security number on a tax return is presumed by the I.R.S. to be entitled to it. So if an estranged spouse files first, claiming exemptions for the children even if they live with the other parent, it can be a headache to straighten out the mess.
"The I.R.S. sometimes takes more than three years to get around to these situations, which means they can't get the money back from the other spouse and they have to pay it out twice," said Sandy Freund, director of the Rutgers Law School tax clinic in Newark. "The I.R.S. hates to eat it and pay twice." DAVID CAY JOHNSTON

HEADLINE: For Gatekeepers at Colleges, A Daunting Task of Sorting

SERIES: Admission Maze

BYLINE: By JACQUES STEINBERG 

DATELINE: MIDDLETOWN, Conn.

 BODY:
The two applications on the card table in front of Raphael Figueroa were for admission to Wesleyan University, sifted from 6,849 requests for 715 seats in the Class of 2004.
One 17-year-old was a standout, an Asian-American from California whose score in the high 1400's on the College Board exam, out of a possible 1,600, earned her a coveted berth as a National Merit Semifinalist. 
 The other, a Dominican from New York City, had struggled, her score in the 900's on the College Boards and "D" in English hardly representative of the private school she was attending on scholarship.
Seated on a folding metal chair on the porch of his home here, the heat off to keep him awake, Mr. Figueroa, an admissions officer, spent 11 hours contemplating whether either of them -- along with 20 others under review that snowy February day -- merited entrance to Wesleyan, a liberal arts college that is among the most selective in the nation.
This is the season when such hard choices are made -- indeed, they have never been harder -- and like many in college admissions, Mr. Figueroa's was personal and even idiosyncratic, reflecting not only shifting views of academic merit but Mr. Figueroa's own background.
Though the Californian's SAT scores exceeded the median of those applying to Wesleyan (1,350), and her race and West Coast origin were in high demand, Mr. Figueroa finally circled the preliminary recommendation "deny." Among his concerns, he said, was that she had not selected very challenging courses.
The New Yorker, despite scores that ranked her among the bottom of this year's applicants, got a tentative "admit." Mr. Figueroa liked the portrait of a scrapper and born leader that emerged from her essay and teacher recommendations. But he conceded that he also saw a grainy reflection of himself -- he is the sixth of seven children raised by Mexican-born parents in California -- staring back from her manila folder.
"She's an academic risk," he said. "It's one I'm willing to take."
Mr. Figueroa is an anxious sentry at Wesleyan's front gate these days, and like those doing similar work at dozens of other selective colleges and universities, his job is tougher than ever .
By every measure, last year was the most competitive on record for students seeking admission to the nation's top colleges. Harvard rejected more than 9 of every 10 students who applied. Columbia spurned suitors at a similar rate. Wesleyan rebuffed 7 out of 10.
Fueled by an increase in the number of high school graduates and a growing perception that top educational credentials create the best job opportunities in an information-based economy, this admissions season is on track to break many of last year's records -- applications to Harvard and Columbia are up 3 percent; those to Middlebury are up 6 percent; Wesleyan, 7 percent.
And while admissions officers are having a hard enough time swimming in such deep applicant pools, they are also fighting a fierce current, one being churned by the rewriting of decades-old affirmative action policies at state universities in California, Texas, Florida and Washington, where critics have put deans on notice that they believe whites have been treated unfairly.
By the last week of March, when Wesleyan and most other colleges mail their decisions, Mr. Figueroa and his eight colleagues here will have read enough applications to fill the freshman class 10 times over.
Because many of those admitted will decide to go elsewhere, about 1,900 students, or 28 percent, will be invited to take one of the 715 spots in Wesleyan's freshman class next fall; 4,949 others, or 72 percent, will not.
 
An Art, Not a Science
The daunting task of determining who makes the cut and who does not is anything but scientific.
Mr. Figueroa, 35, and his colleagues will consider an applicant's grades, class rank and test scores, of course, and an SAT score below 1,400 would immediately be considered a negative. But it would hardly be a deal-breaker.
That's because the officers will also weigh a student's race, ethnicity and hometown, as well as course selections, athletic prowess, alumni connections, artistic skill, musical talent, writing ability, community service and the quality of the school. (A student's means to pay Wesleyan's annual tuition, room and board -- $31,600 -- is not to be considered by the admissions officers.)
The officers will assign the applicant a rating of 1 to 9, with 9 highest, in a half-dozen broad categories. Applicants be warned: letters of recommendation from members of Congress or Gen. Colin L. Powell; boxes of cookies sent by priority mail, and essays about discovering yourself at summer camp have, this year as in others, provided little added traction.
In the end, the officers will often rely as much on their gut feelings as on numbers, balancing an applicant's chances for academic excellence at Wesleyan against the broader contribution the applicant might make to the college community and, ultimately, the world.
Mr. Figueroa, echoing colleagues at Wesleyan and elsewhere, sees himself as a social engineer, one charged with making sure that the Class of 2004 is not only academically sound and ethnically and racially diverse, but also well-stocked with poets, running backs, activists, politicians, painters, journalists and cellists. That philosophy trickles from the very top of the university, from Douglas J. Bennet, Wesleyan's president since 1995.
"It's a mistake to hold out that total fairness is the only objective," said Mr. Bennet, who graduated from Wesleyan in 1959, though he wondered if he would be admitted today. "It's intuitive. It's a guess, to some degree, about where an individual at 16 or 17 will end up at 18 or 20."
Mr. Figueroa's first word in the process will not necessarily be the last, though it often foreshadows an applicant's fate.
From January to early March, each application will be read by at least two officers -- the first selected at random, the second because he or she covers the particular region from which the student is applying. If the readers concur, the director or dean of admissions, who will review the recommendations, will probably endorse them.
But often the officers' verdicts are at odds. And the fate of hundreds of applicants will be left unresolved until late March, when the officers will convene for several days of fiery committee meetings.
 
Role of Gatekeepers' Pasts
The Wesleyan admissions officers are encouraged to bring their own life experiences to the deliberations. For it is their own diversity, both of race and of interests, that the university hopes to replicate in Wesleyan's next class.
One of the nine, for example, is a white, former Philadelphia high school teacher, age 39. Another, who is 25, grew up in Hawaii. A third, now 53, was director of a community halfway house before he came to Wesleyan more than 20 years ago. A fourth graduated from Wesleyan last year with a double major in English and African-American studies.
Mr. Figueroa grew up in Southern California. His mother's father, a miner in Mexico, did not attend college but took engineering courses by mail. Mr. Figueroa still keeps his grandfather's worn correspondence books in his office. And his grandfather's mantra -- "I'd give anything to have worked with my head, and not my hands" -- served as a prod to Mr. Figueroa's father, who became a lawyer, and his mother, who obtained a master's in bilingual education. Mr. Figueroa and his six siblings all graduated from college, and most also received graduate degrees.
Even with a College Board score of 1,400, Mr. Figueroa believes his own application to Stanford University would have been considered anything but unique, had he not been of Mexican descent. The experience of attending and graduating from Stanford, as well as the University of California Los Angeles Law School, has made him a passionate defender of affirmative action.
"These are students who have overcome hardship," he says. "And these are students who, the evidence shows, are much more likely to go back to their communities as leaders."
It was his gratitude for the gifts of affirmative action, he said, and his high regard for his mother's work as a college counselor to Mexican children, that persuaded him to bypass a legal career for admissions.
 
Picking and Choosing
This year's admissions season, at Wesleyan and elsewhere, began in November, when the officers began considering applicants who sought early admission, on the promise that they would attend Wesleyan if accepted. Some 40 percent of the new freshman class was taken up by those accepted early.
But Mr. Figueroa and his colleagues have read the bulk of the applications, including personal statements, high school transcripts and references, over the last few months, from 9 a.m. until 11 at night, six days a week, mostly from home. Of the 22 applications Mr. Figueroa read one recent day, he tentatively recommended admitting nine candidates -- which, he worried, was too many. Accepting no more than six of every 22, given the size of the pool, is more of a goal, he suggested. He says he is always telling himself, "Read faster, say no."
Among the applicants who made strong impressions on Mr. Figueroa that day were a young woman who had competed in the United States National Chemistry Olympiad and a young man who wrote about saving someone's life.
The prospects were less clear for a young Connecticut man who devoted his personal statement to the rules of his Italian family -- "If you're wise to your grandmother, it's a slap upside your head."
But it was the applications of the California merit scholar and the struggling Dominican from New York that ate up much of Mr. Figueroa's time, nearly two hours.
He was disappointed that the merit scholar had taken few advanced placement courses at her top-notch public school and had several C's.
Mr. Figueroa recommended denying the woman admission -- but he added the designation "plus," which meant that if another reader strongly disagreed with him, the committee should discuss it.
The Dominican woman from New York was a harder call. Her grades were worse than the Californian's, as well several others whom Mr. Figueroa had recommended rejecting that day.
But Mr. Figueroa liked that she had gotten herself a scholarship to private school, that she had taken leadership positions there and that, like his own parents, she had already exceeded the education level of her parents. He marked her application "admit minus" meaning he wanted it discussed by the committee.
"Spanish is her first language," he said of the New Yorker, reading to a visitor from her application. "Her parents didn't go to school."
"Coming from the Southwest," he said, "I know that 1 in 4 high school dropouts is a Latina female."
Though Wesleyan -- where this year's freshman class is 9 percent black and 7 percent Latino -- has no quotas for minorities, the officers are encouraged to admit as many as possible. Mr. Figueroa said that such cases often kept him up at night.
"You can't help but thinking, 'Those kids are out there somewhere. They don't even realize we're sitting here and agonizing about them and spending time really worried about their lives and what's happening to them,' " he said.
But a moment later, his mind drifting to the sheer number of applicants for so few slots, he added: "They're going to end up in good places. They're going to have good lives. I don't have the ability to bring them all to Wesleyan."
 
Admissions Maze
Admission to elite colleges is more competitive than at any time in history. The New York Times asked Wesleyan University in Middletown, Conn., to give a reporter full access to it's selection procedure. It did so, permitting him to read applications and attend admissions meetings, on the condition that applicants' identities not be revealed. This is the first of several articles on that process.

 CORRECTION:
An article on Sunday about the admission process at Wesleyan University misstated the criterion used in selecting a 17-year-old student as a National Merit Semifinalist. Semifinalists are chosen through the preliminary College Board exam in their junior year, not the College Board reasoning test.

SECTION: Section 14WC; Page 1; Column 5; Westchester Weekly Desk
