HEADLINE: FALLING BACK: A special report.;
Puerto Rican Presence Wanes in New York

BYLINE: By MIREYA NAVARRO 

 BODY:
A stretch of 116th Street in East Harlem -- increasingly marked by Mexican restaurants and Dominican bodegas -- nonetheless still bears the name "Luis Munoz Marin Boulevard" after the first native son governor of Puerto Rico. On the blocks around "El Barrio," Puerto Rican music still wafts out of certain storefronts.
And Maria Martinez, 62, hangs no fewer than three Puerto Rican flags from her apartment window and fence. "The truth is," she likes to say with a bit of Puerto Rican defiance, "we got here first." 
 These days, though, it is hard to resist the sense that Ms. Martinez is hanging her flags with as much a feeling of nostalgia as pride.
Being Hispanic in New York City used to mean being Puerto Rican. But over the last decade, the number of Puerto Rican New Yorkers decreased for the first time since they began arriving in the city in great numbers 60 years ago -- down by nearly 100,000, or 11 percent, according to data from the Department of City Planning. With the growing presence of Dominicans and Mexicans, Puerto Ricans now account for 37 percent of the city's Hispanic population. Experts say the decline is destined to continue.
In certain ways, city planners say, the trend reflects a traditional immigration pattern: the dispersion of groups from the city to the suburbs and other parts of the country as they make economic headway. But unlike most traditional immigrant groups who came to New York during the early part of the 1900's, more than a third of Puerto Ricans leaving the city have moved to Puerto Rico, including significant numbers of people born in the States.
For many Puerto Ricans, their diminishing presence has provided them with a moment to look back on, and assess, the Puerto Rican experience in New York.
Although there has been no shortage of success stories -- from political gains and lasting contributions to the arts to the less visible but more pervasive expansion of the middle and professional classes -- there is much about the fate of Puerto Ricans that remains puzzling. Why, for instance, are so many among the poorest of the city's poor?
In the 1990's, the percentage of Puerto Rican households in the city living at or below the poverty line increased despite a strong local economy, to a rate greater than that of any other group. According to the most recent data, about 40 percent of New York's Puerto Ricans qualified as poor, a figure considerably higher than that of African-Americans and worse than the average rate for all Hispanics.
Taking stock of the widely differing fortunes of Puerto Ricans in the city, Luis Garden Acosta, founder of the youth agency El Puente and a former member of the Young Lords Party, the militant Puerto Rican group of the 1960's, said, "It's been a period of exploration, of very strong community building, of coming to grips with American reality, with the wonderful promise of great opportunity starkly contrasted sometimes by the horrible aspects of our existence."
Many researchers say there is not enough scholarship to lay out a definitive explanation of why so many Puerto Ricans in the city continue to struggle. It is also difficult to track the poor: are Puerto Ricans -- who as American citizens came to New York as migrants -- mostly a static group, or a constantly changing group driven by the frequent back-and-forth movement between the States and the island?
But there have emerged some dominant theories on the progress of Puerto Ricans as a group, some involving a question of bad timing, others centering on the question of whether their American citizenship has been a mixed blessing in this country. Researchers note that because citizenship has meant no barriers to entry into the United States, the proportion of poor people among the Puerto Ricans coming to New York over the decades has been larger than for immigrant groups. This has made broad economic progress a more daunting challenge.
Many Puerto Ricans who arrived from 1940 to 1970 were especially vulnerable because they wound up concentrated in manufacturing jobs just as industry began its long and nearly complete decline in the city.
The depressed state of nearly half the city's Puerto Rican population is even more striking when set against the more general success of Puerto Ricans in other cities, like Tampa, Fla., and Los Angeles, where the income of Puerto Rican households has resembled that of the overall population.
"There's this almost structural poverty," said Gladys Carrion, a Bronx-born Puerto Rican who is executive director of Inwood House, an agency that serves pregnant teenagers. "They're still living in the poorest communities, segregated communities, with the worst schools, in the lowest-paying jobs. The question is, why do we continue to be on the hit parade for the pathology?"
 
The Exodus
Rediscovering The Homeland
The poverty levels may have something to do with the exodus from New York, some researchers say.
The number of Puerto Ricans in the United States has grown over the last decade or more, up from 2.7 million in 1990 to 3.1 million, according to the Census Bureau's latest count in 1997. But in New York City, the Puerto Rican population fell by more than 96,000 residents in roughly the same period, to 800,000 in 1998.
While Puerto Ricans have moved out, however, the city's broader Latinization has increased, its boroughs filling with Central Americans, South Americans, Dominicans and Mexicans. City planners estimate that in about 10 years, Puerto Ricans will be replaced by Dominicans as the city's largest ethnic group by place of origin.
This shift is mainly because of fewer births and because Puerto Ricans have spread out around the Northeast and beyond, with large numbers going elsewhere in New York State and to New Jersey and Florida.
But an analysis by city planners after the 1990 census also found something distinctly atypical: of those leaving between 1985 and 1990, the single largest group, 38 percent, was going to Puerto Rico. And close to 40 percent of them were born in the States.
For Puerto Ricans, of course, there has always been a great deal of migration back and forth between the island and New York in response to shifts in the economic climate. City researchers found, for instance, that many of those leaving for Puerto Rico tended to be less educated and poorer than the Puerto Ricans who stayed in the city or moved to other states.
Some researchers have blamed such circular migration for interruptions in the family life and schooling of many migrants and for slowing the development of local leadership and institutions. By now, however, many Puerto Ricans also migrate back and forth responding to family ties and better job and educational opportunities throughout the United States, said Jorge Duany, an anthropologist at the University of Puerto Rico.
Retirees and successful younger people have also been drawn back to Puerto Rico, where the unemployment rate of 12 percent today is half what it was in the 1980's. Hildamar Ortiz, a lawyer who left New York with her 14-year-old daughter in 1996, said she moved partly to honor her late father's dreams of retiring in Puerto Rico. But she said she had always wondered what it would be like to be Puerto Rican in Puerto Rico.
Born in the Bronx after her parents migrated north in the late 1930's, Ms. Ortiz, 52, who works for the Federal Housing and Urban Development Department in San Juan, said she grew up in a Puerto Rican neighborhood where the only non-Hispanic whites around were the teachers at school. "The only time we went to Fifth Avenue was for the Puerto Rican Day parade," she said.
Now, she said, she feels so at home that she has no plans to return to New York except to catch the occasional Broadway play.
"You belong here," she said, sitting in the living room of her 10th-floor beach-front condominium. "You feel it; nobody questions it. There's no hostility. I think you don't realize how much that takes a toll."
 
The Obstacles
Educational Lack Feeds Poverty
Back in the neighborhoods of Ms. Ortiz's childhood, more people have become poor.
Central to that stubborn poverty is the question of educational attainment, experts say. Barely 10 percent of Puerto Rican New Yorkers 25 and older have a college degree, said Francisco L. Rivera-Batiz, an associate professor of economics and education at the Teachers College of Columbia University.
The impact of the educational problems among Puerto Ricans in the city has only been deepened because the city's current economic expansion has increased the demand for more highly skilled workers, Mr. Rivera-Batiz said.
Census Bureau officials say the Puerto Rican experience mirrors somewhat that of Hispanics nationwide, who in the 1990's for the first time surpassed blacks in poverty and who have the lowest education rates among the major demographic groups.
But at Aspira, a youth services organization founded 38 years ago to combat high dropout rates among Puerto Rican students, officials say the lack of significant progress in educating Puerto Rican children can no longer be attributed to factors true for other Hispanic groups, like language problems. By now, most Puerto Ricans in the city have been born in the States and speak English.
For second- and third-generation Puerto Ricans, said Alex Betancourt, a director at Aspira, "the issue is schools that have been neglected for many, many years and poor family involvement in education." The problem is abetted by a pattern of residential segregation in New York City that researchers say has been almost as severe for Puerto Ricans as for blacks, historically the most segregated demographic group in the city.
Blacks and Puerto Ricans in the city have faced similar discrimination, researchers said, but the black population has generally done somewhat better for reasons beyond the language barrier and cultural adjustments that Puerto Ricans additionally faced.
One reason is that blacks counted on their autonomous churches as "an enormous laboratory of leadership development," said Andres Torres, director of a Latino public policy analysis institute at the University of Massachusetts and author of a book comparing the two groups. Another, he said, is that the attachment to the homeland among Puerto Ricans has divided their attention, making them less focused than blacks on dealing with problems in New York.
But perhaps more critically, experts say, blacks have had a more diversified labor base, and thus more economic mobility, and found a better niche in civil service jobs.
The poverty among Puerto Ricans is largely rooted in the low-wage manufacturing jobs that first lured migrants to New York City in great numbers during the post-World War II years -- many of them agricultural workers displaced by industrialization on the island, economists and researchers who have studied the Puerto Rican migration say.
But for many, that kind of employment was short-lived because manufacturing had begun its steep decline in the 1950's.
"Puerto Ricans had terrible timing," said Sonia M. Perez, a researcher with the National Council of La Raza, a national Latino civil rights organization. "There was this massive decline of jobs, and they weren't able to bounce back" because of low education levels.
In the 1970's and 80's, she said, "you started to see the breakdown of families, an increase of single-mother families, high unemployment. That's when you started to see indicators of high poverty."
Many believe that the poverty was compounded by the Puerto Rican experience with welfare. Arriving as American citizens, Puerto Ricans were entitled to the social services denied to many new immigrants. Among single mothers with young children, who often found welfare more attractive than a low-paying job with no benefits, public assistance kept many from job experience or started a cycle of poverty, sociologists say.
At 33, Mercedes Gerena, who was born in the Bronx to Puerto Rican migrants, remains a single parent on welfare, just as her mother was while raising her and nine siblings in Mott Haven. She said her fate was sealed when she became pregnant at 16 with the first of her two children.
Ms. Gerena is facing federal deadlines for ending her benefits. She recently found a job cleaning restaurants, and hopes to one day become a nurse's aide or a day care attendant. Although her 16-year-old son dropped out of school, Ms. Gerena has big hopes for her daughter, Jennifer, 13, a seventh grader at St. Jerome Church's parish school who wants to go to college to become a teacher.
"Puerto Ricans in the Bronx, they sometimes feel like quitting, but they hold on," Ms. Gerena said. "We've had our ups and downs. They all can tell you it's rough, but they've managed."
 
Two Worlds
Trying to Decide Where Home Is
For those trying to understand the idiosyncratic history of Puerto Ricans in New York, there has long been the belief that the experience has been complicated by their passionate nationalism. Both Americans and natives of a United States commonwealth with its own culture and sense of nationhood, many Puerto Ricans have resisted acculturation. Many blame American policies in Puerto Rico for the economic conditions that forced them to migrate in the first place.
New York City Councilwoman Margarita Lopez, who was born in Puerto Rico, said she has lived in the city for more than 20 years, but had to decide "to embrace this country."
"You have to make a conscious decision that you're part of this or not," Ms. Lopez, 49, said. "We're always taking the position of outsiders, outsiders that are not willing to give up the dream of going back to the island. You ask yourself, am I betraying who I am? When I answered no, then I was able to move forward. But it wasn't easy."
But other Puerto Ricans had no such doubts. "Why would I want to leave the vital electronic center of the world, the most interesting cultural capital of the world?" said Miguel Algarin, a poet who founded the Nuyorican Poets Cafe on the Lower East Side.
Mr. Algarin, who moved to New York when he was 9, said the Puerto Rican flight is a mistake. "They should have stayed on 116th Street, fixed up the apartments like whites are doing now and maintained their political base in the United States," he said.
After a half century, Puerto Ricans in New York have succeeded in carving out electoral districts and Latino studies programs in universities. They have won bilingual education and civil rights battles and congressional, state and municipal posts. They have created a wide array of organizations, from cultural institutions to nonprofit agencies that now increasingly serve other Latinos.
"Puerto Ricans have to be acknowledged as the front line that opened doors for every Latino that came after them," said Susana Torruella Leval, director of El Museo del Barrio, the Puerto Rican and Latin American art museum.
There are more than 20 Puerto Rican elected officials in New York. But political representation has not necessarily translated into advancement for many constituents.
One major limitation, said Juan Figueroa, president and general counsel of the Puerto Rican Legal Defense and Education Fund, is that the party system in the state is so machine-driven that it leaves little room for those who want "to march to a different beat in order to deal with the many endemic problems of our communities."
But Antonia Pantoja, 77, a pioneering Puerto Rican community organizer who founded Aspira, said the underlying problem is indifference by both government and the public, including economically comfortable Puerto Ricans, to "a society where children are not taught by the schools they attend, where families do not have decent housing to live in, where the color of your skin will keep you out of the services and resources all citizens are entitled to."
She added, "Not only are these facts distressing, they are the shameful reality of a country that professes commitment to a democratic society."
At the southern tip of the Bronx, Edwin Lorenzo is among those staying and refusing to remain indifferent.
Mr. Lorenzo grew up in the Mill Brook Houses in Mott Haven and went to West Virginia University in pursuit of an engineering career. Then, during one spring break in the Bronx, a friend was murdered over a drug deal. After that, he said, "every time I came here, a lot of things bothered me that didn't use to bother me. The streets were dirty. There was graffiti on the walls."
Mr. Lorenzo, the youngest of five children born to a Puerto Rican father and Salvadoran mother, switched majors to become a social worker. At 28, he is director of the East Side House Settlement Mott Haven Community Center, a multiservice agency whose after-school programs he attended as a child. He has a brother in California and another one in Florida, but he is raising his three children in the Bronx.
"For many years we judged success by whether or not we left the inner city," Mr. Lorenzo said. "All the successful people leave, and they have no connection to the community. All the people who stay are viewed as failures. That's a greater harm than the other one.
"You decide it has to stop someplace," he said.

 CORRECTION:
An article on Monday about a decline in the number of Puerto Ricans in New York City referred incorrectly to the distinction borne by Luis Munoz Marin. He became the first native-born elected governor of Puerto Rico in 1948. The first native-born governor was Jesus T. Pinero, in 1946, but he was appointed.
A chart with the article mislabeled bars denoting percentages of Hispanics in New York City in 1999 who were 25 or older and had college degrees. For Puerto Ricans it was 10 percent, not 1 percent; for other Hispanics it was 12 percent.

SECTION: Section C; Page 1; Column 3; Business/Financial Desk

HEADLINE: Confronting the Reality Of a Health Care Vision

BYLINE: By MILT FREUDENHEIM 

 BODY:
It is show-me time for Healtheon/ WebMD.
The Internet-based company has riveted investors the last year, as its stock price rode a roller coaster while Healtheon announced a dizzying array of mergers and alliances. This month alone, Healtheon has used its stock in $5.7 billion worth of takeover deals for three companies. 
 And backers and partners like Microsoft, Rupert Murdoch's News Corporation and DuPont have lent credibility to Healtheon's ambitious talk of transforming the nation's unruly, fragmented health system into a digitally disciplined online universe. The vision is to link patients, doctors and insurers online with just about every player in the vast industry, promoting self-help for patients while saving time and money by eliminating paperwork and insurance company phone centers. But now comes the time when analysts say the company must deliver on its promises, before investors lose confidence.
Jeffrey T. Arnold, the 30-year-old Atlanta marketing entrepreneur who started WebMD in October 1998 and became chief executive of Healtheon/ WebMD after the companies agreed to merge the following May, has heard Wall Street's message -- that it is time demonstrate that Healtheon is more than an acquisitive holding company.
After more than 80 deals in 18 months, "we now have the assets in place to execute," Mr. Arnold said. "We will put everything we've got into making this work."
But Healtheon/WebMD's potentially volatile stock poses risks for investors, in the view of Dr. Bruce Hochstadt, a health care analyst at Thomas Weisel Partners, a merchant bank based in San Francisco. The stock was above $100 last May, before the Healtheon-WebMD merger, then slipped to $30 last August. But the share price is up 60.8 percent since the beginning of the year. The shares closed at $60.3125, down $2.4375, Friday in Nasdaq trading.
"They are the 900-pound gorilla in Internet health care; there is not much room for error," Dr. Hochstadt said. "Once investors caught wind that things were moving slower than expected or were a little stalled, some wouldn't have the tolerance to stick around."
Certainly, Healtheon is not a stock play for the impatient, as it tackles a daunting task that analysts say may take years to accomplish. By most projections, the company will not become profitable until at least 2002.
"They need to slow down, take stock and actually execute some of the necessary integration of the purchases," said Mike Davis, a research director in Denver for the Gartner Group, a technology consulting firm. "They have to merge into a consolidated organization and culture. That's where most companies fail. It's not going to be easy."
In deals this month alone, Healtheon bought the Medical Manager Corporation, a profitable company that provides office management software for doctors; CareInsite, an Internet company that was Healtheon/WebMD's biggest competitor in the physician-to-insurer linkage field; and the OnHealth Network, a Web site that provides health care information for consumers.
It was the stock price that has made possible the many acquisitions and it was the shares that attracted a $930 million investment last month by Janus Capital. "Janus is considered to be very smart money," Dr. Hochstadt said.
Although the company has been spending about $250 million a year working capital should not be an immediate problem. Besides the $930 million from Janus, Healtheon/ WebMD says it has nearly $800 million in unspent commitments from stakeholders and partners.
Mr. Arnold, the chief executive, said that by June, Healtheon/ WebMD would make online connections available to 400,000 doctors, 900 insurance companies, 46,000 pharmacies and 4,500 hospitals. The Healtheon piece of the operation, based in Santa Clara, Calif., has close to 1,000 software programmers and computer engineers working full steam on producing the links under the supervision of Pavan Nigram.
Mr. Nigram is a computer wizard originally hired by Jim Clark, the Silicon Valley billionaire who founded Silicon Graphics and Netscape. Mr. Nigram was given the technical challenge of realizing Mr. Clark's dream, conceived four years ago, to somehow capitalize on the nation's health care economy.
Since Healtheon went public a year ago, Mr. Clark has moved on to less world-shaking Web-based ventures like helping rich people manage their money and consumers store family photos. He is still on the board, however, and still owns Healtheon shares worth $700 million. And one of his initial concepts remains central for Healtheon: playing a gatekeeper role in the daily transactions of the nation's medical money machine.
Ultimately, online transactions -- medical bills, drug prescriptions, lab results and so on -- may cost as little as 25 cents each, compared with $8 or $10 for transactions by paper, fax or phone. UnitedHealthcare, a unit of the UnitedHealth Group and a minority owner of Healtheon/WebMD, encourages the 300,000 doctors in its networks to submit bills and check patient coverage online. It says at least 100,000 are already doing so.
The potential market is a mind-boggling 30 billion health care transactions annually. They include hospital and doctor bills, insurance approvals, drug prescriptions, lab reports and referrals to specialists.
The first task, Mr. Arnold said, is to make the nation's myriad existing computer systems accessible with Web-browser software -- an effort likely to get a big push as new federal regulations take hold over the next two years. Under a 1996 law, the Health Insurance Portability and Accountability Act, all external transactions between hospitals, doctors and insurers must be in standardized electronic language, with safeguards to protect patients' privacy and prevent unauthorized access.
"Every health care company says it needs a Web strategy," Mr. Arnold said. "We will provide them with the technology that meets the Hippa requirements," he said, referring to the 1996 law.
Healtheon acquired a crucial piece of this capability with a deal in January to buy the electronic billing company Envoy from the Quintiles Transnational Corporation for $2.5 billion in Healtheon stock. Envoy is a profitable business, dominating the small fraction of the country's medical bills that are delivered electronically, from computer to computer. But it is not the Web -- yet.
Some analysts say that electronic transactions, alone, are not sufficient to wring costs from the nation's medical system. As long as health care providers continue to do most business the old-fashioned way by using paper, fax and the phone, there are costly inefficiencies -- even if the information is subsequently converted to digital form, said Janice Young, another Gartner analyst.
And while Healtheon, as evidence of a trend, says it counted one million transactions online to insurance companies and laboratories in a single month last fall, some analysts have calculated that fewer than 100,000 of those actually originated as computerized transactions.
"We are waiting to see the number of doctors actually using the Internet," said William McKeever, a PaineWebber Securities analyst. "They run the gamut from just signing up to visiting a physician portal to using the Internet for transactions."
Martin J. Wygod, chief executive of Medical Manager and CareInsite, who will be co-chairman of Healtheon/WebMD when the sale of his companies is completed, is a big promoter of the online business model. "We can change the entire continuum of care and reduce not only the administrative expense but the actual cost of care by getting the right information to the doctor when he is seeing the patient," Mr. Wygod said.
Mr. Wygod said that health insurers like United, and several that signed on with CareInsite in New York, New Jersey and Massachusetts, would help overcome the reluctance of doctors to try new systems. He predicted the insurers themselves would be lured by the potential cost savings, which he said might allow them to eliminate thousands of employees who now deal with doctors by phone and fax.
Since most doctors deal with several health plans, they are likely to be more interested in going online if they can deal with more than one insurer on the same Web site. In New York, for example, CareInsite has contracts with Empire Blue Cross and Blue Shield, H.I.P., and Group Health, and Healtheon serves United.
But Gartner's Ms. Young said in some regions, dominant health insurers were developing their own online systems -- and thus might hesitate to share access to doctors with Healtheon/WebMD.
Besides transactions, which Mr. Arnold said would generate about half the revenue, the company is placing other bets. It has a big staff of writers and doctors producing up-to-date health care information for consumers and to attract physicians to its news and information Web site, WebMD.com. The site's producers also create broadcast material.
Mimicking the America Online model, the WebMD.com site can generate advertising revenue and fees from health care companies that doctors and consumers link to through the WebMD.com portal. For example, Medibuy, an online supplier of medical products and services, is paying $45.5 million for such a link. In addition, Healtheon/WebMD will get royalties from online sales by partners like CVS drugstores.
But for analysts, the big questions remain how quickly and how well Healtheon/WebMD will be able to integrate not only the nation's bewildering health care system but, first, the many pieces of its own far-flung, two-chairman empire.
Besides Mr. Wygod, the other co-chairman will be W. Michael Long. Mr. Long sold his financial technology consulting company, Continuum Inc., for $1.4 billion, in 1996 -- which is why he was available in 1997 when Healtheon's venture capital backer went looking for someone to bring focus Mr. Clark's hazy plans.
Besides seeking management cohesion, Healtheon/WebMD also has a geographic integration challenge. Mr. Arnold remains in Atlanta, where WebMD is based. Mr. Long lives in Austin, Tex., though he spends time at the Healtheon operations in Santa Clara. Mr. Wygod lives near San Diego, far from Medical Manager and CareInsite, which are in Elmwood Park, N.J.
Recognizing the huge the task before him, Healtheon/WebMD's Mr. Arnold tends to speak in measured tones about his prospects. When Jim Clark was wooing early investors, he used to talk about a revolution in health care. Mr. Arnold speaks more modestly about evolution.
Customers "can keep doing it by phone, paper and fax," he said. "We are just asking for access."
A foot in the door, in other words. A very big foot.

HEADLINE: FOREIGN AFFAIRS;
Eyes On the Prize

BYLINE: By THOMAS L. FRIEDMAN 

 BODY:
So China is now telling Taiwan: Marry me or I'll kill you. And Vice President Al Gore is now telling the unions: Vote for me and I'll get you a better deal with China next time, even though my own administration negotiated this deal. And together they are making a God-awful mess of what is a fundamental U.S. interest -- that Congress ratify China's entry into the World Trade Organization.
It's now going to be very tempting for Congress -- caught between the right-wingers who have been energized by the threats from Beijing against Taiwan and the left-wingers who have been energized by Mr. Gore's ambivalent support for his own trade negotiators -- to walk away from the Clinton deal for bringing China into the W.T.O. 
 Nothing could be more reckless. This is the time to keep our eyes on the prize, and the prize remains the stable, steady transformation of China into a responsible member of the world trading system and into a more free and open society. Few things are more important for world stability than that.
The best tool we have for advancing that -- not a foolproof tool, not the only tool, but the best tool -- is to Gulliverize China by globalizing China. And that's what this W.T.O. deal helps to do -- by requiring the Beijing government to permit more Chinese citizens to do business directly with the outside world and be less dependent on their own government, by requiring China to allow U.S. Internet companies to own 50 percent of Internet start-ups in China, and by requiring China to put in place trade rules where corrupt, arbitrary officials now hold sway.
The reason China's leaders cracked down this year is because they are terrified that the personal freedoms the Chinese people have gained in their lives and jobs during the last decade of openness will lead to demands for more political freedom. And they will -- especially with the W.T.O. deal.
That's why if ratifying this W.T.O. accord now means looking past China's bluster on Taiwan, then so be it. If ratifying this deal now means ignoring the pandering of the vice president -- who knows better, who actually understands globalization, and who should be, and could be, the administration's most articulate, in-your-face advocate of this deal, but who has bowed instead to the A.F.L.-C.I.O. and its fatuous nonsense that you improve worker and environmental standards abroad by dictating them to others, not by free-trading with them -- then so be it.
Even if it means ignoring the self-interested lobbying of the U.S. Chamber of Commerce, which just wants W.T.O. passage because it's good for business -- then so be it. As the Council on Foreign Relations expert Michael Mandelbaum says: "Some things are in the national interest even though the Chamber of Commerce believes them."
Our choice with China is not between deterrence and engagement. You always need both. "China is not a threat to dominate Asia the way the Soviet Union was a threat to dominate Europe," notes Mr. Mandelbaum. "It lacks the power, the ideology and the will. The only place where China threatens U.S. interests is with Taiwan, and there U.S. military power is tacitly deterring the Chinese and must continue to do so."
But we keep this deterrence card hidden so as not to embolden the sometimes reckless Taiwanese into thinking that they can achieve independence and security without negotiating their relationship with Beijing. China is bellowing now precisely because it wants negotiations and is trying to strengthen its hand for these inevitable talks. China is so dependent on Taiwan and America today for investment and trade that it cannot afford to invade Taiwan without paying a devastating price. China still may invade. Countries do crazy things. But Beijing will think about it a lot longer than you imagine.
Strategy is about playing the odds. And since we are already deterring China militarily, the odds say that the best way to reinforce that is by bringing China into the W.T.O., increasing the costs for Beijing of any military adventure, and fostering the internal changes inside China that might one day make it more like Taiwan, and therefore better able to solve its differences with Taiwan peacefully.
The odds are in our favor, if we play our cards smart. That means understanding China's bluster for what it is, and it means understanding Mr. Gore's pandering for what it is, and looking beyond both with our eyes on the prize. 
http://www.nytimes.com

HEADLINE: New Providence Journal;
Bahamians Resent the Loss of Beaches to Luxury

BYLINE: By DAVID GONZALEZ 

DATELINE: NEW PROVIDENCE, Bahamas

 BODY:
The tourists who scuba dive among the reefs and tan themselves on the sandy shores have probably seen more of this island's coastline than Therell Bethell ever has. She cannot remember the last time she visited a decent stretch of beach, since the development boom over the last decade brought gated communities and exclusive resorts.
"Bahamians do not have access to the beach," said Ms. Bethel, a legal secretary in Nassau. "Bahamians have to have a puddle in our backyards to swim." 
 The long-simmering resentment over the loss of their beaches has crystallized in opposition to a proposed luxury community on the longest remaining stretch of undeveloped coastline in New Providence, the most heavily populated island.
The development, Clifton Cay, near Clifton Point at the western tip of the island, would take up nearly two miles of seaside land for 600 homes built around a marina and interspersed with amenities like a golf course and a beach club.
The government of Prime Minister Hubert Ingraham supports the development, in keeping with its policy of wooing newcomers who bring money and jobs.
But opponents contend that their battle is not only over access to the beach but also over access to their history. Their cause has been taken up both by environmentalists, who fear damage to delicate reefs, and by preservationists. The site is dotted with the ruins of 18th-century plantations, as well as the buried artifacts from two Lucayan Indian villages that date back 1,000 years.
International preservation and conservation groups have recently offered support, saying this is a rare resource of natural beauty and historical richness. The last week in February, Robert F. Kennedy, Jr., the environmental lawyer and son of the late senator, wrote to the project's main financial backer, a Bechtel family trust, on the opponents' behalf to urge that the environmental impact be studied in more detail.
The developers, who plan to begin construction in the next two or three months, say they will protect the ruins and not harm the environment. But opponents are skeptical.
"The biggest problem in the Bahamas today is that our government does not understand that money is not everything," said Keod Smith, a Nassau lawyer who has been fighting the development. "They feel if they show economic activity, capital injection and tax revenues increase, when you look at the books, it looks great. But do we sell our heritage just because we want some money?"
It is easy to see the site's appeal: turtles and dolphins glide through the water among the reefs, and conch shells, iridescently pink, are scattered on the sandy shore. Thick, elevated inland brush offers protection from winds and floods, and nearby wetlands are filled each winter with flocks of migrating kestrels, ospreys and warblers.
But treasures are also tucked among the trees or buried under the sand. A few years ago, Laurie Wilkie, an archaeologist from the University of California at Berkeley, began to study the remains of a major 18th-century plantation owned by William Wylly, who was known for trying to extend rights to slaves. Ms. Wilkie found bits of ceramic, tobacco pipes and bottles at one part of the site. At another spot, she examined the ruins of slave quarters and a kitchen.
Her most remarkable find came in the late 1990's, after preservationists expressed alarm that the proposed development might destroy remains of a Lucayan Indian village, and the government asked her to research its boundaries.
She said her findings led her to conclude that a luxury development should not be built there. She said that between the information about early Indian inhabitants and 18th century plantation life, the site is a veritable Rosetta Stone for understanding life on the island.
"This represents one of those rare arenas where you can study the whole extent of the human experience in a location, starting with the Lucayan villages," she said. "I can't think of another site that contains all of this."
She said the findings are especially valuable because the government kept good records about slavery during the plantation period. "This is so rare," she said. "To look at African history and talk about individuals, and tie that to archaeological remains is very unusual."
The descendant of one of those slaves has become one of the most outspoken opponents of the development. Vivian Whylly, a 38-year-old Nassau businessman, said he had traced his roots to Esther Whylly, his great-great-great-grandmother, who was a slave on the Wylly plantation. He filed a claim on the land -- unsuccessful so far, but he plans to appeal.
Mr. Whylly would like to see the land become a national park, where people could go to learn about the island's earliest settlers. Preservationists have started a fund to purchase the land, obtaining commitments of nearly $4 million.
Mr. Whylly said he often visits the area, to soak up the spirits that he feels still dwell among the ruins. "It keeps their spirit alive to know that this is where the people who would be my foreparents lived," he said. "Their spirit says don't let us become enslaved again."
The project's developers, Chaffin Light & Associates, said they were willing to protect the ruins and would create a fund financed by home and property sales to create a learning center accessible to the public. They also said they were working with the government to ensure that no environmental harm would come from the construction. The developers have also set aside a 500-foot stretch of public beach, but they contend that the shoreline access issue should be reviewed in a broader context.
"This is an issue we respect," said Jim Anthony, president of the Clifton Cay Co. Ltd. "But the debate is larger than Clifton Cay."
The debate goes even deeper than the vanishing public shoreline and is touching off passions about the island's identity and its future. Independent only since 1973, the Bahamas has had much new foreign development in recent years, thanks to Mr. Ingraham. Developments like Clifton Cay, officials say, create jobs -- a priority for the government.
"That is what our people needed for a better way of life and education," Said Earl Deveaux, the top environmental adviser to the prime minister. "What country is concerned about preserving artifacts when it is scraping by for a living or for food?"
But Sam Duncombe, an advocate here for environmental preservation, said the luxury developments cater too much to outsiders without regard to the needs of Bahamians like herself, who want to see the shoreline preserved for future generations.
"There is a certain ilk of American who believes the world is their oyster," Ms. Duncombe said. "We resent it. To them it's money. To us it's who we are. It's our peace of mind. It's our soul."
To allow the project to continue, she said, would revive one tradition -- that of the pirates that used to hide in the coves and inlets not too far from where the luxury homes are planned.
"The idea of pirates still exists," she said. "They no longer have beards and long coats." She said she sees some government officials as modern-day pirates. "They are selling us out," she said. "We need foreign development. But at what price?"

SECTION: Section C; Page 13; Column 5; Business/Financial Desk

HEADLINE: THE MARKETS;
Senator Proposes Reduction In Fees S.E.C. Puts on Trading

BYLINE: By ALEX BERENSON 

 BODY:
Day traders and lawyers for the Securities and Exchange Commission will each get a raise if Senator Phil Gramm has his way.
Mr. Gramm, the Texas Republican who heads the Senate Banking Committee, introduced legislation yesterday that would substantially cut the fees that companies and investors pay the S.E.C. to register and trade stock. The bill would also give many professionals at the S.E.C., including lawyers and accountants, raises of up to 40 percent to help stem the brain drain at the agency. 
 Over the next 10 years, the legislation would cut fees for companies and traders by almost $15 billion, Mr. Gramm predicted.
"Those taxes are paid by Americans who are trying to save and invest," Mr. Gramm said. "They are a tax on accumulation, thrift and retirement security."
In fact, the cost of the fees is hardly even a rounding error for investors, amounting to about $3 for a $100,000 trade. But securities firms argue that the fees, along with the somewhat larger fees that the S.E.C. charges companies to register their shares, amount to a hidden tax on investors. Nasdaq market makers, which must pay the fees on shares they buy and sell for their own account, are especially anxious to see the fees reduced.
The bill's chances are uncertain. The legislation has some strong Democratic supporters, including Senator Charles E. Schumer, of New York. Arthur Levitt, the chairman of the S.E.C., also spoke out strongly in its favor at a hearing yesterday in downtown New York.
But the White House, which is worried about the loss of a relatively painless revenue source, has not yet indicated if it will support the bill. At yesterday's hearing, Mr. Schumer said he was "getting vibes that the administration will not be supportive on this."
The fees are supposed to finance the commission's operations. But because they are tied to the dollar value of the shares that are registered or traded, the fees have soared along with the markets since 1995. They are now much larger than the S.E.C.'s budget, creating a windfall for the federal government. In 1999, the fees totaled almost $2 billion, more than five times the cost of running the S.E.C. The excess was spent on general government expenses.
Under Mr. Gramm's legislation, the fees would be cut to roughly the amount necessary to pay the S.E.C.'s budget. Charges for registering stock would immediately fall by about 75 percent, and trading fees would probably drop by about half, according to Mr. Levitt.
In addition to cutting fees, Mr. Gramm's bill would give the S.E.C. the chance to raise salaries for many of its lawyers and accountants to the levels already offered by the Federal Reserve and Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation. For example, a second-year lawyer with the S.E.C. makes about $66,000, while a member of the F.D.I.C. staff with similar experience can be paid as much as $91,000, or 39 percent more.
Mr. Levitt called raising salaries essential in light of soaring salaries for lawyers and accountants in private practice. Mr. Levitt said his agency had lost one-fourth of its top professionals in the last two years. "We are in the midst of a terribly serious staffing crisis," he said.

HEADLINE: PUBLIC LIVES;
Leaving Hoop Dreams for the Boardroom

BYLINE: By ROBIN FINN 

 BODY:
IF you're an enterprising type like Steve Mills, this is what you do with your life once you come to grips with a reality that snubs you, that regrets to inform you that you possess just enough game to play ball anywhere in the world except your dream destination, the National Basketball Association. You activate your backup plan.
Then you thank your social worker mom and teacher/ basketball coach dad that you have a plan worth activating. They're the nervous Nellies who made certain that you dropped the ball and left the playground back home in Roosevelt, N.Y., at homework time. 
 If the basketball gods won't allow you -- a 6-foot-1 inch guard who grew up "right around the corner from Dr. J" and like him learned to dribble the ball like a yo-yo -- to pound the boards downstairs with the players, you go upstairs and become a different kind of player. You make your moves behind the scenes and end up the highest-ranking African-American in something you call "the organization": first an express ascent at the N.B.A., now a 14th-floor office with a decent view at 2 Penn Plaza, headquarters of the Knicks.
Here's the little chat that Mr. Mills had with himself when he accepted his entry-level slot as an account executive with N.B.A. Properties in 1983: "O.K., so I wasn't good enough to make it to the N.B.A. and Magic Johnson was, but here he is playing and here I am working for the N.B.A., so we're still moving along the same path." Sort of.
Here's his reminder to himself when he joined the Knicks' front office last September: "There are guys out there that I, quote-unquote, came into the league with, and they played, and I'm doing this, and my N.B.A. career's going to be a lot longer than theirs is."
Here he is, whippet-slim at 40, the executive vice president for franchise operations of his favorite team from boyhood, sitting in a big black leather chair behind a bigger desk, balancing books that include a $70 million player payroll and helping make team policy. That includes last week's decision to cancel the traditional preplayoffs camp in Charleston, S.C., in response to the national debate over that state's tradition of flying the Confederate battle flag.
"As far as we're concerned, it wasn't a political statement and wasn't a public relations maneuver, although the result of it may in fact be a political statement," says Mr. Mills, who starts fiddling with his wedding ring when asked his gut response to the status of the battle flag in a state where he and his wife, Beverly, have often vacationed. "I don't think it would be fair to the organization for me to bring my personal feelings into this," he hedges.
Come on, doesn't he find the flag offensive? "Personally, yes. I think for most African-Americans it clearly represents some negative times for our race."
Clearly for the Knicks, whose roster contains just one white player, returning to South Carolina was not an option anyone favored. "The consensus was not to go back," Mr. Mills says of the feedback management received from the team. (Mr. Mills is a fan of player-management discussions. )
What sort of political climate will it take for them to go back? "That's one we're going to have to play by ear," he demurs, giving the wedding ring the same nervous twists it gets when the conversation veers toward Latrell Sprewell (Is he really reformed?) or management obligations to rule-breakers like Darryl Strawberry. ("I would say the N.B.A. is structured to provide second chances.")
In other words, next question, new subject. Mr. Mills does not court controversy gladly, particularly now that his career is doing what the Knicks haven't done since 1973: peaking. Today the Knicks; tomorrow, who knows?
Mr. Mills calls the N.B.A. "the most colorblind" of all the major sports leagues -- he spent 15 years there, ascending to senior vice president, without noticing any slammed doors -- and he's hopeful that basketball, whenever David Stern's expert tenure ends, might install its first black commissioner. It might be him.
How does an N.B.A. draft-reject like him get here?
You trade the locker room for the corporate boardroom, put on a suit and a rep tie instead of short pants, and let your sociology degree from Princeton, a degree backed by an aptitude for economics, smooth your segue from a bizarre season playing pro ball in Ecuador to a job as an investment banker at a Manhattan megafirm now known as Chase but then called Chemical.
IT helps that your bank recruiter played some ball at Brown back in his day. When you are torn between entering the bank's trainee program or saying yes to playing in South America for $2,500 a month, he utters the magic words: "Go chase your dream." He gives you a rain check on the job, a Princeton political science professor assures you that Ecuador appears to be coup-free, and off you go.
One tropical season of Division II-type ball later, a season during which you exercise your college Spanish and explore the Galapagos, you're ready to become a banker, and you do. But you never forget that while you were growing up wanting to be a Knick, you wore nothing on your feet but "Clyde Pumas," and when a referee friend of your dad's mentions a job opening at the N.B.A., you jump.
Just the way you used to on the playground.

HEADLINE: Solution on Vieques Takes a Step Forward

BYLINE: AP 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Feb. 28

 BODY:
Despite growing protests at home, the governor of Puerto Rico met with White House officials today and reaffirmed his support for President Clinton's plan to resume Navy exercises, which will be at least initially limited, on the island of Vieques.
The governor, Pedro J. Rossello, also took possession of 110 acres of land on the western end of Vieques that the Navy transferred to local control. The transfer was part of a deal to allow the renewed training before residents of the island decide whether to bar the exercises permanently. 
 "The first step in the overall resolution of the issue was taken today," the White House press secretary, Joe Lockhart, said after a meeting between Mr. Rossello and the White House chief of staff, John D. Podesta. "There's obviously a number of other steps that will have to be taken, including a referendum where the people of Vieques will decide their future."
Exercises on Vieques, where the Navy maintains a bombing range that it considers essential to the training of its Atlantic fleet, were suspended last spring after a wayward bomb killed a civilian security guard, an accident that intensified longtime protests against the service's presence.
Mr. Clinton and Mr. Rossello agreed last month that the Navy would be allowed to resume training using inert bombs. The pact calls for a referendum, likely to be held in 2001, that will allow Vieques residents to choose between the unfettered resumption of training, with live bombs, and exercises with dummy bombs until a Navy withdrawal in May 2003.
In San Juan last week, some 85,000 people demonstrated against the agreement. But Mr. Rossello said today that the administration's proposal was the best solution to the standoff between Vieques islanders and the Navy.
He said that as details of the proposal became more widely known, "it will allow much more support for all aspects of that presidential order."
The Navy has not announced a date for resuming training, but Mr. Rossello pledged the Puerto Rican government's full cooperation in clearing the test range of protesters.
"We will take any steps that are appropriate to make sure that the range is available," he said.
The Clinton administration is seeking $40 million to address the health and environmental concerns raised by the training on Vieques.
"The people of Vieques have placed on the table concerns as to what has happened to their health, to their environment, to their opportunity for economic development," Mr. Rossello said today. "The funds that are requested are precisely to respond to those very valid concerns."
The 110 acres transferred today are to be used to extend an airport on Vieques, allowing larger aircraft and so stimulating growth.
A deed for the land was drawn up and signed by Mr. Rossello and Adm. Kevin Green, commander of Naval Forces South, in a small ceremony, said Maria Echaveste, deputy White House chief of staff.
"The governor believes that actually taking steps to implement the president's directives would be helpful in helping reduce the level of opposition down in Puerto Rico," Ms. Echaveste said.

HEADLINE: Couple With Computer Riches Give M.I.T. a Record $350 Million for Study of the Brain

BYLINE: By CAREY GOLDBERG 

DATELINE: CAMBRIDGE, Mass., Feb. 28

 BODY:
A computer publishing billionaire and his wife pledged today to give the Massachusetts Institute of Technology the largest single donation yet to a university -- $350 million over the next 20 years -- to finance research on the brain.
The money, from Patrick J. and Lore Harp McGovern, will create the McGovern Institute for Brain Research, a center with a staff of 300, including computer scientists, psychologists and molecular biologists. The donation comes at a moment when neuroscience is making great advances, powered by recent progress in imaging technology, cell biology and genomics. 
 "We both have a great interest and fascination with this last frontier of discovery," Mr. McGovern said today at the signing of an initial agreement on the institute.
Mr. McGovern, 62, is founder and chairman of International Data Group, a company known for publishing Computerworld, putting out the "For Dummies" book series and organizing huge computer industry conferences.
Back in the 1950's, when he was a mere M.I.T. undergraduate, Mr. McGovern majored in life sciences and studied the human nervous system. And even before that, he said, when he was a teenager visiting the public library in Philadelphia, he came upon a book called "Giant Minds, or Machines that Think" that changed his life, infecting him with ideas about how computers could enhance human reasoning.
Mrs. McGovern, a 55-year-old high-tech entrepreneur, said that she also traced her interest in the brain back some 50 years, to a visit to a science museum when she was 5 years old. Her parents showed her a brain in formaldehyde at a science museum and she asked, "Can you touch thought? Does it have physical presence?"
"And so maybe now we can figure that out," she said. "So let the journey begin."
The first thing the McGoverns had to figure out, they said, was where to put the institute they had decided to create about three years ago. They put together an advisory committee and considered seven universities: Columbia; University of California campuses in San Francisco, Berkeley and San Diego; the California Institute of Technology; Stanford; and M.I.T. Mr. McGovern acknowledged that his emotional ties to M.I.T. had played a role in the decision, but said that more objective factors also came into play, including its neuroscience programs and its "willingness to be very collaborative."
In the style of many Internet-age benefactors, the McGoverns plan to keep a close eye on the institute and will sit on its board, along with directors from M.I.T. and outside neuroscientists.
The money will begin to flow as yearly gifts of about $5 million, Mr. McGovern said. The amounts will slowly scale up until, at the end of 20 years, the McGoverns donate the rest of the $350 million to endow the institute in perpetuity.
The gift beats by $50 million the record for a single donation to a university, according to a list published by the Chronicle of Higher Education. The former record-setting donation was $300 million in stock from the Ingram Charitable Fund to Vanderbilt University, announced in 1998. Last year, the Bill and Melinda Gates Foundation gave $1 billion to create the Gates Millennium Scholars program, but the money, intended to encourage minority students in science and technology, is to be spread among many institutions.
The McGoverns' donation comes at an opportune time, said Phillip A. Sharp, a Nobel-laureate molecular biologist who will serve as the institute's founding director. Among other factors, scientists seeking to unravel the workings of the brain now have at their disposal new knowledge about how nerve cells work, he said, and the soon-to-be-completed mapping of the human genome is expected to contribute insights as well.
"All of this is a basis upon which, not in the next year, not even in 10 years, but perhaps in 20 years, we'll be able to draw out the general and specific functions of the brain in complex phenomena, like learning behavior, those sorts of processes," he said. "And it's terribly exciting."
Mr. McGovern, asked if he had one pet neuroscience problem he would like to see solved, said it would probably be the causes of misperception: why the brain takes in or remembers things incorrectly.
Mrs. McGovern said she was most interested in attacking learning disabilities.
"If we can tap into the intellectual potential and bring everyone up to par," she said, "we can create a so much better society."

HEADLINE: Tijuana Governor Says Slaying Shows Drug Traffickers' Power

BYLINE: By SAM DILLON 

DATELINE: MEXICO CITY, Feb. 28

 BODY:
A day after gunmen killed the police chief in Tijuana, the sprawling city that borders California, the state governor said drug traffickers were out of control there partly because many federal authorities were in their pay.
"The drugs are coming in by land, sea and air," Gov. Alejandro Gonzalez Alcocer of Baja California said in a telephone interview. "The federal authorities have jurisdiction. But frankly we don't have much confidence in them. We worry that if we try to coordinate operations with them, our plans will be communicated to the traffickers." 
 On a visit to Baja on Friday by President Ernesto Zedillo, Mr. Gonzalez urged the president to speak out against the traffickers, who have been blamed for the killings of 60 people in Tijuana in eight weeks. In a speech, the president said, "We have to make these criminals understand that they're not welcome in Baja California, that the only home they deserve are the prisons."
On Sunday, gunmen in cars ambushed and killed Chief Alfredo de la Torre Marquez as he drove on a highway. Dozens of bullets hit him, the police said.
One reasonable hypothesis, Mr. Gonzalez said, is that the traffickers had ordered the assassination as a response to the president. Mr. Zedillo, an economist who was trained at Yale and who has rarely displayed personal interest in the narcotics problems, did not refer today to Chief de la Torre's killing in his speech to a government audience. A spokesman said the president would have no comment on the situation in Tijuana. "Its just a police matter," a presidential aide said.
Mr. Gonzalez, who is in the center-right National Action Party, said he realized on Mr. Zedillo's visit that he had no plans to mention the narcotics violence in Baja. As the two drove to the speaking engagement, in Mexicali, east of Tijuana, Mr. Gonzalez recalled, he asked Mr. Zedillo to address the problem. In his speech, Mr. Zedillo vowed to send to high officials to Tijuana to review federal efforts.

HEADLINE: Incomes Rose Faster Than Spending In January

BYLINE: Bloomberg News 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Feb. 28

 BODY:
Americans' incomes rose in January at the fastest pace in three months, government figures showed today, suggesting that consumers could be poised to increase spending.
An increase in cost-of-living payments for Social Security and other programs and raises for federal employees helped drive up personal incomes 0.7 percent in January, the Commerce Department reported. Incomes rose 0.3 percent in December. 
 "Lots of money means lots of spending, and the slowdown is still nowhere in sight," said Joel L. Naroff, president of Naroff Economic Advisers in Holland, Pa. "Families had tons of money to spend, and that is exactly what they did."
Spending increased 0.5 percent in January after rising 1.1 percent in December, the largest jump since spending grew by that amount in May 1998. January spending was up 8.2 percent when compared with January 1998, the biggest increase in more than a decade.
It was the first time since October, when farm payments caused a 1.2 percent surge, that incomes rose faster than spending. If that trend continues, it could ease concern over the rising level of consumer debt. Personal debt, as measured by the Federal Reserve, surged $27 billion in the last two months of the year, more than double the pace of the previous four months.
The faster rate of incomes compared with spending also pushed the savings rate to 1.4 percent in January from a record low of 1 percent in December. Disposable income, or the money left over after taxes, increased 0.9 percent in January after rising 0.1 percent during December.
A spending surge late last year helped push growth in fourth-quarter gross domestic product to 6.9 percent at an annual rate, the fastest pace in three and a half years. Today's report suggests higher borrowing costs have done little to cool demand for goods and services. Consumer spending accounts for roughly two-thirds of the nation's output of goods and services and helped push the economy to a record 107th month of expansion in February.

HEADLINE: THE MARKETS: STOCKS & BONDS;
Dow Surges Above 10,000 Again on a Blue-Chip Rally

BYLINE: By KENNETH N. GILPIN 

 BODY:
The Dow Jones industrial average rebounded above 10,000 yesterday as investors, at least temporarily, moved back into blue-chip stocks, which have been battered in recent weeks.
The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index also gained ground, helped in part by the Goldman, Sachs market strategist Abby Joseph Cohen, who said yesterday that the slide in the index had left it undervalued. 
 But technology stocks, which have continued to rise in the face of a broad-based decline this year, edged lower yesterday.
With the economy continuing to power ahead and the Federal Reserve showing no sign it is finished in its efforts to slow it down by raising short-term interest rates, most analysts and traders said yesterday's moves were little more than a temporary bounce from what has been a sharp sell-off.
"I am happy the market is up, but it would be nice if I had some reason to think it is sustainable," said Henry Herrmann, chief investment officer at Waddell & Reed, a firm in Overland Park, Kan., that has $40 billion under management. "We are due for a bounce, and we are getting it."
Led by financial and retail stocks like Citigroup, American Express and Procter & Gamble, the Dow rose 176.53 points, or 1.8 percent, to 10,038.65. It was the biggest point gain in the Dow in nearly two weeks and came after a 2.3 percent decline on Friday.
The S.& P. 500 gained 14.69 points, or 1.1 percent, to 1,348.05. Exxon Mobil, Qualcomm and American International Group helped to lift the index.
But investors took some profits in technology stocks. By the close, the Nasdaq composite index was down 12.65 points, or 0.3 percent, to 4,577.85. At one point in the session, the index had fallen as much as 2.7 percent.
Prices of small-company shares were little changed. The Russell 2000 index gained 0.94, or 0.2 percent, to 557.68.
In her comments yesterday, Ms. Cohen said the recent slide in the S.& P. 500 had left the index about 5 percent undervalued. She maintained her target of 1,525 for the S.& P. 500 by the end of the year because of continued vigor in the economy and robust corporate profits.
In reference to interest rate fears, Ms. Cohen said recent and expected interest rate increases by the Fed were aimed "at slowing and elongating, not stopping, economic growth."
Andrew M. Brooks, head of equity trading at T. Rowe Price Associates, said, "Abby's mildly positive comments were a help."
Volume was heavy, but not frantic. On the New York Stock Exchange, just more than a billion shares changed hands. Volume on the Nasdaq stock market was 1.78 billion shares.
Financial stocks, which have been severely battered as interest rates have risen, did better yesterday.
Citigroup, a Dow component, gained 1 1/2, to 49 3/8, after the company, the nation's biggest financial services company said John S. Reed, its co-chief executive, would retire in April.
Chase Manhattan gained 2 1/16, to 77 15/16. Wells Fargo rose 1 7/16, to 34 1/8. Mellon Financial was up 1 1/2, to 30 7/8. Bank of New York closed at 34 3/4, up 1 3/4.

Treasury Prices Fall
 
By Reuters
Bond prices dropped yesterday as blue-chip stocks bounced back from sharp losses last week and robust economic data fanned concerns that the Federal Reserve would keep driving interest rates higher.
Sharp losses by United States stocks late last week sent investors scurrying for a safe haven in the bond market.
But market players scampered back out of Treasuries yesterday, as the Dow Jones industrial average surged nearly 2 percent, climbing above 10,000 after closing below that important psychological level on Friday for the first time in 10 months.
The price of the 30-year Treasury bond declined 22/32, to 100 28/32. The bond's yield, which moves in the opposite direction from the price, rose to 6.19 percent from 6.14 percent on Friday.

HEADLINE: Internet Spurs Huge Job Growth in the New York Area

BYLINE: By TERRY PRISTIN 

 BODY:
Internet-related jobs in the New York area are growing at an explosive rate, to 250,000 now from 106,000 in 1997, and are responsible for an $8.3 billion payroll that has tripled during that period, according to an industry-sponsored survey released yesterday.
In New York City, the growth in new-media jobs is outpacing traditional media companies and other key industries, the survey showed. It was commissioned by the New York New Media Association, a trade group, and conducted by PricewaterhouseCoopers, the accounting firm. 
 The survey defined New York's $9.2 billion new-media business as a broad category encompassing Internet-related jobs in publishing, advertising, telecommunications and software design as well as newer dot-com companies. In more than half of the 8,534 companies included in the survey, the core business did not involve the Internet.
As the home to more than a third of these companies (3,148, up from 2,128), Manhattan remains dominant in this industry. But these businesses are proliferating at a faster rate in New Jersey -- to 2,398 today from 974 three years ago, the survey said. Nearly half operate in more than one location. About 1,700 companies in the survey had moved to New York during the last three years.
In perhaps the survey's most surprising finding, 60 percent of the companies said they showed a profit in 1998. These tended to be smaller companies that design Web sites and provide other services to high-profile companies that are spending heavily to develop a brand, said Brian Horey, the president of Equity Growth Management, a venture capital company, and a founder of the New York New Media Association. Indeed, more than half of the companies reporting profits said they made less than $1 million.
The creation of new jobs has not closed the racial and gender gap, however, even in this tight labor market. The Internet work force in New York is overwhelmingly white (74 percent) and male (62 percent), but not as young as it is in the popular imagination. According to the survey, the average age is 35, with 33 percent younger than 30 and 29 percent older than 40. Of the 249,639 jobs identified in the survey, 185,617 were full-time positions, a big increase from 1997.
Another change since then, said Alice Rodd O'Rourke, the executive director of the New York New Media Association, is that the industry no longer feels it is being ignored. "People are more optimistic, more positive, about the role that government has played," she said. As one example, she cited Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani's proposal to repeal the commercial rent tax.
Although the survey does not compare geographical regions -- or make special claims for New York -- Mr. Horey said the region leads the nation in creating material for the Web.
"I think there's no question that we're the primary hub for content," he said. "The West Coast is the hub for infrastructure and technology."
But in a more skeptical report issued in October 1998, the Federal Reserve Bank of New York said that it might be premature to declare that New York is a new-media hub.
The phenomenal expansion may just represent a shift in the way traditional media companies are operating. "Accordingly," that report said, "one could argue that New York City's new-media boom essentially reflects an Internet-technology-driven boom in the city's traditional media industries -- industries that represent a large share of the city's economy."
Jason Bram, an economist and one of the authors of the Federal Reserve Bank's report, said New York's Internet role should not be overstated. "For years and years, New York City has traditionally been behind the nation in new media, but we're sort of catching up," he said. "Maybe we are ahead, in the overall scheme of things, but we're not Silicon Valley."

HEADLINE: Program of Drug-Testing Pregnant Women Draws a Review by the Supreme Court

BYLINE: By LINDA GREENHOUSE 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Feb. 28

 BODY:
The Supreme Court agreed today to decide whether a South Carolina public hospital conducted unconstitutional searches when it tested pregnant women's urine for drugs so it could report illegal drug use to the police.
The case is an appeal on behalf of 10 women who were arrested as a result of the policy, some while they were still weak and bleeding from childbirth, and who later sued. It presents the Supreme Court a narrow but important aspect of a legal and public policy debate that began in the peak years of the crack epidemic of the 1980's: the extent to which a state may intervene to protect fetal health, and the circumstances under which pregnant women may be held criminally responsible for behavior that endangers their fetuses. 
 A federal appeals court ruled last year that while the urinalysis was a search, it was not unconstitutional because it was justified by "a special need beyond normal law-enforcement goals," namely protecting the health and safety of fetuses and newborn babies.
In their appeal, lawyers for the women are arguing that this conclusion amounts to a drastic expansion of what the Supreme Court has previously treated as a "special-needs exception" to the Fourth Amendment's prohibition against unreasonable searches.
"Nearly every application of the criminal law serves some health or safety purpose" and so could be placed outside the protection of the Fourth Amendment under this approach, the women's brief said.
The appeal is supported by a coalition of public health groups, which told the justices that women who need prenatal care the most will be deterred from seeking it if their drug abuse is treated not as a medical problem but as a criminal one.
None of the women who filed the lawsuit were actually prosecuted, and the particular program they are challenging ended in the mid-1990's, after suits were filed and the United States Department of Health and Human Services began an investigation.
However, Lynn Paltrow, one of the plaintiffs' lawyers and director of National Advocates for Pregnant Women, a program of the Women's Law Project in Philadelphia, said today that drug testing of pregnant women remained widespread in South Carolina under the State Supreme Court's interpretation of the state child endangerment law as applying not only to children but also to viable fetuses. The United States Supreme Court refused two years ago to review that state court interpretation.
"No other state has gone as far as South Carolina," Ms. Paltrow said in an interview, adding: "No other state has made it a crime to be pregnant and addicted. These women had a health problem and needed medical care, but they were taken to jail."
The lead plaintiff in the case, Crystal M. Ferguson, tested positive for cocaine during a prenatal visit to the hospital at the Medical University of South Carolina, in Charleston, in June 1991. She agreed to attend a drug abuse counseling program, but tested positive again when she gave birth in August. She was arrested three days later.
According to the city's statistics, 30 women were arrested under the program. Charges against all but two were dropped when the women entered treatment. Some of the women were taken from their hospital rooms in handcuffs or leg shackles. The plaintiffs are seeking damages and an injunction against future drug testing.
Analytically, the case is quite similar to a Fourth Amendment case the Supreme Court accepted for review last week, on whether police checkpoints that subject motorists to drug-detecting dogs are constitutional. The lower court in that case, Indianapolis v. Edmond, rejected the government's argument that the need to detect and deter drug trafficking was a "special need" that justified the warrantless searches.
In the new case, Ferguson v. City of Charleston, No. 99-936, the city asserts that the hospital adopted its policy, in cooperation with the police and the local prosecutor's office, in 1989 in the face of "an epidemic of cocaine use" among its maternity patients.
"The clinical necessity for the drug screens, the health problems associated with maternal cocaine use and the astronomical economic costs of caring for infants suffering from the effects of cocaine use by their mothers all created special needs beyond normal law-enforcement goals," the city maintains.
That was the analysis endorsed in a 2-to-1 decision by the United States Court of Appeals for the Fourth Circuit, in Richmond, which affirmed a jury's verdict in favor of the hospital.
The hospital did not test all its patients, instead singling out those it regarded as most likely to be using drugs. The criteria included having received no prenatal care, or care that was late or incomplete; displaying certain physical symptoms; unexplained preterm labor, and known drug or alcohol abuse in the past. Many of the public hospital's patients are poor and black; of the 10 plaintiffs, nine are black and one is white.
As the crack epidemic hit its peak, states adopted varying approaches to the issue of drug use by pregnant women. Because a number of state courts ruled, unlike the South Carolina Supreme Court, that a fetus could not be considered a child for purposes of prosecuting pregnant women under child abuse laws, some prosecutors argued that mothers delivered illegal drugs to their newborn babies through the umbilical cord during the brief moments between birth and the cutting of the cord.
A woman was convicted in Florida under this theory in 1989, but the Florida Supreme Court overturned the conviction three years later, ruling unanimously that when the State Legislature made "delivery" of illegal drugs a crime, it did not contemplate a prosecution of this sort.
